<b>Preface</b><br>
This textbook, Practical Geography for Secondary Schools, is written specifically<br>
for Form Five and Six students in the United Republic of Tanzania. It is written<br>
in accordance with the 2010 Geography Syllabus for Advanced Level Secondary<br>
<b>Education, Form V-VI issued by then, Ministry of Education and Vocational</b><br>
Training.<br>
The book consists of five chapters, namely Application of statistics in geography,<br>
Field research techniques, Simple survey and mapping, Maps and map interpretation,<br>
and Photograph interpretation. Each chapter contains illustrations, activities and<br>
exercises.You are encouraged to do all the  activities and exercises as well as  other<br>
assignments that your teacher will provide. Doing so, will enable you to develop<br>
the intended competencies.<br>
<b>Tanzania Institute of Education</b><br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>One</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in</b><br>
geography<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
Statistics is a useful field in our daily life. Statistical information is used by different<br>
people in understanding the nature of the world and its dynamics. In this chapter,<br>
you will learn about concepts and types of statistics, role of statistics, statistical<br>
data and variables. You will also learn about methods of data collection, analysis<br>
and organization. Additionally, you will learn about sources of data, presentation<br>
methods and interpretation. The competencies acquired from this chapter will<br>
enable you to find solutions to the day-to-day geographically related issues  and<br>
make informed decision on statistical geographical related events and phenomena<br>
for personal and community development.<br>
<b>Conceptualising statistics</b><br>
<b>Statistics deals with scientific methods</b><br>
of collecting, organising, analysing,<br>
presenting, interpreting numerical<br>
information and making conclusions<br>
on the basis of the associated analyses. It<br>
is concerned with collecting, organising,<br>
summarising and analysing multitude of<br>
numerical data so as to understand the<br>
essential features and relationship of data.<br>
<b>In other words, the term statistics denotes</b><br>
the study and practice of collecting,<br>
analysing and presenting data that have<br>
geographical and real world dimension<br>
such as census and demography.<br>
<b>Statistics provides a basis for decision</b><br>
making when testing hypothesis that<br>
are formulated through the scientific<br>
methods in geography. Statistics aims at<br>
drawing some insights from population<br>
based on measurements taken from<br>
a sample. A population is a group of<br>
individuals, objects or units from which<br>
we intend to know something.<br>
<b>A sample is a collection of representative</b><br>
members of the population from which<br>
the statistical measurements are taken.<br>
<b>It is the subject of the population from</b><br>
which statistical data will be calculated.<br>
<b>For example, if one is interested in</b><br>
examining statistical information such as<br>
the effect of class-size on advanced level<br>
students’ performance in Geography,<br>
he or she will choose representatives<br>
(sample) among the students. It is<br>
therefore from this sample, data will be<br>
collected, analysed and interpreted. The<br>
obtained information can be generalised<br>
to the entire population.<br>
2<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Types of statistics</b><br>
<b>Statistics is basically divided into</b><br>
two major types namely: descriptive<br>
and inferential statistics. Descriptive<br>
statistics is concerned with describing<br>
and summarising the entire population<br>
or sampled data  with the aim of bringing<br>
out the important facts about data. Mean,<br>
median, mode, range, standard deviation<br>
and percentages are some examples of<br>
descriptive statistics. Inferential statistics<br>
is concerned with studying the sample of<br>
the population for the sake of drawing<br>
conclusions based on the analysis of<br>
data. Inferential statistics though not<br>
covered in this book includes probability<br>
distributions and statistical hypothesis<br>
testing. The essence of inferential<br>
statistics is to make inferences about<br>
a population based on the information<br>
obtained from the sample.<br>
<b>Role of statistics</b><br>
<b>Practically, statistics plays a wide role in</b><br>
simplifying complex mass of data and<br>
present them in a comprehensive way<br>
so that they can easily be understood<br>
and interpreted.  Large raw data can be<br>
summarised into percentages, modes<br>
and means hence become understood<br>
easily compared to raw mass of data.<br>
<b>Moreover, statistical representation of</b><br>
data in form of histograms, bar charts<br>
or pie charts enables one to understand<br>
the  information easily. Statistics present<br>
data in a comprehensive and definite<br>
form. Statistics enables drawing of sound<br>
numerical conclusions that are more<br>
convincing as opposed to conclusions<br>
stated verbally. For example, it sounds<br>
clear and appealing to say 70% of the<br>
candidates sat for NECTA 2021 passed<br>
as opposed to general conclusion that<br>
most of the candidates sat for NECTA<br>
in 2021 passed.<br>
<b>Additionally, statistics present, interpret</b><br>
and predict conditions. Statistics present<br>
conditions by using for example, pie<br>
charts, histograms or bar charts of the<br>
studied phenomenon which can be easily<br>
understood. Some statistical conditions<br>
for presenting data allow the predictions<br>
of the future phenomenon thus giving<br>
clues to finding ways to offset them or<br>
reduce their occurrence in future. Again,<br>
it provides easy way of classifying<br>
numerical data. That is, the method of<br>
classification in statistics often provides<br>
clear features of a variable that is under<br>
investigation. For example, statistical<br>
methods provide an appropriate method<br>
of classifying two or more data by<br>
bringing out the maximum, minimum<br>
and standard deviation of the various<br>
categories.<br>
<b>Moreover, statistics is useful in</b><br>
evaluation. For example, it is easy to<br>
evaluate the Tanzania development vision<br>
of 2025 with statistics. It is also useful<br>
in budgeting at both family and national<br>
level. For example, free basic education<br>
in Tanzania is provided to the known<br>
number of students. Statistics is the basis<br>
of projection of different geographical<br>
events such as climate and outputs of<br>
agriculture. It acts as an evaluative tool<br>
in different fields, such as demography,<br>
education and business. Statistics is<br>
used to evaluate the effectiveness of<br>
the policies under implementation to<br>
understand to what extent the expected<br>
3<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
results will be achieved or not. For<br>
example, in the free basic education<br>
policy, statistics is normally applied<br>
in monitoring the proper utilization of<br>
funds, human resource, construction<br>
and maintenance of learning facilities<br>
among others according to the demand.<br>
<b>Statistics helps to project the future</b><br>
trends and the impact of a particular<br>
social or geographical phenomena. With<br>
the use of probability statistics, we can<br>
accurately project the outcome of natural<br>
and cultural phenomena. For instance,<br>
basing on the existing situations in the<br>
climatic factors, it is possible to spell out<br>
what will happen after a considerable<br>
span of time, and how the society and<br>
environment in particular will suffer.<br>
Statistics is a basis for policy formulation.<br>
<b>Statistical facts and evidence are usually</b><br>
among the international and national<br>
policy foundations. For instance, the<br>
daily reports on the number of Covid-19<br>
infected people worldwide, pressurise<br>
nations to formulate local policies and<br>
to agree with the international policies<br>
such as vaccination. Furthermore,<br>
demographic data may indicate the rate<br>
at which the population of a country<br>
is growing and statistically get clues<br>
in policy formulation for resources<br>
distribution to cater for the needs. Lastly,<br>
it provides an easy way of comparing<br>
data. Statistical analyses make it possible<br>
to compare the relevant analysed data<br>
from two or more different studied areas<br>
or time. Volumes of data from two or<br>
more sites can be compared basing<br>
on descriptive statistics such as mean,<br>
median and mode of their distributions.<br>
<b>Statistical data and variables</b><br>
<b>The word data is  derived from  Latin</b><br>
word datum meaning ‘something given’.<br>
Data can be of numeric, text, graphic,<br>
art, image or symbol that researchers<br>
obtain from the subjects, respondents or<br>
participants of the study. It is a raw or<br>
unprocessed information about certain<br>
phenomenon or event. The raw data<br>
needs to be processed by subjecting it<br>
to some statistical analysis in order to<br>
obtain useful information for decision<br>
making. The data that has been processed<br>
in a context and assigned to give their<br>
meaning is referred to as information<br>
(Figure 1.1). Largely, statistics deals<br>
with quantitative data which exist in<br>
numeral and to some extent integrate the<br>
qualitative data. Data can be obtained<br>
from weather and climate, demography,<br>
transport and communication, as well as<br>
agricultural production. When one does<br>
any sort of inquiry or research, he or she<br>
will collect data of different kinds.  In<br>
fact, data can be seen as the essential raw<br>
material of any kind of research. Data<br>
can be managed by using information<br>
technology in the form of bytes stored<br>
in electronic memory (database).<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>An individual piece of data in a data set</b><br>
is called a score  or observation whereas<br>
a quantity to which any of a set of values<br>
such as (scores or observations) is<br>
assigned  is called variable. For example,<br>
the quantities height, weight or age are<br>
variables, while the values assigned to<br>
them are data. Data can be collected from<br>
the respondents or subjects by using<br>
different methods such as survey, focus<br>
group discussion, document review and<br>
interview. Data may also be collected<br>
through any other method, depending<br>
on the needs of the research.<br>
<b>Nature of data</b><br>
<b>Data may be classified in four categories</b><br>
namely: Discrete, continuous, individual<br>
and grouped data. Discrete data is a<br>
numerical type of data that can only be<br>
given in whole, concrete numbers with<br>
specific and fixed data values determined<br>
by counting. Examples of discrete data<br>
include; number of people, computers,<br>
animals, houses and eggs.<br>
<b>Continuous data include complex</b><br>
numbers and varying data values that<br>
are measured over a specific interval or<br>
within a range. Values in these data set<br>
often carry fraction or decimal points.<br>
Examples of continuous data include;<br>
eight, wind speed, temperature, altitude<br>
and distance.<br>
<b>For example temperature of 23.15° C in a</b><br>
range 0° C to 30° C or an altitude of 483.<br>
23 m in a range 0 to 1000 m.  Individual<br>
data are data through which an exact<br>
value is given for each individual item<br>
in a sample. For example, population<br>
of Tanzania, and number of students at<br>
<b>Mjimwema secondary school which may</b><br>
be 200 students are types of individual<br>
data. Grouped data are data by which no<br>
exact figure is quoted but several values<br>
fall within certain classes or groups.  For<br>
example, grouping people according to<br>
their age ranging from 1-5, 6-10, 11-15,<br>
16-20 to 85 and above.<br>
<b>Classification of statistical data</b><br>
<b>Data are classified into three main</b><br>
categories on the basis of sorting or<br>
distribution; unit of measurement; and<br>
methods and sources. Basing on sorting<br>
or distribution data is classified into<br>
ungrouped and grouped data. Ungrouped<br>
data (raw data) is concerned with raw<br>
facts that have been collected from the<br>
experiment or study and usually not<br>
sorted into categories. For example; 10,<br>
15, 20, 25 and 30 vehicles. Furthermore,<br>
when expressing number of houses,<br>
people, employee and eggs we use<br>
whole numbers and we normally list<br>
them. Grouped data (array data) refers to<br>
a set of raw numerical facts that has been<br>
sorted or distributed  into categories. For<br>
example; Tsh 10 001- 20 000, 20 001-30<br>
000 and 30 001 - 40 000 or heights of<br>
people in centimetres 151-160, 161- 170,<br>
171 - 180 and 181-190.<br>
On the basis of unit of measurements,<br>
there are two types of data namely,<br>
categorical (qualitative) and numerical<br>
(quantitative). Categorical data are<br>
measures that normally describe the<br>
characteristics of the studied subject<br>
and can be in the form of text, graphics,<br>
art, image or symbol. Categorical data<br>
are further grouped into nominal and<br>
5<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
ordinal data. Nominal data are labeled or<br>
named data without quantitative measure<br>
and often without logical sequence. For<br>
example, marital status, occupation,<br>
common elements of weather and Yes/<br>
<b>No responses. Likewise, ordinal data</b><br>
are labeled or named data without<br>
quantitative measure but with logical<br>
sequence. For example, satisfaction,<br>
opinion or feelings. Numerical data,<br>
are measures expressed in numbers.<br>
<b>Numerical data are further grouped into</b><br>
discrete and continuous data. Numerical<br>
data corresponds to interval and ratio<br>
data.<br>
On the basis of methods and sources,<br>
data can either be primary or secondary.<br>
<b>Primary data refers to the first-hand raw</b><br>
facts from an experiment or field. They<br>
are original and freshly gathered from<br>
the source. Primary data have several<br>
uses or advantages including: originality<br>
and independence which increase<br>
validity and enhance reliability of the<br>
data. They are used in both quantitative<br>
and qualitative studies. These are the<br>
only data  which can explore from the<br>
hidden information through appropriate<br>
approaches. The data can also be<br>
transformed to secondary data after<br>
analysis. Primary data however, are<br>
likely to be  influenced by expenses and<br>
time consumed in collecting them. They<br>
are generally difficult to collect due to<br>
their complexity and high demand of<br>
commitment. Unlike the primary data,<br>
secondary data means second-hand<br>
data in published or unpublished form<br>
that was earlier collected by some one<br>
else and often passed through statistical<br>
analysis and usage. Secondary data<br>
can be obtained from public or private<br>
offices and  searched from websites and<br>
internet sources. Secondary data have<br>
a number of merits to researchers such<br>
as: easy manageability; time saving and<br>
low cost of accessing them. In addition,<br>
some are readily available in analysed<br>
form; useful in updating the existing data<br>
and demand less field work. However,<br>
the  applicability of  secondary data are<br>
limited in terms of lack of means to<br>
validate them; demand expertise; and<br>
less accuracy and reliability compared<br>
to primary data. Table 1.1 indicates<br>
the differences between primary and<br>
secondary data.<br>

<b>Types of  variables</b><br>
A variable is any characteristic, number or quantity of a person, object or phenomenon<br>
that can be measured or counted.  A variable may also be called a data item that<br>
varies in magnitude. There are different ways in which statistical variables are<br>
described depending on the way they are studied. Variables are grouped basing<br>
on the scale of measurement, study designs and association. Basing on the  scale<br>
of measurement variables are described as numerical and categorical; while in the<br>
study design they are described as active and attribute variables; and  in causal<br>
relationships they are dependent and independent variables (Figure 1.2).<br>
<b>Variables</b><br>
<b>Study design</b><br>

Figure 1.2: Schematic representation of variables and respective classification criteria<br>
Numerical variables are  variables that can be described as either continuous or<br>
discrete variables. The measurements for these variables are numbers. Continuous<br>
variable refers to quantitative variable which take any value in measurements<br>
normally within some ranges. They can be expressed in decimal places or fractions,<br>
Example of continuous variables are; height, time, age and temperature. Discrete<br>
variables are quantitative variables, which take isolated values in measurements.<br>
In other words, they are variables which cannot be expressed in form of a fraction<br>
or decimal places. Examples of discrete variables include the number of registered<br>
cars, houses and children which are all measured as whole number.<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Categorical variables categorical</b><br>
variables are qualitative in nature as they<br>
are represented by non-numeric values.<br>
<b>They normally take a form of text, image</b><br>
or symbol. Based on the nature of data,<br>
categorical variables are classified as<br>
ordinal or nominal variable. Ordinal<br>
variables deal with the value that can<br>
be logically ordered or ranked higher or<br>
lower than another without establishing a<br>
numeric difference. Examples of ordinal<br>
categorical variables include academic<br>
grade such as  A, B and C; and clothing<br>
size such as small, medium, large and<br>
extra-large. Nominal variable deals with<br>
values and the classification of variables<br>
in which the logical sequencing of<br>
variables is not applicable.<br>
<b>Active and attribute variables differ with</b><br>
regard to manipulation. Active variables<br>
are those that can be manipulated,<br>
changed or controlled. Active variables<br>
are independent variables. While attribute<br>
variables are variable that cannot be<br>
manipulated, changed or controlled,<br>
and that reflect the characteristics of<br>
the study population. For example age,<br>
weight, height, income and level of<br>
education.<br>
<b>Independent  and dependent variables</b><br>
<b>Independent variables are not usually</b><br>
affected or influenced with external<br>
factor for change. For example,<br>
someone’s age cannot be influenced<br>
to change by either eating or better<br>
health services. Dependent variables<br>
are easily affected or influenced by<br>
external factors for change. For example,<br>
temperature, amount of rainfall, body<br>
weight and academic performance are<br>
all likely to be affected or influenced by<br>
external factors. Generally, a dependent<br>
variable depends on the independent<br>
variable. The statistical measure that<br>
indicates the extent to which two or<br>
more variables are related is known as<br>
correlation. Correlation is concerned<br>
with quantifying the degree and direction<br>
to which two variables are related.<br>
<b>It can be positive, negative or zero</b><br>
relationship. Though in correlation it<br>
is observed that change in one variable<br>
may result to a change on another still<br>
such a change on the latter does not often<br>
imply causation. Sometimes there may<br>
be an unknown factor that affects both<br>
variables in the same way. A relevant<br>
example of correlation is good market<br>
price of agricultural produce which may<br>
result into the increased production of<br>
crop since farmers enjoy good prices.<br>
<b>Similarly, a  relatively large  Geography</b><br>
class may be associated with poor<br>
performance of the students.<br>
<b>Relationship between variables</b><br>
<b>Variables are related in three different</b><br>
ways namely positive, negative and no<br>
relationship as subsequently described<br>
as follows.<br>
(i) Positive relationship.<br>
<b>This is the relationship which exists</b><br>
when an increase in independent variable<br>
results to an increase in  dependent<br>
variable and  a decrease in independent<br>
variable results to a decrease in   dependent<br>
variable. For example, enrolment of form<br>
five students at Mtakuja High School<br>
increased with time for four consecutive<br>
years (2012-2015) as shown in Table 1.2<br>
and Figure 1.3.<br>

<b>Statistical measures</b><br>
<b>The common important statistical</b><br>
measures that are used to summarise and<br>
describe data are: measures of central<br>
tendency, measure of dispersion and<br>
measures of asymmetry (skewness).<br>
<b>Measures of central tendency and</b><br>
measures of dispersion are also called<br>
summary statistics. The measures of<br>
central tendency computed from the<br>
entire population are called parameters<br>
whereas the measures obtained from the<br>
sample are called statistics. Parameter is<br>
a descriptive property of the population<br>
while statistics is a descriptive property<br>
of a  sample (Figure 1.6). Regardless of<br>
the similarity of  procedures adopted in<br>
obtaining parameters and statistics, they<br>
are often expressed by different symbols.<br>
<b>For example, the mean for population</b><br>
is symbolised with a Greek letter µ<br>
(pronounced as mew) while that of a<br>
sample is symbolised by Latin letter x  (x<br>
bar). Still the difference is also maintained<br>
in expressing measure of dispersion. For<br>
example, standard deviation which is one<br>
of the parameters of dispersion when<br>
addressing a population is abbreviated<br>
by a Greek letter σ (pronounced as<br>
sigma) while when addressing with a<br>
sample standard deviation as one of the<br>
sample statistic is expressed by small<br>
letter “s”. Measure of central tendency<br>
and measure of dispersion enable a<br>
geographer to summarise the data in<br>
a frequency distribution with a single<br>
number and make interpretation of the<br>
obtained statistical information.<br>
<b>Population</b><br>
mean = µ<br>
<b>Sample</b><br>
mean =  x<br>
<b>Figure 1.6: Illustration of mean for a</b><br>
population and sample<br>
<b>Measures of central tendency</b><br>
<b>In frequency distributions, the tabulated</b><br>
values show a distinctive tendency to<br>
cluster or to group around a typical<br>
central value. The characteristics of the<br>
data to concentrate the values around a<br>
central part of distributions is termed<br>
as central tendency of the data. In other<br>
words, central tendency is the average.<br>
<b>An average is a single value within a</b><br>
range of the data that is used to represent<br>
the entire values in a series. Both central<br>
tendency and average are concerned with<br>
central values. Based on these concepts<br>
measure of central tendency (average)<br>
is a single value that is used to describe<br>
a set of data by identifying the central<br>
position within the given set of data. It<br>
is the value tends to lie centrally within<br>
the set of data arranged according to the<br>
magnitude that is in ascending order.<br>
<b>Measure of central tendency enables one</b><br>
to compare two or more distributions<br>
pertaining to the same period of time or<br>
within the same distribution over time.<br>
<b>For example, the average consumption</b><br>
of tea in two different geographical areas<br>
for two years, say 2003 and 2004, can<br>
be obtained  by means of an average.<br>
12<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>The most commonly used measures</b><br>
of central tendency are mean, mode<br>
and median. All measures of central<br>
tendency are useful, however, in<br>
different conditions some have become<br>
more appropriate than others. It is<br>
thus, important to understand a good<br>
measure of central tendency. Often<br>
a good measure possesses some<br>
main characteristics such as easy to<br>
understand; simple to compute; based<br>
on all observations; uniquely defined;<br>
has possibility of further algebraic<br>
treatment; Possesses sampling stability<br>
and  not unduly affected by extreme<br>
values. Simple statistical measures of<br>
central tendency can be calculated by<br>
various formula using both individual<br>
and grouped data as indicated in the<br>
subsequent subsections:<br>
(a) Mean<br>
<b>This is sometimes referred to as arithmetic</b><br>
mean. It should, be noted that mean is not<br>
a synonym of average. Instead measures<br>
of central tendency and averages are<br>
used interchangeably. Mean is the most<br>
popular measure of central tendency and<br>
may be defined as the value which we<br>
get by dividing the total of the value of<br>
various  given items in a series by the<br>
total number of items. Mean is often<br>
used in reporting the weather and climate<br>
specifically for elements like temperature<br>
and rainfall. For instance, in Tanzania,<br>
we have been frequently hearing  the<br>
reports and information related to the<br>
changes in mean annual temperatures<br>
and rainfall from sources such as the<br>
<b>Tanzania Meteorological Authority</b><br>
(TMA). Thus, mean can be used to<br>
compute the central values of different<br>
variables and explain its importance on<br>
the phenomena in question. For example,<br>
the mean agricultural and livestock<br>
produce/yield. Mean is categorised<br>
into arithmetic mean, geometric mean,<br>
harmonic mean and quadratic mean. In<br>
this book, only arithmetic mean has been<br>
covered.<br>
(i) The mean of ungrouped data<br>
<b>This is obtained by diving the sum of</b><br>
value or scores in the range of individual<br>
data by the number of observations. It is<br>
the sum of all values in the set divided<br>
by the number of observations.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
calculating mean of ungrouped data.<br>
1.	 Find the sum of all data values or<br>
observation<br>
2.	 Divide by the total number of<br>
observation<br>

<b>Calculating mean by assumed mean</b><br>
method<br>
<b>When the values or scores under</b><br>
observation are extremely large and in<br>
fractions, the use of the direct method<br>
becomes inconvenient. As a result the<br>
use of the assumed mean method can<br>
be done. Assumed mean  method is also<br>
known as the assumed mean method.<br>
<b>The method uses the concept of the</b><br>
arbitrary mean or assumed mean.<br>
<b>Example:</b><br>
<b>Find the mean of school Y students’</b><br>
scores in Geography using the mean<br>
by assumed mean method.<br>

<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)	 Find the midpoint/class mark of<br>

(ii)	  Assume one value as mean. In the<br>
table, the largest f is on intervals 30-<br>
40, which also happens to be almost<br>
in the centre of the distribution, 35<br>
is taken as assumed mean;<br>
(iii)	Subtract the arbitrary mean from<br>
each value of mid-point and the<br>
resultant value is shown in column<br>
d;<br>
(iv)	 Multiply each d by respective<br>
frequencies (f) to get (fd);<br>
(v)	 Find the algebraic sum of the ∑fd<br>
and divide this sum by ∑f<br>
(vi)	  Sometime ∑fd will be positive and<br>
sometimes negative;<br>
(vii)	 Multiply this value by class mark.<br>
<b>This gives the correction factor to</b><br>
be applied to the Assumed Mean;<br>
and<br>
(viii)	The assumed Mean + the<br>
correction factor = the Actual<br>
<b>Mean. Correction factor is</b><br>
computed by ∑f .<br>

<b>Therefore, the central scores for the</b><br>
school Y students in Geography is 33.45<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
mean<br>
<b>The use of mean in analysing and</b><br>
summarising data has both advantages<br>
and disadvantages. Some of the<br>
advantages are in cases of small data set<br>
it is fast and easy to calculate; it includes<br>
all values in the distribution; it is useful<br>
for statistical summarisation of data;<br>
and it is used for making comparison<br>
in statistical data. It is also widely<br>
understood compared to other averages<br>
and suited to further statistical analysis.<br>
<b>Some of the disadvantages are: the mean</b><br>
is highly distorted by outliers. Outliers<br>
refer to the extremely high or low values<br>
in the observation. It is also impossible<br>
to locate the mean by inspection as in<br>
the case of mode and median. Value<br>
of mean will be effective only if the<br>
frequency is normally distributed.<br>
<b>Otherwise in case of skewness the</b><br>
results become ineffective.<br>
(b) Median<br>
<b>The median is value of the middle</b><br>
item.  This is a middle item for a set of<br>
data that has been arranged in order of<br>
magnitude or the point below and above<br>
50 % of the scores in distribution (the<br>
mid-point in a distribution). There are<br>
for grouped data and un-grouped data.<br>
<b>Median for individual data</b><br>

descending order.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>If the total number of items is in</b><br>
odd, add 1 to the total number of<br>
observations (n) then divide by 2<br>
to acquire the position of median.<br>
(iii)	 The value of the median for un-<br>
grouped data is given by the<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
median<br>
<b>The major advantages from the use of</b><br>
median in statistical geography are:<br>
it is easy to understand because of<br>
considering half way point of the data<br>
set under observation. Therefore, it is<br>
suitable for distributions with extreme<br>
values. The median is not distorted by<br>
the extreme value(s) in distribution; and<br>
relevant in skewed data distribution.<br>
<b>However, median has some limitations</b><br>
such as unsuitability for further<br>
mathematical treatment or advanced<br>
statistical processing; not based on all<br>
values under observation; compared to<br>
mean, median is highly affected by the<br>
fluctuation of sampling. Furthermore,<br>
it is not reliable in testing geographical<br>
hypothesis, particularly in advanced<br>
levels of the field of Geography.<br>
(c)  Mode<br>
<b>Mode is the most frequent score in</b><br>
a distribution. The mode of a set of<br>
data is the observation which occurs<br>
most frequently.  It is also defined as a<br>
point of maximum frequency density in<br>
continuous data. It is presented by the<br>
highest column (s) in the histogram.<br>
<b>In some data set, the mode may not</b><br>
exist, and if it does, there may be two<br>
or more modes. The occurring mode<br>
can be either uni-modal, bimodal or<br>
multi-modal. Mode is one of the useful<br>
averages in the field of climatology as<br>
it can be used in the classification of<br>
weather patterns in various geographical<br>
areas. In case of Tanzania, many areas<br>
receive rainfall per season called uni-<br>
modal areas while the rest of areas<br>
receive two seasonal rainfall called<br>
bimodal areas. Some of the areas that<br>
receive bimodal rainfall include Dar es<br>
Salaam, Musoma, Morogoro, Tanga,<br>
<b>Kagera and some parts of Mwanza</b><br>
region. There are also areas that receive<br>
three seasonal rainfall per year, such a<br>
pattern is called trimodal.<br>
<b>Mode for the ungrouped data</b><br>
<b>Uni-modal occurs when there is one</b><br>
(1) mode value in a distribution. For<br>
example; 4, 5, 6, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 10, 10.<br>
<b>Mode is 10. Because; it has occurred</b><br>
three times.<br>
<b>Bimodal is when set of data has two</b><br>
modes, for example, 1, 2, 3, 3, 4, 4, 4,<br>
5, 5, 6, 6, 6 11. The modes are 4 and 6.<br>
<b>This is  because; they have both occurred</b><br>
three times.<br>
<b>Multi-modal exist when data set has</b><br>
more than two modals, for example; 1,<br>
3, 5, 8, 9, 2, 4, 8, 7, 20, 5, 6, 2, 1, 2, 3,<br>
4, 5, 5, 6, 7, 8, 8, 9, 20, 2.<br>
The modes are 2, 5 and 8. This is because;<br>
these numbers have more occurrences<br>
than others.<br>
<b>Mode for the grouped data</b><br>
<b>In grouped data, mode can be calculated</b><br>
by the following formula:<br>
<b>Mode = Lm +</b><br>
t1<br>
t1 + t2<br>
⎛<br>

<b>That is the difference between</b><br>
frequency of modal class and<br>
frequency of post modal class.<br>
i  = class width or the size of the class<br>
interval.<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of a</b><br>
mode<br>
<b>Mode is of paramount importance in</b><br>
geography. Some of its advantages are<br>
that: it is not affected by the occurrence<br>
of a few extreme values under the<br>
distributions; and it, determines various<br>
phenomena such as the magnitude of<br>
agricultural production or the trend<br>
of commodities. In addition, it is the<br>
only average to be opted for categorical<br>
data; easy to read and interpret in the<br>
given data; and easy to understand,<br>
calculate and interpret. However, mode<br>
is disapproved of the various limitations.<br>
<b>It is not strictly defined in such a way</b><br>
that it is unstable with large samples;<br>
it is not based on all data values under<br>
observation; and it is not  suitable for<br>
further mathematical treatment. Its<br>
existence when data are the same in some<br>
of the distributions discourages its use<br>
in analysis of data and its usefulness<br>
is less common in advanced statistical<br>
geography. Compared to mean, mode<br>
is severely affected by the fluctuation<br>
of sampling.<br>
<b>Relationship between different</b><br>
measures of central tendency<br>
<b>The various measures of central value</b><br>
give us one single figure that represents<br>
the entire data. But the average alone<br>
cannot adequately describe a set of<br>
observations, unless all observations are<br>
the same. Measures of central tendency<br>
often portray some relationships between<br>
them. The relationship is determined by<br>
the nature and pattern of distribution<br>
of scores in the sample or population.<br>
<b>Normally, the relationship between</b><br>
mean, median and mode of a population<br>
generates symmetrical and skewed<br>
curves. When the population mean,<br>
population median and  mode are all<br>
located at the centre, they result to a<br>
symmetrical bell-shaped curve called<br>
normal distribution curve in which the<br>
curve can be divided into two equal<br>
halves (one half is a mirror image of<br>
the other) as shown in Figure1.8 (a).<br>
<b>In this case mean = median = mode. In</b><br>
case the distribution is skewed to the<br>
right, it is positive skewness in which<br>
the longer tail is on the right side, then<br>
mean &gt; median &gt; mode. Generally,<br>
income distribution is skewed to the right<br>
where a large number of families have<br>
relatively low income and only a small<br>
number of families have extremely high<br>
income. In this case the mean is pulled<br>
up by the extreme high incomes and the<br>
relation among these three measures is<br>
as shown in Figure1.8 (b):  that is mean<br>
&gt; median &gt; mode.<br>
(a)  Normal distribution curve<br>
(b) Positive skewed distribution curve<br>
23<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
(c) Negative skewed distribution curve<br>
<b>Figure 1. 8: Types of distribution curves</b><br>
<b>When a distribution is skewed to the left</b><br>
negative skewness in which the tail is on<br>
the left, then mode&gt; median&gt; mean. In<br>
this case, the mean is pulled down below<br>
the median by extremely low values as<br>
shown in Figure1.8 (c).<br>
<b>The general limitations of measures</b><br>
of central tendency<br>
<b>On summarising data, various measures</b><br>
of central value give us one single figure<br>
that represents the entire data from<br>
sample or population being studied. It<br>
should be noted that the average alone<br>
could not adequately describe a set of<br>
observations, unless all the observations<br>
are the same. Mean, median and mode<br>
fall short of indicating the extent of<br>
dispersion or variability in distribution.<br>
<b>Dispersion and variability are important</b><br>
because they enable us to understand the<br>
pattern of the data, which is limitedly<br>
explained by the measures of central<br>
tendency. In some cases, two or more<br>
distributions can have same central value<br>
yet there can be a wide discrepancy in<br>
the formation of distribution. In this<br>
ground, measures of dispersion become<br>
helpful for us in understanding more<br>
characteristics of a distribution.<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Measures of dispersion</b><br>
<b>The degree to which numerical data</b><br>
tend to spread about an average value<br>
is called dispersion or variation of data.<br>
<b>The measure of dispersion is also known</b><br>
as measure of variation or spread. Two<br>
distributions can have identical means<br>
and medians, yet their difference can<br>
be identified by using  measures of<br>
dispersion. For example, the distribution<br>
of these data A: 18, 20, 25, 35, 39 and<br>
<b>B: 2, 3, 25, 30, 75.</b><br>
<b>The mean in both of these distributions</b><br>
is 27 and the median in both is 25.<br>
However, the distribution differs greatly.<br>
Just by observing the distribution in A,<br>
the scores are closer together and tend<br>
to cluster around the mean distribution<br>
while in B the scores are much more<br>
spread out. The difference of two or<br>
more distributions is termed by the<br>
statisticians as variability. Measures<br>
of dispersion show how spread out or<br>
dispersed the data are, something that<br>
cannot be achieved by the measures of<br>
central tendency. As a result, the central<br>
tendency becomes less appropriate for<br>
some analysis.<br>
<b>Moreover, consider a statement that</b><br>
by 2014 the mean annual salary for<br>
public servants in Tanzania was<br>
200 billion. This claim falls short of<br>
hiding information on the fact that<br>
some workers earned far less than the<br>
mentioned amount and others earned<br>
more than 200 billion. Therefore,<br>
knowing the mean is insufficient to<br>
understand the entire distribution of<br>
workers’ salaries. This limitation pave<br>
way to the need for applying other<br>
statistical measures that are capable of<br>
describing the spread or variability that<br>
exists within the distribution of a given<br>
data set. The commonly used measures<br>
of spread are range, mean deviation,<br>
variance and standard deviation.<br>
(a) Range<br>
<b>Range is the difference between the</b><br>
smallest and the largest value in the<br>
distribution. The overall range represents<br>
the distance between the highest and<br>
lowest scores in the distribution.<br>
<b>Range for  an individual data</b><br>
<b>Range can be calculated by subtracting</b><br>
the smallest value (S) from the largest<br>
value (L) in the distribution . Range is<br>
the difference between the maximum<br>
value and the minimum value of the data<br>
set. Range takes into consideration the<br>
extreme values in a data set.<br>
<b>Example:</b><br>
<b>Study the following distribution of data</b><br>
and calculate the range: 2, 3, 5, 7, 14,<br>
15, 24, and 25.<br>
<b>Solution:</b><br>
<b>From the given data maximum value</b><br>
is 25 and the minimum value is 2.<br>
<b>The range = Maximum value (L) –</b><br>
minimum value (S) = 25 – 2 = 23<br>
<b>Therefore, the range = 23</b><br>

<b>Coefficient of range</b><br>
<b>Coefficient of range is the ratio of difference between the highest and lowest</b><br>
value of frequency to the sum of the highest and lowest value of frequency. It is<br>
a relative measure of the distribution based on the range of any given data set.<br>
<b>The coefficient of range is calculated by the formula: L −S</b><br>
<b>L + S</b><br>
⎛<br>
⎝⎜<br>
⎞<br>
⎠⎟. This is the relative<br>
measure.<br>
Whereby;<br>
<b>L is the highest value in the frequency distribution</b><br>
S is the  lowest value in the frequency distribution.<br>
In other words;<br>
<b>The coefficient of range =</b><br>
<b>Highest value in frequency – Lowest value in frequency</b><br>
<b>Highest value in frequency + Lowest value in frequency</b><br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
The coefficient of range is more appropriate for the purposes of comparison.<br>

<b>Set 1	 28 – 8= 20</b><br>
<b>Set 2	 50 – 30 = 20</b><br>
<b>Coefficient of range in Set 1 is:</b><br>
28−8<br>
28+8 = 0.55<br>
<b>Coefficient of range in Set 2 is:</b><br>
50 −30<br>
50 + 30 = 0.25<br>
<b>The coefficient of range tells the degree</b><br>
of dispersion in a set of data. The larger<br>
the value, the higher the dispersion.<br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
the range<br>
<b>Range is opted because it is easy to</b><br>
calculate and understand. It also<br>
gives a quick estimate of variability<br>
in distribution. Regardless of the<br>
simplicity of obtaining range just by<br>
considering only two values of a set<br>
of data, it is important to many fields<br>
and individuals including statisticians,<br>
and geographers in many ways. It<br>
is used in circumstances which one<br>
intends to get a quick understanding<br>
of  variability or a set of data. In case<br>
of small sample range is considered as<br>
a sufficient measure of the variability.<br>
<b>For example, it is often used in quality</b><br>
control where a continuous check<br>
on the variability of raw materials<br>
or finished products is required. The<br>
range is also an appropriate measure<br>
in weather forecast. For example, in<br>
daily reports on weather forecast from<br>
<b>Tanzania Meteorological Authority</b><br>
(TMA) we normally hear of maximum<br>
and minimum temperatures, rainfall,<br>
wind, and humidity and sunshine. Such<br>
statistical information may seem useless<br>
to some people but it is very important<br>
for others whose plans and activities<br>
need updates on weather so as to make<br>
appropriate decisions.<br>
<b>Range has some drawbacks. It is</b><br>
highly affected by the extreme values<br>
in a distribution, hence it cannot depict<br>
the nature of dispersion of items in a<br>
distribution. Range can mislead the<br>
interpretation of data if there were<br>
some errors during collection of raw<br>
data. It is also based on only two items,<br>
hence does not cover all the items in a<br>
distribution. The other weakness is its<br>
susceptibility to wide fluctuations from<br>
sample based on the same population. It<br>
fails to give any idea about the pattern<br>
of distribution; and in the case of open-<br>
27<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
ended distributions, it is impossible to<br>
compute range.<br>
(b)	Mean deviation (MD)<br>
<b>The mean deviation is also known as</b><br>
the average deviation. Mean deviation<br>
denotes the amount by which individual<br>
value deviates from the mean in<br>
irrespective of sign (+ or   ̶ ). The negative<br>
and positive signs are ignored during<br>
computation since the deviations are<br>
always equal irrespective of their signs.<br>
<b>It is the average difference between</b><br>
various measurements and the mean.<br>
<b>Mean deviation for un grouped data</b><br>
<b>The formula for computing the mean</b><br>
deviation is given below.<br>
<b>Mean deviation =</b><br>
<b>Σ xi −x</b><br>
n<br>
<b>Where:</b><br>
xi −x<br>
=  deviation of an item from<br>
the mean irrespective of positive<br>
and negative signs. It is the absolute<br>
difference between each value and the<br>
mean.<br>
n =  the total number of observations<br>
∑= summation<br>
x= observation or values<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
calculating mean deviation<br>
(i)	 Compute the mean of the given data<br>
set;<br>
(ii)	 Find the individual deviation by<br>
subtracting the mean from each of<br>
the given data set as shown in the<br>
table below;<br>
(iii)	Sum the absolute deviation; and<br>
(iv)	Divide by the total number of<br>
observations or values.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
mean deviation<br>
<b>The use of mean deviation in statistics</b><br>
has a number of advantages. It is simple<br>
to understand and easily calculated;<br>
it also takes into consideration all the<br>
dataset under observation and it is<br>
less affected by the extreme values.<br>
<b>Since the deviations are taken from the</b><br>
central value, it is possible to make a<br>
meaningful comparison of the setup of<br>
different distributions.<br>
29<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>However, mean deviation has the</b><br>
following weaknesses: it falls short of<br>
allowing further algebraic treatment; and<br>
it sometimes fails to give accurate results.<br>
<b>Mean deviation gives best results when</b><br>
deviations are taken from the median<br>
rather than the mean. In a series, with<br>
wide variations in items, mean deviation<br>
is not a satisfactory measure; and from<br>
mathematical perspectives, the method<br>
is wrong for it disregards the algebraic<br>
signs when deviations are taken from the<br>
mean. Because of these limitations mean<br>
deviation is seldom used in geography<br>
studies. Such limitations pave the way<br>
to a better standard deviation which is<br>
often used in geography.<br>
(c)	Variance<br>
<b>Population variance often denoted by</b><br>
s2 is the mean of the squares of the<br>
differences between each data value and<br>
the mean. For the case of the sample,<br>
variance often denoted by S2. Variance<br>
is also a measure of the  spread between<br>
given scores or data set. Measures the<br>
distance  of each number from the mean<br>
and from one number and the other.<br>
<b>Therefore, the large variance indicates</b><br>
that scores are  far from each other as<br>
well. It can be culculated for ungrouped<br>
and grouped data.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
calculating variance<br>
(i)  	Calculate the mean x<br>
(ii) 	Find the deviation from the mean<br>
x −x<br>
(<br>
).<br>
(iii) Square the deviations from the mean<br>

(iv) Sum all the square deviations and<br>
apply the formula.<br>

(d)	Standard deviation<br>
<b>It is the value which shows how far</b><br>
the scores are spread from the normal<br>
or how the numbers are spread in a<br>
distribution. It is the most common<br>
index of variability. Standard deviation<br>
(SD) can be calculated for individual or<br>
grouped data.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>Procedures involved in calculation of</b><br>
the standard deviation are:<br>
(i)	 Calculate the mean of distribution;<br>
(ii)	 Subtract the mean from each score;<br>
(iii)	Square each of these scores;<br>
(iv)	Add  all the squares of these scores;<br>
(v)	 Divide the total by the number of<br>
observation;<br>
(vi)	The result obtained in (v) above is<br>
called the variance; and<br>
(vii)	Take the square root of the<br>
variance; this will give the standard<br>
deviation(s).<br>
<b>The formula for computing standard</b><br>
deviation is given as:<br>
<b>S =</b><br>
<b>Σ</b><br>
i=n<br>
n<br>
xi −x<br>

)<br>

<b>Interpretation when using standard</b><br>
deviation for individual data<br>
<b>The result of the calculated standard</b><br>
deviation is 23.44. SD can easily<br>
be interpreted when calculated and<br>
compared from two or more separate<br>
groups on the same subject  studied.<br>
<b>For example, SD for students’ scores</b><br>
in Geography terminal examination.<br>
<b>The greater the SD in one of the group</b><br>
say stream A compared to stream B will<br>
mean that the spread of the scores in<br>
stream A is greater compared to that of<br>
stream B . While dealing with standard<br>
deviation it should be noted that if the<br>
calculated standard deviation is a large<br>
value, it means the scores are more<br>
spread. Thus, the greater the standard<br>
deviation the greater the spread of the<br>
scores. The closer the scores are to the<br>
mean, the less spread they are, hence the<br>
smaller the standard deviation.<br>
<b>Strength and weakness of standard</b><br>
deviation<br>
<b>Standard deviation is proved to have</b><br>
strengths as follows. It is strictly defined<br>
and its value is always definite and based<br>
on all observations. It uses the actual<br>
signs of observations; it is based on<br>
arithmetic mean; hence it has all merits of<br>
arithmetic mean. It is the most important<br>
and widely used measure of dispersion;<br>
and gives possibility for further algebraic<br>
expression. Furthermore, it is minimally<br>
affected by the fluctuations of sampling,<br>
hence it is stable and it creates a basis for<br>
measuring the coefficients of correlations<br>
and sampling.<br>
<b>Despite its strength, standard deviation</b><br>
has some weaknesses such as: it is<br>
complex to understand and calculate;<br>
its tendency to give more weight to<br>
extreme values by squaring them up<br>
during computation is a drawback; and<br>
it is an absolute measure of variability,<br>
hence it cannot be used for comparison<br>
purposes.<br>

<b>Methods of presenting statistical</b><br>
data<br>
<b>There are different methods of</b><br>
presenting data in statistics and<br>
<b>Geography. Generally, tables and graphs</b><br>
are among the effective communication<br>
technique that interpret and convey<br>
statistical data and information. They<br>
enable readers to understand the content<br>
of Geographical statistics, sustain<br>
their interest, and effectively present<br>
huge quantities of information. In this<br>
context, it is the role of a geographer<br>
and a researcher to choose appropriate<br>
methods of data presentation that will<br>
not only help the readers understand<br>
the content but also guide them through<br>
making a meaningful interpretation of<br>
the data presented.<br>
<b>Once the geographical data has been</b><br>
collected, it is classified and organized<br>
for easy readability and interpretation<br>
that is converted to information. Various<br>
methods can be used in presenting the<br>
data.There are three (3) major methods<br>
used in presenting data, namely<br>
statistical graphs, statistical charts and<br>
diagrams, and statistical maps (Figure<br>
1.9).<br>
<b>Flow maps</b><br>
<b>Isoline maps shading</b><br>
(Choropleth) maps<br>
<b>Isoline maps</b><br>
<b>Dot maps</b><br>
<b>Wind roses</b><br>
<b>Graduated range of</b><br>
symbols<br>
<b>Dispersion graphs or</b><br>
circular graphs<br>
<b>Age & sex pyramids</b><br>
<b>Bar graphs</b><br>
<b>Line graphs</b><br>
<b>Proportional diagrams</b><br>
<b>Divides rectangles</b><br>
<b>Divide circles</b><br>

<b>Statistical graphs</b><br>
<b>This group is basically concerned with</b><br>
the relationship between quantities and<br>
does not stress the idea of location.<br>
<b>Usually, the horizontal and vertical</b><br>
axis must appear as a basic and integral<br>
part of the drawing. These graphs are<br>
subdivided into line graphs, bar graphs,<br>
age and sex pyramids and dispersion<br>
graph or circular graphs.<br>
<b>Line graphs</b><br>
<b>Line graphs may be represented in four</b><br>
(4) ways: Simple line graph, group<br>
line graph, compound line graph, and<br>
divergence line graph.<br>
<b>Procedures for drawing line graphs</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>The horizontal axis is normally</b><br>
used to represent the independent<br>
variable, for example time<br>
whether in hours, day, month’s<br>
years or any other period of time;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>The vertical axis is normally</b><br>
used to represent the dependent<br>
variable for example quantities or<br>
values, sometimes as percentages;<br>
(iii)	 Select the suitable scale by<br>
considering the highest value in<br>
the graph space;<br>
<b>If drawn on plain paper, it is</b><br>
preferable to draw two vertical<br>
axes, one at each end of the<br>
horizontal axis;<br>
(iv)	 Do not indicate large numbers<br>
with long strings of roughs, for<br>
example 100 000 or 200 000 but<br>
write either at the top corner or<br>
along the side, the value of the<br>
units expressed in figures. For<br>
example tonnes; and<br>
(v)<br>
<b>There must be a title, a scale and</b><br>
a key.<br>
<b>Note: 	 The procedures above are stated</b><br>
in general way however, they<br>
may slightly vary depending<br>
on the type of line graph dealt<br>
with.<br>
(a) Simple line graph<br>
<b>The simple line graphs are normally</b><br>
drawn to represent the time series data<br>
related to the temperature, rainfall,<br>
population growth, birth rates and<br>
death rates. They are called simple<br>
because they have a single line. They<br>
are commonly used in hospitals as well<br>
as meteorological and dermatological<br>
stations. The data in Table 1.26 show the<br>
mean annual temperature that has been<br>
used to construct a simple line graph<br>
shown in Figure 1.10.<br>
Table 1.26: Average temperature for Chololo village, in Dodoma from 2010 to 2018<br>

graph<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the types of variables</b><br>
from your given data to<br>
horizontal scale (independent)<br>
and another in vertical scale<br>
(dependent);<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Select the suitable scale by</b><br>
considering the highest value<br>
and the graph space;<br>
(iii)	 Draw the horizontal and<br>
vertical lines according to<br>
the scale;<br>
(iv)	 Plot the points and join them<br>
by straight line; and<br>
(v)<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
simple line graphs<br>
<b>Use of simple line graphs in geographical</b><br>
data has numerous advantages including:<br>
<b>Simplicity in drawing and interpreting</b><br>
them. The continuous nature of the line<br>
or curve makes the technique suitable<br>
for displaying continuous data like<br>
temperature and rainfall variations<br>
over time.  The simple line graph is also<br>
useful in displaying the relationship of<br>
two variables as shown in Figure1.11<br>
where the variation of temperature over<br>
years is shown. The line graph enables<br>
visualisation of variation of statistical<br>
data with rise or drop pattern. It is also,<br>
easy to read the exact values against<br>
the plotted point in straight line graph.<br>
<b>Nonetheless, the simple line graph</b><br>
encounters some shortfalls such as the<br>
constrain of record limit which just<br>
represents only one item on the graph.<br>
<b>Another limitation of simple line graph</b><br>
is that it gives false impression of<br>
continuity of data even when there are<br>
periods the data is missing. The method<br>
is also criticised for giving unclear visual<br>
impression of actual quantities.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Group line graphs</b><br>
<b>Group line graphs also known as</b><br>
comparative or multiple grouped line<br>
graphs are graphs which present more<br>
than one item or series of data. Group<br>
line graphs display the relationship<br>
between sets of similar statistics for<br>
two or more items. Note that the drawn<br>
line should not be uniform and on the<br>
other hand presentation of five lines<br>
per graph is recommended. Table1.27<br>
presents mean annual temperature from<br>
five stations in Tanzania. These data<br>
have been used to draw the graph in<br>
Figure 1.11.<br>

<b>The following are procedures for constructing multiple line graphs</b><br>
(i)<br>
Identify the variables from the given data;<br>
(ii)<br>
Identify the item with highest value and use it to choose the scale;<br>
(iii)	  Draw the horizontal and vertical lines;<br>
(iv)	  Plot the points and join them with lines of different texture or colour; and<br>
(v)<br>
Write the title, scale and show the key.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
multiple line graph<br>
<b>The use of group line graphs have a</b><br>
number of advantages, especially in<br>
making comparative analysis of data.<br>
<b>They are detailed since they represent</b><br>
many items at once; and save time<br>
and space. They also have good visual<br>
impression especially if drawn correctly.<br>
<b>The fluctuations of data can easily be</b><br>
noted. However, the line graphs face<br>
some limitations. They can be time<br>
consuming in both construction and<br>
interpretation. With crossing lines they<br>
may lead to confusion in interpretation.<br>
<b>Sometimes they can be overcrowded in</b><br>
incidences of massive set of data and<br>
sometimes they can easily be confused<br>
with compound line graphs.<br>
(c ) Compound  line graphs<br>
<b>Compound line graphs also known as</b><br>
composite  cumulative or divided line<br>
graphs are drawn with several different<br>
components. On a compound line graph,<br>
the differences between the points on<br>
adjacent lines give the actual values. It is<br>
a good alternative to grouped line graph<br>
because the procedures for constructing<br>
are the same. The only difference is that<br>
instead of drawing lines in different<br>
colour or shade, they are all shown in<br>
bold form but the space between one<br>
line and the other is shaded differently.<br>
<b>It is commonly suggested that values</b><br>
should be arranged in a certain order,<br>
with the highest values at the top and<br>
lower value at the bottom. Lines should<br>
not cross each other and data should<br>
be arranged in a cumulative manner.<br>
<b>The data in Table 1.28(a) in a line graph</b><br>
showing electricity generation in Giga<br>
<b>Watt per hour (GWh) have been used</b><br>
to construct a compound line graph as<br>
shown in Figure1.12.<br>

<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for constructing compound line graph</b><br>
(i)<br>
Prepare a cumulative table by adding individual items to previous items;<br>
(ii)	 Draw the x and y axes and choose a suitable horizontal and vertical scale;<br>
(iii)	 Plot the dots for cumulative values of independent variables corresponding<br>
with the dependent variables from each item by rearranging from largest to<br>

smallest or vice versa. This  rearrangement should be for the  first year then<br>
in other years items should follow the order of the first year;<br>
(iv)	 Join the dots with portions of straight lines;<br>
(v)	 The area occupied by each component presented on the graph, has to be<br>
coloured or shaded differently so as to give a clear distinction between the<br>
components;<br>
(vi)	 Always start with the item with highest value and end with item with lowest<br>
value or vice versa;<br>
(vii)	 Lines should not cross each other and data should be arranged in a cumulative<br>
manner; and<br>
(viii)	Write the title, scale and key.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
compound line graph<br>
<b>The compound line graphs are of</b><br>
beneficial in many ways specifically<br>
their ability to display multiple values<br>
for overall conclusion and suggestion;<br>
bringing visual impression that<br>
encourages understanding to interpreters;<br>
and combining several graphs at once.<br>
<b>However, the method is associated with</b><br>
some drawbacks including calculations<br>
that may be difficult and time consuming.<br>
<b>The interpretation of data is likely to be</b><br>
complicated. Cumulative data also hide<br>
the reality of original data.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Divergent line graph</b><br>
<b>It is used to show fluctuations in value</b><br>
in terms of “positives” or “negatives”<br>
also known as ‘profits or losses’, ‘gains<br>
or losses’ and ‘increases or decreases’.<br>
<b>Such fluctuations are common in</b><br>
imports and exports, population trends,<br>
production of goods and commodities.<br>
<b>The graph, can also address the increase</b><br>
and decrease pattern of temperature and<br>
rainfall trends. As such it can be used<br>
by climatologists, meteorologists and<br>
geographers in  drawing insights on<br>
the extent of extreme weather events,<br>
climate change and variability as well as<br>
their effects to environment and welfare.<br>
<b>Data in Table 1.29(a) which shows the</b><br>
average temperature for Chololo village<br>
in Dodoma from 2010 to 2018 has been<br>
used to draw Figure 1.13.<br>

<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are the procedures for construction of divergent line graph</b><br>
(i)<br>
Find the sum of the number of observations in the set of data;<br>
(ii)<br>
Calculate the mean;<br>
(iii)	 Subtract the mean from each data/value given;<br>
(iv)	 Plot the divergences (positive and negatives) on a graph with positive<br>
on the   upper part of mean (zero) line and negatives below it by putting<br>
dots; (zero) line must be bolded; and<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Finally join the dots sequentially. The zero line represent the mean on</b><br>
one side of (zero) line should indicate the mean.<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
divergent line graph<br>
<b>Some of the major advantages of the</b><br>
divergent line graphs are: fluctuation<br>
from the mean can be noted easily,<br>
simple to read and interpret; and<br>
simple in presenting values. They easily<br>
compare the items hence facilitate sound<br>
conclusion. Other benefits include their<br>
merits in showing the positives (profits)<br>
and negatives (losses) and reasonably<br>
easy to construct, read and interpret.<br>
<b>Despite such advantages still the</b><br>
divergent line graphs have the following<br>
limitations: inability to show the sums<br>
under the study instead they display only<br>
the positive and negative divergences.<br>
<b>They can be difficult to interpret</b><br>
particularly to some individuals who<br>
have limited statistical skills. Normally,<br>
they are only restricted to one item per<br>
graph. Positive and negative values may<br>
mislead the interpretation if not skilled.<br>
<b>Bar graphs</b><br>
<b>Bar graph also known as a column</b><br>
graph refers to an x-y graph  showing<br>
the tendencies of rainfall, population<br>
and other quantities like goods. Each<br>
tendency is shown by a column or bar<br>
whose length or height represents its<br>
value along y-axis. The purpose of<br>
the graph is to show numerical facts<br>
in visual form so that they can be<br>
understood quickly, easily and clearly.<br>
<b>Bar graphs are appropriate when there is</b><br>
a need to present trends or comparison.<br>
<b>In showing comparison it may consist</b><br>
of two or more parallel verticals (or<br>
horizontal) bars or rectangles.<br>
a) Simple bar graphs<br>
Simple bar graphs consist of parallel,<br>
usually vertical bars or rectangles with<br>
length proportional to the frequency<br>
with which specified quantities occur<br>
in a set of data. It can be defined as<br>
quantitative comparison by rectangles<br>
with lengths proportional to the measure<br>
of the data or things being compared.<br>
<b>It can be drawn to show rainfall and</b><br>
total exports or imports. Data in Table<br>
<b>1.30 which show hydroelectric power</b><br>
generation in Giga Watt per hour (GWh)<br>
in Tanzania from 2011 to 2017 has been<br>
used to draw Figure 1.14:<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing simple bar graph<br>
(i)<br>
<b>From the given data, identify the</b><br>
types of variables;<br>
(ii)<br>
Select the suitable scale;<br>
(iii)	 Draw horizontal and vertical<br>
line and construct bars vertically<br>
above the horizontal lines;<br>
(iv)	 Shade the bars equally;<br>
(v)<br>
Write the title and the key;<br>
(vi)	 On a graph, draw two lines<br>
perpendicular to each other,<br>
intersecting at zero;<br>
(vii)	 The horizontal line is x-axis and<br>
vertical line is y-axis;<br>
(viii)	 Along the horizontal axis, choose<br>
the uniform width of bars and<br>
uniform gap between    the bars<br>
and write the names of the data<br>
items whose values are to be<br>
marked;<br>
(ix)	 Along the vertical axis, choose<br>
a suitable scale in order to<br>
determine the heights of the bars<br>
for the given values. (Frequency<br>
43<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
is taken along y-axis); and<br>
(x)<br>
<b>Calculate the heights of each bar</b><br>
according to the scale chosen and<br>
draw the bars.<br>
<b>Other things to bear in mind when</b><br>
constructing simple bar graphs<br>
include:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>The horizontal scale usually</b><br>
represents the independent<br>
variable whereby vertical scale<br>
represents dependent variable;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>A vertical bar may occupy the</b><br>
space (either completely or<br>
partially) between two vertical<br>
lines;<br>
(iii)	 All bars must start at zero. Bar<br>
graphs drawn for purposes of<br>
comparison must be drawn on<br>
the same scale; and<br>
(iv)	 The width of the bar is a matter<br>
of choice, avoid bars that are too<br>
thick or too thin.  The value of<br>
each bar can be assessed  easily<br>
if a space or gap is left between<br>
each bar.<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
simple bar graph<br>
<b>The simple bar graphs are beneficial</b><br>
because: they are easy to draw and<br>
interpret; they can be used in conjunction<br>
with line graphs; their rise and fall<br>
patterns at a given time can be easily<br>
visualised; they also present tangible<br>
quantities better than line graphs and<br>
simple bar graphs. However, have some<br>
limitations. The major ones include<br>
inability to present many items and in<br>
cases of scale exaggeration the graph<br>
may be distorted. Furthermore, they<br>
consume more space in cases of huge<br>
data.<br>
b) Grouped bar graph<br>
<b>Grouped bar graph also known as</b><br>
comparative or  multiple bar graph<br>
is where two or more simple bars are<br>
grouped side by side on the same vertical<br>
scale for the sake of comparison.  It is<br>
a graph that uses rectangular bars to<br>
represent different values for showing<br>
comparisons among categories such<br>
as the amount of rainfall in  different<br>
months of a year, or the average salary<br>
in different states. Grouped bar graphs<br>
are commonly drawn vertically, though<br>
they can also be depicted horizontally.<br>
<b>Data in Table1.31 which show electricity</b><br>
generation in Giga Watt per hour in<br>
<b>Tanzania from 2011 to 2017 have been</b><br>
used in drawing Figure 1.15.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
construction of group bar graph<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Draw and label the vertical and</b><br>
horizontal sides (axes);<br>
(ii)	 Choose a scale that suits the data;<br>
(iii)	 Place dots on the graph to represent<br>
the data;<br>
(iv)	 Connect the dots in order;<br>
(v)	 Write a title above the graph;<br>
(vi)	 To give an impression of totality,<br>
bars are usually drawn touching<br>
each other that is without a gap<br>
between them, but attention may<br>
be drawn to individual components<br>
by leaving a small space between<br>
the bars. Also, groups of bars must<br>
be separated from each other with<br>
similar space or gap;<br>
(vii)	 It is a custom to draw the bars<br>
of each group in ascending or<br>
descending order for comparison<br>
purposes;<br>
(viii)	All bars must be of the same width<br>
and drawn at right angles to the<br>
axis; and<br>
(ix)	 Write the title,scale and show the<br>
key.<br>
<b>Example</b><br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
grouped bar graph<br>
<b>The grouped bar graph is useful</b><br>
in the field of geography  in many<br>
ways. It enables a reader to get a good<br>
visual impression about the totality<br>
and individuality of the studied<br>
item(s). It enables easy comparison<br>
of the investigated components.<br>
<b>Interpretation is relatively simple</b><br>
and easy. The bars may be drawn<br>
within one another particularly for<br>
the overlapping bars. Lastly, many<br>
items can be presented together in<br>
the same graph hence, save space.<br>
<b>Despite the given merits,  grouped</b><br>
bar graph has  limitations such as<br>
comparison of (sum) of items is<br>
relatively difficult, for example rainfall<br>
patterns with varying years cannot be<br>
expressed. Composing the scale is<br>
also challenging. Lastly they are time<br>
consuming in constructing and difficult<br>
to compare the sum in each year when<br>
there are many groups of bars.<br>
(c) Compound bar graph<br>
<b>Compound bar graph refers to a graph</b><br>
which combines two or more types<br>
of information in one graph. It can<br>
also compare different quantities. A<br>
compound bar graph is a type of bar<br>
chart where columns can be split into<br>
sections to show breakdown of data.  It<br>
is drawn by subdividing one bar into<br>
component parts. The total length of the<br>
bar represents the total value of the entire<br>
component in which parts are shown in<br>
such division. Data in Table 1.29 which<br>
shows  electricity generation in Giga<br>
<b>Watt per hour in Tanzania from 2011</b><br>
to 2017 have been used to draw Figure<br>
1.16.<br>
46<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing compound (divide) bar<br>
graph.<br>
(i)<br>
Identify the types of variables;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Find the item with the highest</b><br>
total;<br>
(iii)	 Prepare the cumulative<br>
table and enter the values<br>
cumulatively starting with<br>
the highest or the smallest<br>
to largest item. This<br>
rearrangement should be for<br>
the first year and the following<br>
years should follow the<br>
established order;<br>
(iv)	 Use the highest total among<br>
the total in the table to select<br>
a suitable vertical scale.<br>
<b>For the case of horizontal</b><br>
scale, the number of items of<br>
independent variable should<br>
be considered;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Draw the vertical and</b><br>
horizontal lines;<br>
(vi)	 Draw bars vertically above<br>
the horizontal line,the  height<br>
of each depends on its total in<br>
the cumulative table;<br>
(vii)	 Divide and shade bars,<br>
accordingly; and<br>
(viii)	Write the title, scale and the<br>
key.<br>
<b>Electricity production in GWh</b><br>
<b>Scale: V.S: 1cm to 1000  GWh  and  H.S: 1cm to 1 Year</b><br>

<b>Tanzania from 2011 to 2017</b><br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
compound bar graph<br>
<b>Compound bar graph is easy to construct</b><br>
and make comparison; the associated<br>
colouring and shading of the graphs<br>
improves visual impression and simplify<br>
interpretation. It allows expressing more<br>
than one quantities within the chart.<br>
<b>However, the compound bar graph has</b><br>
some  drawbacks including difficulties<br>
in assessing the value of one component<br>
or tracing its fluctuation over a period of<br>
time. It involves cumulative data which<br>
demand some calculation, so it is time<br>
consuming. Furthermore, compound<br>
bar  graph has a limitation in  presenting<br>
many  components due to limited space<br>
of accommodating long graphs. It is<br>
difficult to compose scale if the range<br>
values is very large.<br>
(d) Divergent bar graphs<br>
<b>In this type of graphs, data spread  is</b><br>
both positive and negative and it is<br>
displayed divergently. These could be<br>
constructed on either the x or y axis.<br>
<b>Divergent bar graphs are used when one</b><br>
set of data is provided for part of the<br>
period under consideration and then this<br>
dataset is split into separate components<br>
for another part of the period. Data in<br>
<b>Table1.32 which shows passengers</b><br>
transport in thousand (‘000) by Tanzania<br>
<b>Railways from 2010 to 2015 has been</b><br>
used to draw Figure 1.17: Note that<br>
the Tanzania and Zambia Railway<br>
Authority (TAZARA) is excluded.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing divergent bar graph.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Find the sum of the number of</b><br>
observations in the set of data;<br>
(ii)<br>
Calculate the mean;<br>
(iii)	 Subtract the mean from each<br>
data or value given to get<br>
deviation;<br>
(iv)	 Select a suitable vertical and<br>
horizontal scales;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Plot the divergences (positive</b><br>
and negatives) on a graph with<br>
positive on the upper part of<br>
mean (zero) line and negatives<br>
below it by putting dots; zero<br>
line must be bold;<br>
(vi)	 Draw bars up and down the<br>
line of average and shade them<br>
equally; and<br>
(vii)	 Write the title, and scale.<br>
<b>Note: The zero line must be clearly</b><br>
indicated usually by thickening. As the<br>
bar, the horizontal scale is in fact best<br>
written at the bottom and top of the<br>
graph. The vertical axis must be scaled<br>
both above and below the zero line, the<br>
upper part for positive and the lower for<br>
negative values.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
divergent bar graph<br>
<b>The divergent bar graphs have</b><br>
advantages including the fluctuation in<br>
values which are helpful in detecting<br>
problem in general terms as shown<br>
in Figure 1.17. It is also important in<br>
comparing the negatives and positives<br>
of the phenomenon under observation.<br>
<b>It further enables easy deduction of the</b><br>
profit (loss/rise) or loss (failure or rise) of<br>
the phenomenon observed.  It is simple to<br>
construct, read and interpret. However,<br>
the graphs have  some limitations like<br>
time consuming as its construction<br>
involves many steps. It demands skills<br>
in mathematics and it is confined to<br>
analysis of only one variable. Also it<br>
does not present actual data but presents<br>
data showing variations from the mean.<br>
<b>A combined line and bar graph</b><br>
<b>Sometimes a simple line and a bar graph</b><br>
may be combined in the same graph to<br>
show more often climate data such as<br>
temperature and rainfall. This type of<br>
graph is termed as a climograph.<br>
<b>The hypothetical annual temperature</b><br>
(Table 1.33) for weather station X in<br>
<b>Ikombe Village is used to present data</b><br>
by combining lines and  graphs (Figure<br>
1.18).<br>
<b>Procedure:</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing a combined line and bar<br>
graph.<br>
<b>Similar procedure for the construction</b><br>
of simple line and bar graphs need to be<br>
used to construct a combined line  and<br>
bar graph.<br>
<b>Note: You may choose a different scale</b><br>
for rainfall and temperature. In this case<br>
as well, rainfall will be in bars as shown<br>
but temperature is plotted in line which<br>
is above the bars.<br>
