<b>Age - sex graph</b><br>
<b>A population pyramid also known as age</b><br>
and sex pyramid,  population structure<br>
or age and sex structure refers to the<br>
geographical representation of age<br>
structure or distributions of population<br>
according to age groups. The graph is<br>
commonly used by demographers. The<br>
graphs can be constructed to represent<br>
composition of a population in a region,<br>
country or worldwide. It is not only<br>
restricted to showing composition of<br>
age group but also extend to sex of the<br>
population composition. Normally,<br>
age groups appears along the vertical<br>
(y-axis) while sex is placed on horizontal<br>
(x-axis). Basically, female is located on<br>
the right side and male on the left side<br>
shown in absolute number or percentage<br>
in successive age groups (Figure 1.19).<br>
<b>Pyramids are useful in  presenting the</b><br>
population which depends on the birth,<br>
death rates and migration. These graphs<br>
are relevant in summarising the age and<br>
sex data collected from census survey.<br>
<b>Through these types of graphs, the</b><br>
country can deduce various insights and<br>
information useful for decision-making<br>
such as distribution of social services<br>
and intervention measures as well as<br>
identifying the working group and<br>
the dependant, with regard to varying<br>
social groups. Often the population for<br>
comparison purposes is constructed on<br>
the same scale and should depict the same<br>
age groups. Bars should be of the same<br>
height. Demographers identify three<br>
types of pyramids namely: expansive<br>
51<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
or rapid growth, stationary or slow<br>
growth and constrictive or contractive or<br>
negative growth pyramids. The structure<br>
of the pyramids is dynamic depending<br>
on the changes of population structure.<br>
<b>The demographics are changing from</b><br>
pyramid and finally to barrel which end<br>
the point of population pyramids.<br>
<b>Types of population pyramids</b><br>
<b>The population pyramids are of varying</b><br>
shapes. Though different countries can<br>
have unique pyramids, still the pyramid<br>
in the same countries can take different<br>
shapes over different periods of time.<br>
<b>Normally, this is influenced by spatial</b><br>
and temporal variations over time.<br>
<b>Changes in number of the population</b><br>
whether by age or sex are among the<br>
quantitative variables of interest in<br>
statistics in describing the population.<br>
<b>The graphical representation of the</b><br>
population pyramids ultimately relies<br>
on age and sex structure of a given<br>
population.  Such shapes may take the<br>
form of a triangular pyramid, have a<br>
columnar or rectangular (with vertical<br>
sides rather than sloped sides), or have<br>
an irregular profile. Below are the major<br>
categories of population pyramids:<br>
<b>Expansive pyramid: This is also known</b><br>
as rapid growth pyramid. It has a broad<br>
base with successive decline in the share<br>
of population of higher age groups. The<br>
pyramid represents a relatively high<br>
fertility and mortality rates; low life<br>
expectancy;  higher population growth<br>
rates; and low share of old age persons.<br>
<b>The pyramids portray the expansion of</b><br>
population as the size of each cohort<br>
gets larger than the size of the same in<br>
previous time. Expansive age pyramids<br>
are common for developing countries<br>
mainly in Africa and Asia. Figure 1.19<br>
which shows the population distribution<br>
in Tanzania basing on the census survey<br>
of 2012 is a typical representation of an<br>
expansive pyramid. As such, distribution<br>
varies with time and space; the slight<br>
or complete change in structure of<br>
population in the country for the<br>
subsequent census survey  will not<br>
be a surprise. In drawing population<br>
pyramids, you should consider the<br>
following.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>The age groups, are usually based</b><br>
on quinquennial (5years) periods<br>
(0 - 4, 5 - 9, 10 - 14) while the<br>
youngest age group forming the<br>
base of the graph;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>In calculating percentage, two</b><br>
methods are possible either<br>
the individual male or female<br>
population or each group may be<br>
calculated as percentages of the<br>
total population; and<br>
(iii)	 It should  be noted that the<br>
procedures for constructing the<br>
population pyramids are common<br>
across all types of pyramids. The<br>
shapes of resultant pyramids are<br>
also the  result of a population<br>
composition at a particular time<br>
and space.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are the procedures for</b><br>
constructing age and sex pyramids graph<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify types of variables and</b><br>
suggest suitable scales. For<br>
vertical scale, consider the<br>
52<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
number of  age groups, and for<br>
the horizontal scale, consider the<br>
highest value or percent;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Draw two vertically standing</b><br>
lines of not more than 2cm apart<br>
however, 2 cm wide is suitable;<br>
at the centre of the graph paper;<br>
(iii)	  From the bottom of the lines, are<br>
two horizontal lines away from<br>
each other to represent the sex.<br>
<b>Male is predominately on the left</b><br>
while female on the right side;<br>
(iv)	 The bars are drawn horizontally<br>
and their length correspond to the<br>
size of the age groups.  It is in fact<br>
a comparative bar graph drawn<br>
horizontally; and<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Shade the bars, write the title and</b><br>
indicate the scale.<br>

stable population. The stationary or near<br>
stationary population pyramid displays<br>
some what equal share of juvenile and<br>
adult age groups (Figure 1.21).<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of age</b><br>
and sex graph or population pyramid<br>
<b>The age and sex graphs are beneficial</b><br>
in many ways. They clearly show the<br>
comparison between males and females.<br>
<b>They give a clear picture of summary of</b><br>
population composition that is visually<br>
attractive. On one hand, they define<br>
economic status of a given country,<br>
its fertility and mortality rates and life<br>
expectancy. On the other hand, they<br>
show the trend of population change in<br>
terms of birth and death. The limitations<br>
of the population pyramids are time<br>
consuming caused by tedious steps of<br>
calculations involved in tabulating the<br>
pyramid and determining the scale.<br>
<b>A compounded population pyramid</b><br>
<b>This is also refered to as superimposed</b><br>
population pyramid. It is a population<br>
pyramid  which comprises different<br>
population categories  superimposed<br>
in one bar.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing a compounded pyramids<br>
graph<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the types of variables for</b><br>
this case age,sex and employment<br>
variable and suggest suitable<br>
scales;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Draw two vertically standing</b><br>
lines of not more than two (2cm)<br>
apart;<br>
(iii)	 The bars of sex and employment<br>
are drawn horizontally and their<br>
lengths correspond the size of the<br>
age groups; and<br>
(iv)	 Other procedures are as  in<br>
constructing the normal<br>
population pyramids in section<br>
(c) above.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
compound population pyramids<br>
<b>This type of pyramid is useful to show</b><br>
comparison between males and females<br>
in different categories: For example in<br>
employment and education. It also shows<br>
characteristics of population that is either<br>
from developed or developing nations. It<br>
gives a clear visual impression although<br>
the pyramids are tedious to construct.<br>
<b>Circular graph</b><br>
<b>A circular graph is also known as</b><br>
dispersion, clock or polar graph due to<br>
its resemblance to the face of a clock<br>
or lines of longitude radiating from the<br>
pole. The analogy between the twelve<br>
months of the year and the twelve hours<br>
of the clock face adds attraction to the<br>
use of this type of graph.  It is mostly<br>
used to show  farmers the  seasonal<br>
calendar in a year for farmers.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are pocedures for</b><br>
constructing a circular graph<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the types of variables</b><br>
and select a suitable scale;<br>
(ii)<br>
Draw seven concentric circles,<br>
the smallest at the center<br>
should be not more than 2cm in<br>
diameter; However, it depends<br>
57<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
on the size of the paper. Distance<br>
from one circle to another<br>
should be 1cm;<br>
(iii)	 Draw 12 radii of 30o apart,<br>
starting from 12 o’clock radius<br>
clockwise. The 12 radii stand<br>
for   months of the year, named<br>
clockwise from the 12 o’clock<br>
radius;<br>
(iv)	 Along the radii, draw bar for<br>
rainfall;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>For temperature, plot the points</b><br>
and join them with a curved line;<br>
(vi)	 Write the title, key and<br>
scale. Remember vertical<br>
scale represents rainfall and<br>
temperature,  while horizontal<br>
scale represents months;<br>
(vii)	 As  clock graph is  frequently<br>
used to represent climatic<br>
statistics, radii are scaled in oC.<br>
<b>The scale is indicated on either</b><br>
the 12 o’clock or 6 o’clock<br>
radius.  Points plotted are then<br>
joined as a continuous circle;<br>
(viii)	Bar can also be drawn along<br>
the radii to indicate  monthly<br>
mean  rainfall.  In order to<br>
avoid congestion at the centre<br>
of the circle, zero is normally<br>
represented as a circle; and<br>
(ix)	 Write the title, scale and key.<br>

and  draw a polar chart.<br>

<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
polar chart<br>
<b>The polar charts is beneficial in many</b><br>
ways. The common use is to guide<br>
the farmers through identifying their<br>
seasonal calender in a year. It can also<br>
be used in calculating  monthly mean<br>
rainfall and temperature data.It is detailed<br>
by drawing two concentric circles. Some<br>
of its limitations include inability to<br>
display much of information, as they<br>
are squeezed in a circle. It also time<br>
consuming as both organising the scale<br>
and drawing are complicated. Finally it<br>
is confined to weather conditions only.<br>
<b>Activity 1.3</b><br>

<b>Statistical charts and diagrams</b><br>
<b>Statistical charts and diagrams methods</b><br>
differ from statistical graphs as they do<br>
not depend on squared paper or a map<br>
in data presentation. Rather they display<br>
data in circular graphs, rectangles,<br>
repeated symbols, proportional<br>
diagrams, graduated symbols and wind<br>
roses.They may be used in conjunction<br>
with a map for the purpose of defining<br>
or describing a location but they can<br>
also be drawn independently. They are<br>
not necessarily drawn on a graph paper<br>
but even plain or ruled papers. There<br>
are six (6) major statistical charts and<br>
diagrams, which include: divided circles<br>
(pie charts), divided rectangles, repeated<br>
symbols, proportional diagrams,<br>
graduated range of symbols and wind<br>
roses.<br>
<b>Divided circle</b><br>
<b>Divided circle also known as pie chart</b><br>
refers to a diagram consisting of a<br>
circle divided into the slices which<br>
are proportional in size to the value<br>
represented.  The slice of the circle may<br>
be shaded or coloured and labeled.  The<br>
largest slice is plotted first clockwise<br>
from 12 o’clock in ascending order for<br>
easy comparison. The divided circle<br>
can be sub-divided into three parts<br>
namely simple divided circle (pie<br>
chart), proportional divided circles and<br>
proportional divided semi-circles.<br>
(a)   Simple divided circle<br>
<b>It is a simple pie chart which is used to</b><br>
represent simple data such as exports,<br>
imports or production. Simple divided<br>
circle is also known as simple pie chart.<br>
59<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Data in Table 1.37(a) which show</b><br>
mineral imports in (‘000) metric tonnes<br>
in Tanzania in 2015 have been used to<br>
draw Figure 1.25.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing simple divided circle<br>
(i)<br>
Find the total amount of all values;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Change each of the values into</b><br>
percentage, and then into degrees;<br>
(iii)	  Draw the circle of suitable radius;<br>
(iv)	 Divide the circle into parts<br>
corresponding to the value of each<br>
radius of respective components.<br>
<b>Drawing should be done  clockwise</b><br>
from the 12`starting with the<br>
highest degree value;<br>
(v)<br>
Shade each portion differently;<br>
and<br>
(vi)	 Write the title and the key.<br>
<b>Note: The circle may be of any</b><br>
convenient size, too small circle must<br>
be avoided.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
divided circles<br>
<b>The main advantages  of divide circles</b><br>
are that they are simple to construct,<br>
useful for comparison purposes<br>
as items can be clearly seen. They<br>
are easier to obtain accurate data<br>
presentation. The circles however, have<br>
the following limitations: in case of<br>
zero degree, usually the data cannot<br>
be shown. Other weaknesses include<br>
accommodating limited data and they<br>
are time consuming due to calculations<br>
and drawing. Another weakness is on<br>
their rigid method of data presentation<br>
which requires using pie charts only<br>
when total observation of the parts<br>
makes a meaningful whole. Pie charts<br>
are not recommended to use if the<br>
observations of different parts are not<br>
mutually exclusive.<br>
(b)      Proportional divided circle<br>
<b>It is a graph drawn in a circle whose</b><br>
radius is proportional to the total figures<br>
represented by all sectors of circle.<br>
<b>They are used for showing a quantity</b><br>
(for example, population of a country)<br>
that can be divided into parts such<br>
as different ethnic groups. A circle is<br>
drawn to represent the total quantity.<br>
<b>Two or more circles are drawn in such</b><br>
a way that each one is proportional to<br>
the value it represents. It is then divided<br>
into segments which are proportional<br>
in size to the components. The actual<br>
size of the circle can also be used to<br>
represent data.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following ar procedures for</b><br>
constructing proportional divided circle<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Find the total of each item</b><br>
under observation;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Compute the radius for each</b><br>
by applying the square roots<br>
on  each of the total items<br>
under observation;<br>
(iii)	 Determine the scale to be<br>
used;<br>
(iv)	 Divide the calculated radii to<br>
the scale determined;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Draw the circle based on the</b><br>
calculated radii; and<br>
(vi)	  Write the title and key.<br>
<b>Example</b><br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
proportional divided circles<br>
<b>The proportional divided circle is</b><br>
beneficial in a number of ways including<br>
displaying relative proportions of<br>
multiple classes of data; size of the circle<br>
can be constructed proportionally to the<br>
quantity of data it represents; it is also<br>
useful in summarising a large data set in<br>
visual form and it  is simple  compared<br>
to other forms of graphs. It allows a<br>
visual checking of the accuracy of the<br>
calculations. However, it is associated<br>
with limitations such as failure to easily<br>
reveal the exact values. It can be easily<br>
manipulated to give false impressions.<br>
<b>Divided semicircles</b><br>
<b>These are half circle which are</b><br>
partitioned. There are two kinds of<br>
divided semicircles. These are, simple<br>
divided semicircles and Proportional<br>
divided semicircles<br>
<b>Simple divided semicircles</b><br>
<b>These are semi circular in nature</b><br>
but segmented accordingly. The<br>
segmentation are guided by 180 degree<br>
instead of 360 degrees as used in pie<br>
chart.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing simple semi-circles<br>
<b>Note: The procedures are similar to</b><br>
that of drawing simple piechart except<br>
the degrees are obtained by using 180<br>
degree instead of 360 degrees.<br>
<b>Example:</b><br>
<b>Refer Table 1:38(a), draw a simple</b><br>
divided semi-circle to represent number<br>
of lion hunted from 2009 to 2012.<br>

<b>Proportional divided semicircle</b><br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
construction of proportional divided<br>
semi-circles<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Calculate the total of two</b><br>
semicircles given, let radii be<br>
represented by R1 and R2;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Find the radius of both totals by</b><br>
applying the square roots to the<br>
obtained total;<br>
(iii)	 Determine the scale of each<br>
semicircle first, then divide the<br>
totals by scales to obtain two<br>
radii;<br>
(iv)	 Use the two radii  to determine<br>
the size of semi circles;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Express each semi-circle by</b><br>
percentage or degree (fraction<br>
of 360º) as in simple pie chart<br>
or proportional pie chart. It is<br>
recommended to follow either<br>
ascending or descending order;<br>
(vi)	 Draw the divided semicircle; and<br>
(vii)	 Write the title and key.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
proportional semicircle<br>
<b>The proportional semicircle,  is used</b><br>
in making comparison that is why<br>
two semi circles are used; the value<br>
of each component can be analysed and<br>
provide accurate statistical information<br>
through percentage or degrees<br>
calculated. However, the method<br>
has some limitations including time<br>
consuming during  calculations. On<br>
the other hand, the actual values may<br>
not be known easily as calculations<br>
involve percentages or degrees.<br>
<b>Additionally, presentation of very small</b><br>
values cannot be accommodated by<br>
the method since their clarity can be<br>
distorted.<br>
<b>Divided rectangle</b><br>
<b>It is a rectangle whose total value</b><br>
or quantity is sub-divided into its<br>
constituents or parts. The divided<br>
rectangle is similar to  compound bar<br>
graphs and even the procedures for<br>
drawing are the same. Divided rectangles<br>
can be categorised into two major groups,<br>
namely, simple divided rectangle and<br>
compound divided rectangle.<br>
<b>Simple divided rectangle</b><br>
<b>This is a single bar graph which is large</b><br>
in size. The bar is sub- divided into<br>
divisions or parts depending on the data<br>
given. The data in Table 1.40 have been<br>
used to construct Figure 1.29 which<br>
presents simple divided rectangle.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
simple divided rectangle<br>
<b>The simple divided rectangle is</b><br>
convenient in presenting data on a<br>
diagram due to its simple scale.  Only<br>
the scale for dependent variable is<br>
considered. It is not associated with<br>
application of complicated calculations<br>
hence easily drawn; the information<br>
displayed allows easy comparison of data<br>
given in the diagram. It also, presents<br>
numerous items with better  visualisation<br>
due to colouring of the diagram which is<br>
attractive. Nonetheless, simple divided<br>
rectangle falls short of presenting few<br>
data as compared to the compound<br>
divided rectangle. Furthermore, the<br>
vertical scale is not considered when<br>
drawing the independent variable.<br>
b)   Compound divided rectangle<br>
<b>This is a type of divided rectangle</b><br>
which involves more information due<br>
to presenting several data as shown<br>
in Figure 1.30. It can be employed<br>
in describing land uses in different<br>
countries, states, regions or districts.<br>

(Table 1.41(a)) in each given<br>

(ii)<br>
<b>Calculate the % of each</b><br>
number of items for this case<br>
number of small industries<br>
(Table 41(c));<br>
(iii)	 Find the horizontal scale using<br>
the grand total from sub-totals<br>
on procedure (i) above,;<br>
(iv)	 Calculate the portion or<br>
segment for each  number<br>
of items given basing on<br>
horizontal scale.  That is the<br>
total length of horizontal<br>
baseline;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>The vertical scale: depends on</b><br>
percentages calculated for each<br>
council then present the data<br>
from each council, depending<br>
on the vertical scale which is<br>
usually1cm:10%;<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
compound divided rectangles<br>
<b>The compound divided rectangle is useful</b><br>
in the following ways: it can convey<br>
much more statistical information than a<br>
compound bar graph and a divided circle.<br>
<b>The comparison of data can be easily</b><br>
visualised and interpreted. Furthermore,<br>
it is capable of displaying for example<br>
area in square kilometers in relation<br>
to economic activities. However, its<br>
limitations include the methods  being<br>
too tedious in calculating and identifying<br>
a scale which should be compromised<br>
between vertical and horizontal scale.<br>
<b>It is  also, time consuming due to</b><br>
complex procedures to access data for<br>
constructing the graph.<br>
<b>Repeated symbols</b><br>
<b>Symbols are simple signs that are used to</b><br>
represent different features. Since maps<br>
require large amounts of information<br>
to be conveyed in a limited space, the<br>
use of symbols to represent particular<br>
features is necessary. Symbols are small<br>
but many are immediately recognisable.<br>
<b>This  means that they have an advantage</b><br>
over drawing or writing all of this<br>
information onto a map. It is a method<br>
in which statistical information can be<br>
represented on a map by the repetition of<br>
one symbol of uniform size or character<br>
or by a variety of symbols placed on<br>
their location on a map. Therefore,<br>
two types of repeated symbols can be<br>
distinguished. These are the qualitative<br>
and the quantitative symbols. Repeated<br>
70<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
symbols are the simplest methods of<br>
conveying non statistical and statistical<br>
information on a map and they are<br>
commonly observed on maps dealing<br>
with agricultural products, minerals,<br>
economic development and maps and<br>
guides generated for specific purposes;<br>
for example, tourism for promotion<br>
purposes.<br>
<b>The qualitative symbols</b><br>
<b>It is basically pictorial or descriptive</b><br>
in nature, for example, crops by their<br>
initial letters such as CN for cotton; FR<br>
for fruits; SS for sisal and R for rubber<br>
(Figure 1.31). Others are  illustrations of<br>
plants for crops, drawings of cattle for<br>
ranching, pictures of tree for forests or<br>
a range of symbols for minerals and so<br>
on. The symbols can either be pictorial<br>
or descriptive. The  qualitative repeated<br>
symbols are essentially descriptive<br>
devices which give a visual impression<br>
of the represented data item. They do<br>
not represent the actual characteristics<br>
of the observed variable.<br>
<b>Figure 1.31: Cash crops production in Tanzania</b><br>
71<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>The quantitative symbols</b><br>
<b>These are symbols of the same size and shape  placed at a place to show the</b><br>
quantity of what the symbol represents.  For example, each symbol represents a<br>
given quantity. Fish = 10000 tonnes (one  fish represent 10000 tonnes of fish).<br>
<b>Therefore, the number of fish on a map are counted and then multiplied by 10000</b><br>
to get the total amount of tonnes (Figure 1.32).<br>
= 10 000 tonnes<br>
<b>Figure: 1. 32: Number of fish in Tanzania’s water bodies</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
repeated symbols<br>
<b>Repeated symbols have both advantages</b><br>
and disadvantages; some of the  benefits<br>
of the symbols include their usefulness in<br>
presenting statistical information which<br>
is easy to read and interpret; simplifying<br>
comparison of the presented data items<br>
such as economic production related<br>
data. Simple construction of the scale<br>
and the use of calculation is minimised<br>
in the cases of qualitative  symbols.<br>
<b>On  the other hand, the symbols have</b><br>
some drawbacks such as; limitations of<br>
drawing symbols of the same size by<br>
free hand. The needs for drawing sketch<br>
map may be difficult. The limited use of<br>
scale especially in qualitative approach<br>
may cause problems in understanding<br>
the actual product in terms of amount.<br>
<b>Another drawback with repeated map</b><br>
symbols is congestion and or overlap,<br>
72<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
especially if there are large variations<br>
in the size of symbols  if numerous data<br>
locations are close.<br>
<b>Proportional diagrams</b><br>
<b>A proportional diagram is a diagram that</b><br>
compares two or more values by using<br>
the area of shapes, usually squares,<br>
rectangles, or circles. They are popular<br>
because they instantly communicate the<br>
differences in the values to a viewer.<br>
<b>These diagrams are commonly used</b><br>
in newspapers and magazines. The<br>
proportional diagrams can be regarded<br>
as an extension of the proportional<br>
divided circles (pie chart). Proportional<br>
diagrams can be presented in four (4)<br>
ways, which are proportional circles,<br>
proportional squares, propositional<br>
cubes and proportional sphere.<br>
<b>Proportional circles</b><br>
<b>Proportional circles can be used to</b><br>
represent various items, for example<br>
population, crop yield, quantities of<br>
imported and exported goods. The<br>
scale is drawn to show the value of<br>
a given circle. Usually, each circle is<br>
proportional to the quantity it represents.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing proportional circles.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Find the square roots of each</b><br>
item of data that will denote a<br>
radius of the circle;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Estimate the length of the</b><br>
radius for the largest circle<br>
first, example if the population<br>
of a village is 3000 people,<br>
the square root will be 54.7.<br>
<b>Therefore, it should be noted</b><br>
that a radius of 54.7mm could<br>
be too large to draw;<br>
(iii)	 Thus, it should be halved, it<br>
may be suitable at 27.3mm;<br>
(iv)	 For this case all other radii must<br>
also be halved;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>The proportional of circle will</b><br>
be drawn in the ascending<br>
order by starting with that of<br>
the smallest circle to the largest<br>
depending on the number of<br>
data items;<br>
(vi)	 Label each of the circle with<br>
respective quantity represented<br>
by the proportional circle; and<br>
(vii)	 Indicate the unit of the items<br>
represented and a scale.<br>
<b>Example of procedures for constructing</b><br>
proportional circle<br>
(a) Items<br>
<b>Population 625</b><br>
(b) Square root<br>
625<br>
2<br>
= 25<br>
2<br>
(c) Therefore, a circle is drawn with<br>
a radius 12.5 mm<br>
<b>Showing a proportional circle of</b><br>
a population.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
proportional circle<br>
<b>The proportional circle is essential for</b><br>
making comparison of the data items<br>
under the study. It can be combined with<br>
other method in the same map. It  can<br>
also be used where other methods such<br>
as dots may result in overcrowding.<br>
<b>The cycle has some limitation such</b><br>
as: the comparison posing difficulties<br>
in making when the size of circles<br>
becomes almost the same due to small<br>
74<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
differences of represented values. Other<br>
limitations involve time consuming due<br>
to long processes of scale calculation<br>
and  the method falls short of presenting<br>
absolute values.<br>
<b>Proportional squares</b><br>
<b>Proportional squares may be used in</b><br>
the same way as proportional circles.<br>
<b>The area of the square proportionally</b><br>
reflects the quantity it represents. On<br>
the other hand, the length of the side<br>
of the square  related to the square root<br>
reflects  directly the square root of the<br>
number to be represented. This method is<br>
useful in cases where there are  relatively<br>
numbers to be dealt with, such that by<br>
applying the square roots, numbers<br>
are reduced and become manageable.<br>
<b>Proportional squares may be employed</b><br>
without reference to the map.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are precedures for  the</b><br>
construction of  proportional squares<br>
(i)	 Calculate the square root of the<br>
total values to be represented.<br>
<b>Having drawn the scale, value</b><br>
will be the  length of the side of<br>
the square; and<br>
(ii)	 Show the key clearly.<br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
proportional squares<br>
<b>Even though the proportional squares are</b><br>
more difficult to draw, their relative areas<br>
are probably easier to assess than circles;<br>
and are more suitable for comparison of<br>
items under observation.<br>
<b>Proportional cubes</b><br>
<b>Proportional cubes can be drawn</b><br>
independent of a base map. They are<br>
usually  located on a base map as<br>
presentative of quantity distribution<br>
or production. Cubes have additional<br>
component on dimensions that is, length<br>
× width × height which differentiate them<br>
from rectangles. Cubes have a strength of<br>
presenting statistics which have greater<br>
values as compared to linear methods.<br>
<b>The side of the proportional cube bears</b><br>
a direct relationship to  the square root<br>
of the quantity being represented. For<br>
instance, if the length of the side of  the<br>
proportional is square 5 mm it means it<br>
represents 25 units of production while<br>
a cube of the same measurements will<br>
represent 125 units.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are precedures for</b><br>
constructing proportional cubes<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Calculate the cube root of the</b><br>
quantity to be represented<br>
such as 3 R , where R is the<br>
quantity to be represented;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Draw the cubes independently</b><br>
of the base-map, for purposes<br>
of comparison;<br>
(iii)	  Cubes can be drawn in several<br>
ways either isometrically, in<br>
which all sides are of equal<br>
lengths or prospectively the<br>
sides are one-halved to three<br>
quarters the length of the<br>
front. In single map all drawn<br>
cubes should be with same<br>
pattern; and<br>
(iv)	 Provide a key.<br>
75<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
cubes<br>
<b>The advantage of proportional cubes</b><br>
is the easy with drawing than spheres<br>
and the assessment of  volume of two<br>
cubes of different sizes is perhaps less<br>
difficult that the relative volumes of<br>
two different spheres. Proportional<br>
cubes however, disapproved of less<br>
visualisation, unless the presented items<br>
by themselves  have a cube shaped like<br>
bales of cotton. Another disadvantage is<br>
that the components of the  cube cannot<br>
be splited . Moreover, the use of cubes<br>
is limiting in making comparison of<br>
the quantities being represented, unless<br>
statistical information is provided on<br>
the cube.<br>
<b>Proportional sphere</b><br>
<b>Usually, concept of proportional sphere</b><br>
is somewhat  similar concept to the<br>
proportional cube. Likewise, the three-<br>
dimension characteristics possessed<br>
by the spheres widens that horizon of<br>
serving nearly the same purpose as the<br>
cube. Three dimensions enables it to<br>
accommodate a wide range of values<br>
to be represented, the volumes of the<br>
sphere are usually proportional to the<br>
quantity represented.<br>
<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
proportional spheres<br>
<b>The proportional spheres enable</b><br>
easier visualisation of items under<br>
the study same as proportional cube.<br>
<b>They serve as alternative options in</b><br>
incidents great quantity of elements<br>
which become impractical on others<br>
methods. However, the sphere has<br>
some disadvantages including difficult<br>
in drawing, calculations challenges and<br>
limitations of some readers to assess<br>
relative volumes of different spheres<br>
<b>Activity 1.4</b><br>
<b>In group of five students visit the nearby</b><br>
government/private institution engaged<br>
in documenting data on agricultural<br>
production  in your  locality and collect<br>
data on the production of three to five<br>
crops grown in the area for the past three<br>
to five years. Use them to construct a<br>
simple divided circle (simple pie chart)<br>
and present the results to the class for<br>
the detailed discussion.<br>
<b>Wind roses</b><br>
<b>Wind roses are used to show the average</b><br>
frequency and directions of the wind in<br>
different speed at a given area.  They<br>
can  also be used to show the delivery of<br>
quantities such as milk, newspapers or<br>
other goods through star diagram. Wind<br>
roses are of two types  namely; simple<br>
wind rose and compound wind rose.<br>
<b>Simple wind rose</b><br>
<b>Simple wind rose is a simple linear</b><br>
method used to show the direction and<br>
frequency of the wind only.<br>
Example: Study the data given in Table 1.44 and draw a simple monthly wind rose.<br>

<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for constructing  a simple wind rose</b><br>
(i)	 The centre of the wind rose is usually a circle of any convenient size;<br>
(ii)	 Average  wind direction and speed values recorded monthly or annually are<br>
presented separately. Eight cardinal points of the compass are usually sufficient;<br>
(iii)	The length of the columns or aims can be drawn proportional to the actual<br>
number of days; and<br>
(iv)	 Draw the simple wind rose, put  the title and indicate the scale.<br>

<b>Compound wind rose</b><br>
Compound wind roses may be used to display wind direction, frequency and speed.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for constructing compound wind rose</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>The basic method of construction is similar to that of simple wind rose, only</b><br>
that compound wind rose uses either actual or percentage values;<br>
(ii)	 The speed of the wind is then indicated by adjusting the width of the column.<br>
An increase in width signifies an increase in wind speed;<br>

<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>

(iii)	 The divisions chosen are usually<br>
less than 4 mph;<br>
(iv)	 When it is  calm the force is  zero<br>
(0) and  the speed is less than<br>
1mph;<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Compose a convenient scale for</b><br>
depicting the number of hours per<br>
direction;<br>
(vi)	 Determine the pattern of speed<br>
as a key;<br>
(vii)	 Plot diagram and put a good title;<br>
and<br>

<b>Statistical Maps</b><br>
<b>Statistical map is another method of</b><br>
data presentation in geography whereby<br>
statistical data can be presented through<br>
with a sketch map. Statistical maps are<br>
classified into four (4) groups namely;<br>
dot maps (distribution of maps), isoline<br>
maps (isopleth maps), chloropleth maps<br>
(shading or destiny maps) and flow line<br>
maps (flow maps).<br>
<b>Dot map</b><br>
<b>Dot map is the simplest and most widely</b><br>
used type of distributions map that uses<br>
a point to visualize the geographical<br>
distribution of a phenomena. The dot<br>
map results from a combination of<br>
repeated symbols of uniform size and a<br>
dispersion map.  A distribution map is<br>
essentially the representation of absolute<br>
or actual quantities on a map in such a<br>
way that a single quantitative dot has<br>
a specific and fixed value of a quantity<br>
represented. Therefore, it is imperative<br>
(though not always practical) to count the<br>
number of dots on a map and multiply<br>
with the quantity they are assigned to<br>
for accurately estimation of total value.<br>
<b>Construction of dot maps</b><br>
<b>The construction of dot maps</b><br>
(distribution dot maps) is established<br>
by the components, namely: dot<br>
value, size, location and followed by<br>
drawing. Deciding on dot-value can<br>
possibly affect the representation of<br>
quantity on a map so it should neither<br>
be undermined nor exaggerated. That<br>
79<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
is, if not carefully considered, it would<br>
result into unnecessary placing of too<br>
many dots while the dot-value is too low<br>
(overcrowded maps) especially in areas<br>
with greater concentration. Similarly,<br>
placing  too few dots where the dot-<br>
value is too high could give an equally<br>
wrong impression. Decision on dot map<br>
should be guided by the range of figures<br>
to be represented that has implication<br>
to the value and number of dots to be<br>
drawn. It is recommended to prepare<br>
a trial map  which despite  consuming<br>
time will guide you through obtaining<br>
reliable results.<br>
<b>Dot size in terms of its diameter is</b><br>
importance. Dot size cannot be thought<br>
in isolation with the dot-location.<br>
<b>Extreme sizes of dots should be</b><br>
avoided, the number and size of dots<br>
must be in a way that they bring a clear<br>
visual impression of the differences in<br>
distribution, contrasting with regard to<br>
the varying concentration across areas<br>
such as, areas with greater concentrated<br>
versus sparsely or scattered areas.<br>
<b>Dot location however, is concerned with</b><br>
placing the dots based on two methods<br>
namely, distributing evenly over the<br>
area concerned or based on quantities<br>
represented by the precise location of the<br>
dots on a map. The former is of limited<br>
value although it can be resorted  to in<br>
case of absence of precise distribution of<br>
the dots on a map.In this method dots are<br>
evenly distributed after calculation of the<br>
number of dots. This method falls short<br>
of conveying limited information except<br>
the total quantity (the number and value<br>
of dots) and it is impractical in indicating<br>
the possible true distribution of the<br>
dots. It is however, useful in presenting<br>
the visual impression of comparative<br>
densities.<br>
For the case of the latter method,<br>
consideration is on the prior first-hand<br>
information of the area. However, in<br>
the absence of first-hand information<br>
relies on other reasonably accurate<br>
maps of the same area. For instance,<br>
maps showing relief, drainage, geology,<br>
soils, vegetation, rainfall, land use,<br>
communication, water supply and<br>
settlements. Basing on analysis and<br>
collating of such information give<br>
hints though not perfectly accurate on<br>
possible areas to be distributed with<br>
either sparce or concentrated kind of<br>
dots. In case concentrated areas, it is<br>
advised to address those areas first by<br>
calculating the number of the required<br>
dots and thereafter complete the rest of<br>
the dots in other areas.<br>
<b>Importantly, drawing the dots can be</b><br>
unclear particularly to non-professionals.<br>
<b>However, preparation should thoroughly</b><br>
be done by marking on the map the<br>
position of all dots, very slightly, with a<br>
special pencil. Some of  the recommended<br>
material for drawing are dotting pens,<br>
since the subsequent drawing of dots<br>
with correct size, circular and uniform<br>
character, cannot successfully be done<br>
with an ordinary pen or pencil. Probably<br>
the non-professionals are advised to use<br>
fibre -or nylon-tipped pens which are<br>
commonly available and affordable. A<br>
good drawing result can be achieved by<br>
a firm and vertically held pen on the non-<br>
absorbent paper. Data in Table 1.43(a)<br>
80<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
which shows the hypothetical number  of<br>
antelope in the selected gazetted national<br>
parks in Tanzania have been used to draw<br>
Figure 1.36.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing dot maps<br>
(i)	 Calculate the scale by identifying<br>
the dot value;<br>
(ii)	 Determine the size of a dot<br>
whereby too large or small dot is<br>
not appropriate;<br>
(iii)	Draw the sketch map where dots<br>
will be allocated; and<br>
(iv)	 Prepare an appropriate scale. For<br>
example, let 1 dot represent 300<br>
people.<br>

<b>The advantages and disadvantages</b><br>
of dot maps<br>
<b>The dot maps are simple to use since the</b><br>
lower the dot value, the more accurate<br>
the picture of distribution and value. The<br>
map can be interpreted quantitatively,<br>
that is the number of units within each<br>
boundary can be counted. The value and<br>
location of several items can be shown<br>
on the same map by using different<br>
colours and symbols. For example,<br>
goats and donkey, different ethnic<br>
groups and range of crops or minerals.<br>
<b>Similarly, calculation and drawing</b><br>
may be fairly easy and the result is<br>
graphically pleasing. It is also simple<br>
to delete the dots used on the map.<br>
<b>However, the dot maps have some</b><br>
limitations, such as the  possibility of<br>
faults in construction, if dots merging<br>
may not become apparent until the map<br>
is nearly completed, this may lead to<br>
re-calculation and redrawing of the<br>
map. If no topographical information is<br>
available, evenly spaced dots will give<br>
a false impression, like the chrolopleth.<br>
82<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Moreover, with large dot value, spatial</b><br>
distribution cannot be accurately shown,<br>
only the general distribution. Dots must<br>
be placed in the gravitational centre of<br>
the actual distribution. Furthermore, the<br>
approximation of decimals for example,<br>
<b>6.51 ≈ 7 distorts numbers of the total</b><br>
population. Finally the method  is time<br>
consuming due to scale calculation and<br>
sketch map drawing.<br>
<b>Isoline map</b><br>
<b>Isoline map is also known as isopleth</b><br>
map. It is one of the popularly used<br>
methods of statistical data representation.<br>
<b>Isoline stands for line joining places or</b><br>
points with equal value. The term isoline<br>
is interchangeably used with isarithm<br>
and isometric lines. The word isoline is<br>
derived from Greek Word isos meaning<br>
equal. The following are phenomena<br>
on a map which can be represented by<br>
isolines:<br>

for example scale 5 °C;<br>
(ii)	Interpolate or insert intermediate<br>
value or lines on the map that is<br>

21°C, 25 °C and above 26 °C.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
isoline maps<br>
<b>Isoline maps have both merits and</b><br>
limitations. The main benefits with<br>
isoline map, value can be obtained at<br>
any point on the map.  Where points<br>
are not on isolines is that the , their<br>
values can be obtained by interpolation.<br>
<b>The interval between each isoline</b><br>
suggests gradual and abrupt change,<br>
hence shading must be well graded<br>
to maintain this aspect. Furthermore,<br>
isolines can be combined with other<br>
data for example, population and crop<br>
distribution and  related to isohyet<br>
(rainfall map). Some of its limitations<br>
include the interpolation of value<br>
points which is subjective and  relies<br>
on the judgment of the individual<br>
cartographer. Some data distributions<br>
can be interpreted in different ways and<br>
can result into duplication of isoline<br>
maps. Furthermore, in a situation with<br>
a large number of data points much<br>
time can be required to accomplish<br>
the process of drawing isoline maps.<br>
<b>Another disadvantages, is  lines drawn</b><br>
for one feature may automatically<br>
interfere with other linear features on a<br>
map. In short, it is difficult to represent<br>
more than one feature in a single map.<br>
<b>Choropleth map</b><br>
<b>A Choropleth map is derived from Greek</b><br>
word choros means area or region and<br>
plethos means multitude. It  is a type of<br>
a thematic map which is proportionally<br>
shaded, coloured, patterned or striped<br>
to indicate the pre-defined areas to a<br>
statistical variable represented on a<br>
map as an aggregate summary of a<br>
geographical characteristic within an<br>
area. Furthermore, Choropleth map is<br>
a map in which population densities of<br>
different areas are shaded in different<br>
coulours or patterns. Choropleth map<br>
is also known as the real density map.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for  the</b><br>
construction of choropleth map<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Calculate average densities</b><br>
(population density) for each<br>
statistical unit as  required;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Choose and draw according</b><br>
to scale of densities; and<br>
(iii)	  Shading should be heavier<br>
or dense as values increases.<br>
<b>Example: Construct a choropleth map</b><br>
for population density in selected regions<br>
in Tanzania as shown in Table 1.48.<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
choropleth map<br>
<b>Basically, choropleth maps are useful</b><br>
in a number of ways in the statistical<br>
information representations. The major<br>
ones include providing a good visual<br>
impression of the elements studied;  easy<br>
to compare the population densities of<br>
each unit, easy to interpret with the aid<br>
of key and  simple to draw when a map<br>
and a table have been provided. More<br>
over, choropleth map can accommodate<br>
any scale. Conversely, some  limitations<br>
of  the choropleth maps included<br>
giving a wrong impression that density<br>
changes suddenly at boundaries in a<br>
given map. Furthermore, variations of<br>
the density within each boundary are<br>
not shown hence, giving impression<br>
that there is uniform density within<br>
each administrative unit. Shading may<br>
obscure/hide other features.Lastly,<br>
drawing of the Choropleth maps is<br>
tiresome and time consuming particularly<br>
shading of large data.<br>
<b>Flowline map</b><br>
<b>Flowline map  also known as flow chart</b><br>
is a diagrammatically presentation of<br>
the movement of goods or number of<br>
vehicles, people, cattle etc. from one<br>
place to another.  It may show the<br>
movement of goods from production<br>
area to the market or point of export. The<br>
volume of flow is shown by line, whereby<br>
the width of the flow is an important<br>
factor which varies proportionally with<br>
the varying number of goods or people<br>
traveling along the  given routes. Also,<br>
width may be determined by the types<br>
and frequency of traffic ( by road, rail, air<br>
or water ) of the migratory movement of<br>
people, or stock, the paths of cyclone or<br>
ocean currents, the direction, quantity or<br>
character of exports or imports and many<br>
other statistics involving movement.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
<b>The following are procedures for</b><br>
constructing flowline map<br>
(i)	 Choose a scale which is appropriate<br>
to the data given;<br>
(ii)	 Consider the width of the flow line,<br>
which must be proportional to the<br>
quantity or value of the element to<br>
be represented;<br>
(iii)	Draw the sketch map and indicate<br>
the important towns and cities<br>
involved such as  along the pass<br>
and the destination of movement<br>
in question;<br>
(iv)	 In drawing the flow line on the base<br>
map, it is not necessary to   follow<br>
all the twisting and windings of<br>
road or railway; and<br>
(v)	 The meeting place of various<br>
flow line (for example, a market<br>
town, bus station, port of import<br>
or export, collecting centre and<br>
so on), can be shown by various<br>
methods and the easiest method<br>
being to bring shaped nucleus.  It<br>
should be noted that the nucleus<br>
may develop but the interest is<br>
in the movement of commodities<br>
and not representation of meeting<br>
places.<br>
<b>The data in Table 1.49(a) which shows</b><br>
the movement of rice from Morogoro to<br>
other regions in Tanzania has been used<br>
to draw flow line map (Figure 1.39).<br>

<b>Advantages and disadvantages of</b><br>
flowline maps<br>
<b>The flowline maps are widely useful</b><br>
as flow data in statistical form are<br>
replaced by simple methods of<br>
presentation and interpretation. The<br>
flowline chart maps can clearly and<br>
insightfully give clue on the problems<br>
that are likely to hinder movement for<br>
example, traffic congestion. Flowline<br>
maps are very versatile and can show<br>
two traffic and various methods of<br>
transport. Additionally, the method<br>
of construction is fairly easy once the<br>
scale has been decided. However, the<br>
flowline maps have some limitations<br>
such as very low volumes  represented<br>
by a non-scaled line. Moreover, log or<br>
square root flowlines lose their direction<br>
proportions and the reader has to use the<br>
key frequently. Furthermore, the wide<br>
variation between the highest and the<br>
lowest volume of data makes the scale<br>
difficult to assess.<br>

any other nearby library,<br>
study geographical maps from<br>
geography books and resources.<br>

extensive discussion.<br>
<b>Limitations of statistics</b><br>
<b>Despite the practicality of statistics in</b><br>
many fields, The following are some<br>
of its limitations: Firstly, statistics<br>
deals with a group or set of data and<br>
attach less importance to individual<br>
items. Statistics is  inadequate where<br>
knowledge of individual items<br>
becomes necessary. It is most suited<br>
to those problems where aggregate<br>
characteristics are required. Secondly,<br>
statistics deals  with quantitative or<br>
numerical data. Basically, not all subjects<br>
can be expressed numerically. There<br>
are situations where qualitative data<br>
are required. For example, poverty,<br>
behaviour, intelligence and health are all<br>
qualitative data which cannot be directly<br>
quantified. So, these types of data are<br>
not suitable for statistical analysis.<br>
<b>Thirdly, errors during sampling can lead</b><br>
to establishment of wrong conclusions<br>
if not handled by experts. Incorrect<br>
application of methods could lead to<br>
the drawing of wrong conclusions in<br>
statistics. Fourthly, statistical data in<br>
most cases is usually approximated and<br>
not very exact. Emphasis is often put<br>
on sampling method of data collection,<br>
that means if a limited number of items<br>
are selected, the result of the sample<br>
may not be a true representation of the<br>
population.<br>
<b>Statistics and computer</b><br>
<b>After surveying various methods for</b><br>
data analysis and presentation, it is<br>
important  to shade light on the existing<br>
sophisticated means  of data management<br>
and storage for  statistical geography.<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>The growing wave of science and</b><br>
technology has attracted the statisticians<br>
in integrating the field of computer and<br>
information technology in statistics.<br>
<b>Though not covered extensively in this</b><br>
book it is important to understand how<br>
the era of computer science has usefully<br>
simplified management of numerical data<br>
in statistics. It is undeniable truth that<br>
to date computer can usefully be used<br>
in recording and storing of a massive<br>
numerical data collected from the field<br>
and in turn be summarised to make<br>
meaningful information for interpretation<br>
and reporting. The modern electronic<br>
computer can be used in performing<br>
a number of statistical calculations<br>
within a short time. Geographers can<br>
make use of computer in number of<br>
ways including recording,  analysing,<br>
summarising data and commanding the<br>
computer software to generate figures<br>
and tables based on recorded data thus<br>
simplify interpretation.  The increasing<br>
knowledge and skills on computer has<br>
enabled drawing of computer-based maps<br>
indicating distributions of data such as<br>
temperature, rainfall and population in<br>
a given location or area.<br>
<b>Currently, geographers are developing</b><br>
interests in understanding computer<br>
software and programme that are vital<br>
in managing statistical data. Some useful<br>
computer software and programme that<br>
can be employed in data analysis include<br>
<b>Microsoft Excel and  Statistical Product</b><br>
and  Service Solution (SPSS) which<br>
were known for the past several decades.<br>
<b>The desired statistical output of data</b><br>
from the computer relies on the good<br>
data entry for analyses and the  choice<br>
of the  appropriate output command.<br>
<b>Application of statistics</b><br>
<b>Today the methods used in statistics are</b><br>
universal. Basing on this fact, will suffice<br>
to show how statistics is important.<br>
<b>However, there are many people who</b><br>
in one way or another practise statistics<br>
unknowingly not familiar with statistics.<br>
<b>Statistical methods are common ways of</b><br>
thinking  something practised by many<br>
people. A number of examples can be<br>
made to link human behaviour  statistical<br>
methods. It should be clear that statistical<br>
methods are so closely connected with<br>
human actions and behaviour, thus<br>
practically all human activities can be<br>
described through statistical methods.<br>
<b>From this broad view of the importance</b><br>
of statistics and its universality, below<br>
are some significant uses of statistics in<br>
daily life.<br>
<b>Statistics in planning</b><br>
<b>Statistics is crucial in planning  different</b><br>
forms of systems,  it may be in business,<br>
environmental and natural resources;<br>
management economy and in other<br>
sectors in the government. Statistics<br>
is used by different organisations and<br>
social institutions in informing and<br>
helping to formulate policy decision.<br>
<b>Because of the position of statistics in</b><br>
our daily life; to date, we commonly<br>
hear people talking about statistical<br>
data either relating to production,<br>
consumption, birth, death, investment,<br>
income, environmental quality, rate of<br>
environmental degradation or status<br>
of environmental rehabilitation. All<br>
89<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
these are helpful in making informed<br>
decisions in our practices in life.<br>
<b>Generally, environmental policy making</b><br>
and implementation, monitoring and<br>
evaluation are of planning and decision-<br>
making activities that are supported  by<br>
the use of statistics.<br>
<b>Statistics is beneficial in many ways to</b><br>
the government. Most of the decision<br>
making made by the government are<br>
largely informed by the findings from<br>
various statistical studies.  Statistics<br>
is useful tool for policy framing,<br>
implementation and evaluation. It<br>
is also utilised in the planning and<br>
distributions of various resources in<br>
different  government  sectors such<br>
as education health, transportation,<br>
infrastructures and many others.<br>
<b>Statistics in mathematics</b><br>
<b>Statistics depends on mathematics in</b><br>
achieving its role and objectives. The<br>
modern theory of statistics has its<br>
grounds in mathematics for example,<br>
statistical probability and statistical<br>
reasoning. Ever increasing role of<br>
mathematics into statistics has led to<br>
the development of a new branch of<br>
statistics called Mathematical Statistics.<br>
<b>Essentially, statistics is  one among  the</b><br>
branches of mathematics. Thus, Statistics<br>
may be considered to be an important<br>
component of the mathematics discipline<br>
and it is a branch of applied mathematics<br>
which is confined on data.<br>
<b>Statistics in economics</b><br>
Statistics and Economics are inseparable.<br>
<b>The advancement made in the modern</b><br>
statistical methods have  led to an<br>
extensive application of statistics in<br>
<b>Economics. The main important branches</b><br>
of Economics such as consumption,<br>
exchange, distribution and public<br>
finance are making use of statistics for<br>
the sake of comparison, presentation and<br>
interpretation. Furthermore, problems<br>
resulting from spending of income<br>
on and by different sections of the<br>
people, production of national wealth,<br>
adjustment of demand and supply and<br>
effects of economic policies on the<br>
economy etc, simply justify the use of<br>
statistics in the field of economics and<br>
in its different branches. Essentially,<br>
statistics of public finances helps us<br>
to impose tax, to provide subsidies to<br>
spend on various sectors and determine,<br>
amount of money to be borrowed or lent.<br>
<b>Hence statistics can never be thought of</b><br>
without economics or economics can<br>
never be thought without statistics.<br>
<b>Statistics in social sciences</b><br>
<b>In daily life every social phenomenon</b><br>
is in one way or another affected to a<br>
marked extent by several factors which<br>
bring out the variations in observations<br>
from time to time, place to place and<br>
object to object. That is why statistical<br>
methods strive in establishing and<br>
describing the relationships and<br>
independence between factors by<br>
studying and isolating the effect of each<br>
of these factors on the given observation.<br>
<b>The most important application of</b><br>
statistics in human geography is in<br>
the field of demography for studying<br>
mortality (death rates), fertility (birth<br>
rates), marriages, population growth and<br>
so on.<br>
90<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Application of statistics in geography</b><br>
<b>Statistics in trade</b><br>
<b>Statistics is embodied with methods for</b><br>
making wise decisions in the face of<br>
uncertainties. It is clear that business is<br>
often full of uncertainties and risks. In<br>
this case we always need to forecast at<br>
every step and time. The future trend<br>
of the market can only be projected<br>
if we make use of statistics. Failure<br>
in anticipation will mean failure of<br>
business. For example, changes in<br>
demand, supply, habits and  fashion<br>
can largely be anticipated through the<br>
statistical methods. Statistics is vital in<br>
determining prices of  various products,<br>
determining the phases of boom and<br>
depression. Hence, the use of statistics<br>
smoothens the process of running a<br>
business, reduce  uncertainties and<br>
thus contributing to the success of the<br>
business.<br>
<b>Statistics in research work</b><br>
<b>The task of a researcher is to disseminate</b><br>
the findings of the research to the<br>
community. The explanations on<br>
the effects of a certain variable on<br>
a particular problem, under varying<br>
circumstances and situations, can better<br>
be known by the researcher only after<br>
making use of the statistical methods.<br>
Statistics is basic to research activities.<br>
<b>Researchers  maintain and enliven their</b><br>
research interests and research activities,<br>
by employing knowledge and skills from<br>
statistics.<br>
<b>Statistics in weather forecasting</b><br>
<b>Understanding weather information and</b><br>
climate behaviour are crucial to human<br>
well being. Weather information is needed<br>
on a daily basis in the course of planning<br>
from the national to the individual<br>
level. For examples, farmers need it for<br>
planning their farming activities for good<br>
harvest.  Also, weather can be used in<br>
prediction and forecasts of extremely<br>
weather events that may require some<br>
measures to reduce its effects. Similarly,<br>
climate is a determinant of life on<br>
<b>Earth, particularly the distribution of</b><br>
resources and determination of activities.<br>
<b>Following this, statistical information</b><br>
will is significant  in facilitating the<br>
making of  informed decision hence<br>
contributing to the development in<br>
different spheres of life.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Two</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
Geography as a subject which is often studied theoretically in the classroom, can<br>
be validated through the real-life field experiences using sense organs. In this<br>
chapter, you will learn about sources of scientific knowledge acquisition, types,<br>
and importance of research, research problem formulation, literature review,<br>
hypotheses or questions formulation and process of selecting relevant research<br>
designs.  Furthermore, you will learn about sample and sampling designs, methods<br>
and tools for data collection, data analysis, and interpretation of the results and<br>
reporting. The competencies developed from this chapter will enable you to identify<br>
the existing gaps in geographical knowledge and practices, conduct geographical<br>
research and use the evolved solutions in promoting proper human practices on<br>
the interaction with the environment.<br>
<b>Conceptualising research</b><br>
<b>Research refers to a search for knowledge</b><br>
in order to discover the truth. It is done<br>
systematically on a particular topic<br>
or issue for explaining, describing,<br>
or predicting it. Field is an area or<br>
environment where the research is<br>
being carried out. Thus, field research is<br>
normally carried out in a natural setting<br>
rather than structured environment<br>
like laboratories and classrooms. It is<br>
the science of observing, evaluating,<br>
selecting and reporting on geographical<br>
phenomena from a specific area. It<br>
involves physical data collection from the<br>
real-life environment. Before conducting<br>
a research, there are important things to<br>
consider. These include identification<br>
of research problem selection of an<br>
appropriate research site, identifying<br>
methods and preparing tools for data<br>
collection. Also to some extent having<br>
clues on the attitude of the respondents<br>
and carrying out a pilot study. Field<br>
research allows the researcher to interact<br>
with the natural environment through<br>
observations and conversations to elicit<br>
information concerning the data sought to<br>
answer the research questions. A number<br>
of methods and techniques are used in<br>
field studies. Some of the commonly<br>
methods and techniques used include;<br>
observation of events in the natural<br>
settings, studying of information from<br>
the existing records (archival research),<br>
field experiments (experiments carried<br>
out in natural settings) to understand<br>
relations among variables. Furthermore,<br>
field research can use surveys for<br>
collecting data from people’s actions,<br>
98<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
thoughts, and behaviours through asking<br>
questions related to their natural settings.<br>
<b>The process of conducting field research</b><br>
needs good preparation from budgeting,<br>
time schedule and  well set research tools<br>
for data collection.<br>
<b>Criteria of a good research</b><br>
<b>A common characteristics to all types</b><br>
of research is application of scientific<br>
method. Research being a process of<br>
collecting, organizing, analyzing and<br>
interpreting information to answer<br>
per-determined questions adhere the<br>
established criteria. A good research<br>
has to be;<br>
<b>Systematic: meaning that, any research</b><br>
should be carried out in a well-structured<br>
framework with a clear step by step<br>
process in attaining the solutions or<br>
conclusions. In this case, research<br>
starts by defining the research problem<br>
followed by review of literature then<br>
stating research questions or formulating<br>
hypothesis, selecting a research design,<br>
selecting the study area, collecting data,<br>
organizing data, analyzing data to answer<br>
questions or to test hypotheses, and<br>
finally writing a report.<br>
<b>Logical: this means that, any research</b><br>
should be guided by the rules of<br>
appropriate organisation and flow of<br>
ideas throughout the research process.<br>
<b>This is mainly focusing on inductive and</b><br>
deductive reasoning approaches which<br>
are compulsory in decision making<br>
process.<br>
<b>Empirical: meaning that a  research</b><br>
should basically be related to one or<br>
more aspects of a real-life situations or<br>
conditions from which data are gathered<br>
in achieving valid research results. In<br>
other words, conclusions are being<br>
drawn based on true evidences collected<br>
through life experiences and  repeated<br>
observations.<br>
<b>Replicable: this means that  research</b><br>
methods and findings of a given study<br>
should be stated in such a way that they<br>
allow to be verified or tested by other<br>
researchers. In this regard, a research is<br>
thought to be replicable if independent<br>
researcher (s) elsewhere will apply the<br>
same methods used by the previous<br>
researcher and arrive to the same<br>
conclusion made in the previous study.<br>
<b>Cumulative: knowledge is accumulated</b><br>
as a result of time to time studies, in the<br>
sense that new studies should be built<br>
over what has already been done  on the<br>
subject matter of interest. Therefore, new<br>
knowledge adds up to the existing one.<br>
<b>Theory driven: theories are very</b><br>
important in research since they connect<br>
researchers with the existing knowledge<br>
as they offer a conceptual model from<br>
which data are collected. Generally, there<br>
is no research without a theory. It may<br>
start with theory or end-up generating<br>
a new theory.<br>
<b>Objectivity; means that any research</b><br>
should be strongly related to the research<br>
problem and it relies on observations<br>
from actual studies which can be either<br>
cross-sectional or longitudinal. Similarly,<br>
conclusions should be drawn from  the<br>
available set of evidence with the aim<br>
of avoiding biasness.<br>
99<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Generalisability: research as a scientific</b><br>
process allows conclusions to be<br>
generalised and universal. In the case,<br>
generalisation is made from a sample to<br>
the population.<br>
<b>Clarity: scientific research should be</b><br>
precise and with  good explanation.<br>
<b>Rigorous; research should ensure that the</b><br>
methods used in answering the research<br>
questions are relevant and justifiable.<br>
<b>Sources of knowledge acquisition</b><br>
and approaches<br>
<b>Throughout our lifetime we have</b><br>
accumulated a body of knowledge.<br>
<b>The curiosity to know the environment</b><br>
and the need to improve our life is<br>
natural to human beings. Humans have<br>
used numerous methods and sources<br>
to acquire knowledge. Knowledge<br>
acquisition has been mainly from;<br>
authority from which  human being<br>
learns. Some of these authorities are<br>
teachers, parents, leaders at work or<br>
any expert who may provide the needed<br>
knowledge. Such authorities may be<br>
a source of knowledge, experience or<br>
both. The authorities also can be books,<br>
newspapers, dictionary, encyclopedia,<br>
journal, articles or websites. Knowledge<br>
is also obtained from our traditions<br>
in which human being accepts many<br>
traditions of their forefathers or<br>
culture. For example, aspects such as<br>
food, dress, religion, home remedies,<br>
discipline, ways of behaving and others<br>
can be learned from traditions.<br>
<b>Furthermore, knowledge can be</b><br>
acquired through experiences. That is<br>
through education, own experiences<br>
on problem solving or understanding<br>
of a phenomena. These are the most<br>
commonly sources of gaining experience<br>
which we are familiar with and are<br>
fundamental sources of knowledge. In<br>
sharpening comprehension and accept<br>
learning through these sources, several<br>
approaches are used. Among them are:<br>
(a) 	Empiricism: this is based on our<br>
senses. For example, through<br>
hearing and seeing we can associate<br>
some phenomena like sounds and<br>
their sources. Through senses we<br>
can compare objects, phenomena<br>
or events. Hence, our senses<br>
help us to study and understand<br>
relationships between various<br>
concepts. For example, we can<br>
associate changes/variations in<br>
temperature with climate change<br>
by observing temperature trends<br>
for over 30 years.<br>
(b) 	Rationalism: this relies on mental<br>
reflections on ideas rather than<br>
materials. The logical links between<br>
two or more ideas, can lead us into<br>
accepting those ideas. For example,<br>
we may reason that, appropriate<br>
farm management is expected to<br>
improve crop yield per piece of<br>
land.<br>
(c) 	Fideism: through beliefs, emotions<br>
and reactions. We acquire<br>
knowledge from religion by<br>
believing through the teachings<br>
provided by our religious leaders<br>
rather than the use of our own<br>
senses or need for logical proof.<br>

<b>The purpose of doing research</b><br>
<b>Normally, research strives to answer</b><br>
questions through the use of scientific<br>
procedures. The aim of a researcher is<br>
to uncover the hidden or undiscovered<br>
knowledge. Though there are varieties<br>
of research yet the following research<br>
purposes may suffice. Firstly,  to<br>
gain familiarity on the researched<br>
phenomenon or acquire new insight.<br>
<b>Secondly, to depict accurately the</b><br>
characteristics or nature of a particular<br>
individual, situation or a group under<br>
the study; thirdly, to determine the<br>
frequency or recurrence of some subjects<br>
and fourthly, to test hypothesis or causal<br>
relationship between variables in order<br>
to develop theories and criticisms.<br>
<b>Types of research projects</b><br>
<b>Search for new knowledge has resulted</b><br>
into multiplication of researches. The<br>
emerged variety of researches can be<br>
classified into four main categories;<br>
basing on the nature of information<br>
sought, utility of content, the research<br>
approach employed and objective<br>
perspectives.<br>
<b>On the basis of nature of information</b><br>
sought: research can be grouped into two<br>
categories namely, qualitative research<br>
and quantitative research. Qualitative<br>
research is a study which deals with<br>
non-numerical data. This is a form of<br>
field research which is carried out in a<br>
naturalistic setting that mainly generates<br>
qualitative data through observations<br>
and interviews. Qualitative research<br>
aims at describing the characteristics<br>
of samples. Quantitative research is<br>
the study which uses numerical data<br>
to address behaviour and attitude.  It is<br>
used when quantitative data are sought<br>
of. It is mainly concerned with making<br>
inferences from randomly selected<br>
samples to a larger population. However,<br>
with time there has been a growing need<br>
for integrating both qualitative and<br>
quantitative data in order to cross-check<br>
101<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
the results obtained from the combined<br>
methods and  this has given rise to mixed<br>
methods research. Recently, most of<br>
the researchers have found it logical<br>
to opt for mixed methods research in<br>
answering research questions.<br>
<b>Basing on the utility of content or nature</b><br>
of subject matter, the research streams<br>
into two categories: first category is<br>
basic research which is also known<br>
as fundamental research, pure or<br>
theoretical research. The aim of these<br>
researches are to find out the basic truth<br>
or principles. Normally, the generated<br>
findings in this case are universal<br>
likewise the utility is universal. These<br>
researches are generally guided by<br>
theories for example, Newton’s law of<br>
universal gravitation, Newton’s laws<br>
of motion, Albert Einstein’s general<br>
theory of relativity and many more.<br>
<b>The second category is experimental</b><br>
or applied research or action research.<br>
<b>This is concerned with finding new</b><br>
applications of scientific knowledge<br>
to solve scientific problems such as<br>
development of  new system. Normally,<br>
the  findings from this research has a<br>
confined utility  to the individuals who<br>
benefit from them.<br>
<b>With regards to the approach of research</b><br>
applied there are two main categories<br>
namely, longitudinal research and<br>
cross-sectional research. Longitudinal<br>
research deals with studying the same<br>
site at varying intervals of time in order<br>
to establish change. This is exemplified<br>
in historical and case study research.<br>
<b>Cross-sectional is concerned with</b><br>
collecting data at one point in time from<br>
a predetermined sites and individuals.<br>
<b>An example, of crossectional research is</b><br>
studying child development behaviours<br>
using different groups of children with<br>
different ages but examining them at<br>
one point in time.<br>
<b>Concerning objectives perspectives as</b><br>
criteria we have a descriptive research.<br>
<b>Descriptive research is confined on</b><br>
explaining the conditions of variables<br>
based on the situation at which data<br>
were taken. This type of research is<br>
further streamed into observational,<br>
survey and case study researches. While<br>
observational research is concerned<br>
with seeing and tapping data in a natural<br>
condition of the research participants<br>
or objects, a case study deals with an<br>
in-depth study confined to a single<br>
participant or group. Whereas, survey<br>
research deals with the study of the<br>
present phenomena and it is quantitative<br>
in nature.<br>
<b>Other types based on objective</b><br>
perspectives are correlation, explanatory<br>
and exploratory research. Correlational<br>
research is devoted on explaining or<br>
discovering the extents at which two<br>
or more variables have associations or<br>
interdependence. Whereas explanatory<br>
research focuses on giving reasons and<br>
mechanisms on the existing relationships<br>
between two aspects or phenomena<br>
that are studied. The last category is<br>
exploratory research which deals with<br>
investigating a situation where little is<br>
understood in the body of knowledge.<br>
<b>It is used to examine the possibilities</b><br>
of undertaking a study in defined areas.<br>
<b>Because of its flexibility the findings</b><br>
102<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
from this research provide answers to<br>
what, who and why questions.<br>
