<b>Importance of research</b><br>
<b>Most of the researches conducted</b><br>
aim at solving practical or theoretical<br>
problems existing in the society. Based<br>
on such view, research provides the<br>
basis for decision-making and planning<br>
for government policies formulation,<br>
implementation, monitoring and<br>
evaluation. On the other hand, research<br>
may be geared towards addressing<br>
the problems that directly affect the<br>
community.<br>
<b>The importance of research cuts across</b><br>
the scientific and non-scientific fields.<br>
<b>Furthermore, it extends to our emerging</b><br>
life problems, events, phenomena and<br>
processes that we often come across.<br>
<b>Hence, the generated solutions and</b><br>
suggestions are required to solve the<br>
frequently occurring problems. The role<br>
of the researcher is to find their causes,<br>
explanations, solutions and applications.<br>
<b>In short, research familiarises us with</b><br>
the man-made and natural phenomena<br>
or issues surrounding us. Specifically,<br>
research is important in the following<br>
areas.<br>
<b>Firstly, in addressing a research</b><br>
problem which may be an existing<br>
issue or phenomenon that requires<br>
solutions based on scientific studies.<br>
<b>Such issues may emerge anywhere in</b><br>
the society and fields. Provided the<br>
problems require scientific analysis,<br>
103<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
our interests are to get an understanding<br>
of the problems and propose possible<br>
solutions. Secondly, research is one of<br>
the most potential sources of knowledge<br>
that provides us with guidelines on<br>
how to verify knowledge which we<br>
acquire. Thirdly, researches directed<br>
to the existing theories and concepts are<br>
helpful in understanding such theories<br>
and finding out ways to utilize them.<br>
<b>Fourthly, research plays part as a basis</b><br>
for governments’ planning and decision-<br>
making. This demand enhances the<br>
emphasis that researchers should always<br>
strive for valid and reliable researches<br>
for backing up the decisions. Research<br>
provides a basis for many government<br>
policies in a variety of dimensions. For<br>
example, research on the effectiveness<br>
of strategies to empower community<br>
adaptation to climate change and<br>
dynamics of the crop farming practices<br>
can improve decision making in the<br>
formulation of environmental and<br>
natural resources management policies.<br>
<b>Fifthly, research can benefit a number</b><br>
of sectors in improving practices for<br>
example, in production, markets of<br>
goods and services and shade light on<br>
the environmental management options<br>
like clean production for sustainable<br>
development. Sixth, research can lead<br>
to identification and characterisation<br>
of brand-new goods and services.<br>
<b>Through research we can also widen our</b><br>
understanding of various phenomena<br>
such as climate change, its effects and<br>
suggested measures to be taken for<br>
either mitigation or adaptation. Seventh,<br>
research findings act as one of the<br>
sources for deliberating further actions<br>
in areas with noticeable progress, such<br>
as the case of malaria. Despite measures<br>
taken to eradicate malaria, the cases<br>
have kept on increasing. Thus, research<br>
directed to this problem will be helpful<br>
in changing practices and measures to<br>
readdress the problem. Eigth, research<br>
results enable us to address the existing<br>
social problems and phenomena and<br>
seek solutions.  Ninth, research can<br>
provide us with some new life style<br>
and ways of living. For example, the<br>
increased understanding of COVID<br>
-19 through research on the way it<br>
spreads and preventive measures, we<br>
have experienced a drastic change in<br>
the custom of shaking hands when we<br>
meet as one of the  ways of preventing<br>
the spread of the pandemic.<br>

<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Research proposal and report</b><br>
writing<br>
<b>Field research should be preceded by</b><br>
a research proposal and finalised by<br>
report writing. Writing of a report must<br>
be carefully done  in three major sections<br>
which are the preliminary pages, the<br>
main text and appendices.<br>
<b>Research proposal</b><br>
<b>A research proposal is a document written</b><br>
by a researcher that gives  detailed<br>
explanations on how a researcher<br>
plans to do the research. It is a plan<br>
suggesting what the researcher intends<br>
to do, means of doing it, and proposes<br>
resources to accomplish the plan. It is<br>
a descriptive plan of action, which is to<br>
be followed in carrying out a particular<br>
research. It is like an outline of the whole<br>
research process that gives a reader the<br>
summary of the information discussed<br>
in the plan. Preparation of research<br>
proposal is needed since it facilitates<br>
the planning of  different research<br>
operations, hence making the research<br>
as efficient as possible, yielding adequate<br>
information with minimal expenditure.<br>
<b>In fact, the research proposal is the</b><br>
conceptual structure within which<br>
research is conducted; it comprises of<br>
the plan for the collection, organisation<br>
and analysis of data. A good research<br>
proposal quickly and easily answers the<br>
following questions; what do you want<br>
to do? How much will it cost? How long<br>
will it take? What difference will it make<br>
to your school, society or nation?  What<br>
has already been done in the topic of<br>
your interest? How do you plan to do<br>
it? Will the results be evaluated? How<br>
will the results be communicated? The<br>
questions will be answered in different<br>
ways depending on the nature of the<br>
proposed plan. Most proposals are<br>
between ten and twenty pages in length.<br>
<b>The proposal should not be longer than</b><br>
2500 words without  list of references.<br>
<b>Importance of a research proposal</b><br>
<b>Research proposal helps the researcher</b><br>
to focus on important issues about the<br>
study. It enables the researcher to focus<br>
on which research questions need to be<br>
answered, how the data will be collected,<br>
who will provide the data and where<br>
will the data be obtained. It gives the<br>
researcher a chance to evaluate the study<br>
by predicting the difficulties which are<br>
likely to appear and planning to solve<br>
them before. The proposal acts as a guide<br>
to general strategies from the beginning<br>
of research to its completion. It shades<br>
light on the expected costs of the research<br>
to enable budgeting for its completion.<br>
<b>It also provides time schedule for the</b><br>
research. It guides the researcher to<br>
prepare material and resources in a<br>
logical manner. The proposal enables the<br>
researcher to define the boundaries of the<br>
study and the concepts to be included.<br>
<b>It should be noted that, the better  you</b><br>
organise your ideas at this stage, the<br>
more effective time and resources will be<br>
spent. Normally, a research proposal is<br>
written in future tense, since it is a plan<br>
to be implemented in future.<br>
105<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>The format of a research proposal</b><br>
<b>The research proposal has a format which</b><br>
is the general pattern of the organisation<br>
and arrangement of the study. This<br>
involves the following parts; preliminary<br>
pages which include the title of the study,<br>
the name of the researcher, year of the<br>
study, abstract, table of contents and list<br>
of tables; the main body of the research<br>
proposal and the appendices. Basically,<br>
the main body of a research proposal is<br>
made up of the following:<br>
<b>Title of the proposal: It should be short</b><br>
and precise ranging between 10-15<br>
words written in such a way that it gives<br>
a very quick picture on what the proposal<br>
is about. When writing the title  there<br>
should be a consideration that, it draws<br>
attention, creates interest and desire<br>
to the reader to go through the entire<br>
document.<br>
<b>Abstract: This section offers an overview</b><br>
of the entire research proposal ranging<br>
from the title to the methodology. The<br>
abstract has to be short but, capturing<br>
all important issues.<br>
<b>Background to the research problem:</b><br>
<b>In this part, a researcher provides</b><br>
background information on the topic<br>
of interest and arguments starting from<br>
global, regional to local levels. It is under<br>
this part a researcher highlights on what<br>
is already known in the field, what is not<br>
known, and what the researcher wishes<br>
to be known in the proposed study.<br>
<b>Statement of the research problem:</b><br>
<b>This refers to the  statement on what</b><br>
is the issue that need a researcher’s<br>
attention and its magnitude. The origin<br>
of the research problem may come as a<br>
result of reviewing literatures, own life<br>
experiences, discussions with colleagues<br>
or experts in the same field and others.<br>
<b>Research objectives: In this section a</b><br>
researcher should  have the general  and<br>
specific objective. The general  objective<br>
articulate what the researcher intends to<br>
achieve while specific objectives  show<br>
how the main objective will be attained.<br>
<b>Research questions or hypotheses: In</b><br>
this section the researcher  prefers to use<br>
either research questions or hypotheses,<br>
depending on the nature of the problem<br>
and the field of study. For example,<br>
questions are very common in social<br>
sciences while hypotheses are widely<br>
used in natural sciences.<br>
Significance of the study: In  this section,<br>
a researcher explain  why a particular<br>
research work is needed. Basically,<br>
it offers justification of conducting<br>
the proposed research and the impact<br>
it will develop. Moreover, it clarifies<br>
possible contributions to  knowledge<br>
and highlights on how other researchers<br>
will benefit from it.<br>
<b>Scope of the study: This section narrows</b><br>
down specific issues which will be<br>
addressed in a particular research<br>
work. Given the challenges in time and<br>
finances, it is practically not possible to<br>
study everything but focusing on one<br>
issue at a time.<br>
<b>Limitations of study: In this section</b><br>
it is important to highlight on the<br>
challenges that the researcher is likely<br>
106<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
to encounter. They are basically potential<br>
weaknesses to the study that are out of<br>
the researcher’s control associated with<br>
choice of research design, statistical<br>
models, funding and other factors.<br>
<b>Generally, limitations affect the study</b><br>
design, results and conclusions. Thus,<br>
the researcher should explain how each<br>
limitation will be managed without<br>
affecting the quality of the study.<br>
<b>Delimitation of the study: It addresses</b><br>
how the study will be narrowed down<br>
in scope. The purpose of this section is<br>
not to respond to the question that, ‘why<br>
<b>I did this?’ but focusing on the question</b><br>
‘why I did not do it like this?’ in other<br>
words, it provides reasons for rejecting a<br>
certain course of actions in the research<br>
process. As such delimitation are in the<br>
researcher’s control.<br>
<b>Review of the literature: This involves</b><br>
reviewing sources which are related to<br>
the subject matter stated in the title. It is a<br>
continuation of the information provided<br>
in background section but not repitition<br>
also it has several subheading. It includes<br>
both theoretical and empirical reviews.<br>
<b>Through reviewing literature, research</b><br>
gap is identified; hence the researcher<br>
avoids repeating what has already been<br>
done by other researchers. Therefore,<br>
the researcher becomes familiar with<br>
the area of study choosen.<br>
<b>Research methodology: In this section, a</b><br>
researcher precisely gives reasons for the<br>
choice of the study area, define methods,<br>
tools, and techniques that will be used<br>
in selecting sample, data collection, data<br>
organisation, data analysis, and results<br>
presentation. Additionally, the researcher<br>
indicates how the study will adhere to<br>
research ethics.<br>
<b>References: It refers to all documents</b><br>
which have been used in preparing<br>
a research proposal and are cited in<br>
the text. It includes published and<br>
unpublished sources such as reports,<br>
journal articles, books, book chapters,<br>
<b>Newspapers, conference proceedings</b><br>
and others.<br>
<b>Appendices: It consists of important</b><br>
supportive attachments such as;<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Data collection tools that will be</b><br>
used in the field.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Time frame: This is the time that</b><br>
will be taken from writing of the<br>
proposal to data analysis and<br>
reporting.<br>
(iii)	 Funding and sources of funds: This<br>
is the proposed budget and the<br>
breakdown which specifies how<br>
funds will be used to complete<br>
the work.<br>

choosen. The research proposal<br>
should be geographically related<br>
and organised in accordance<br>
with the format presented in this<br>
chapter.<br>

down<br>

<b>Research report</b><br>
<b>Report writing is the last step in</b><br>
the research process. In this step the<br>
researcher has to write a complete<br>
report of scientific research undertaken.<br>
<b>Research report is the process of</b><br>
communicating the results and the care<br>
that has been exercised throughout the<br>
study. In general, research report is a<br>
detailed account of the study conducted<br>
or systematic report of the findings of<br>
a research which describes the process<br>
and the data used in the study.<br>
<b>Components of a research report</b><br>
<b>Normally, the research report should</b><br>
have the following format as shown in<br>
Table 2.1.<br>
<b>Table 2.1: Research report format</b><br>
Preliminaries title page, abstract, table of contents, list of figures and tables,<br>
certification, declaration, dedication, acknowledgement and<br>
acronyms/abbreviations used.<br>
<b>Chapter 1</b><br>
<b>Introduction – background to the research problem, statement of</b><br>
the research problem, research hypotheses or questions, objectives<br>
of the study, significance of the study and scope of the study.<br>
<b>Chapter 2</b><br>
<b>Literature review – discusses related works, it discusses what is</b><br>
already known about the research topic as a whole and outlines<br>
the key ideas and theories for familiarising the current research.<br>
<b>Chapter 3</b><br>
<b>Methodology – describes procedures used in research, data</b><br>
collected and how it was obtained, organised, analysed and<br>
presented.<br>
<b>Chapter 4</b><br>
<b>Results and discussion– show the meaning of the presented</b><br>
research results, compares results from different sources included<br>
in the study (for example from question and observation) and<br>
relates the results to other researchers’ works in the same topic.<br>
This is followed by conclusion in each of the subsection.<br>
108<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Chapter 5</b><br>
<b>Summary, conclusion and recommendations – the conclusion</b><br>
normally summarises and interprets the major findings to describe<br>
what they  mean. This part gives reasons as to why the findings<br>
are in that manner. That is, answers the question, “So what?”.<br>
<b>The recommendations explain what should be done and  who</b><br>
should act to make the findings of the study meaningful to the<br>
society. Recommendations should be done in relation to research<br>
findings and not otherwise. In addition, this section also provide<br>
/identifies area for furthery study according to stud findings.<br>
<b>References</b><br>
<b>A collection of resources such as books, documents and reports</b><br>
that were used by researcher in writing the report and appear in<br>
the text are listed down in alphabetical order.<br>
<b>Appendices</b><br>
<b>Attachments which may contain some of the information</b><br>
related to the study such as  research tools, figures, tables and<br>
photographs.<br>

<b>Research process</b><br>
<b>In order to study and solve a problem, a</b><br>
research has to undergo several stages in<br>
a well regulated and systematic manner.<br>
<b>Failure in any stage is likely to affect</b><br>
the whole research process. In order to<br>
work systematically, a research process<br>
or stages goes through a series of actions<br>
which are:<br>
<b>Formulating the research problem</b><br>
<b>Formulating of a research problem is an</b><br>
important step that will uncover what<br>
problem  is worthy studying by explaining<br>
clearly what has been documented, what<br>
has not been documented and what needs<br>
109<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
to be documented.  The main function<br>
of a research problem is to determine<br>
what needs to be researched. Basically,<br>
the main sources of a research problem<br>
include conversation with people<br>
you can gain insight and find out the<br>
existing imbalances, research gaps<br>
and issues to be researched. Moreover,<br>
a research problem can be identified<br>
by exploring the interventions and<br>
programmes that have been in place.<br>
<b>For example, exploring from the projects</b><br>
and activities aiming at empowering<br>
the community adaptation to the impact<br>
of climate change. Similarly, exploring<br>
from projects on promoting students’<br>
performance in secondary schools,<br>
poverty eradication programmes,<br>
restoration of soil fertility programmes<br>
and intervention on increasing the<br>
dissemination of weather and climate<br>
change information. Therefore, through<br>
critical studying in such areas one may<br>
decide to evaluate the effectiveness of<br>
one of those interventions.  Another way<br>
of establishing a research problem is<br>
based on experiences evaluating some<br>
existing phenomena and establishing<br>
some areas worth to be researched.<br>
<b>For example, one may decide to focus</b><br>
on the problem of water scarcity in<br>
arid and semi-arid areas and decide to<br>
study the existing mechanism for water<br>
scarcity. As a starting point in thinking<br>
how to develop a research problem the<br>
following samples of a research problem<br>
can be useful guides to you:<br>
<b>Sample A: It is generally accepted that</b><br>
the use of contraceptive pills is one of<br>
the effective methods of controlling<br>
conception. However, one of the<br>
studies found out that forty percent<br>
of  women who were administered<br>
with contraceptive pills, conceived.<br>
<b>Some of the possible explanation on</b><br>
this could be timing of the use of the<br>
pills, health conditions of the users, the<br>
effectiveness of the pills themselves in<br>
terms of chemical composition, genetic<br>
variations among the users, the body<br>
resistance to the pills and the integrity<br>
of information given by the clients.<br>
<b>Despite having detailed studies on</b><br>
contraceptives which address types and<br>
their advantages to women health, there<br>
have been limited studies on the factors<br>
affecting the use and functioning of<br>
contraceptives among women. Lack of<br>
studies in this area increases confusions<br>
and suffering among women and their<br>
families. Therefore, this study is going to<br>
determine factors affecting effectiveness<br>
of contraceptive pills among post-user<br>
women.<br>
<b>Sample B: Various measures have been</b><br>
taken by the government of Tanzania<br>
in collaboration with various internal<br>
and external stakeholders in eradicating<br>
malaria in the country. Such measures<br>
include improvement of health facilities;<br>
diagnostic measures and medications;<br>
awareness raising on environmental<br>
cleanliness; removal of stagnant water<br>
in our neighborhoods; and provision<br>
of protective gears such as mosquito<br>
nets. Despite all these measures, malaria<br>
incidences have kept on increasing in<br>
various areas including the village in<br>
which this study will be conducted.<br>
<b>Although various studies have been</b><br>
110<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
conducted on the challenges of malaria<br>
and the effectiveness of malaria control<br>
measures, information is lacking on the<br>
sociocultural factors influencing control<br>
of malaria in the country. Therefore, this<br>
study is geared towards that end. Lack<br>
of studies of this nature is likely to limit<br>
the government efforts towards creating<br>
a malaria free society, thus government’s<br>
burden on treating the victims will keep<br>
on increasing.<br>
<b>Sample C: It is an acceptable fact that the</b><br>
impact of climate change is affecting all<br>
people equally but different in different<br>
parts of the world with slightly variations<br>
across gender differences. Most of the<br>
studies have documented on how women<br>
have been affected by the impact of<br>
climate change and associated adaptation<br>
measures. However, there are limited<br>
studies on the extent of impact of climate<br>
change to the elderly population and the<br>
adaptation measures to this social age<br>
group. Failure to document this area<br>
means that the vulnerability to the impact<br>
of climate change and social disturbance<br>
will keep on increasing and many of<br>
the elderly in society will be negatively<br>
affected. Therefore, this study is an<br>
attempt to that end.<br>
<b>Considerations in selecting a research</b><br>
problem<br>
<b>For manageable and sustainable</b><br>
motivation, a researcher should consider<br>
the following when selecting a research<br>
area or a research problem. Firstly,<br>
interest in the area make the  researcher<br>
to be motivated throughout the research<br>
process. Secondly, it is important to<br>
consider the magnitude of the selected<br>
topic. This will enable the researcher<br>
to balance time and resources for<br>
completing the study on time. Therefore,<br>
a researcher is required to narrow down<br>
the topic hence make it manageable,<br>
specific and clear. Thirdly, the researcher<br>
should make sure that the indicators<br>
and  concepts studied are measurable<br>
and verifiable. Fourthly, the researcher<br>
should have adequate and appropriate<br>
research knowledge and skills to address<br>
the problem to be studied. Fifthly, the<br>
researcher should focus on a relevant<br>
research problem that is likely to fill<br>
the existing knowledge gap, add new<br>
knowledge and improve practices in the<br>
researched area. This will be an additional<br>
aspect to sustain the interest of the study.<br>
<b>Sixthly, consideration of ethical issues in</b><br>
relation to the area the researcher plans<br>
to study is of great importance. This item<br>
requires the researcher’s professionalism<br>
and flexibility especially when the<br>
previous plans affected the research<br>
ethics. For example, dealing with<br>
sensitive researches in areas like rape,<br>
early marriages, HIV/AIDS and other<br>
diseases, flexibility and professionalism<br>
are highly required in order to approach<br>
the problem wisely and successfully.<br>
<b>Criteria for a good research problem</b><br>
<b>There are a number of criteria that need</b><br>
to be considered in writing a research<br>
problem. A good research problem<br>
is likely to adhere to the following<br>
qualities: Firstly, it should be novel<br>
that, the problem should come up with<br>
a new process, product or principle<br>
that can help in improving practices.<br>
111<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Secondly, it should be interesting in the</b><br>
sense that it draws attention and interest<br>
to other people. Thirdly, it should be<br>
innovative meaning that it improves the<br>
current or existing state of affairs and<br>
possibly technology. Fourthly, it should<br>
be cost effective in such a way that it<br>
produces good value for money, time<br>
and resources.  It should be addressing<br>
the problem of the community.<br>
<b>Challenges of writing a research</b><br>
problem<br>
<b>There are many challenges facing</b><br>
researchers in their attempt to write<br>
quality research problems. Some of<br>
these challenges include; difficulties in<br>
deciding on the topic for research, lack<br>
of good knowledge of the methodology<br>
to be used, inability of finding current,<br>
specialized and related references such<br>
as books, lack of interest in research,<br>
lack of understanding the subject<br>
matter, structure of time limit and lack<br>
of good research guidance. Others are<br>
misconception of the research problem<br>
for example, one may think if some<br>
people in the community do not have<br>
money, therefore, a research problem<br>
will be inadequate fund. This will make<br>
a study something different from the<br>
problem, because lack of money is an<br>
outcome of something else such as<br>
unemployment.<br>
<b>Research objectives</b><br>
<b>Research objectives are normally</b><br>
developed after stating research problem.<br>
<b>They emerge from the problem as they</b><br>
intend to make the research focused and<br>
answer the research questions or cover<br>
the gap developed in the statement of<br>
the problem. Research objectives should<br>
be closely related to the statement of<br>
the problem and summarise what is<br>
intended to be achieved by the study.<br>
<b>Good research objectives are useful</b><br>
in defining the focus of your study;<br>
clearly identifying the variables to be<br>
measured; describe action to be taken in<br>
establishing the limit of the study; they<br>
guide the researcher through avoiding<br>
collecting unwanted data for answering<br>
the research questions. Normally,<br>
research objectives are stated in such<br>
a way that they start with action verbs<br>
that can easily be measured for example,<br>
‘to compare’…, ‘to calculate’, ‘to<br>
assess…’, ‘to determine…’, ‘to verify…’<br>
‘to identify…’, ‘to ascertain…’, ‘to<br>
measure…’, ‘to explore…’. Strictly,<br>
avoid the use of vague non-active verbs in<br>
stating objectives such as: to appreciate,<br>
to understand, to believe, to study, and<br>
to think because it is difficult to evaluate<br>
whether they have been achieved.  Also<br>
address an abstract situation, such that<br>
even in the field it will be difficult to get<br>
data from respondents in case of research<br>
dealing with people.<br>

visit the library or internet<br>
sources to search for the current<br>
documentation. While reading,<br>
write a summary of the current<br>
knowledge then;<br>

like that.<br>

known and highlight on what<br>
will happen if the unknown<br>
will not be uncovered with<br>
reference to the discussion of<br>

<b>Formulate research objectives</b><br>

<b>Review of the literature</b><br>
<b>Once the problem is formulated, a</b><br>
brief critical review and summary of it<br>
should be written down. At this point, the<br>
researcher should undertake extensive<br>
literature survey connected with the<br>
problem. Literature review is concerned<br>
with reading of various previously<br>
related publications in order to enable<br>
a researcher to be aware of how other<br>
researchers have addressed the same<br>
or related research problem. Literature<br>
review helps to avoid unnecessary<br>
repetition of studies which have already<br>
been conducted. It helps the researcher<br>
to redefine his or her research problem,<br>
to select appropriate sample, appropriate<br>
tools and the research design. Generally,<br>
literature review is important in clarifying<br>
and focusing the research problem; and<br>
in sharpening the research methods that<br>
you will use in your study by looking at<br>
how others have used them. Literature<br>
review will also enable the researcher<br>
to broaden knowledge in the selected<br>
area to situate the study against other<br>
related research to avoid duplication.<br>
<b>Furthermore, it helps to identify the</b><br>
research gap and challenges likely to<br>
face the research process.<br>
<b>A good literature review can be</b><br>
distinguished by several criteria. First<br>
of all, a literature review should be<br>
capable of outlining the important study<br>
trends showing the current situation,<br>
information and documentation done<br>
in the area being studied. The second<br>
criterion is that, literature review needs<br>
to assess the strengths and weaknesses<br>
of the existing researches in different<br>
orientations including whether<br>
methodological approaches used in the<br>
existing studies  they were relevance or<br>
not. Furthermore, literature review should<br>
assess the strengths and weaknesses of<br>
arguments, conclusions and assumptions<br>
made by the existing studies. The third<br>
113<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
criterion is that, literature review should<br>
be capable of identifying knowledge<br>
gaps from the existing studies. In other<br>
words, literature review should not be<br>
written plainly that is, agreeing with<br>
most of the past literature in their entire<br>
dimension. In this case there would be no<br>
need of conducting another one because<br>
it will be a duplication of the research<br>
unnecessarily and wastage of resources<br>
and time. The fourth criterion is that, a<br>
good literature review should be based<br>
on most recent existing literature of<br>
which one can establish research gap and<br>
position their work among other related<br>
studies. To sum up literature review acts<br>
as a lock and key that well specifies the<br>
research gap. In this part you will be in a<br>
better position to know what is needed in<br>
the research you are conducting, where<br>
to conduct it, and how your findings fill<br>
in the gap you have established.<br>

(from literature) have<br>

problem.<br>
(iii)	 State the main and specific<br>

<b>Exercise 2.7</b><br>

features.’ Elaborate<br>
<b>Hypothesis formulation and research</b><br>
questions<br>
<b>After extensive literature survey, a</b><br>
researcher should state in clear terms<br>
the working hypothesis.  Hypothesis is<br>
a tentative assumption made in order to<br>
draw out and test its logical or empirical<br>
consequences.  It is a tentative statement<br>
about the relationship between two or<br>
more variables. It provides the focal<br>
point for research, guide the researcher<br>
by delimiting the areas of research<br>
and keep him/her on the right track.<br>
<b>Hypothesis also indicates type of data</b><br>
required, methods of data analysis and<br>
draws conclusion.<br>
<b>Basically, there are two types of</b><br>
hypotheses; null hypothesis and<br>
alternative hypothesis.  Null hypothesis<br>
is stated in a negative way or by using<br>
negative statement for example, there<br>
is no relationship between population<br>
growth and development; rate of survival<br>
will not increase after surgery; a high<br>
cholesterol intake is not associated with<br>
development (risk) of cancer; smoking<br>
is not a cause of cancer; existence of<br>
informal institutions is not associated<br>
with minimal conflicts in project<br>
areas. Alternative hypothesis is stated<br>
to indicate the actual expectation or<br>
114<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
relationship. It is usually a positive<br>
statement about certain variables, for<br>
example, there is a relationship between<br>
truancy in schools and poor performance<br>
in academics; Other examples, the rate<br>
of survival will increase after surgery;<br>
a high cholesterol intake is associated<br>
with the development of heart disease;<br>
cigarette smoking is a cause of cancer;<br>
existence of informal institutions<br>
in water projects is associated with<br>
minimal conflicts in the project areas.<br>
<b>Generally, there are many sources that</b><br>
can enable a researcher to formulate<br>
a hypothesis.  The main sources of<br>
hypothesis formulation include personal<br>
experiences, imagination and thinking,<br>
observation of phenomena, scientific<br>
theories, reviewing previous studies and<br>
cultural disposition.<br>
<b>A good stated hypothesis can be</b><br>
identified by observing the following<br>
criteria: it should be stated in the simplest<br>
terms which ensure easy understanding<br>
by others; it should not conflict with<br>
any law of nature which is known to be<br>
true; and it permits the application of<br>
desirable reasoning. Other qualities are<br>
hypothesis should be limited in scope<br>
and must be specific; it should be capable<br>
of being tested within specific time; and<br>
should allow the application of deductive<br>
reasoning.<br>
<b>Research questions</b><br>
<b>Research questions are the specific</b><br>
issues that the study wants to<br>
investigate from data collection and<br>
that data will answer them. In writing<br>
research questions, one can replace<br>
the first words used in the hypothesis<br>
“There is” with the words “Is there”<br>
and also replacing the period with<br>
question mark. For examples, Is there<br>
any relationship between population<br>
growth and development?<br>
<b>Types of research questions</b><br>
<b>There are three types of research</b><br>
questions which are;<br>
Firstly, a descriptive research question;<br>
this seeks to identify and describe<br>
some phenomenon. For example:<br>
<b>Will the rate of survival increase</b><br>
after surgery? Secondly, a differences<br>
research question; this asks if there<br>
are differences between groups on<br>
some phenomenon.  For example: do<br>
students who engage in remedial classes<br>
perform better than students who<br>
engage in sports activities? Thirdly,<br>
a relationship research question; this<br>
asks if two or more phenomena are<br>
related in some systematic manner. For<br>
instance, Is the existence of informal<br>
institutions associated with minimal<br>
conflicts in the areas?<br>

<b>Research design</b><br>
<b>Research design is concerned with a</b><br>
systematic and well-planned means for<br>
conducting a research. It is a systematic<br>
way of finding out new knowledge. A<br>
research design is a conceptual structure<br>
for conducting the research. Preparation<br>
of the research design will smoothen<br>
the processes of sampling, methods<br>
and tools for data collection, analysis,<br>
interpretation and reporting.  It will<br>
also reduce unnecessary expenditure<br>
by having predefined activities and<br>
resources. Usually, research designs<br>
vary with varying nature of studies.<br>
The commonly used research design are,<br>
‘snap-shot’ or baseline sometimes called<br>
case-study, cross-sectional, longitudinal<br>
and experimental research design.<br>
<b>Snap-shot or baseline is concerned with</b><br>
in-depth studies aimed at searching<br>
of the current and past behaviors and<br>
experiences for a single person, family,<br>
group, or organization. Usually, the<br>
findings from this kind of design cannot<br>
be generalized. Cross-sectional research<br>
design is a survey design in which data<br>
are collected at one point in time from a<br>
predetermined population. Data from this<br>
design is normally used to describe the<br>
characteristics of the studied sample with<br>
regard to the population when data were<br>
collected. Longitudinal research design<br>
is a form of survey in which data from<br>
the same area is collected at different<br>
time interval for the sake of investigating<br>
the changes of a studied population over<br>
time. It can be after several months or<br>
years. For example, studies of climate<br>
change in mountain climate before<br>
awareness on climate change and studies<br>
at different intervals after awareness<br>
have enabled us to note rapid decrease<br>
in icecaps over the mountain than it was<br>
in the past. Meanwhile, experimental<br>
research design can be used to establish<br>
cause - effect relationships between the<br>
independent and dependent variables<br>
by means of manipulating the variables<br>
studied through controlling them or<br>
116<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
randomisation. Alternatively, the studies<br>
conducted by using this design can<br>
compare groups that are closely related<br>
or introduce an intervening variable<br>
from which a researcher can examine<br>
changes among the groups. For example,<br>
studying two groups in which one of the<br>
two was intervened with an activity or<br>
project and the other not subjected to<br>
an activity.<br>
<b>Activity 2.8</b><br>

<b>Target population, sample and</b><br>
sampling techniques<br>
<b>In statistical geography, sampling</b><br>
is concerned with the selection of a<br>
subset of elements or individuals from<br>
a population to estimate characteristics<br>
of the whole population (also, known<br>
as target population or universe).<br>
<b>Each observation measures one or</b><br>
more properties (such as weight,<br>
location or colour) of observable<br>
bodies distinguished as independent<br>
objects or individuals. Below are<br>
important definitions of the commonly<br>
terminologies used in studies dealing<br>
with population.<br>
<b>Target population: This is entire</b><br>
population that the results of the survey<br>
should be representing. The target<br>
population can be the entire country,<br>
region, district, intervened villages,<br>
cropland or rivers or cattle. Normally,<br>
the sample is selected from the target<br>
population. For the case of smaller<br>
population, it is advised to study the<br>
entire population.<br>
<b>Elements: These include individual</b><br>
persons, objects, or units about which<br>
information is collected. Thus, totality<br>
of elements forms population.<br>
117<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Sample: The sample is also known</b><br>
as the subset of the target population<br>
because it is selected from the<br>
population. It is also referred to as<br>
the composition of the set of elements<br>
from the population. It must be selected<br>
according to principles of sampling and<br>
this will make it a more representative<br>
of the total population.<br>
<b>Sample Size: is a proportional set</b><br>
of elements selected from the target<br>
population. Often in probability studies<br>
it is recommended that sample to be<br>
optimal enough for data collection. Too<br>
small size is likely to increase errors<br>
in the data collected while too large<br>
sample size will have implications on<br>
time and cost. However it is generally<br>
recommended that sample size should<br>
be proportional to the size of population<br>
in case of finite universe. That is the<br>
larger the population, the larger the<br>
sample and the smaller the population<br>
the smaller the sample. In probability<br>
studies sample size can be estimated<br>
by using scientific methods as shown<br>
in the formula (a) and (b) below.<br>
(a)	For the finite population<br>

<b>Sampling techniques</b><br>
<b>Sampling is a process of selecting a</b><br>
representative of a population from<br>
which the data will be drawn on behalf<br>
of the entire population. Carefulness is<br>
crucial at this stage as the researcher<br>
narrows the data source to the sample.<br>
<b>Dealing with a sample is rewarding</b><br>
in many ways. The appropriately<br>
selected sample will save resources<br>
and time; ensure accuracy and produce<br>
manageable data. A well-designed<br>
sample can represent the intended<br>
population. The major categories of<br>
sampling techniques are probability<br>
and non-probability sampling.<br>
<b>Probability sampling</b><br>
<b>It is a method of selecting sample</b><br>
whereby every individual in the<br>
population has equal chance of being<br>
selected. Probability methods include;<br>
simple random sampling, systematic<br>
sampling, stratified sampling, cluster<br>
sampling, and multi-stage sampling.<br>
<b>Simple random sampling technique</b><br>
<b>It is the basic sampling technique</b><br>
whereby each member from the<br>
population has an equal chance of<br>
being chosen. Each individual is<br>
chosen entirely by chance and each<br>
member of the population has an equal<br>
chance of being included in the sample.<br>
<b>For example, a class of twelve students</b><br>
may write their names on a piece of<br>
paper, and then the papers are rolled<br>
and mixed. Then one of the students<br>
can pick randomly only four names to<br>
be included in the sample (Figure 2.1).<br>
119<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Sample</b><br>
<b>Population</b><br>
Figure 2.1: Simple random sampling technique.<br>
<b>One of the best things about simple</b><br>
random sampling is its ease of use in<br>
extracting the sample. It is also considered<br>
as a fair way of selecting a sample<br>
from a given population since every<br>
member within a target population is<br>
given equal opportunity of being chosen.<br>
<b>Another interesting feature of the simple</b><br>
random sampling is the representation<br>
of the population. It is unbiased and the<br>
representative sample enables drawing<br>
conclusions from the results of a study.<br>
<b>Therefore, simple random sampling is</b><br>
reasonable in generalising the results of<br>
the sample to population from which it<br>
is drawn. However, the technique has<br>
the following limitations. It needs a<br>
complete list of all the members of the<br>
population. It is only convenient when<br>
working with small population that has<br>
already been identified and listed. Simple<br>
random sampling can provide accurate<br>
results but it will not give you detailed<br>
information about specific groups of<br>
people. Furthermore, the technique<br>
is not practical to a large sampling<br>
frame, distortion of representation of<br>
the minority groups of interests, and<br>
it is time consuming with high labour<br>
requirements in case of large population.<br>
<b>Systematic sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Systematic sampling is a random</b><br>
sampling technique in which members<br>
from a larger population are selected<br>
based on regular interval and systematic<br>
order. Thus, a sampling interval is<br>
required.<br>
<b>Sampling interval K =</b><br>
Where;<br>
<b>N = The number of element in the</b><br>
population.<br>
n   = 	The number of elements for the<br>
sample.<br>
<b>In systematic random sampling, the</b><br>
researcher first randomly picks the first<br>
item or subject from the population.<br>
120<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
Then, the researcher will select each subject from the list. For example, in a class<br>
a researcher may decide to pick every third student in a row to get the total of four<br>
students as a sample, for example, if N = 12 and n = 4, therefore K =<br>
=3. In the<br>
first 3 elements number 2 is picked randomly. Then for the next three numbers,<br>
one number is picked at every third member (Figure 2.2).<br>
<b>Figure 2. 2:  Systematic sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Systematic sampling is simple since it</b><br>
allows the researcher to add a degree<br>
of system or process into the random<br>
selection of subjects. The assurance that<br>
the population will be evenly sampled to<br>
ensure even coverage of an area is another<br>
advantage. It is very simple to use since it<br>
saves time and cost. However, systematic<br>
sampling technique is accompanied with<br>
some shortfalls including the hidden<br>
periodicity within the population may<br>
distort the representation of a population.<br>
<b>Furthermore, systematic sampling is</b><br>
difficult to adjust sample to suit the<br>
circumstances and it is not practical for<br>
fragmented strata.<br>
<b>Stratified sampling technique</b><br>
<b>This is a probability sampling technique</b><br>
whereby the researcher divides the entire<br>
population into different sub-groups or<br>
strata, then randomly selects the final<br>
proportionally from different strata. The<br>
population is based on strata. Stratification<br>
is the process of dividing members of the<br>
population into homogeneous subgroups<br>
before sampling. Every element in the<br>
population must be assigned to only one<br>
stratum; then simple random sampling<br>
or systematic sampling is applied within<br>
each stratum. Examples of strata or sub-<br>
groups from a population include men<br>
and women, rich and poor, employed<br>
and unemployed.<br>
<b>The measurements within strata have</b><br>
lower standard stratification which gives<br>
a smaller error in estimation. Many<br>
applications, measurements become<br>
more manageable and/or cheaper when<br>
the population is grouped into strata.<br>
<b>It is often desirable to have estimates</b><br>
of population parameters for groups<br>
within the population. Several conditions<br>
must be met for it to be used properly.<br>
<b>Researchers must identify every member</b><br>
of a population being studied and<br>
classify them into one, and only one sub-<br>
population. However, the sorting process<br>
becomes more difficult and inaccurate<br>
for each member of the population<br>
stratum. It is also inconvenient as it may<br>
121<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
require more administrative clearance in various strata and the computationally<br>
complexity is another constraint. Figure 2.3 shows three strata, A, B, and C, one<br>
stratum with 6 members and the other two with 3 members each. Proportionally<br>
the 2 strata with 3 members each were presented by 1 member and the one with<br>
six was represented randomly by 2 members.<br>
<b>Figure 2. 3:  Stratified sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Cluster sampling technique</b><br>
<b>It is a method of sampling used when</b><br>
the total area of interest is large. The<br>
sample is obtained by dividing the area<br>
into a number of small non- over lapping<br>
areas and then samples are selected<br>
randomly from these smaller areas called<br>
clusters (Figure 2.4) This is applied<br>
when the entire population is unclear<br>
or unknown and the sample clusters<br>
are geographically convenient. When<br>
the clusters are natural in a population,<br>
cluster sampling is less expensive and<br>
quicker. Cluster sample permits each<br>
accumulation of large samples. The<br>
loss of precision per individual case<br>
is compensated by the possibility of<br>
studying larger samples without extra<br>
cost. A cluster sampling procedure<br>
enables obtaining information from one<br>
or more areas.<br>
<b>In a cluster sampling, each cluster may</b><br>
be composed of unit that are not similar.<br>
<b>This pattern has a likelihood of producing</b><br>
large sampling error and reduce the<br>
representatives of the sample. In cluster<br>
sampling, when unequal size of some<br>
of the subsets is selected, an element<br>
of sample bias will rise. This type of<br>
sampling does not allow generalisation<br>
of its findings to another area. In Figure<br>
122<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>2.4 there are six clusters; A, B, C, D, E, and F where two clusters C and F have</b><br>
been randomly picked.<br>
<b>Figure 2. 4: Cluster sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Multi-stage sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Multi-stage sampling is concerned with</b><br>
taking samples of preceding random<br>
samples. This sampling technique is more<br>
complex than cluster sampling which<br>
contains two or more stages in a sample<br>
selection. In simple terms in multi-stage<br>
sampling large clusters of population are<br>
divided into smaller clusters in several<br>
stages in order to make primary data<br>
collection more manageable (Figure<br>
2.5).  This technique probably solves<br>
more of the problems inherent in random<br>
sampling. It is more useful in incidents<br>
when there are completely no sampling<br>
frames. Moreover, by avoiding the use of<br>
all sample units in all selected clusters,<br>
multistage sampling avoids the large and<br>
perhaps unnecessary costs associated<br>
with traditional cluster sampling. By<br>
considering a study with already pre-<br>
determined households in Tanzania,<br>
through simple random sampling one<br>
can choose number of regions let us say<br>
five, and out of five may choose through<br>
randomisation four districts in each<br>
region. Furthermore, from the districts<br>
chosen may choose four wards and lastly<br>
two villages from all wards. From these<br>
villages, it is where the households will<br>
be picked randomly for administering<br>
a questionnaire (Figure 2.5). This<br>
technique is effective in primary data<br>
collection from geographically dispersed<br>
population when face-to-face contact<br>
is required. It is also time and cost<br>
effective and has high level of flexibility.<br>
<b>However, it has limitations such as, high</b><br>
level of subjectivity, lack representation<br>
of a population and complex planning<br>
and administrative issues are required<br>
to accomplish.<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Figure 2. 5: Multi-stage sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Note: The shaded boxes indicate the randomly selected cluster or a participant</b><br>
who has been selected.<br>
<b>Non probability sampling</b><br>
<b>This is a type of sampling in which</b><br>
members of the population have equal<br>
chance of being included in the sample.<br>
<b>Members to be included in a sample</b><br>
are chosen from the population in some<br>
non-random manner. Non probability<br>
sampling consists of quota sampling,<br>
convenience sampling, purposive<br>
sampling, snowball sampling and<br>
voluntary (self-selected) sampling.<br>
<b>Quota sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Under quota sampling the interviewers</b><br>
are simply given quotas to be filled<br>
from different strata with some<br>
restrictions on how they are to be filled.<br>
<b>In other words, the actual selection of</b><br>
the item for the sample is left to the<br>
interviewer’s discretion. For example,<br>
the researcher may be asked to draw<br>
a sample of 35 females and 45 males<br>
aged between 45 and 60 from a certain<br>
population. The technique lacks the<br>
representativeness and it is biased.<br>
<b>Convenience sampling technique</b><br>
<b>This is the type of non-probability</b><br>
sampling method which is applied<br>
when the members of the population<br>
are convenient to the sample.<br>
<b>Convenience sampling is also known</b><br>
as grab or opportunity sampling or<br>
accidental. In this technique, most<br>
of the elements in a population that<br>
happens to be present at the time<br>
of conducting research are selected<br>
for study. The researcher opts for it<br>
when he/she is interested in obtaining<br>
information cheaply. Like many other<br>
non-probability sampling it faces the<br>
limitation of lacking representativeness<br>
and it is subjected to biasness.<br>
124<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Purposive sampling technique</b><br>
<b>This is the type of non-probability</b><br>
sampling where a researcher selects<br>
only those cases, thought to be typical<br>
characteristics of the population.<br>
<b>Purposive sampling is also known as</b><br>
judgmental sampling. The researcher<br>
selects the samples based purely on the<br>
self knowledge and credibility. In other<br>
words, researchers choose only those<br>
people whom they deem fit to participate<br>
in the study. It is one of the most cost<br>
and time-effective sampling methods<br>
available. However, the technique has<br>
the following limitations: vulnerability<br>
to errors in judgment by researcher,<br>
low level of reliability and high level of<br>
biasness which subjects the research to<br>
inability to generalize research findings.<br>
<b>Snowball sampling technique</b><br>
<b>Snowball sampling is a special non-</b><br>
probability method used when the desired<br>
sample characteristic is rare. It may be<br>
extremely difficult or cost prohibitive to<br>
locate respondents in these situations.  It<br>
relies on referrals from initial subjects<br>
to identify additional subjects.  This<br>
technique is preferable in sensitive areas<br>
like drug abuse, domestic violence and<br>
investigative studies on sex workers.<br>
<b>Though the costs of conducting research</b><br>
are lowered; however, it introduces<br>
biasness because the technique itself<br>
reduces the likelihood that the sample<br>
will be representative of the entire<br>
population.<br>
<b>Activity 2.10</b><br>
<b>In a group of five students do the</b><br>
following:<br>
(a)	 Identify all the subject<br>
combinations available at your<br>
school and consider them as a<br>
target population and establish<br>
the total number of students.<br>
(based on the attendance sheets/<br>
registration lists).<br>
(b)	 Then pick small pieces of paper<br>
and list the names of classes/<br>
combinations (for example Form<br>
<b>I, Form II, Form III  Form IV or</b><br>
<b>EGM, HGL, HGK, PCB and</b><br>
<b>PCM) collect those paper put them</b><br>
in a container shake and pick one<br>
at a time. Repeat the exercise until<br>
you get the number of classes you<br>
wish to study (make sure classes<br>
to be studied are more than 2).<br>
(c)	 Find the total number of the<br>
chosen classes and apply the<br>
formula for finite population to<br>
determine the sample size to be<br>
studied.<br>
(d)	 Proportionately distribute the<br>
calculated sample based on the<br>
total number of the specific<br>
chosen group.<br>
(e)	 Summarise all the results obtained<br>
in each step and present your<br>
work to the class for an intensive<br>
discussion.<br>
(f)	 Later organise your work properly<br>
after effecting amendment from<br>
the discussion and submit it for<br>
evaluation.<br>

<b>Data collection</b><br>
<b>Proper planned methods and tools for</b><br>
data collection is one among the means<br>
for increasing accuracy, correctness,<br>
precision and validity of data to be<br>
collected. Data collection is a fieldwork<br>
where the researcher goes to the field<br>
and collects facts expected to answer<br>
the identified problem.<br>
<b>Data collection methods</b><br>
<b>Data may be collected from primary</b><br>
and secondary sources.  Primary data<br>
are the data collected directly from the<br>
field by the researcher using his or her<br>
own sense organs such as, mouth, eye,<br>
ear, skin and nose.  They are first-hand<br>
data collected through the use of various<br>
methods such as survey, observation,<br>
interview, focus group discussion and<br>
documentary review or measurement.<br>
<b>Secondary data refers to data collected</b><br>
by the researcher from existing sources<br>
such as books, magazines, pamphlets,<br>
journal articles and other unpublished<br>
documents. These are second hand<br>
information obtained from already made<br>
material or documents. Methods for data<br>
collection are defined depending on<br>
mode of conducting research and types of<br>
data collected. Data collection tools also<br>
known as instruments for data collection<br>
stands for various pre-designed means for<br>
capturing data in the field. The research<br>
tools are classified with respect to the<br>
data collection methods. The following<br>
are data collection methods:<br>
<b>Household survey</b><br>
<b>Household survey is a method of</b><br>
collecting data achieved by asking a<br>
sample of participants questions in order<br>
to get information about a population<br>
represented by the sample. Usually, it<br>
has slightly different names depending<br>
on the type and extent of data required.<br>
<b>For example, there are households’</b><br>
surveys, census surveys, land surveys<br>
and engineering surveys. In the context<br>
of this topic, the focus is on household<br>
survey. Despite survey being preferred in<br>
gathering of data related to perceptions,<br>
opinions and ideas still they have several<br>
limitations such as less accuracy in<br>
measuring behaviour, too much demand<br>
for sample representativeness and   low<br>
response rates.<br>
<b>The data collection instrument in the</b><br>
household surveys is called questionnaire.<br>
<b>The questionnaire can be divided into</b><br>
structured and unstructured. Structured<br>
questionnaire refers to systematically<br>
prepared questions in a written form<br>
with a range of pre-determined responses<br>
126<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
(options/answer) that the respondent<br>
can select. The prepared questions<br>
with answers are called closed-ended-<br>
questions. For the case of unstructured<br>
questionnaire the same questions are<br>
composed but mostly dominated with<br>
open-ended questions. For example, are<br>
you aware of cigarette smoking? How<br>
did you come to know the effects of<br>
smoking? Can you list those effects?<br>
<b>How can the non-smokers be affected</b><br>
with smoking? What is your opinion<br>
to people addicted with smoking? The<br>
following is an example of the structured<br>
questionnaire for household survey;<br>
<b>Survey questionnaire (Sample)</b><br>
This research aims at collecting data to complete a research project as a requirement to<br>
successful completion of advanced level secondary school education. Therefore, the<br>
data collected from this research will strictly be for educational purposes and not any<br>
other uses.<br>

<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Usually, structured questionnaires are</b><br>
tedious to prepare but easy to fill in. They<br>
are easy to analyse and more efficient<br>
when dealing with large sample. On the<br>
contrary, unstructured questionnaires<br>
are easy to construct since they are<br>
dominated with open ended questions<br>
but difficult for the respondents to fill in<br>
cases when they are required to do it on<br>
their own time. For example, those which<br>
are sent through mail. In addition, they<br>
pose difficulties in analysing although<br>
they provide rich data. Furthermore,<br>
their interpretation is subject to bias. The<br>
questionnaire can be administered to the<br>
respondents in different ways commonly<br>
through face-to-face interaction which<br>
is helpful in overcoming language<br>
barriers and influencing good response<br>
rates. However, it is time and resource<br>
consuming. Other methods are phone call<br>
and mailing or posting questionnaires<br>
in websites but the methods are more<br>
challenging in terms of response rates<br>
and managing language barrier which<br>
has additional limitation related to<br>
the unguaranteed turn-up of answered<br>
questionnaire.<br>
<b>Interview</b><br>
<b>Key informant interview is mainly</b><br>
concerned with collecting qualitative<br>
data from skilled people on the<br>
topic not based on their educational<br>
knowledge and level but their stake<br>
on the topic investigated. For example,<br>
if the research is about assessing the<br>
effectiveness of a given project, the<br>
research should involve people from the<br>
government or private institutions who<br>
in one way or another were involved in<br>
some activities during implementation<br>
of the project. When dealing with the<br>
key informant interview, the interview<br>
guides are the main instruments used<br>
in collecting data from experienced<br>
people in the field with regard to the<br>
study conducted. Through in this<br>
method, data will solely be qualitative,<br>
challenging to analyse and cannot<br>
be generalized. The following is an<br>
example of the key informant interview<br>
guide for assessing the effectiveness of<br>
the project.<br>
<b>Focus group discussion (FGD)</b><br>
<b>Focus group discussion is concerned</b><br>
with collecting qualitative data from a<br>
small sample, often homogeneous group<br>
of people within the studied population<br>
to explore their ideas on a particular<br>
topic based on their life experiences.<br>
<b>The group should not be too large to</b><br>
allow everyone to have a chance to<br>
participate and should not take long time.<br>
<b>The group should also not be very small</b><br>
to allow wide range of ideas from group<br>
members. The method is recommended<br>
when the researcher aims at not only<br>
collecting interesting information, but<br>
also identifying issues and themes<br>
that are related to the objectives of the<br>
research be conducted. Generally, the<br>
focus group discussion is impractical in<br>
situations where the language barriers<br>
cannot be controlled, the researcher<br>
has little control over the situation;<br>
trust among the participants cannot<br>
be established; and free expressions<br>
and confidentiality cannot be ensured.<br>
<b>In this method, checklist is used in</b><br>
data collection. Checklist is a set of<br>
128<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
logical pre-designed questions for data<br>
collection from focused group members<br>
in the field.<br>
<b>Some of the advantages of focus group</b><br>
discussion are as follows: can be<br>
conducted relatively quickly and easily;<br>
it allows flexibility in modifying the<br>
process and questions and it can explore<br>
different perspectives from the group<br>
participants. Some of its disadvantages<br>
include: analysis of the collected data is<br>
time consuming and participants are not<br>
true representatives of the population<br>
from which are drawn. For instance,<br>
if drawn from a village they will not<br>
represent all villagers. Thus, the data<br>
will be biased and some members can<br>
be dominated by others. The following<br>
is an example of focus group discussion<br>
checklist.<br>
<b>Focus group discussion checklist (sample)</b><br>
1.	 What are your opinions about the ongoing water project management practices<br>
in your village?<br>
2.	 Are you satisfied with the way village water project management is done?<br>
3.	 What is going well in village with the project management?<br>
4.	 What is not going well in the project management that you are dissatisfied with?<br>
5.	 What kind of things would you like to see happening?<br>
6.	 How about the issue of transparency among the water committee leader entrusted<br>
with overseeing the project and collecting revenue? How about accountability?<br>
What do you think about these?<br>
7.	 Some people have said that one way to improve X is to do Y. Do you agree<br>
with this? (Or, how do you feel about that?).<br>
8.	 Are there other recommendations that you would like to make?<br>
9.	 Are there any other things you would like to say before we wind up?<br>
•<br>
<b>Can you say more about that? [mention the aspect]</b><br>
•<br>
Can you give an example?<br>
•<br>
Jane says X. What is your opinion on that?<br>

<b>Observation method</b><br>
<b>Observation is a data collection method in</b><br>
which a researcher collects information<br>
in the field-based on visualisation.<br>
<b>Tools used for data collection are</b><br>
observation guide, recording sheet<br>
and field notes. The observation guide<br>
can be divided into semi-structured<br>
and structured observations guides.<br>
<b>Normally, observation method and</b><br>
the associated tools are opted when<br>
there is a need for direct information,<br>
to understand the ongoing behavior.<br>
There is physical evidence, products,<br>
activities or outputs that can be observed<br>
and need for alternative data in cases<br>
other means of data collection seem<br>
to be impractical. The following is an<br>
example of semi-structured observation<br>
guide for forest surveillance activity in<br>
a given forest studied;<br>
How does the activity take place in semi-structured observation guide (sample)? For<br>
example.<br>
•<br>
Who is taking part?<br>
•<br>
Number of participants ..........<br>
•<br>
Nature of activity and forest surveillance ..........<br>
•<br>
Time and location of the activity.<br>
•<br>
How is the activity organised?<br>
•<br>
What are the roles of participants and responsibilities?<br>
•<br>
Who makes decision and for who?<br>
•<br>
Are the resources made available to environmental surveillance team? For example,<br>
special equipment, mobile phones and means of transport for surveillance.<br>
130<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
<b>Although, observation method is</b><br>
beneficial in collecting direct and real<br>
time data still it has some limitations<br>
such as it is observer biased, potentially<br>
unreliable, interpretation and coding<br>
challenges; sampling can be a problem<br>
and it can be labour intensive.<br>
<b>Transect walk</b><br>
<b>Transect walk is a team-based field</b><br>
walk along a defined path (transect)<br>
across the community or project area<br>
together with the local people often<br>
for the sake of collecting geographical<br>
data on various aspects by observing,<br>
asking, listening, watching and at the<br>
end producing a transect map or diagram.<br>
<b>The data collection tools in this method</b><br>
include observation guide, recording<br>
sheet and field notes. Transect walks are<br>
usually preferred when the researcher<br>
is interested in collecting direct data<br>
by observing people, surroundings<br>
and resources in their natural settings.<br>
<b>However, this data collection method</b><br>
demands good observation skills.<br>

following:<br>
1.	 Select a  topic in geography and<br>

<b>Present the work to the class for</b><br>
discussion and make any useful<br>
amendments for improving it.<br>
<b>Data analysis and presentation</b><br>
<b>After the data has been collected, the</b><br>
researcher has to do analysis. The<br>
researcher should classify and organise<br>
the raw data into some purposeful<br>
and usable categories. Data analysis<br>
involves recording and storage of data<br>
in a computer. For quantitative data will<br>
be followed by computing mean, mode,<br>
median, range and standard deviation<br>
where required. The analysed data are<br>
presented in various formats such as<br>
tables, graphs, charts and maps, while<br>
qualitative data are grouped in themes<br>
or topics.<br>
However, in case of qualitative data,<br>
this can be analysed through thematic<br>
and content analysis. Thematic analysis<br>
emphasises on identifying, analyzing<br>
and interpreting the pattern of meaning<br>
of themes within qualitative data.<br>
<b>Content analysis examines patterns in</b><br>
communication in a systematic manner.<br>
<b>The purpose of data presentation is</b><br>
to display the results in a presentable<br>
manner to enable easy interpretation and<br>
report writing.<br>
<b>Hypothesis testing</b><br>
<b>In this stage the researcher finds out</b><br>
whether the facts from the field support<br>
the formulated hypothesis.  Meaning<br>
that, research results obtained from the<br>
field are used to make decisions whether<br>
they support the hypothesis or not. On<br>
131<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Field research techniques</b><br>
testing the hypothesis, two possible<br>
outcomes are expected, that is, the<br>
findings may support or not support the<br>
hypothesis.  In case the findings do not<br>
support the hypothesis, new hypotheses<br>
can be formulated,  restarted basing on<br>
the findings and re-tested.  In case the<br>
results are supported by the hypothesis<br>
you can go directly to generalisation.<br>
<b>Generalizations and Interpretation</b><br>
<b>If a hypothesis is tested and supported</b><br>
several times, it can increase confidence<br>
to the researcher in arriving to<br>
generalisation and building a theory.<br>
<b>As a matter of fact, the real value of</b><br>
research lies in its ability to arrive at<br>
certain generalizations. As researcher<br>
who has no hypothesis to test might seek<br>
to explain the findings on the basis of<br>
some relevant theory or theories that<br>
underpinned the study. Finally, the<br>
researcher has to prepare the report of<br>
the findings.<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Three</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>Surveying and mapping have remained  important disciplines throughout the</b><br>
history of human beings. In the past, humans used land and astronomic surveys<br>
to set important marks of positions on their land or water. However, in the modern<br>
era, surveys are used for determining site locations, demarcating boundaries of<br>
land parcels, setting out engineering structures, and map making for various<br>
land uses. In this chapter you will learn the concept of survey, process in survey,<br>
classification of surveys, chain survey, compass survey, plane table survey and<br>
levelling survey. The competencies acquired from this chapter will enable you to<br>
determine locations of points using different land surveying methods.<br>
<b>Concept of  land survey</b><br>
<b>Since ancient time, land surveying has</b><br>
been used to set important marks on<br>
the land. The established marks, also<br>
referred to as control points were used<br>
to establish position of features. For<br>
instance, in ancient Egypt surveyors<br>
called rope stretchers used the control<br>
points and simple geometry to re-<br>
establish marks of boundaries swept-off<br>
by annual floods of the Nile River. The<br>
name rope stretchers originated from a<br>
marked rope which was their principal<br>
tool of survey, and the today’s chain<br>
survey originated from this marked<br>
rope surveying. It was associated with<br>
making linear measurement between<br>
the established points or stations. In his<br>
work, “The sea island Mathematical<br>
<b>Manual” published in 263 AD, the</b><br>
ancient Chinese mathematician, Liuhui,<br>
described ways of measuring distant<br>
objects. The work of Liuhui founded<br>
the growth of survey which was later<br>
recognized by Romans as a profession.<br>
<b>Thus, land surveying or geomatics as a</b><br>
profession can be defined as the science,<br>
art, and technology of determining the<br>
relative positions of points or features<br>
above, on, or beneath the earth’s surface.<br>
<b>The determination of such relative</b><br>
position involves measurements of<br>
distances, height and angles; which<br>
results to graphical or numerical<br>
presentation of measured values.<br>
<b>Land surveying supplies data by which</b><br>
accurate space-based plans and maps<br>
of the earth’s surface or part are made.<br>
<b>Survey can aid proper land management</b><br>
and administration in ecumene. The<br>
planning of proper layout of streets and<br>
134<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
water supplying network, division of arable land for farming and other services<br>
are significant contributions of land survey.<br>
<b>Influence of technological development in surveying</b><br>
<b>Land survey is part and parcel of</b><br>
human life and his development.<br>
<b>Land surveying aims at establishing</b><br>
boundaries, creating navigation<br>
maps, and creating plan and maps for<br>
different land uses. To accomplish<br>
such tasks, humans have been<br>
innovating tools for taking linear,<br>
angular, and area measurements.<br>
<b>Figure 3.1: Ancient Egyptian  survey</b><br>
<b>While the purpose of  surveying has remained the same since ancient Egyptian</b><br>
survey (3000 BC) (Figure 3.1), surveying instrument have evolved drastically with<br>
technological development. The linear measurement instrument have evolved from<br>
chains and Gunter’s steel band through steel band and metal tapes to  Electronic<br>
<b>Distance Measurement (EDM) instruments (Figure 3.2). Subsequently, the Global</b><br>
<b>Positioning Systems (GPS) devices have improved efficiency and greater accuracy</b><br>
of measurement than any other preceding instruments.<br>
<b>Chain</b><br>
<b>Invar-tape</b><br>
<b>GPS device</b><br>
<b>Electronic distance</b><br>
measuring device (EDM)<br>
<b>Figure 3. 2: Evolution of distance measuring instruments</b><br>
Just as it was for linear measurements, instruments for angular measurements have<br>
also evolved significantly from the Egyptian Groma (which has been perpetuated by<br>
the cross-staff and its successor, the optical square) through dioptra to the compass<br>
like-instruments called astrolabe. The dioptra and astrolabe were followed by<br>
sextants, which are more professional and accurate. Evolution of modern angular<br>
135<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
measurements began with compasses that were followed by Transit, Theodolites and<br>
Total stations (Figure 3.3). A total station is a multipurpose surveying instrument<br>
that combine the functions of Transit level or Theodolite and electronic distance<br>
measurement (EDM) into a single instrument.<br>

<b>Modern angular measurement instruments use principles of electronics to</b><br>
calculate angles and distances. Morphologically, there is only a slight difference<br>
between Theodolite and Total station. The main difference is on their function<br>
and applications. Total station is used to measure both angles and distances while<br>
Theodolite is used to measure angles only.<br>
<b>However, in this chapter you will learn basic surveying techniques using basic</b><br>
instruments to understand principles of surveying, which also apply when using<br>
modern instruments.<br>
