<b>Procedures and process in land</b><br>
surveying<br>
<b>Most of land surveying involves three</b><br>
important processes: reconnaissance,<br>
fieldwork<br>
and<br>
office<br>
work.<br>
<b>Reconnaissance is the first process in</b><br>
land surveying in which the surveyor<br>
gets a general view of the area to be<br>
surveyed. In this step, a surveyor<br>
gathers information related to the area<br>
to be surveyed then for familiarization<br>
with its landscape and get an overview<br>
of what may be required before the<br>
commencement of a fieldwork. This<br>
stage helps the surveyor in planning<br>
for the execution of the survey project,<br>
particularly in identifying appropriate<br>
survey methods, instruments, required<br>
man power, preparation of budget and<br>
time schedule.<br>
<b>Fieldwork is the actual execution of</b><br>
survey work which involves observations<br>
and measurements of distances and<br>
angles, recording of measurements in a<br>
field notebook, preparing field sketches<br>
and performing simple calculations.<br>
<b>The office work is the last process which</b><br>
is carried out in the office and involves<br>
correction of survey data, reduction<br>
of levels, calculation of coordinates,<br>
preparation of plans, maps and other<br>
graphics, as well as calculation of areas<br>
and volumes of Earth quantities.<br>
<b>Classification of land survey</b><br>
<b>Although land surveying is usually</b><br>
classified on the basis of multitude<br>
of criteria, geodetic and plane survey<br>
make the general classification in the<br>
discipline. The two classes are types of<br>
surveys with their differences rooting<br>
on the key assumptions they hold.<br>
<b>Geodetic land survey assumes the Earth</b><br>
as curved surface (ellipsoid) and that<br>
any computation must consider the<br>
ellipsoidal nature of the surface, while<br>
the plane surveys assume the earth’s<br>
surface as flat. However, the terms plane<br>
surveys and plane table surveying should<br>
be treated different. The earlier is a<br>
type of surveys, the later represents a<br>
technique of plane surveying. Geodetic<br>
methods are employed in solving a<br>
relatively large land masses, usually<br>
over 250 square kilometers at national<br>
and continental scale, and widely spaced<br>
monuments or features. Plane surveys on<br>
the other hand, are used in mapping areas<br>
covering less than 250 square kilometers.<br>
<b>Contrary to geodetic survey which treats</b><br>
all lines joining stations as arcs, plane<br>
surveys consider all lines joining two or<br>
more points as straight lines.<br>
<b>A demand of high accurate data in</b><br>
geodetic surveys requires instrument<br>
of high precision, accuracy and economy<br>
than those employed in plane surveys.<br>
<b>Prior to 1970, accurate observation of</b><br>
angles and distances to collect spatial<br>
data in geodetic surveys was  difficult<br>
and pains taking.  Angles were measured<br>
using precise ground-based Theodolites<br>
while distances were measured using<br>
special tapes made from metal with<br>
low coefficient of thermal expansion.<br>
<b>Although these instruments are still used</b><br>
for angles and distances measurement<br>
to date, satellite positioning has almost<br>
replaced other instruments in geodetic<br>
137<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
survey. The global positioning system<br>
relies upon signals transmitted from<br>
satellites for its operation. The entire<br>
scope of satellite systems used in<br>
positioning is referred to as global<br>
navigation satellite systems (GNSS)<br>
(Figure 3.4).<br>
<b>Figure 3. 4: Global navigation satellite</b><br>
systems<br>
<b>Receivers that use multiple satelite</b><br>
system like GPS, GLONASS, Galileo,<br>
and Beidou are known as global<br>
navigation satellites system (GNSS)<br>
receivers (Figure 3.5). GNSS receivers<br>
use satellites as their reference points.<br>
<b>The GPS, GLONASS, Galileo and</b><br>
<b>BeiDou positioning systems are satellites</b><br>
constellations managed by different<br>
states or countries. The systems provide<br>
precise timing and positioning anywhere<br>
on the Earth with high reliability and low<br>
cost. The systems operate during day and<br>
night, rain or sunshine, and do not require<br>
cleared lines of sight between survey<br>
stations. Currently, GNSS receivers are<br>
used in all forms of surveying including<br>
hydrographic, construction and boundary<br>
surveying.<br>
<b>Figure 3. 5: Global navigation satellite</b><br>
systems receiver<br>
<b>Although (GPS), and ‘GNSS’ are treated</b><br>
synonymously and used interchangeably<br>
by many surveyors, the terms are<br>
distinctive and each has its own unique<br>
meaning.  A term GNSS is an inclusive<br>
term that describes satellite navigation<br>
systems from any country or region<br>
while ‘GPS’ refers specifically to the<br>
<b>NAVSTAR satellite navigation system</b><br>
of the United States. The most common<br>
<b>GNSS are GPS (United States), Global</b><br>
<b>Navigation Satellite System - GLONASS</b><br>
(Russia), Galileo (European Union),<br>
<b>BeiDou (China) and Quasi - Zenith</b><br>
Satellite System - QZSS (Japan).<br>

<b>Specialised types of tape survey</b><br>
<b>The existence of many types of</b><br>
surveys, named after principal devices,<br>
objective and surface surveyed among<br>
other criteria implies existence of<br>
specialised type of survey in each<br>
area. There are many specialised types<br>
of surveys including chain or tape<br>
survey, compass survey, plane table<br>
survey, leveling survey, hydrographic<br>
survey, topographic surveys, control<br>
surveys, cadastral surveys, mine<br>
survey, tacheometric surveying, aerial<br>
surveying, photogrammetric surveying<br>
and satellite surveying. However, in this<br>
chapter the emphasis is put on chain<br>
surveying, compass surveying, plane<br>
table surveying and levelling surveying.<br>
<b>Chain surveying</b><br>
<b>Chain surveying  also known as tape</b><br>
survey is one of the methods of land<br>
surveying in which sides of various<br>
triangles are measured directly in the<br>
field. It is the simplest but accurate<br>
method of land surveying. The method<br>
involves measuring a series of straight<br>
lines using chain or tape measure. The<br>
method is purposely carried out to map<br>
flat small areas  or near flat terrain and<br>
the associated features like foot paths<br>
and buildings; to determine the area of<br>
surveyed land; to prepare an accurate<br>
plan of a plot of land; to restore lost<br>
boundary marks; to divide a plot of land<br>
into a number of smaller units; to update<br>
an existing large scale map, and to set<br>
out engineering structures such as roads,<br>
railways and dams when implementing<br>
engineering projects.<br>
<b>Equipment used in chain survey</b><br>
<b>Equipment used in chain surveying</b><br>
include chain or tape, ranging rods or<br>
pole, plumb bob, wooden or iron pegs,<br>
arrow pins, Abney level, clinometer,<br>
cross-staff, optical squares, offset rods,<br>
drawing and recording materials like<br>
pen, pencil,notebook rubber and field<br>
sheet or notebook. Modern  devices<br>
such as Total station, GPS and surveyor<br>
band has replaced the chain as basic<br>
instrument in chain surveying.<br>
<b>Chain</b><br>
<b>A chain is primary equipment in chain</b><br>
surveying. It is a tempered steel wire<br>
with the length ranging from 20 m to 30<br>
m long.  The commonly used chains are<br>
composed of 100 or 150 links formed<br>
through pieces of galvanised mild steel<br>
wire of 4 mm diameter. The ends of<br>
every link are looped and connected<br>
together through means of three circular<br>
or oval shaped wire rings to give<br>
flexibility to chain. The length of each<br>
link is measured as the distance between<br>
the centres of two consecutive middle<br>
rings (Figure 3.6). The joints of links<br>
are welded to prevent length changes<br>
because of stretching.  Survey chains are<br>
generally grouped into three main types<br>
such as the Gunter’s chain which is 66<br>
ft long, divided into 100 links with each<br>
link measuring 0.66 ft; the Engineer’s<br>
chain which is 100 ft long and divided<br>
into 100 links and each link measuring<br>
1 ft;and the metric chain which is 20 m<br>
or 30 m long.<br>
139<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.6: Chain</b><br>
<b>Chains are marked with tallies to indicate</b><br>
distances from the handle. Commonly<br>
used chains have plastic or brass markers<br>
placed at some particular distances.<br>
Tallies in a chain are marked at 1 m,<br>
5 m, 10 m, and 15 m. The shape of a<br>
tally symbolises a distance it represents<br>
wherever placed in the chain. Nowadays,<br>
chains are replaced by cheap and readily<br>
available measuring tapes, Surveyor<br>
band, Total station and GPS.<br>
<b>Tape measure</b><br>
<b>A tape measure is also a primary</b><br>
equipment in chain/tape surveying. It<br>
is used to measure distance. It consists<br>
of a ribbon of cloth, plastic fibre or metal<br>
strip with linear measurement markings.<br>
<b>Surveyor’s tape are normally measures</b><br>
in lengths from 50m to over 100m<br>
(Figure 3.2).<br>
<b>Cross staff</b><br>
<b>This is a metal or wooded cross fitted</b><br>
on a short ranging pole with eye slits<br>
at right angle (Figure 3.7). It is the<br>
simplest instrument used for setting out<br>
perpendicular lines from a chain line.<br>
<b>Establishment of right angles in chain</b><br>
surveying is the easiest and quickest<br>
method though it is not very accurate.<br>
<b>If a survey project requires data of great</b><br>
accuracy, survey instruments such as<br>
Theodolite or Total stations can be used.<br>
<b>Cross staff</b><br>
<b>Eye slit</b><br>

<b>Ranging pole and ranging rods</b><br>
<b>The two equipment have always been</b><br>
used interchangeably due to their visual<br>
appearance and mode of formation.<br>
<b>Ranging rods and poles are wooden, or</b><br>

steel rod made round or rectangular in shape. However, there is a slight difference<br>
between ranging rod and a ranging pole. Ranging rods are used for marking<br>
temporarily positions of stations while straightening a line. They are made of well-<br>
sectioned straight grained timber of teak or deodar and are generally available in<br>
2 m or 3 m length and 3 cm in diameter. A rod is divided into equal parts, each<br>

They are generally painted alternatively red and white, black and white or yellow<br>
and white throughout their length to facilitate visibility. On the contrary, ranging<br>
poles are similar to a ranging rod except that they are of heavier section of length<br>
of 4 m to 6 m long. They are used for ranging very long lines in undulating ground.<br>

<b>Offset rods</b><br>

for pulling or pushing a chain through<br>
obstruction like bushes, rocks, and<br>
small water streams (Figure 3.11). Two<br>
narrow vertical slots passing through<br>
the centre of the section at right angles<br>
to one another are provided at the eye<br>
level. It is used for aligning the offset<br>
over traversing.<br>

<b>Terms used in chain/tape surveying</b><br>
<b>There are several important technical</b><br>
terms used in chain/tape surveying.<br>
These terms are; main survey station,<br>
subsidiary survey station, main survey<br>
lines, check lines and auxiliary or<br>
subsidiary or tie lines.<br>
<b>Main survey stations: are points where</b><br>
two sides of a main triangle meet. They<br>
are point at either end of a chain line.<br>
<b>Main survey stations are usually inter</b><br>
visible from either side of observation.<br>
<b>Subsidiary survey station (or tie station):</b><br>
refers to a station which is selected on the<br>
main survey lines for running auxiliary<br>
lines.<br>
<b>Main survey lines: are chain lines joining</b><br>
the two main survey stations. In chain<br>
surveying, the main survey line is a<br>
baseline that surveyors establish to take<br>
lateral measurements.<br>
<b>Auxiliary, subsidiary or tie lines: are</b><br>
chain lines joining two subsidiary survey<br>
stations. Usually, they are established to<br>
locate the interior details which are far<br>
away from the main lines.<br>
<b>Check lines: are lines established to</b><br>
check the accuracy of the fieldwork.<br>
<b>If the measured length of a check line</b><br>
correspond with the length scaled off<br>
the plan, the survey is accurate. Each<br>
triangle is generally provided with a<br>
check line. The check lines may be laid<br>
in such a way that maximum numbers<br>
of details are intersected by it. Check<br>
lines may also be laid by joining the<br>
apex of the main triangle to any point<br>
on the opposite side or by joining two<br>
points on any two sides of the triangle.<br>
144<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Preparation for field chain/tape survey</b><br>
Field chain or tape surveying involves several operations. Before field operations,<br>
chain surveying involves several activities including testing and adjusting the<br>
chain length; preparing the geographical area; working schedule; transport and<br>
precaution like first aid.<br>
<b>Testing and adjusting a chain</b><br>
Prior to executing field chain surveying the chain length is checked whether it is of<br>
the correct length as prescribed by the manufacturer. Chain length can be distorted<br>
by bents of chain links during its use, or opening up of rings which consequently<br>
decreases length of the chain. On the contrary, the length of a chain can increase<br>
due to: wearing and tearing of chain rings; over stretching of the links and the<br>
joints during chaining and opening out of small rings due to prolonged usage and<br>
rough handling. Pulling of a chain through hedges, fences, rocks and thickets is<br>
also a cause for the chain damage. Thus, it is necessary to check the length of the<br>
chain before and after surveying. If the chain is not tested, the measurements will<br>
be unreliable. Before checking a chain, the surveyor should ensure that its links,<br>
are not bent, openings are not too wide and there is no mud attached to them, and<br>
all connecting rings are circular.<br>
<b>Procedures in testing and adjusting chains</b><br>
(i)	 Fix two pegs at the required distance 20m or 30m apart; measured by a standard<br>
chain and insert nails into their tops to mark their exact points of 20th or 30th<br>
m from peg-tops A and B (Figure 3.16).<br>
<b>Figure 3. 16: Distance AB measured by a standard chain</b><br>
(ii)	Compare the overall length of the chain against the fixed pegs and note down<br>
the difference if any.  If after comparison, a chain is found to be longer or<br>
shorter than its standard, then length may be adjusted.  Several ways can<br>
help to adjust the discrepancy between the tested lengths and the original<br>
manufacture’s length. Closing the opened joints of the rings can adjust the<br>
chain. However, straightening the bent links; flattening the circular rings;<br>
replacing one or more small circular rings by bigger ones and inserting<br>
additional circular rings serves the chain from being obsolete.<br>
145<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
(iii)	If the chain is not adjusted,<br>
correction should be done whenever<br>
such chain is used to take distance<br>
measurements.<br>
<b>Activity 3.2:</b><br>
1.	 Fix two pegs with nails on their<br>
top at a chain distance apart using<br>
a standard chain.<br>
2.	 Measure the distance between the<br>
two pegs using a field tape and<br>
compare the measured value with<br>
that of a standard chain.<br>
<b>Field procedures in chain surveying</b><br>
A team of three people that is the leader,<br>
follower and the booker or surveyor<br>
usually carries out Chain surveying<br>
fieldwork. The procedures involved in<br>
chain surveying are explained using<br>
an example of village Y. Assuming a<br>
village Y shown in Figure 3.17 wants to<br>
implement a water supply project from<br>
a newly constructed water tank, land<br>
surveys will be required to locate the<br>
route of water supply network and show<br>
the longitudinal profile of the located<br>
route.<br>
<b>N</b><br>
<b>Figure 3. 17: Sketch map of village Y</b><br>
<b>The route of water supply network could be located by chain surveying; compass</b><br>
surveying, plane table surveying or other advanced surveying methods. The<br>
longitudinal profile could be prepared after carrying out levelling surveying. Two<br>
field procedures; establishment of baseline and point fixation are followed in chain<br>
surveying.<br>
<b>Establishment of a baseline</b><br>
In chain surveying, position of points are fixed relative to a baseline. Thus, before<br>
fixing points, a baseline should be established on a level ground for visibility of<br>
146<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
stations. It is further recommended that, a baseline should be run through the center<br>
of the area to be surveyed. Establishment of a baseline involves three activities:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identifying locations for main stations. Each main station should be</b><br>
located at a place where the preceding and the next main stations are<br>
visible and most of target points are visible from a baseline.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Marking the main stations; the main stations are permanently marked</b><br>
using pegs or iron pins. In Figure 3.18, base stations A, B, and C are<br>
permanently marked to define a baseline in a village Y where water<br>
supply project is to be implemented.<br>
<b>N</b><br>
<b>Figure 3. 18:  Baseline establishment for village Y</b><br>
(iii)	 Measuring the length of a<br>
baseline; distance between any<br>
two adjacent main stations is<br>
measured using a chain/tape. To<br>
measure a distance, intermediate<br>
stations are temporarily marked<br>
by arrows along a line connecting<br>
two consecutive base (main)<br>
stations that are more than chain/<br>
tape length apart. For example,<br>
if two adjacent base stations are<br>
50 m apart and a field chain/tape<br>
is of 30 m, intermediate stations<br>
need to be marked. When marking<br>
intermediate stations, the leader<br>
and the surveyor hold ranging<br>
poles at two base stations, while<br>
a follower moves with another<br>
ranging pole and arrows to mark<br>
the intermediate stations. Then, the<br>
surveyor directs a follower to move<br>
the ranging pole to the right or left<br>
until it is aligned with the other<br>
two ranging poles. The follower<br>
is then signaled to mark a point<br>
where the alignment of the ranging<br>
pole was achieved. In Figure 3.18,<br>
intermediate station 1 is temporary<br>
147<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
marked between base stations A,<br>
and B while intermediate stations<br>
2 and 3 are temporary marked<br>
between base stations B and C.<br>
<b>To measure distance between</b><br>
stations, a chain/tape is thrown<br>
to extend to the marked stations.<br>
<b>The follower erects ranging pole</b><br>
at the first base station and places<br>
the handle of chain/tape against it.<br>
<b>The leader pulls and straightens</b><br>
the chain/tape towards the first<br>
intermediate stations and takes the<br>
reading at a point where the chain/<br>
tape intersects the intermediate<br>
stations. The leader reads aloud<br>
the observed values and the booker<br>
reads back aloud while recording<br>
the values to avoid recording<br>
errors. The distance should be<br>
measured twice, thrice or more<br>
times to provide check for errors.<br>
<b>Then, the leader moves to the</b><br>
second intermediate station and the<br>
follower to the first intermediate<br>
station to measure a distance<br>
between the two intermediate<br>
stations. The same procedures<br>
should be followed in measuring<br>
distances of other intermediate<br>
lines. Thus, the distance between<br>
the two main stations is the sum<br>
of the lengths observed between<br>
the intermediate stations. For<br>
example, the length of baseline<br>
<b>ABC in Figure 3.18 is the sum of</b><br>
the length between points A and<br>
1, 1 and B, B and 2, 2 and 3 then<br>
3 and C.<br>

<b>Fixing points relative to baseline</b><br>
<b>In chain surveying, positions of lateral</b><br>
details like culverts, houses and<br>
boundaries right or left of the baseline<br>
are located with respect to the chain line.<br>
<b>The lateral measurement taken right or</b><br>
left of the main chain line are termed as<br>
offsets. There are two types of offsets in<br>
chain surveying; perpendicular offsets<br>
and oblique offsets. Their differences lie<br>
on the definition implied by their names;<br>
the ‘oblique’ and ‘perpendicular.’When<br>
a lateral measurement to fix location<br>
of a particular detail is made at right<br>
angle (90°) to a chain line, the offset<br>
is called perpendicular offset or right<br>
angled offset. In the Figure 3.19 (a), line<br>
<b>EN is a perpendicular offset on the left</b><br>
side of a chain line AB. On the contrary,<br>
when a lateral measurement to fix details<br>
made at any angle less than (90°) to the<br>
chain line, the offsets are referred to as<br>
oblique offsets. Offsets DF, and CF in<br>
the Figure 3.19 (b) are oblique offsets. In<br>
chain surveying, offsets with lengths less<br>
than 15 m are short offsets and those with<br>
lengths greater than 15 m are referred to<br>
as long offsets.<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>

(b)<br>

obstacles, select one major line try to<br>
maintain triangle between 300 cm and<br>

take accurate measurements to simplify<br>

<b>Folding and unfolding a chain/tape</b><br>
during field operation<br>
<b>In chain surveying, careful handling</b><br>
of equipment including folding and<br>
unfolding of chain/tape before and after<br>
a day’s field work, is a career-based<br>
civilization. The practices do not only<br>
reduce risks of equipment damage, but<br>
also increases the life span of equipment<br>
and ensures accuracy and reliability<br>
in taking measurements. Unfolding a<br>
chain/tape must be done with great care.<br>
<b>After removing the leather strap, both</b><br>
151<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
the handles should be held in the left<br>
hand and the chain should be thrown<br>
well forward with the right hand. The<br>
leader, then should take one handle of the<br>
chain and move forward until the chain is<br>
extended to its full length (Figure 3.24).<br>
<b>The chain is then examined to see if</b><br>
there are any bent links. After a chain<br>
surveying work, the chain is folded into<br>
preservable bunch and fastened with a<br>
leather strap. To do this, the handles of<br>
the chain should be brought together<br>
by pulling the chain at the middle.<br>
<b>Commencing from the middle, take two</b><br>
pairs of links at a time with the right<br>
hand and place them obliquely across the<br>
other in the left hand. When the chain is<br>
collected in a bundle which some what<br>
resembles a bundle of corn, it is tied with<br>
a leather strap.<br>

surveying<br>
<b>Plotting a chain survey</b><br>
<b>The presentation of the surveyed data</b><br>
according to their size and shape is<br>
termed as plotting. It is a representation<br>
of the booked survey details on a paper<br>
or suitable flat surface, to a suitable scale.<br>
<b>Plotting is done based on survey details</b><br>
recorded in field notebook and it starts<br>
after completing a field-work. Transfer of<br>
booked chain surveying data into a paper<br>
aims at developing a plan or map of the<br>
surveyed area. Plotting chain surveying<br>
data involves three steps: determination<br>
of a suitable scale, plotting survey lines<br>
(framework) and plotting offsets.<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Determination of a suitable</b><br>
scale: plotting of survey data<br>
commences with determination<br>
of a suitable scale. The choice of<br>
scale depends on variables such<br>
as the importance of the work,<br>
the extent of survey and the paper<br>
size.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Plotting survey lines (framework):</b><br>
having determined the scale,<br>
plotting of survey lines follows.<br>
During this phase a base line,<br>
is first drawn in the appropriate<br>
position on the sheet. The<br>
positions of the intermediate<br>
survey stations are carefully<br>
scaled and marked with fine pencil<br>
dots. Other chain lines forming<br>
triangles with base line are plotted<br>
by describing short intersecting<br>
arcs with the lengths of their sides<br>
as radii. The accuracy of plotting<br>
of these triangles can be checked<br>
by fitting in the check lines. The<br>
whole framework must be plotted<br>
and checked before plotting of the<br>
details of chain lines commences.<br>
(iii)	 Plotting offsets: this activity<br>
is done after plotting the main<br>
survey lines and may be done in<br>
two ways:<br>
<b>In the first method, chainage of</b><br>
perpendicular offsets are marked along<br>
the survey lines and their lengths are<br>
plotted at right angles. In plotting short<br>
offsets, perpendicular offsets may be<br>
estimated by eyes, but, for long offsets,<br>
pencil lines are drawn perpendicular to<br>
survey lines by set squares.<br>
152<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
In the second method, a short scale,<br>
called an offset scale is used. An ordinary<br>
scale is laid parallel to the chain line such<br>
that the zero value of the offset scale<br>
coincides with the chain line. The chain<br>
ages can be read on an ordinary scale.<br>
<b>The lengths of offsets are read on offset</b><br>
scale. The offset scale slides along the<br>
ordinary scale that is held by weights.<br>
<b>The various offset lengths are pricked off</b><br>
rapidly. If the offset scale is graduated<br>
such that its zero division is at the centre<br>
of its length, the ordinary scale is laid<br>
down parallel to the chain line and at a<br>
distance equal to half the length of the<br>
offset scale so that the zero value of the<br>
offset scale coincides with the chain line.<br>
<b>The offset scale may then slide to various</b><br>
chain ages. The offsets are marked on<br>
both sides of the chain line. Straight or<br>
curved lines join the plotted points as<br>
the case may be. It must be noted that<br>
changes in direction of the boundary<br>
occurs only at the end of offsets.<br>
In whatever method one may chose,<br>
plotting chain surveying data demands<br>
equipment like scale ruler, square ruler<br>
protractor, T-square, pair of compasses,<br>
drawing board and drawing pins.<br>
<b>Activity 3.5</b><br>
<b>Plot chain surveying data recorded on</b><br>
a notebook during activity 3.4.<br>
<b>Calculation and correction of errors</b><br>
caused by incorrect chain length<br>
<b>Chains/tapes used in surveying are not</b><br>
manufactured to last forever. They wear-<br>
out in everyday of their use. Improper<br>
chain handling such as dragging through<br>
bushes, rocky surface and rough grounds,<br>
causes perturbations of the oval rings and<br>
bent of links, which elongates or shortens<br>
the chain. Lengths obtained by faulty<br>
chain are either too short or too long<br>
than the length that could be obtained<br>
by using standard chain. This means<br>
that, if a chain has its length increased<br>
and exceeds the standard length, the<br>
measured distance will be less. On the<br>
contrary, if the chain is shorter than the<br>
standard lengths, the measured distance<br>
will be more. The correction for faulty<br>
chain can be done through the following<br>
formula.<br>
<b>True Length = L'</b><br>
<b>L ×  measured length</b><br>
of a chainline<br>
<b>Where:</b><br>
<b>L’ = Faulty length of a chain</b><br>
<b>L = True length of a chain</b><br>
<b>Example</b><br>
<b>In a process of chain surveying the school</b><br>
boundaries, a survey line was measured<br>
with a 30 m chain, and the total length<br>
found was 700.5 m. Unfortunately, when<br>
compared with the standard chain, the<br>
chain used in the measurement was found<br>
to be 0.5 m longer than the standard<br>
length. Determine the correct total length<br>
of the measured line.<br>
To correct the faulty total chain lengths,<br>
the following procedures are followed:<br>
<b>Identification of True length (L) and</b><br>
faulty Length (L’)<br>
<b>L’ = 30 m + 0.5 m = 30.5</b><br>
<b>L = 30 m</b><br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>The total measured length of chain</b><br>
line = 700.5<br>
<b>Therefore, the True length of a measured</b><br>
chain line =<br>
'<br>
<b>L</b><br>
<b>L ×Measured length of a</b><br>
chain line<br>
= 30.5<br>
700.5<br>
30 ×<br>
=712.175 m<br>
<b>Therefore, the true length of a measured</b><br>
chain line = 712.175 m.<br>
<b>Sometime, in chain surveying it often</b><br>
happens that at the beginning of<br>
measurement, a chain length is correct<br>
but is damaged as the chaining process<br>
continues. In a scenario similar to that,<br>
a surveyor at Tambalang’ombe school<br>

<b>Common errors encountered during</b><br>
chain survey process<br>
<b>There are number of causes of errors in</b><br>
the data obtained from chain surveying.<br>
<b>Human source of errors out pace other</b><br>
sources including instrumental and<br>
environmental causes. Errors resulting<br>
from; improper arrangement of ranging<br>
poles, incorrect reading, misplacing<br>
decimal points, surveyors’ negligence<br>
and inexperience leading improper set<br>
of instruments, use of incorrect unit of<br>
measurement, use of steel tape whose<br>
length varies with changes in temperature<br>
154<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
and use of out dated equipment lead<br>
to incorrect data acquisition  and loss<br>
of resources including funds and time.<br>
<b>However, the effects of environmental</b><br>
factors such as land obstacles, weather<br>
effects like wind and rainfalls and steep<br>
slopes on a land to be surveyed can be<br>
overcome.<br>
<b>Obstacles in chaining</b><br>
<b>It is common to be obstructed by barriers</b><br>
along survey line. These barriers could<br>
be of three categories: obstacles to<br>
chaining, obstacles to ranging, and<br>
obstacle to both ranging and chaining.<br>
<b>Obstacles to chaining obstructs chaining</b><br>
but not ranging. That is the follower sees<br>
the leader but the distance cannot be<br>
measured, for example a pond. Obstacle<br>
to ranging obstructs ranging but not<br>
chaining, for example a rising groove<br>
like  hill. Obstacles to both chaining and<br>
ranging are like intervening buildings<br>
such as a house this means that the ends<br>
of the chain are not inter visible and it is<br>
not possible to lay out a chain between<br>
the two points. Reciprocal ranging as<br>
described in the next paragraph, however,<br>
can control these barriers.<br>
<b>Avoiding an obstacle to ranging by</b><br>
reciprocal ranging<br>
<b>Reciprocal ranging is done when ends</b><br>
of a survey line are not inter visible due<br>
to an intervening hill or ends of survey<br>
line are far distance apart. Consider A<br>
and B as two survey stations along which<br>
chain survey is to be conducted. They are<br>
not mutually visible due to high ground<br>
between them, yet the measurement has<br>
been recorded. Towards making sure<br>
the actual distance between the stations<br>
which are not inter visible is obtained,<br>
a surveyor, first erects a ranging rod<br>
tall enough to be seen from another<br>
temporary point that is established on<br>
the hill or any other barring feature. In<br>
<b>Figure 3.25, the chain man established</b><br>
point A and D along the survey line and<br>
erected ranging rods on both ends. Then<br>
a chain man at point A goes up hill and<br>
establishes station B and C. The Point<br>
<b>B and C are established in such a way</b><br>
that B can see C and D clearly while C<br>
can clearly see B and A.<br>
Having points B and C established,<br>
the chain man at B instructs C to shift<br>
to C1 so that C1 align straightly with<br>
<b>D and that C can see both C1 and D</b><br>
clearly. Then, again C1 instructs B to<br>
shift to B1 so that A, B1 and C1 are<br>
visibly aligned in one straight line. The<br>
practice continues as B1 instructs C1<br>
to shift to C2 so that B1, C2 and D are<br>
visibly aligned.  C2 then tells B1 to shift<br>
to B2 so that C2, B2 and A are aligned.<br>
<b>The procedure continues as C2 shifted</b><br>
to C3 so that A, B2, C3 and D are in one<br>
line that can easily be measured.  The<br>
finalisation of this ranging will lead to<br>
the establishment of lateral stations A,<br>
B2, C3 and D, right or left of the barrier.<br>
<b>A chain surveyor then stretches or drag</b><br>
the chain from station A through B2, C3<br>
to D and takes the intended measurement<br>
as illustrated in Figure 3.25.<br>
155<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.25: Reciprocal chaining</b><br>
<b>Avoiding obstacles to chaining</b><br>
On either side of obstacles, measurement are required to avoid obstacles to chaining.<br>
With the kind of obstacles being at our face towards the chain line, the following<br>
two situations usually a rise: (i) It is not possible to chain round the obstacle or<br>
(ii) it is possible to chain round the obstacle. In the case when chaining round the<br>
obstacle is possible, the nature of the obstacle and the terrain around the obstacle<br>
can dictate the type of a method to be employed.  Some methods likely to fit include<br>
the rectangle method, triangle method specifically right-angled triangle method,<br>
and similar triangle method.<br>
<b>Chaining around an obstacle by rectangle method</b><br>
<b>In rectangle method, a chain man must understand how to establish perpendicular</b><br>
lines  with or without ready-made equipment. For example, to determine the distance<br>
C to D along the chain line AB  shown in Figure 3.26, establish two perpendiculars<br>
CE and DF of equal lengths on the same side on either side of the obstruction.<br>
Measure the distance between E and F, that equals to the desired length of CD.<br>
Thus, distance AB (AB) = AC + EF + DB .<br>
<b>Figure 3.26: Avoiding an obstacle by rectangle  method</b><br>
156<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Chaining avoiding obstacle by triangle method</b><br>
Right-angled triangle method typically applies a Pythagoras theorem. To determine<br>
the distance obstructed by an obstacle in figure 3.27, in the first step establish a<br>
perpendicular CE longer enough to avoid the obstruction. Make sure AĈE is an<br>
accurately measured right angle. Measure both CE and BE accurately (Figure 3.27).<br>
<b>B</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.27: Avoiding obstacles and triangle methods</b><br>
Similarly, the same obstacle can be avoided by taking right angle triangle of 90°<br>
at another position. You should note that, using a right-angle construction method<br>
in avoiding obstacles, a chain surveyor can establish a right angle at point C or E<br>
see figure 3.27). On either side of the obstacle, for example at point E (see figure<br>
3.28) as it may be found convenient.<br>

Figure 3.28: Avoiding an obstacle by triangle method with right angle on either side of the obstacle<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>

<b>After establishing point E on either side of the obstacle, measure distance</b><br>
<b>CE and EB. Using pythagoras  theorem, distance</b><br>

<b>Chaining across the obstacle by similar triangle method</b><br>
<b>Consider a river obstacle shown in Figure 3.29. The chain line AB is crossing a</b><br>
river. A surveyor can overcome river obstruction by, first establishing stations C<br>
and D as shown in Figure 3.29. Then, the surveyor fixes poles at C and D and<br>
sets out the  right angle at C. From the established point C, a surveyor establishes<br>
a convenient perpendicular line to station E and measures distance CO and EO.<br>
<b>Figure 3. 29: Avoiding obstacles by similar triangle method</b><br>
<b>A chain surveyor should then establish</b><br>
a line joining station E and F, which is<br>
perpendicular to line CE and parallel<br>
to line AB. Point F is at a position such<br>
that a line joining it with point D crosses<br>
point O. A distance between station E<br>
and F is then measured. Since  EF is<br>
parallel to line AB and lines CD and FD<br>
are shared by triangle COD and OEF in<br>
figure 3.29, the two triangles are similar.<br>
<b>Thus distance CD is calculated as CD =</b><br>
CO/EO x EF.<br>
<b>Avoiding obstacle to both chaining and</b><br>
ranging<br>
<b>When both chaining and ranging are</b><br>
obstructed, a rectangle method, also<br>
refered to as “parallel diversion”  method<br>
explained in Figure 3.26 may be adopted.<br>

<b>Compass surveying</b><br>
<b>The compass surveying involves fixing</b><br>
of an object’s position in the field<br>
by measuring the angles of bearings<br>
between the line of magnetic North<br>
(0°) and the line of sight to the object<br>
(Figure 3.30). This section exposes<br>
readers into concepts related to compass<br>
survey. Geography students especially of<br>
survey class must adopt some important<br>
personalities necessary for theoretical<br>
and practical studies through learning<br>
to check for the equipment’s condition,<br>
handling them with care and wearing<br>
proper field-dress. Concepts covered<br>
in this section are history of compass<br>
surveying, types of compass, compass<br>
bearing, compass traversing, local<br>
attraction, closing error adjustments,<br>
intersection method, and resection<br>
method.<br>
90<br>
<b>N</b><br>
θ<br>
θ<br>

<b>Reading end</b><br>
<b>Line of sight to an object</b><br>
0<br>
180<br>
270<br>
<b>Figure 3.30: Measuring angle by compass</b><br>
<b>Concept  of compass surveying</b><br>
<b>Compass surveying is commonly</b><br>
employed when the land area to be<br>
surveyed is comparatively larger than area<br>
covered in chain surveying. Compared to<br>
chain, compass surveying is suitable for<br>
rough landscapes. Compasses for land<br>
surveying originated from a Chinese<br>
‘iron-attracting mineral (lodestone)<br>
and have been in use since 400 years<br>
<b>BC (Figure 3.31). Ancient Chinese</b><br>
suspended the lodestone with their hands<br>
or on boats and other navigation facilities<br>
to help them point ‘south’. The use of<br>
lodestone as a compass to point to the<br>
south gave birth to its names such as<br>
“South-governor” and “South-pointing-<br>
fish”. Swinging of the lodestone, the<br>
south governor, or the south-pointing<br>
fish as they called it to point the south,<br>
then became the origin of the today’s<br>
compass used in survey.<br>
159<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.31: Lodestone</b><br>
<b>Types of compass survey</b><br>
<b>There are two major types of compass</b><br>
surveys which are the prismatic and<br>
surveyor’s compasses. The compasses<br>
are differentiated by key features<br>
including; their body size, the bearing<br>
system they use to designate readings,<br>
and their mode of formation.<br>
<b>Surveyor’s compass</b><br>
<b>A surveyor’s compass is an old form</b><br>
of compass used by surveyors which is<br>
similar to the prismatic compass but with<br>
few modifications. It is used to determine<br>
the magnetic bearing of a given line. The<br>
instrument has commonly been referred<br>
to as the Circumferentor. Surveyor’s<br>
compass is larger and more accurate than<br>
prismatic compasses and designated to<br>
read bearings in quadrant bearing system<br>
(QBS) as shown in Figure 3.32.<br>
<b>Figure 3.32: Surveyor’s compass</b><br>
<b>Prismatic compass</b><br>
<b>This is a non-magnetic metal case</b><br>
with a graduated ring and glass top<br>
used for determining angles, bearings<br>
and direction to objects of surveyor’s<br>
interest. It is a small, hand-held device<br>
in a circular box of about 100 mm in<br>
diameter used in fixing objects in the<br>
field and from which the angle of bearing<br>
is measured between the line of magnetic<br>
north and the line of sight to the object. It<br>
is usually used for surveying works that<br>
do not require very high accuracy. For<br>
example preliminary survey for minerals<br>
exploration.<br>
<b>The morphology of prismatic compass</b><br>
can be identified by its elements. Its<br>
cylindrical metal box, lifting pin and<br>
lifting lever, magnetic needle, graduated<br>
circle or ring, prism, object vane, eye<br>
vane, glass cover, sunglasses, reflecting<br>
mirror, and spring brake pin. Prismatic<br>
compass is a cylindrical metal box with<br>
a graduated ring supported by pivot at<br>
its center. Provided just below the sight<br>
vane is lifting pin and lifting lever which<br>
assist in pressing the lifting pin when<br>
a sight vane is folded. The lifting pin<br>
with the help of lifting lever lifts the<br>
magnetic needle out of pivot point to<br>
prevent damage to the pivot head. The<br>
magnetic needle of a prismatic compass<br>
is a core part of the instrument. It is<br>
attached to the graduated aluminum ring<br>
marked in degrees from 0˚ to 360˚. It<br>
measures bearings of lines from magnetic<br>
meridian. The needle that always points<br>
towards north-south pole at its two ends<br>
when freely suspended (Figure 3.33)<br>
measures angles to the objects.<br>
160<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.33: Prismatic compass</b><br>
<b>Another important element in the</b><br>
prismatic compass is prism. This is why<br>
the technique is termed as prismatic<br>
compass. A prism assists surveyors to<br>
read graduations on graduated compass<br>
ring and to take the exact degree values.<br>
<b>The prism is made up of a hole called</b><br>
prism hole. The prism hole is protected<br>
by prism cap to protect it from dust and<br>
moisture. The eye vane provided with<br>
an eye hole and the top glass to protect<br>
the ring, complements the operations of<br>
other compass parts such as sun glasses,<br>
reflecting mirror and spring brake.<br>
<b>Designation of compass bearing</b><br>
<b>There are two types of bearing</b><br>
designations. They include Quadrant<br>
<b>Bearing System (QBS) and Whole Circle</b><br>
<b>Bearing system (WCB).  In QBS, the</b><br>
bearing of survey lines is measured from<br>
either north line or south line whichever<br>
is the nearest to the given survey line. The<br>
bearing readings can be done clockwise<br>
or anti-clockwise. Bearings in QBS<br>
range from 0° to 90°. The NORTH and<br>
<b>SOUTH poles are designated by 0° while</b><br>
WEST and EAST are designated by 90°.<br>
<b>In QBS, the angular value is preceded by</b><br>
a prefix N or S and followed by a suffix E<br>
or W based on its quadrant. For example,<br>
an angle of 45° magnitude measured<br>
from North towards East, which is in<br>
first quadrant is written as N 45° E. In<br>
<b>WCB designation, bearings are measured</b><br>
clockwise from North direction and<br>
range from 0° to 360°. Bearing in QBS<br>
are WCB are also refered to as bearing<br>
and azimuth, respectively.<br>
<b>Compass survey by traverse method</b><br>
<b>The term traverse is used to refer to</b><br>
a series of connected lines of known<br>
lengths related to one another by<br>
known angles. There are two types of<br>
traverses namely; the open traverse<br>
and closed traverse. When a series of<br>
connected lines forms a closed circuit,<br>
it is called ‘closed traverse. In closed<br>
traverse, reading and taking of bearings<br>
or azimuth starts  and ends at the same<br>
station as shown in Figure 3.34(a). On<br>
the contrary, when the survey lines start<br>
from one point and end at another point<br>
which is not the starting point, this is<br>
known as open traverse as shown the<br>
Figure 3.34(b).<br>

<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>

<b>Reference meridians</b><br>
<b>A bearing is horizontal angle which is</b><br>
made with reference to a particular line<br>
called meridian to line of sight. Basing<br>
on  the meridian, a surveyor chooses<br>
the reference. There are four types of<br>
meridians; the true meridian; magnetic<br>
meridian, grid meridian and  arbitrary<br>
meridians. True bearing of a line is<br>
the horizontal angle between the true<br>
meridian and the line of sight. It is also<br>
defined as the line along  the earth’s<br>
surface connecting the  North and  south<br>
poles. The true North is also called<br>
geodetic north, the true meridian or North<br>
as frequently referred to by geographers,<br>
is different from the magnetic north<br>
which is the direction pointed by the<br>
compass, and it is different from the grid<br>
north which is in the direction along the<br>
grid lines towards the north.  The true<br>
bearing is measured from the true north<br>
in the clockwise direction (Figure 3.35).<br>
<b>True Bearing</b><br>
<b>Magnetic</b><br>
<b>Bearing</b><br>
<b>True Meridian</b><br>
<b>Magnetic Meridian</b><br>
<b>A</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.35: Magnetic variations</b><br>
<b>The magnetic bearing on the other hand</b><br>
refers to the horizontal angle which the<br>
line of sight meets with the magnetic<br>
north. It is the direction which is pointed<br>
by the compass needle in response to<br>
the earth’s magnetic field. The deviation<br>
between the true north and the magnetic<br>
north varies from place to place as the<br>
earth’s magnetic poles are not fixed<br>
with respect to its axis. The earth’s<br>
magnetic poles are not aligned to the<br>
actual geographic north and south poles.<br>
<b>Instead, the magnetic South Pole is in</b><br>
<b>Canada which is in the north while the</b><br>
magnetic north pole lies in Antarctica<br>
which is in the south. The magnetic poles<br>
are inclined by about 10 degrees to the<br>
earth’s rotational axis (Figure 3.36).<br>
162<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>

pole<br>
<b>S</b><br>
<b>N</b><br>
<b>Magnetic field</b><br>
<b>South magnetic</b><br>
pole<br>
<b>Figure 3.36: Distribution of earth’s</b><br>
magnetism<br>
<b>Grid bearing means horizontal angle and</b><br>
the line of sight AB to an object meets<br>
with the grid meridian.  An arbitrary<br>
bearing is the horizontal angle between<br>
the line of sight AB and any arbitrary<br>
meridian established. An arbitrary<br>
meridian serves as temporary north<br>
whose magnetic or true bearing could<br>
later be determined. The meridians,<br>
also termed as Norths that is, Magnetic<br>
<b>North, True North, Arbitrary North and</b><br>
<b>Grid North, are major reference points</b><br>
in compass surveying.<br>
<b>Back and forward bearing</b><br>
<b>In prismatic compass survey, a survey</b><br>
line can be defined by two bearings, the<br>
forward bearing which is taken from one<br>
station to the other in the direction of a<br>
survey and back bearing which is taken<br>
in the opposite direction of the survey<br>
direction. Both bearings are expressed<br>
in whole circle bearing (WCB). The<br>
forward and back bearing in WCB differs<br>
by 180°. That is, the bearing (α) of the<br>
line AB in the survey direction from A<br>
to B is a forward bearing  whereas the<br>
bearing (B) of the line AB in opposite<br>
to the survey direction from B to A, is a<br>
back bearing (β) (Figure 3.37).<br>
<b>MN</b><br>
<b>MN</b><br>
<b>A</b><br>
<b>B</b><br>
β<br>
α<br>
<b>Figure 3.37:  Forward (α) and back (β)bearing</b><br>
<b>This means, if the forward bearing is</b><br>
greater than 180o, then subtract 180o to<br>
get  back bearing and when the forward<br>
bearing (FB) is less than 1800, add 1800<br>
to get back bearing (BB). However, it<br>
must be noted that, this can only be<br>
meaningful when checking the accuracy<br>
of the data collected from the field. In the<br>
field, the FB and BB must be measured<br>
for each survey line.<br>

and BB = FB±180<br>
<b>Field procedures for compass survey by</b><br>
traverse method<br>
1.	 Prepare all necessary equipment<br>
for the prismatic compass survey.<br>
<b>Important equipment includes:</b><br>
Prismatic compass, chain or tape,<br>
ranging rod or poles, tripod stand<br>
163<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
if available, field note book and<br>
other drawing materials like rubber<br>
and pencils. Do not dress or carry<br>
metal material during the compass<br>
surveying. Iron materials like a<br>
bunch of keys, ear and finger rings,<br>
watch, metal frame or rimmed<br>
eyes glasses can interfere with the<br>
compass readings and there after<br>
provide incorrect readings.<br>
2.	 Establish stations through which<br>
your traverse will be carried out.<br>
<b>For example, in Figure 3.38 stations</b><br>
<b>A, B, C, D, and F are established at</b><br>
farm corner points.<br>
<b>Figure 3.38: Corner points of a farm to be</b><br>
surveyed<br>
<b>Prismatic compass surveying has</b><br>
important preliminary procedures to be<br>
considered before the actual field practice<br>
is carried out.  Any attempt to ignore<br>
them can lead to erroneous reading of<br>
the bearings. Therefore, adjustment of<br>
a prismatic compass before any activity<br>
is conducted is necessary. Two types of<br>
adjustment can be done; Temporary and<br>
permanent adjustments.<br>
a)	 Temporary adjustment<br>
<b>A prismatic compass needs to be adjusted</b><br>
at every station. The adjustments of an<br>
instrument required to be made at every<br>
set up of the instrument are known as<br>
temporary or Station Adjustment. On<br>
the contrary, adjustments which are<br>
made only if the fundamental relations<br>
between the various parts of a compass,<br>
are disturbed due to careless handling<br>
or otherwise, are called permanent<br>
adjustments. Temporary adjustments<br>
include the following operations:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Centering the compass; this</b><br>
is a setting of a compass at its<br>
center over the ground station<br>
mark. It involves making the<br>
pivot exactly vertical over the<br>
ground station mark. A station<br>
mark can be a boundary beacon<br>
or a brick established for that<br>
purpose. Centering is done by<br>
adjusting the legs of the tripod.<br>
<b>To ensure a compass is on top of</b><br>
the starting point, a plumb bob<br>
is hunged from the centre of the<br>
circular box, to define a vertical<br>
line. If no plumb bob is provided,<br>
the centering may be judged by<br>
dropping a small pebble freely<br>
from the centre of the bottom of<br>
the circular box. If the compass<br>
is centred perfectly, the pebble<br>
will fall exactly over the ground<br>
station mark.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Levelling; is the other adjustment</b><br>
which involves the setting of the<br>
compass horizontally such that<br>
its graduated ring swings freely.<br>
<b>Levelling of a compass can be</b><br>
done by the level specially made<br>
for that purpose or estimated by<br>
eye. Generally, the compass<br>
is provided with a ball and<br>
socket arrangement attached to<br>
164<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
the tripod for achieving quick<br>
levelling of the instrument. In<br>
surveyor’s compass two plate<br>
levels at right angles to each<br>
other, are sometimes provided.<br>
<b>The ball and socket arrangement</b><br>
is adjusted till the two bubbles<br>
remain central in both plate<br>
levels.<br>
(iii)	 Focusing the prism: The process<br>
of moving up or down the prism<br>
for obtaining the figures and<br>
graduations sharply and clearly,<br>
is called focusing the prism.<br>
<b>This adjustment is for prismatic</b><br>
compass only.<br>
(b) Permanent adjustments: on the<br>
other hand, are the permanent setups<br>
of instrument damaged or its parts<br>
disturbed by surveyors’ careless handling<br>
or unfortunate dropping. It involves<br>
adjusting the plate level, sight vane,<br>
magnetic needle and pivot.<br>
<b>Other survey methods for fixing objects</b><br>
and lines during prismatic survey<br>
<b>Fixing object or line of survey in</b><br>
prismatic compass survey can be done by<br>
methods other than traversing. A detail<br>
in prismatic survey can be positioned<br>
by intersection and resection methods.<br>
<b>Just bear in mind that the intention of</b><br>
the whole process in surveying is to tell<br>
the where-about of some geographical<br>
features. One way of identifying location<br>
of the intended objects in relation to other<br>
nearby permanent objects is through<br>
prismatic compass survey.<br>
3.	 Set a compass over station A perform<br>
temporary adjustment and take a<br>
forward bearing to B. Then, shift<br>
the instrument to station B and<br>
read the back bearing to A to check<br>
for angular error. Then measure a<br>
distance between the station A and<br>
<b>B and record all measurement in the</b><br>
field note book.<br>
4.	 Continue taking forward and back<br>
bearings and measuring distances for<br>
all remaining stations (Figure 3.39).<br>
<b>Figure 3.39:  Clockwise reading of forward</b><br>
(blue arrows)and back bearing (black arrows)<br>
5.	 Correct the observed field data for<br>
any error.  Correction for the error<br>
can be done in two ways: graphically<br>
and mathematically. However, in<br>
this chapter the focus will be on the<br>
graphical methods. In the graphical<br>
method, the data should be plotted<br>
and if there are some errors, the<br>
traverse will not close.  That is,<br>
the last survey line will not join<br>
the station where a survey started<br>
(Figure 3.40).<br>
165<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Closing error gap</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.40: Traverse closing error gap</b><br>
between A’ and A<br>
<b>Graphical correction of traverse</b><br>
closing error<br>
<b>Commonly, the north end of a freely</b><br>
suspended magnetic needle of compass<br>
always points to the magnetic north, if it<br>
is not influenced by any other external<br>
forces except the earth’s magnetic field.<br>
<b>Sometimes,  the magnetic needle gets</b><br>
deflected from its normal position, if a<br>
compass survey is carried near magnetic<br>
rocks, iron ores, cables carrying current<br>
or iron electric poles. Those forces<br>
disturbing compass needle are called<br>
‘Local attraction’.  Prismatic compass is<br>
therefore not reliable in areas with these<br>
characteristics, unless these are checked<br>
against the presence of local attraction at<br>
each station and their elimination.<br>
<b>The presence of local attraction at any</b><br>
station may be detected by calculating<br>
forward and back bearings of the line<br>
to see if the difference between them<br>
is 180°.  If checked and found that the<br>
difference between forward and back<br>
bearings is 180°, then both end stations<br>
are free from local attraction. If not, the<br>
discrepancy maybe due to;<br>
(i)<br>
<b>An error in observation of either</b><br>
forward or back bearings or both<br>
for example, not holding the<br>
comps steadily at 1800 when taking<br>
bearings.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Presence of local attraction at</b><br>
either station or around.<br>
(iii)	 Presence of local attraction at both<br>
stations.<br>
(iv)	 Slugging pivot or needle.<br>
<b>Local attraction at any station affects</b><br>
all the magnetic bearings by an equal<br>
amount and therefore, the included<br>
angles deduced from the affected<br>
bearings are always correct. In case,<br>
the forward and back bearings of lines<br>
of  traverse differ by the permissible<br>
error of reading, the mean value of the<br>
bearings of the line least affected, may be<br>
accepted. The correction to other stations<br>
may be made according to the following<br>
methods: graphical method based on<br>
local attraction are graphical method<br>
based on Bowditch rule.<br>
<b>Graphical method based on local</b><br>
attraction<br>
<b>Graphical method based on local</b><br>
attraction involves calculation of error<br>
due to local attraction at each station while<br>
graphical method based on Bowditch<br>
rule does not involve calculations. The<br>
procedures for correcting errors due to<br>
local attraction are illustrated based on<br>
traverse data presented in Table 3.1<br>
166<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Plot a traverse framework from</b><br>
uncorrected data presented in<br>
Table 3.1 (Figure 3.41).<br>
<b>Figure 3.41: Uncorrected traverse ABCDE</b><br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Correct the forward and back</b><br>
bearings observed in traverse<br>
ABCDE shown in Table 3.1.<br>
<b>In correcting data provided in Table</b><br>
3.1, use the formula FB = BB ± 180º<br>
to check for bearing error in each line.<br>
<b>For example, in line AB, BB  ̶ 180° =</b><br>
226°  ̶ 180° = 46° which is greater than<br>
the measured FB by 1°. Thus, 1° is the<br>
bearing error due to local attraction in<br>
line AB. Continue determining errors<br>
in the remaining lines. Then, choose<br>
a leg not affected or least affected and<br>
investigate from there a leg or legs which<br>
are affected by local attraction causing<br>
the discrepancy. Then correct each<br>
affected leg by adding or subtracting the<br>
error. If the observed bearing of a line is<br>
more greater than the calculated bearing,<br>
the error is subtracted and vice versa.<br>

<b>Compass survey by intersection method</b><br>
In intersection method, a point where two lines meet is determined. Thus, intersection<br>
is a method of fixing the position of object relative to  two or more points of known<br>
positions. It is a method that locate points of intersection by taking forward bearing<br>
from two or more fixed points. The procedure followed in intersection methods are:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>From two known points, say C and D, for instance, take forward bearing to</b><br>
point X that you want to locate on a map (Figure 3.46).<br>
<b>Figure 3.46: Fixing objects by intersection</b><br>
170<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Check the accuracy of the forward bearing from points C and D to point X</b><br>
by taking forward and  back bearing of line CD;<br>
(iii)	 Lastly convert all magnetic bearings to true bearing; and<br>
(iv)	 On a map, draw a  line parallel to true north at point C and D. Then, using<br>
a protractor mark the direction (basing) of lines CX and DX, and  extend<br>
these lines until they cross to work position of point X.<br>
<b>Compass survey by resection method</b><br>
Resection differs from intersection since, instead of setting a compass at known<br>
points, you set the compass at the unknown  points, for example station Y. Thus,<br>
forward bearing are read from station Y to points or features of known map<br>
locations (Figure 3.47).<br>
<b>Figure 3.47: Fixing objects by resection method</b><br>
<b>Procedures for resection are:</b><br>
(i)<br>
Set a compass at unknown  station Y and perform all temporary adjustment;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Take forward bearings from point Y to two or more known points.  Do not</b><br>
record a car or animal as an object to fix your point (tower and house).<br>
Animals and cars are mobile and therefore cannot be shown on the map;<br>
(iii)	 Then convert all magnetic bearing to the fixed point you have chosen into<br>
true bearing;<br>
(iv)	 To fix point Y on a map, calculate the back bearings from the forward bearing<br>
obtained in step (ii) above. Then, on a map where the location of a house and<br>
171<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
mobile tower in Figure 3.45, draw a fine pencil line parallel to true north at<br>
the chosen objects, in this case, a house and a mobile tower; and<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Measure the respective true back bearing calculated in step (iii) at the two</b><br>
chosen objects and extend these lines until they cross each other to mark<br>
the position of point Y.<br>

<b>Earth’s magnetic field</b><br>
<b>Earth’s magnetism is a result of the</b><br>
convection currents of molten iron and<br>
nickel in the earth’s core. These currents<br>
carry streams of charged particles<br>
and generate magnetic fields. These<br>
magnetic fields deflect ionising charged<br>
particles  from the sun (called solar<br>
wind) and prevent them from entering<br>
our atmosphere.Without preventing this<br>
magnetic shield, the solar wind could have<br>
slowly destroyed our life on earth. Mars<br>
does not have a strong atmosphere that<br>
can sustain life because it does not have<br>
a magnetic field protecting it. The earth’s<br>
magnetic field has three components<br>
that govern its magnitude and direction;<br>
namely: magnetic declination, magnetic<br>
inclination or the angle of dip and the<br>
horizontal component.<br>
<b>Magnetic declination</b><br>
<b>Geography students must understand</b><br>
that the horizontal angle between true<br>
north and magnetic north at a place and<br>
at a time of observation is what we term<br>
as magnetic declination. The angle of<br>
convergence between the true north<br>
and magnetic north at any place does<br>
not remain constant. It depends on the<br>
direction of the magnetic meridian at<br>
the time of observation. If the magnetic<br>
meridian is on the eastern side of true<br>
meridian, the angle of declination is<br>
called the eastern declination or positive<br>
declination. On the other hand, if the<br>
magnetic meridian is on western side, the<br>
declination angle is called the western<br>
declination or negative declination.<br>
<b>When both true North and magnetic</b><br>
meridians coincide, magnetic declination<br>
is zero.<br>
<b>The imaginary lines joining the places</b><br>
of equal declination either positive or<br>
negative, on the surface of the earth,<br>
are called ‘‘Isogonic lines’’. As the<br>
earth magnetism is not regular and the<br>
intensity of its magnetic field also varies,<br>
the isogonic lines do not form complete<br>
circles but these follow irregular paths.<br>
The isogonic lines with zero declination,<br>
are known as “Agonic lines”.<br>
174<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Determination of magnetic</b><br>
declination/ variation<br>
<b>True meridians and compass observations</b><br>
are important inputs in determining<br>
magnetic declination of any place. True<br>
meridians in many places are determined<br>
by making astronomical observations,<br>
especially to stars. Compass observations<br>
are made by sighting of the true<br>
meridians at the places. The angle of<br>
inclination between the true meridian<br>
and the magnetic meridian given by a<br>
compass reading is the desired magnetic<br>
declination that can be determined as<br>
the difference between the true bearing<br>
and the magnetic bearing. Therefore;<br>
magnetic declination = True bearing -<br>
<b>Magnetic bearing. For instance, if the true</b><br>
and magnetic bearings of a line are 78°<br>
45' and 75° 30' respectively. Use negative<br>
sign for eastern declination and positive<br>
sign for western declination. As shown<br>
in Figure 3.50, therefore the Magnetic<br>
<b>Declination (MD) = 78°45'  ̶  75°30'  =</b><br>
3°15'. Having determined the magnetic<br>
bearing of a line and the magnetic<br>
declination at that place,  true bearing<br>
of the line, may be calculated from the<br>
formula: true bearing = magnetic bearing<br>
± magnetic declination. Similarly, to<br>
calculate magnetic bearing, we must be<br>
sure that we have calculated true bearing<br>
of a line and magnetic declination of<br>
that place. This formula can therefore<br>
be used:<br>
<b>Magnetic bearing = True bearing ±</b><br>
<b>Magnetic declination</b><br>
(a)<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Figure  3.50: (a) East declination and (b) west declination</b><br>
175<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Variation of declination</b><br>
<b>The magnetic declination at any place</b><br>
does not remain constant but keeps on<br>
changing from time to time therefore it<br>
may increase or decrease. We classify<br>
the changes into four subheadings as<br>
secular variation, annual variation,<br>
diurnal variation, and irregular variation.<br>
a)<br>
Secular variation.<br>
<b>The continual changing of the magnetic</b><br>
meridian relative to geographical poles<br>
affects the declination of a place. Secular<br>
variation is a slow continuous change<br>
in declination of places. It alters the<br>
declination more frequently and in a<br>
less regular manner from year to year.<br>
<b>Due to its greatness, secular variation is</b><br>
considered the most important for land<br>
surveyors. It appears to be of periodic<br>
character and follows a sine curve.<br>
<b>The swing of declination at a place over</b><br>
a period of centuries, may be compared<br>
to a simple harmonic motion. A secular<br>
change from year to year is also not<br>
uniform for any given place. It is also<br>
different for different places. To convert<br>
magnetic bearings into true bearings,<br>
an accurate amount of declination is<br>
essentially required. As such it is very<br>
important for a surveyor to know the<br>
exact amount of declination. When<br>
observations for the declination are<br>
made in different years of a century,<br>
it is revealed that magnetic meridian<br>
moves from one side of true meridian<br>
to the other. The change produced<br>
annually by secular variation at different<br>
places amounts from 0.02 minute to 12<br>
minutes. The variation depends on the<br>
geographical position of different places.<br>
<b>The annual secular change is the greatest</b><br>
near the middle point of meridians and<br>
the last at its extreme limits.<br>
b)	 Annual variation<br>
<b>This is the change in declination at a</b><br>
place over a period of one year. It is<br>
observed at different places over a period<br>
of 12 months. Annual variation is about<br>
1 minute to 2 minutes, depending upon<br>
their geographical positions.<br>
c)<br>
<b>Diurnal variation</b><br>
<b>The departure of declination from its</b><br>
mean value during a period of 24 hours<br>
at any place is called diurnal variation.<br>
<b>The diurnal variation is a variation of</b><br>
the following variables:<br>
(i)	 The geographical position of the<br>
place. Diurnal variation is the<br>
greatest for the places in higher<br>
latitudes and lesser near the equator.<br>
(ii)	 Season of the year. Diurnal<br>
variation is comparatively more<br>
in summer than in winter at the<br>
same place.<br>
(iii)	The time at the place. It is more<br>
during the day and less at night.<br>
(iv)	 The year of the cycle. It is different<br>
in different years in the complete<br>
cycle of secular variation.<br>
d)	 Irregular variation<br>
<b>Abrupt changes of declinations at places</b><br>
due to magnetic storms, earthquakes<br>
and other solar influences, are called<br>
irregular variations. These disturbances<br>
may occur at any time and  place and<br>
cannot be predicted. The displacement<br>
176<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
of a needle may vary in extent from 1°<br>
to 2°.<br>
<b>Magnetic dip</b><br>
<b>The magnetic dip is defined as the angle</b><br>
made with the horizontal by the earth’s<br>
magnetic field lines. It is also known as<br>
dip angle or magnetic inclination and<br>
was discovered by George Hartman in<br>
the year 1544. When the inclination<br>
is positive, it indicates that the earth’s<br>
magnetic lines are pointing downward to<br>
the Northern Hemisphere and when the<br>
inclination is negative it indicates that<br>
the earth’s magnetic lines are pointing<br>
upward to the Southern Hemisphere.<br>
<b>In the year 1581, Robert Norman</b><br>
discovered a dip circle which is a method<br>
used to measure the dip angle. The other<br>
terms used are isoclinic lines (when the<br>
dip of the earth’s magnetic field is the<br>
same along the line) and aclinic lines<br>
(when the locus of the points has zero<br>
dips).<br>
<b>Avoidance of errors in compass</b><br>
survey<br>
<b>To reduce possible errors during a</b><br>
compass survey, the following are<br>
very crucial. Check the accuracy of<br>
the compass by comparing it with an<br>
accurate compass or with location of<br>
magnetic north (MN) for the year of<br>
the  survey with updated MN correction<br>
adjustment for field readings. Always<br>
check every reading with a back bearing<br>
and adjustment by adding or subtracting<br>
the mean of the error. Hold the compasses<br>
steady at 180° and  ensure accuracy of<br>
all chain or tape measurements between<br>
ends of legs and to points of observation<br>
on legs.<br>
<b>Check that all conversions from magnetic</b><br>
bearing to true bearing are accurate and<br>
all scaled conversions are accurate.<br>
<b>Ensure the booker takes down reading</b><br>
accurately by asking him to repeat where<br>
necessary. Avoid areas where there are<br>
deposits of metalliferous ores steel<br>
structures, metal gates, railway lines and<br>
others, which would influence reading.<br>
Avoid wearing metal rimmed spectacles,<br>
metal bangles or steel watches when<br>
using the compass.<br>
<b>Advantages of compass survey</b><br>
First, fairly rapid method in the field,<br>
compared to other old survey methods,<br>
like simple chain survey. Secondly, a<br>
check can be made on all compass<br>
bearing, simply by calculating the<br>
forward and back bearing. Thirdly,<br>
cumulative error is reduced and can<br>
be easily rectified, with the help of<br>
advanced devices used. Fourthly,<br>
near and distant objects can be pin-<br>
pointed with accuracy by using<br>
various instruments which are  able to<br>
accommodate the distance of object.<br>
<b>Fifthly, the method can be combined</b><br>
with other methods such as chain<br>
levelling and plane table, and in fact<br>
the prismatic compass is sometimes<br>
needed for mapping.<br>
<b>Disadvantages of compass survey</b><br>
<b>In elementary survey, compass</b><br>
observations over long distance object<br>
cannot be checked by back bearings<br>
unless transport is provided. Human<br>
error is involved in taking as it is difficult<br>
to hold the compass absolutely steady;<br>
177<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
taking the mean of the difference between forward and back bearing reduces error<br>
but does not entirely eliminate it. The presence of ore bodies may not be known<br>
to the surveyor and this would affect readings. It is always better to consult the<br>
geological map or geologist first but the presence of iron ores in large quantity<br>
would necessitate using another method.<br>

<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Plane table survey</b><br>
<b>Plane table surveying is a graphical</b><br>
method of survey in which the field<br>
observation and plotting are done<br>
simultaneously. It is a graphical<br>
construction of straight lines, angles, and<br>
triangles for plotting the ground detail<br>
points. This method of land surveying<br>
is simple and cheaper than theodolite<br>
survey and only suitable on small areas.<br>
<b>The plan is drawn by the surveyor in the</b><br>
field while the area to be surveyed is<br>
in front of his eyes. Therefore, there is<br>
low possibility of omitting the necessary<br>
measurements. Hence, the method has<br>
very low chances for committing errors.<br>
<b>Just as it is for the case of compass and</b><br>
chain surveying, plane table surveying<br>
is named after the principal instrument<br>
used, the plane table. The earliest<br>
mention and account of a plane table<br>
and plane table survey dates back to 1551<br>
by Abel Foullon’s in his work “Usage et<br>
description de l’holomètre”, which was<br>
published in Paris.  Prior to 1830, the<br>
method was called plane table (Figure<br>
3.51).<br>
<b>Board</b><br>
<b>Tripod stand</b><br>
<b>Iron shoe</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.51: Plane table</b><br>
<b>Plane table are available in 750 mm ×</b><br>
600 mm in dimension and the legs of the<br>
tripod are usually 1200 mm long. They<br>
are made entirely of well-seasoned wood<br>
except for the metal plate, bolts, nuts and<br>
screws, which are made of brass and the<br>
shoes of the legs, which are made of iron.<br>
<b>Principles of plane table survey</b><br>
<b>The plane table technique operates on</b><br>
the principle that every established ray<br>
to various details must pass through the<br>
survey station. This principle is called<br>
parallelism which means that, all the<br>
lines drawn through various details<br>
should pass through the survey station.<br>
<b>This principle can be best understood</b><br>
by considering the graphical reduction<br>
of a triangle to the given dimensions.<br>
<b>The base of the triangle is plotted on</b><br>
the desired scale and the base angles are<br>
plotted directly by turning the alidade<br>
at each end. In plain table survey, the<br>
position of plane table at each station,<br>
must be maintained identical, that is, at<br>
each survey station the table must be<br>
oriented by magnetic north.<br>
<b>Equipment used in plane table</b><br>
survey<br>
<b>In plane tabling equipment can be</b><br>
grouped into basic equipment and<br>
accessories. Equipment includes a plane<br>
table; tripod stand and alidade. While<br>
accessories include trough compass,<br>
plumbing fork, plumb bob,chain or tape<br>
and ranging poles spirit level, U-fork,<br>
water proof cover, drawing paper, pins,<br>
pencil and eraser. The later supporting<br>
instruments are what this book is<br>
referring to as accessories.<br>
180<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Plane table this is the principal equipment</b><br>
in plane table surveying. It is a board<br>
from which rays to the target objects<br>
are established, measured and located.<br>
<b>Drawing board for plane tabling is made</b><br>
up of well-seasoned wood with its upper<br>
surface exactly plane. Plane tables are<br>
in a number of sizes. The common<br>
dimensions of plane tables are those<br>
rectangular in shape with size 75 cm X<br>
60 cm.  Plane table comes with wooden<br>
or aluminum tripod stand to mount on<br>
during the surveying process. They are<br>
provided with clamps to fix it in any<br>
direction. The table can revolve around<br>
its vertical axis and can be clamped in<br>
any position when necessary. The plane<br>
table is mounted on a tripod stand (see<br>
Figure 3.52(b)).<br>
<b>Tripod stand is provided with three-</b><br>
foot screws at its top for levelling and<br>
adjustable legs for adjusting the height<br>
of the plane table. The height of the<br>
tripod stand is normally 120 cm (Figure<br>
3.52(a)).<br>
<b>Figure 3.52(a): Tripod stand</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.52(b): Tripod stand with plane table</b><br>
<b>Alidade is multi-task purpose equipment</b><br>
useful throughout plane table surveying<br>
in assisting as a ruler and a sighting<br>
instrument. It is a straight edged ruler,<br>
attached with a sighting device. One edge<br>
of the ruler is beveled and graduated.<br>
<b>Usually, the graduated edge is used for</b><br>
drawing lines of sight to objects from<br>
the plane table station. There are two<br>
types of alidades available for plane<br>
tabling; the simple alidade and telescopic<br>
alidade.<br>
Simple alidade is used for ordinary work.<br>
<b>It consists of gun-metal or wooden ruler</b><br>
with two vertical vanes at the ends. The<br>
eye-vane is provided with a narrow<br>
slit while the object vane is open and<br>
carries a horse hair. Both the slits,<br>
provide a definite line of sight which<br>
can be made to pass through the object<br>
to be sighted. To draw the rays, one of<br>
the edges of alidade is beveled and its<br>
perfectly smooth working edge is known<br>
as the fiducial edge. The fiducial edge<br>
is graduated to facilitate the plotting of<br>
distances to a scale (Figure 3.53).<br>

<b>Figure 3.53: Simple alidade</b><br>
<b>Telescopic alidade: is used when it</b><br>
is required to take inclined sights. It<br>
essentially consists of a small telescope<br>
with a level tube and graduated arc<br>
mounted on horizontal axis. It gives<br>
higher accuracy and more range of sight<br>
(Figure 3.54).<br>

<b>Trough compass  is  a thin narrow wooden</b><br>
box containing a magnetic needle  about<br>
120 mm long. The box is moved around<br>
on plane table surface so that when the<br>
needle points to the magnetic North, it<br>
is parallel to the sides of the box. It is<br>
required for drawing the line showing<br>
magnetic meridian on the paper. It is<br>
used to orient the table to the magnetic<br>
meridian (Figure 3.55).<br>

<b>Spirit levels are common and cheaply</b><br>
available for masonries. They are flat-<br>
based aluminum or woody bar fitted with<br>
bubble tube and are used for levelling in<br>
brick laying. In plane table surveying,<br>
spirit levels are used to level the plane<br>
table during surveying or ascertaining<br>
if the table is properly leveled. Board’s<br>
levelling is done by placing the level on<br>
the board in two positions at right angles<br>
and getting the bubble at the center of<br>
bubble tube. To ensure that a plane table<br>
is perfectly leveled, a bubble of spirit<br>
level must be at the center of the bubble<br>
tube and checked with its positions in any<br>
two mutually perpendicular directions as<br>
indicated in Figure 3.56.<br>
