<b>Apart from the instruments for the plane</b><br>
table, there are also accessories which<br>
include the following;<br>
<b>U-fork with plumb bob is used for</b><br>
centering the table over the survey<br>
station. U-fork is also called a U-frame.<br>
<b>It is attached on the plane table during</b><br>
the field plotting and fixed with a plumb<br>
to point on a station when the plotted<br>
position of that point is already on the<br>
sheet. In the beginning of the work the<br>
<b>U-fork with its plumb bob is used to</b><br>

as indicated in Figure 3.57.<br>

<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Field practical operations</b><br>
<b>Form five, and six geography students</b><br>
should note that proper handling of plane<br>
table equipment and its accessories<br>
and performing surveying activities<br>
diligently and in accordance with the<br>
practice and rules which ultimately build<br>
a foundation for  obtain reliable, and<br>
accurate field data is very important. In<br>
performing plane table surveying, two<br>
main steps are involved: Setting up a<br>
plane table and locating the position of<br>
points or features as required.<br>
a)	 Setting up the plane table over the<br>
chosen station<br>
<b>Setting up a plane table involves three</b><br>
operations. Centering, levelling and<br>
orienting. Centering the plane table<br>
means putting the table exactly over<br>
the starting point you have chosen.  The<br>
procedure involves setting up of tripod<br>
by fixing them firmly and properly<br>
spread on the ground and mounting<br>
a plane table board on a tripod at a<br>
convenient height. The table should be<br>
approximately leveled by tripod legs by<br>
judging by the eyes. Then the operation<br>
of centering is carried out by means of<br>
u-frame and plumb bob.<br>
It is a preliminary stage to plane tabling,<br>
after mounting the board, then a point on<br>
the ground is transferred to a paper sheet,<br>
spread on top of the board (Figure 3.59).<br>
<b>Figure 3.59: Plane table on ground beacon station</b><br>
<b>The table then is leveled using the</b><br>
spirit level (Figure 3.60). The board<br>
is considered level when a spirit level<br>
bubble is at the center. After mounting a<br>
plane table on tripod stand, the process of<br>
making a plane table perfectly horizontal<br>
is what we phrase in this book as plane<br>
table levelling. The spirit level is placed<br>
parallel to the two perpendicular sides<br>
of a plane table at each corner. Then, its<br>
bubble is brought to the center by either<br>
turning the three foot-screws or adjusting<br>
the three legs of a tripod stand.<br>
<b>Figure 3.60: Spirit level</b><br>
<b>Orienting the table is another equally</b><br>
important and necessary process for<br>
setting up of a plane table before any<br>
measurement is taken. It is a process<br>
of aligning setting up a plane table in<br>
such a way that, all the lines plotted on<br>
184<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
a sheet are parallel to corresponding<br>
lines on the ground. The procedure<br>
and processes of making lines drawn<br>
on sheet of paper on the board parallel<br>
to the corresponding  lines on ground,<br>
are refered to as orientation of plane<br>
table. The accuracy of plane table survey<br>
mainly depends upon the accuracy of<br>
plane table orientation at each station<br>
point. The orientation of a plane table can<br>
be achieved by either trough compass<br>
or back-sighting method. Therefore,<br>
orientation here means assuring that the<br>
plane table is strictly oriented towards<br>
the magnetic meridian.<br>
<b>In orienting by trough compass, a line</b><br>
representing a meridian is drawn on a<br>
drawing sheet to orient a plane table. A<br>
trough compass is then placed along this<br>
line, and a table is turned until the trough<br>
compass points towards the north-south<br>
direction. At this position, the plane table<br>
is clamped. This method is quick but<br>
unsuitable in areas with metal ferric ores<br>
which affect the direction of magnetic<br>
needle due to local attractions. Consider<br>
<b>ABCD and E step by step using radiation</b><br>
method (see Figure 3.61).<br>
<b>Figure 3.61: Plane tabling the farm ABCDE</b><br>
<b>Procedures:</b><br>
(i)	 Set the plane table at P and perform centering and levelling using spirit<br>
level as described in the preceding paragraphs. Put a mark on paper sheet<br>
at a point above point P. The point P is transferred to p on the sheet paper<br>
by using U-fork and plumb bob;<br>
(ii)	 Then using a trough compass, establish north direction somewhere on the<br>
right-hand side on the sheet of paper.  To indicate north direction, place a<br>
trough compass where you would like to draw it and keep twisting to make<br>
sure a compass arrow points to the North. Then draw a line towards the<br>
185<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
meridian as shown by a compass to<br>
indicate north direction as shown<br>
in Figure 3.61. After fixing the<br>
north direction, then the direction<br>
to orient a plane table is ready and<br>
all lines will be drawn parallel to<br>
it; and<br>
(iii)	Then start taking measurement<br>
from the table to the established<br>
stations.<br>
<b>The orientation by back-sighting is a</b><br>
common method used in plane table<br>
surveying. After finishing surveying<br>
from the plane table at A, if the table is<br>
to be shifted to  station B, a line is drawn<br>
from the plotted position of station A<br>
towards station B. Then distance AB<br>
is measured, scaled-down and plotted<br>
position of station B is obtained. Then<br>
the table is shifted to station B, centered<br>
and levelled.  Keeping alidade along line<br>
<b>BA, the table is turned to sight station</b><br>
<b>A and clamped. Thus, the orientation</b><br>
of the table is achieved by the back<br>
sighting. Orientation may be checked<br>
by observing already plotted objects.<br>
<b>Therefore, the orientation procedures in</b><br>
plane table survey aim at making sure<br>
that the plane table at each station, is<br>
parallel to all other successive station,<br>
hence the emergence of the principle of<br>
parallelism.<br>
b) Locating position of points or features<br>
<b>All surveys exist to accomplish one</b><br>
important objective, which is locating<br>
or positioning of both natural and<br>
man-made features. Plane table survey<br>
is not different from this objective.<br>
<b>It may aim at updating information</b><br>
in a school map by locating the new<br>
infrastructure established recently or it<br>
may aim at demarcating land parcels for<br>
housing, farming and others. In plane<br>
table, position of points or features<br>
is determined by scaling down the<br>
measured distances and plotting them on<br>
a drawing sheet. There are three common<br>
methods used to determine the position<br>
of points or features in a plane table<br>
which are; radiation, intersection or<br>
triangulation; traverse and resection.<br>
<b>Plane table survey by radiation</b><br>
method<br>
<b>For form five secondary students, a word</b><br>
radiation should be taking you back to<br>
the radiation topics where you generally<br>
made association of radiation and the<br>
sun. You may apply your knowledge in<br>
this topic too. Radiation is a method in<br>
which all measurements starts from one<br>
point on the plane table out widely to<br>
the object or details intended.  Radiation<br>
method is only effective when the entire<br>
area can be surveyed from one single<br>
station. The plane table will be set up<br>
at one station from where all the other<br>
points that need to be fixed are visible.<br>
For example, positions of points A, B,<br>
<b>C, D, and E in a farm, which are visible</b><br>
from station P, can be fixed using the<br>
radiation method through the following<br>
procedure:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Set up a Plane table at point</b><br>
<b>P where all other points to be</b><br>
fixed (that is, A, B, C, D, E)<br>
are easily seen. Then decide a<br>
scale for the measurement that<br>
you are going to make, let’s<br>
186<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
say, 1cm represents 10 metres. However, preliminary set up of plane table<br>
must be conducted as outlined in a setting up plane table section above;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Put the alidade on point ‘P’ marked on the drawing paper vertically</b><br>
above point ‘P’, and the first point, say A is sighted from it . A ground<br>
distance from P to A is measured and presented on the sheet the line of<br>
sight connecting points P and A is drawn (Figure 3.62) ;<br>
<b>Figure 3.62: Plane tabling setup and procedures</b><br>
Continue measuring all other stations using the alidade to sight to points B, C, D,<br>
and E and present their distances on the paper as shown on the board as  ‘b’, ‘c’,<br>
‘d’, and ‘e’ (see Figure 3.63).<br>
<b>Figure 3.63: Farm ABCDE</b><br>
(iii)	 Then join the Points ‘a’, ‘b’, ‘c’, ‘d’, and ‘e’ as shown in Figure 3.63; and<br>
(iv)	 Finally, produce a plan of a farm surveyed. However, it should be<br>
remembered that, point ‘P’ must be located in such a way that a surveyor<br>
is not there and the plan is found, any other person can determine its<br>
location.<br>

<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
Generally, the points that the surveyor wants to locate are boundary point A, B, C,<br>
D and E. In this case point ‘P’ is just a reference point from which the stations A,<br>
B, C, D and E can be identified. It should be remembered that A, B, C, D, and E,<br>
can also be used to locate ‘P’. It must be remember that, radiation method, fixes<br>
location or position of details from a single point.<br>
<b>Plane table survey by intersection method</b><br>
Different from radiation method, where a line of sight is defined and the distance<br>
to a fixed point is measured, in intersection method, only a line of sight is defined.<br>
This means that in order for a point or feature, such as a building, a water well and<br>
others to be fixed, two lines of sight are defined from two different points towards<br>
a point to be fixed. For example, two corners, A and B of one side of a building,<br>
are fixed using the intersection method from two points, S1 and S2.<br>
For instance, in the Figure 3.64 if the interest is to locate a form two class building<br>
from point S2 near water tank and point near S1the headmaster’s house, a plane<br>
table survey through intersection method can go through the following procedures:<br>
<b>S1</b><br>
<b>S2</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.64: Plane table setting at stations S1 and S2</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Firstly, set up a plane table at stations where points A and B can easily</b><br>
be seen.  In Figure 3.64, we establish stations  S1 and S2.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Secondly, the alidade is put on point S1 marked on the drawing paper</b><br>
vertically above point S1 and A is sighted from S1. The line of sight<br>
connecting points S1 and A is drawn. The same is done to S1 and B<br>
which are also drawn on a drawing paper.<br>
(iii)	 Thirdly, a baseline connecting stations ‘S1’ and S2 is defined by measuring<br>
the distance between stations S1 and S2 on the ground and plotting it to<br>
a suitable scale on the drawing sheet between points S1 and S2 (Figure<br>
3.65).<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.65: Intersection of points A and B from survey stations S1 and S2</b><br>
(iv)	 In the fourth step, a plane table<br>
is moved to station S2 and set up<br>
such that S2 is vertically above<br>
point S2. Then, the alidade is put<br>
along a line connecting points S2<br>
and S1. A plane table is turned<br>
horizontally about a vertical axis<br>
until the alidade bisects a ranging<br>
pole held at station S1 and the<br>
table is clamped.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Finally, the alidade is put on point</b><br>
<b>S2, turned to bisect stations A</b><br>
and B to define their respective<br>
lines of sight on a drawing paper.<br>
<b>A point where lines of sight for</b><br>
station A from stations S1 and<br>
<b>S2 intersect is a position of point</b><br>
<b>A, marked as a on the drawing</b><br>
paper. Similarly, a point where<br>
lines of sight for station B’ from<br>
stations S1 and S2 intersect is a<br>
position of point B, marked as b<br>
on the drawing paper.<br>
<b>Plane table survey by traverse method</b><br>
<b>This method has been used in previous</b><br>
lessons. It has been used in chain and<br>
prismatic surveying. Traverse involve<br>
establishing a series of connected lines<br>
whose positions are to be determined.<br>
<b>A traverse method in plane table</b><br>
surveying is different from traverse in<br>
chain and prismatic surveying. In plane<br>
table survey, plotting is done instantly<br>
in the field. Plane table is also used in<br>
fixing survey lines between stations of a<br>
closed or open traverse. Through traverse<br>
method, a plane table is set up at each<br>
traverse station. For example, a traverse<br>
method is used to fix five corners A, B,<br>
C, D and E of a farm (Figure 3.66).<br>
189<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
Figure 3.66: Fixing positions of five corners A, B, C, D, and E of a farm using traverse methods<br>
of plane table surveying.<br>
<b>The following procedures are followed</b><br>
to fix position of points using traverse<br>
method of plane table survey:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>A plane table is set up at</b><br>
one of the corner points, say<br>
corner point ‘A’, where the<br>
next  corner point, say ‘B’,<br>
is easily seen. The setting-<br>
up involves the procedures<br>
discussed.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>From point ‘A’, a sight is taken</b><br>
towards B and the distance AB<br>
is measured and plotted to a<br>
suitable scale as ‘ab’ on the<br>
drawing sheet.<br>
(iii)	 The plane table is moved to<br>
station ‘B’ and set up such<br>
that ‘b’ is vertically above ‘B’.<br>
<b>Then, the alidade is put along</b><br>
a line connecting points ‘b’<br>
and ‘a’. A plane table is turned<br>
horizontally about a vertical<br>
axis until the alidade bisects<br>
a ranging pole held at station<br>
‘A’, then the table is clamped.<br>
(iv)	 The alidade is put on point ‘b’,<br>
turned to sight a ranging rod<br>
held at C, then the line of sight<br>
connecting points B and C is<br>
drawn on a drawing sheet. A<br>
distance BC is measured and<br>
plotted to a suitable scale.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Steps (iii) and (iv) are repeated</b><br>
for traverse stations C, D, and<br>
<b>E.</b><br>
<b>Plane table survey by resection</b><br>
method<br>
<b>Resection is the method used in</b><br>
determining a location of an unknown<br>
point in relation to known points. The<br>
plane table is fixed at an unknown point<br>
to allow sighting of points of known<br>
positions. The method is usually applied<br>
190<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
when some objects may be difficult to<br>
see them from the base line stations.<br>
<b>To do this, the board is moved to the</b><br>
position of the object position, oriented<br>
by trough compass and clamped. Rays<br>
are drawn on a tracing paper from<br>
three objects in the field A, B, and C<br>
as shown in Figure 3.67. If the board is<br>
correctly oriented, the rays will intersect<br>
at unknown point D.  If the positions<br>
of rays A, B, and C coincide with their<br>
positions on the map, the position of D<br>
can then be established on the map.<br>
(a)<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Figure 3.67: Fixing position of object by</b><br>
plane table resection method.<br>
However, due to incorrect orientation,<br>
the rays may form a small triangle with D<br>
either within or outside the triangle. This<br>
is referred to as the triangle of error. The<br>
error can be fixed through the following<br>
procedure;<br>
<b>Case 1</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>If the position of D is within</b><br>
the triangle of error, it can be<br>
found by drawing short lines<br>
within the triangle, each line<br>
vertically from the ray line<br>
and  proportional to the length<br>
of their respective rays. If they<br>
are properly drawn with a<br>
compass they should intersect<br>
at D’s position as shown in<br>
Figure 3.68.<br>
<b>Figure 3.68: Correcting error when point D is</b><br>
within the triangle.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>To check the new position of</b><br>
<b>D, lay the alidade on D, turn</b><br>
the board  slightly until A and<br>
<b>D are aligned and draw a new</b><br>
ray. Do the same for B and C.<br>
<b>The new rays should meet at</b><br>
point D,  if they do not, repeat<br>
the  process.<br>
191<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Case 2</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>If the position of D lies outside</b><br>
the triangle of error,   point<br>
<b>A, B, and C being in front of</b><br>
the observer, D may be on<br>
the right or left of the triangle<br>
of error. The board may be<br>
moved slightly to the right to<br>
orient it correctly with A, B,<br>
and C. Thus, the position of<br>
<b>D will be to the left. To fix D</b><br>
use vertical lines from rays<br>
proportional to the length of<br>
the rays as shown in Figure<br>
3.69.<br>
<b>Figure 3.69: Correcting error when point D is</b><br>
outside the triangle.<br>
<b>Source of errors in plane table</b><br>
Plane tabling like other survey methods,<br>
is not free from errors. Errors in plane<br>
table survey could fall into three<br>
categories, mainly instrumental errors,<br>
errors in manipulation and sighting and<br>
errors in plotting. Instrumental errors<br>
include instability of the table due to<br>
the shrinking of the paper, thickness of<br>
the pencil and faults related with poor<br>
or failure of proper setting of the plane<br>
table at the start. Some faults from<br>
human error include: improper setting<br>
of the drawing board; improper setting<br>
of the fixing clamp and alidade being not<br>
parallel with the line of sight. Errors from<br>
manipulation include those resulting<br>
from defective levelling, sighting,<br>
orientation, and centering. Consistently,<br>
defective scale of a map and wrongly<br>
intersecting the rays drawn from two<br>
different stations contribute highly to<br>
production of faulty survey data.<br>
<b>Significantly, plane table surveying is a</b><br>
simple and rapid method for survey work<br>
of small land areas where by a map or<br>
plain can be produced. But, the methods<br>
provide little room for surveyors to omit<br>
necessary measurement in the field. The<br>
fact that, the land area surveyed is in<br>
front of the surveyors’ eyes, surveyors<br>
are able to compare feature sizes easily.<br>
<b>It demands fairly simple skills. It is</b><br>
being more field based, the possibility<br>
of making mistakes is minimized.<br>
<b>Despite  advantages mentioned, plane</b><br>
table surveying has some weakness<br>
such as; the method cannot be used in<br>
rainy and windy conditions. The plane<br>
table equipment is heavy to carry so this<br>
may require the use of a car which adds<br>
expenses. A single error in the ground<br>
base line measurement can throw out all<br>
angle measurement. The table uses a lot<br>
of  equipment and accessories which are<br>
easier to forget while in the field. Lastly,<br>
the method cannot be used for a large<br>
scale survey.<br>

<b>Levelling survey</b><br>
<b>Levelling is a method of land survey</b><br>
that focus on determining the heights of<br>
given points above or below a datum line<br>
or determining difference in elevation<br>
between points. Datum is the point or<br>
the surface with respect to which levels<br>
of other points are calculated.<br>
<b>Most of survey methods have their</b><br>
names derived from the main survey<br>
equipment employed or the activity<br>
that is carried out, the technique used<br>
or the survey objective. Levelling<br>
survey derives its name from the survey<br>
objective, levelling, and its principal<br>
equipment level. Mentioning a word<br>
“level” may seem new to some students<br>
but it has been used quite often in our<br>
daily activities. A spirit level shown in<br>
<b>Figure 3.70 is the most common level</b><br>
that is used in masonry works, especially,<br>
maintaining perfect wall horizontality<br>
and verticality during brick laying.<br>
<b>Figure 3.70: Spirit level</b><br>
<b>Levelling survey uses the same principles</b><br>
used by masons to maintain the<br>
horizontality and verticality during brick<br>
laying.  Levelling survey has been very<br>
useful in contouring mapping, preparing<br>
land cross section and longitudinal<br>
section. Levelling survey provides data<br>
helpful for construction works of narrow<br>
sections of the earth such as sewers,<br>
pipelines, roads and railways.<br>
<b>Common terms in levelling survey</b><br>
<b>There are many special and non-special</b><br>
terms commonly used in levelling.<br>
<b>Some of these are highlighted in this</b><br>
section without emphasis and detailed<br>
explanation. Other terms will be<br>
explained in each section they appear.<br>
These terms are;<br>
<b>Instrument station: is the point where</b><br>
instrument is set up for observations.<br>
<b>Station: is the point where levelling staff</b><br>
is held. It is the point whose elevation<br>
is to be determined or the point that is<br>
to be established at a given elevation.<br>
<b>Height of instrument (HI): is the elevation</b><br>
of the line of sight with respect to the<br>
assumed datum. It is also referred to as<br>
height of collimation (HC). In levelling it<br>
does not mean the height of the telescope<br>
above the ground level where the level<br>
is set up.<br>
<b>Back sight (BS): is the first sight taken on</b><br>
a levelling staff held at point of known<br>
elevation. It ascertains the amount by<br>
which the line of sight is above or below<br>
the elevation of the point. Back sight<br>
enables the surveyor to obtain the height<br>
of the instrument.<br>
<b>Fore sight (F.S): is the sight taken on a</b><br>
levelling staff held at a point of unknown<br>
elevation to ascertain the amount by<br>
which the point is above or below the line<br>
of sight. Fore sight enables the surveyor<br>
to obtain the elevation of the point. It is<br>
also generally known as minus sight as<br>
the foresight reading is always subtracted<br>
194<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
from the height of the instrument (except<br>
when the staff is held inverted) to obtain<br>
the elevation.<br>
<b>Change point (CP): is a point on which</b><br>
both the fore sight and back sight are<br>
taken during the operation of levelling.<br>
<b>Two sights are taken from two different</b><br>
instrument stations, a fore sight to<br>
ascertain the elevation of the point while<br>
a back sight is taken on the same point<br>
to establish the height of the instrument<br>
of the new setting of the level.<br>
<b>Intermediate sight (IS): is the fore</b><br>
sight taken on a levelling staff held at<br>
a point between two change points or<br>
a benchmark and a change point, to<br>
determine the elevation of that point. It<br>
may be noted that are stations sighted<br>
between BS and FS.<br>
<b>Bench mark (BM): refers to a relatively</b><br>
permanent and fixed reference point of<br>
known elevation above the assumed<br>
datum.<br>
<b>Line of collimation (LC): refers to the</b><br>
line passing through the optical centre of<br>
the objective and the point of intersection<br>
of the cross hairs stretched in front of the<br>
eye piece and its continuation.<br>
<b>Line of sight (LS): refers to a line</b><br>
passing through the optical centre of<br>
the objective, traversing the eye-piece<br>
and entering the eye.<br>
<b>Vertical datum: refers to any level surface</b><br>
to which elevations are referenced.<br>
<b>Means sea level is one of the vertical</b><br>
datum assigned on elevation or reduced<br>
level of zero.<br>
<b>Mean sea level datum (M.S.L): is a mean</b><br>
sea level obtained by making hourly<br>
observations of the tides at any place<br>
over a period of 19 years. The M . S . L<br>
datum adopted by the survey of India for<br>
determining the elevations of different<br>
points in India is that of Mumbai. In<br>
<b>East Africa, the datum line is situated in</b><br>
<b>Mombasa, while in Nigeria it is in Lagos</b><br>
and for South Africa it is in Cape Town.<br>
<b>Reduced Level (R.L): is the height or</b><br>
depth of a point above or below the<br>
assumed datum. It is also known as<br>
elevation of the point. Elevations of<br>
the points below the datum surface are<br>
known as negative elevations.<br>
<b>Vertical line: is a line that follows the</b><br>
local direction of gravity as indicated<br>
by a plumb line.<br>
<b>Level surface: is a curved surface that at</b><br>
every point is perpendicular to the local<br>
plumb line (i.e., the direction in which<br>
gravity acts)<br>
<b>Level line: is a curved line on a level</b><br>
surface<br>
<b>Horizontal place: is a plane perpendicular</b><br>
to the local direction of gravity and<br>
tangential to the level surface.<br>
<b>Horizontal line: is a line on a horizontal</b><br>
which is perpendicular to the vertical<br>
line.<br>
<b>Vertical control: is a series of benchmarks</b><br>
or other points of known elevation<br>
established throughout an area.<br>
195<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Equipment used in levelling survey</b><br>
Traditional levelling survey requires three major equipments for its operations.<br>
The equipments includes; a level, tripod stand and a levelling staff.<br>
A level is an instrument with a telescope and bubble tube and levelling head used<br>
for sighting targets, particularly, levelling staff (see Figure 3.71). A telescope is<br>
an optical instrument mounted on levels to magnify and view distant objects. It<br>
provides a line of sight. A bubble tube is used together with the levelling head to<br>
make the line of sight horizontal by bringing the bubble to the center of its run.<br>
<b>Diaphragm</b><br>
<b>Bubble tube</b><br>
<b>Objective</b><br>
<b>Vertical</b><br>
spindle<br>
<b>Levelling</b><br>
screws<br>
<b>Vertical axis</b><br>
<b>Tripod</b><br>
<b>Trivet stage</b><br>
<b>Tribrach</b><br>
<b>Eyepiece</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.71: Components of a level instrument</b><br>
There are three basic types of level instruments, namely: Dumpy levels, engineers’<br>
or tilting levels and automatic levels. The first two are spirit bubble levels. The<br>
dumpy level is a very simple basic instrument, while the tilting level has certain<br>
modifications which give it greater convenience of operation and a possibility of<br>
greater clarity. However, the most often used and “quick set” level is a tilting level<br>
without foot-screws. The automatic type of levels which gives a horizontal line of<br>
sight automatically, are the most favorable to use, but they are relatively expensive.<br>
(a)	 Dumpy level: is characterized by their telescope being rigidly attached to the<br>
vertical spindle (see Figure 3.72). The levelling of the instrument is done by<br>
means of three-foot screws separating their two plates. The upper plate with<br>
the vertical spindle on which the telescope and bubble tube are mounted has<br>
to be levelled with the foot screws.<br>
196<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.72: Dumpy level</b><br>
(b)	 The engineer’s level: also known as<br>
tilting level has a telescope which<br>
is not rigidly fixed to the vertical<br>
spindle (Figure 3.73). Instead,<br>
the telescope is capable of tilting<br>
slightly in the vertical plane about<br>
a point just below the telescope.<br>
<b>This vertical movement of the</b><br>
telescope is made by rotating a<br>
tilting screw below the eyepiece.<br>
<b>They have two bubbles, circular</b><br>
bubble on the upper plate is used<br>
to achieve approximate levelling<br>
by means of the three foot screws<br>
and telescope bubble (tube bubble)<br>
to be levelled for each sighting by<br>
the tilting screw only. This type of<br>
levels exists in two features, the<br>
“quickest” and normal tilt levels.<br>
<b>Contrary to the “normal” tilting</b><br>
level the “quickset” level does not<br>
have foot screws in the levelling<br>
head. A ball-and-socket joint is<br>
provided to level the instrument<br>
quickly, but only approximately.<br>
<b>Accurate levelling of the instrument</b><br>
must be completed with the tilting<br>
screw for each sighting.<br>
<b>Figure 3.73: Engineer’s level</b><br>
(c)	  The automatic level: automatic<br>
levels are fairly advanced than<br>
dumpy and tilting levels. They are<br>
provided with either foot screws<br>
or a ball-and-socket joint (Figure<br>
3.74). However, they do not have<br>
a bubble tube for precise levelling,<br>
instead they use compensators to<br>
automatically to make the line of<br>
sight horizontal once an approximate<br>
levelling has been achieved. Setting<br>
up and approximate levelling is<br>
done with the circular bubble in<br>
the same way as for a tilting level.<br>
<b>Figure 3.74: Automatic level</b><br>
(d)	 Tripod stand: is an important<br>
component in any levelling process<br>
for it carries a level instrument<br>
during levelling survey.  The level<br>
197<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
is mounted on a tripod stand which<br>
consists of three solid wooden or<br>
aluminum framed legs. At the lower<br>
ends, the legs are provided with<br>
pointed iron shoes for driving them<br>
on the ground during the levelling<br>
process (Figure 3.75).<br>
<b>Figure 3.75: Tripod stand</b><br>
(e)	  Levelling staffs: are wooden or<br>
metallic rods, graduated into metre<br>
or feet and further smaller divisions<br>
of 10 mm intervals and marked<br>
in red, black and white for easy<br>
reading (Figure 3.76). The staffs<br>
are available in 3 m to 5 m long.<br>
<b>The bottom of the levelling staff</b><br>
represents the zero reading. The<br>
reading given by the line of sight<br>
on a levelling staff held vertically<br>
is the vertical distance above the<br>
point on which the staff is held.<br>
<b>Levelling staffs may be grouped</b><br>
into two classes; self-reading staffs<br>
and target staffs.<br>
<b>Figure 3.76: Reading on a levelling staffs</b><br>
(i)	  Self-reading staff: are those in<br>
which the readings are directly<br>
obtained by the observer through<br>
the telescope. In self-reading staff<br>
any ordinary man can hold the staff<br>
and keep it in plumb. Self-reading<br>
staffs are of three types: the solid<br>
staff, the folding or hinged staff and<br>
the telescopic staff.  The Solid staff<br>
are usually available in 3 m long in<br>
one length. The absence of hinge or<br>
socket on these staffs, makes them<br>
more accurate in reading compared<br>
to hinged ones. However, the staffs<br>
are inconvenient to carry in the<br>
field. Use of a solid staff is generally<br>
restricted to only precise levelling<br>
work. Folding or hinged staff are<br>
made of well-seasoned timber<br>
with 4 m long. They consist of two<br>
portions, each being 2 m hinged<br>
together. The width and thickness of<br>
the staff, is kept 75 mm and 18 mm<br>
respectively. Telescopic or Sopwitch<br>
type of staff consists of three pieces;<br>
198<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Top piece is solid 1.25 m long</b><br>
whereas central piece 1.25 m and<br>
lower piece 1.5 m are hollow. The<br>
top portion slides into the central<br>
portion telescopically. When fully<br>
extended, total length of the staff is<br>
4 m. The upper two pieces are held<br>
by brass spring catches.<br>
(ii)	  Target staff: is a class which consists<br>
of two ordinary rods, the upper rod<br>
6 ft. in length and which slides into<br>
lower one which is 7 ft. in length.<br>
<b>A target which can be moved up</b><br>
and down is attached to the staff.<br>
<b>The rod is graduated in feet, and</b><br>
its tenths and hundreds. For taking<br>
readings the level man directs the<br>
staff man to raise or lower the target<br>
till it is bisected by the line of sight.<br>
<b>The staff man clamps the target and</b><br>
takes the reading. This means that<br>
the duties of a target staff-man are<br>
as important as those of the observer<br>
and demand the services of a trained<br>
man.<br>
(f)	 Theodolite and total station:<br>
although levels are still highly<br>
in use, it is not because they are<br>
the only equipment that can be<br>
used for levelling. Technological<br>
development has provided options<br>
to other advanced and sophisticated<br>
equipment like Theodolites  and<br>
Total stations (Figure 3.77).<br>
<b>Figure 3.77: (a) Electronic theodolite</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.77: ( b) Total station</b><br>
199<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Theodolite is an optical or electronic</b><br>
equipment for measuring horizontal and<br>
vertical angles or distances. Total station<br>
is an electronic instrument for measuring<br>
both horizontal and vertical angles and<br>
distances. The two equipment are used in<br>
a trigonometric levelling. Trigonometric<br>
levelling applies trigonometric<br>
relationship to determine elevation of<br>
points from measured vertical angles and<br>
measured horizontal or slope distances.<br>
<b>Types of levelling</b><br>
<b>Different books classify levelling</b><br>
differently based on different perspectives<br>
and context. All levelling practice<br>
can be divided into direct levelling<br>
which involves all methods which take<br>
vertical distances direct from the field<br>
and indirect levelling in which vertical<br>
distances are not measured directly in<br>
the field. Direct levelling is of two types<br>
namely differential levelling and profile<br>
levelling.<br>
<b>Differential levelling</b><br>
<b>Differential levelling is carried out to</b><br>
determine the elevation of a distant point<br>
that cannot be determined with a single<br>
set up of the instrument. It involves<br>
setting up a level several times to take<br>
reading along the route between the<br>
benchmark and the distant point. At<br>
each instrument set up, only two staff<br>
readings, a back sight and a fore sight<br>
are observed (Figure 3.78).<br>
<b>Figure 3.78: Differential levelling</b><br>

<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
(a)	 Profile levelling<br>
Profile levelling is another category of direct levelling which determines elevations<br>
of a series of points along a line before shifting a level to another station. In this<br>
type of levelling, an instrument can determine elevation of several points between a<br>
back sight and fore sight. As such a back sight, a fore sight and as many intermediate<br>
sights as possible are taken in profile levelling (Figure 3.79). Profile levelling is<br>
particularly considered important in drainage and terrace layout.<br>

<b>Levelling procedures</b><br>
<b>The execution of levelling starts with</b><br>
the adjustments of the level instrument<br>
and followed by leveled observation and<br>
record observed levels in level sheets.<br>
<b>Instrument adjustment</b><br>
<b>The instrument adjustment done in</b><br>
levelling is categorized into temporary<br>
adjustment and permanent adjustment.<br>
(a)	 Temporary adjustment<br>
<b>These are adjustments performed at</b><br>
each instrument station where a level is<br>
set-up before taking any observation.<br>
<b>Temporary adjustment involves setting</b><br>
up a level, levelling and eliminating<br>
parallax. The following procedures are<br>
carried out:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Setting up the level: this operation</b><br>
includes fixing the instrument<br>
on the tripod and  levelling the<br>
instrument approximately by leg<br>
adjustment. The tripod legs are so<br>
adjusted that the telescope is at a<br>
convenient height and the machine<br>
(tribrach) is approximately<br>
levelled. To fix the instrument<br>
on tripod stand, tripod legs are<br>
well spread on the ground to place<br>
a tripod’s head nearly level and<br>
at convenient height, a level is<br>
mounted and fixed on the tripod.<br>
<b>Then adjust the tripod’s legs and</b><br>
bring all the foot screws of the<br>
level in the centre of their run.<br>
<b>Modern levels such as automatic</b><br>
levels, are provided with a small<br>
circular bubble on the tribrach for<br>
achieving approximate levelling<br>
of the instrument.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Levelling is done with the help</b><br>
of foot screws and bubbles. The<br>
purpose of levelling is to make<br>
the vertical axis truly vertical.<br>
<b>The method of levelling depends</b><br>
upon whether there are three-<br>
foot screws or four-foot screws.<br>
<b>In all modern instruments three-</b><br>
foot screws are provided and<br>
only procedures for levelling<br>
instruments with three-foot screws<br>
are explained. In the first step, a<br>
201<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
tubular bubble is aligned parallel<br>
to any two-foot screw which are<br>
then simultaneously turned inward<br>
or outward to bring the bubble<br>
to the centre. Then, the tubular<br>
bubble is aligned perpendicular<br>
to these two foot screws and a<br>
third screw is turned to bring the<br>
bubble to the centre. The tubular<br>
bubble is turned to different<br>
positions to check whether the<br>
bubble remains central. When<br>
the bubble is central, the level<br>
is levelled, otherwise permanent<br>
adjustment is required.<br>
(iii)	 Elimination of parallax involves<br>
two operations, focusing on the<br>
eye piece and focusing on the<br>
objective. To focus on the eye-<br>
piece, first direct the telescope<br>
either towards the sky or hold a<br>
sheet of white paper in front of<br>
the objective. Then move the eye<br>
piece in or out till the cross hairs<br>
appear distinct and very dark.<br>
<b>In some levels, the eye-piece is</b><br>
graduated and numbered. Once<br>
the eye-piece is focused, the<br>
observer may note this position<br>
to save much of his time at other<br>
settings.<br>
<b>The objective is focused to adjust</b><br>
the visibility of a staff through<br>
a telescope. To focus on the<br>
objective, direct the telescope<br>
towards the levelling staff to see<br>
the staff. If the staff is not seen<br>
from the telescopic level, turn<br>
the focusing screw till the staff<br>
image appears clear and sharp.<br>
<b>The image of the staff should be</b><br>
formed on the plane of cross hairs.<br>
(b)	 Permanent adjustment<br>
Contrary to temporary adjustment,<br>
permanent adjustment can be carried<br>
when the device has major technical<br>
problem. Two types of permanent<br>
adjustment are usually carried out in<br>
levelling: adjustment of bubble tube<br>
error and collimation error. The bubble<br>
tube error happens when the vertical<br>
axis of a level is not truly vertical after<br>
bringing the bubble to the centre. The<br>
collimation error happens when the line<br>
of sight is not horizontal after bringing<br>
the bubble to the centre.<br>
<b>Field procedures and field data booking</b><br>
<b>Field levelling procedures involve</b><br>
setting up the instrument as described<br>
in temporary adjustment, reading vertical<br>
distances on the levelling staff and<br>
recording them on field note books.<br>
<b>Reading vertical distances</b><br>
<b>To take the staff readings the following</b><br>
procedure are carried out:<br>
(i)	 The level is set up at a convenient<br>
position, such as X and the level<br>
staff is placed over the benchmark<br>
(BM), such as the BM of a reduced<br>
level of 100 m (Figure 3.80). Then,<br>
the first reading, called back sight<br>
(BS) is taken at the BM. Let its value<br>
be 1.82 m.<br>
(ii)	 The staff is now moved to points A<br>
and B in turn and readings, called<br>
intermediate sights (IS) are taken.<br>
<b>Let their values be 1.0 m and 0.6</b><br>
m. The staff is moved from station<br>
<b>B to C and the last reading let its</b><br>
value be 0.25 m, called foresight<br>
(FS) is taken with the first set up of<br>
the instrument.<br>
202<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
(iii)	 After taking the last reading in the first set up of the instrument, the level<br>
is moved to the second position, say Y. Then, the staff held at station C is<br>
turned towards station Y and a back sight reading is taken at it. Let its value<br>
be 1.94 m (Figure 3.80). The staff is moved from station C to D and the last<br>
reading, called foresight (FS) is taken before the instrument is shifted again,<br>
or the levelling observation stops. In Figure 3.80, BM is a benchmark, A<br>
and B are intermediate sights and E is the last station while X, Y, and Z are<br>
instruments stations.<br>
1.94<br>
<b>BM</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.80: Levelling procedures</b><br>
<b>Recording staff reading</b><br>
<b>The staff readings are then recorded on a field note book and reduced using the</b><br>
rise and fall method or the height of collimation method.<br>
<b>Rise and fall method</b><br>
In rise and fall method, the amount which a point is above or below another point<br>
is determined by subtracting each staff reading, the IS or FS from the preceding<br>
staff reading, which could be the BS or IS. The method uses a table of eight<br>
columns, namely, Station, BS, IS, FS, Rise, Fall, RL and Remarks. At each row,<br>
staff readings and reduced level at a particular station are recorded. The following<br>
procedures are followed in Rise and Fall method:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>In the first row, record reading taken at the first station,  BM. The station</b><br>
identifier is recorded in the first column, the BS in the second column and<br>
the RL in the seventh column. In the last column a remark is provided<br>
(Table 3.3).<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>In the second row, record the station identifier,  A and the IS in the third</b><br>
column.<br>
203<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
(iii)	 Subtract staff reading at A (IS) from sight reading at BM (BS). If the<br>
difference is positive, point A is relatively above BM, meaning there is<br>
a rise in ground surface from BM to A. Record the difference in the fifth<br>
column called Rise. If the difference is negative, point A is relatively<br>
below BM, meaning there is a fall in ground surface from BM to A.<br>
Thus, the difference should be recorded in the sixth column, called Fall.<br>
<b>The negative sign should be ignored when recording Fall values, that is</b><br>
only magnitude values should be recorded.<br>
(iv)	 Calculate the RL of station A by adding the Rise to or subtracting the<br>
<b>Fall from the RL of the BM. In this case, a Rise at A is added to the RL</b><br>
of the BM and recorded in the seventh column. Repeat the procedures<br>
(i) to (iv) for stations B, C, D and E (see Table 3.3).<br>
(v)<br>
<b>At the end of the table arithmetic check must be shown. The check is</b><br>
given by:<br>
<b>ΣBS - ΣFS = Σ(Rises) – Σ(Falls) = RLLAST – RLFIRST</b><br>

Since ΣBS – ΣFS = Σ(Rises) – Σ(Falls) = RLLAST – RLFIRST = 1.701,<br>
Therefore, the recording and reduction of levelling was correctly done.<br>

<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Height of collimation method</b><br>
In height of collimation method, the height of collimation (HC) is determined at<br>
each instrument set up by adding the BS to the respective RL. The method uses<br>
a table of seven columns, namely, Station, BS, IS, FS, HC, RL and Remarks. At<br>
each row, staff readings and reduced level at a particular station are recorded.<br>
<b>The following procedures are followed for   height of collimation method:</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>In the first row, record reading taken at the first station, say BM. The</b><br>
station identifier is recorded in the first column, the BS in the second<br>
column and the RL in the seventh column. Add a staff reading at BM<br>
(BS) to the RL of the BM to get the height of collimation (HC) at the first<br>
instrument set up, and record it in the fifth column. In the last column<br>
a remark is provided (see Table 3.5) that uses data from Figure 3.78.<br>
<b>HC at first set up = BS at BM + RL of station BM</b><br>
(ii)<br>
<b>In the second row, record a staff reading taken at A (IS) in the third</b><br>
column. Then, subtract staff reading at A (IS) from HC of the first setup<br>
of instrument to get the RL of station A and record it in the sixth column.<br>
<b>Continue determining and recording RL at all stations observed in the</b><br>
first set up (see Table 3.5).<br>
<b>RL at A = HC at first set up – IS at A</b><br>
<b>RL at B = HC at first set up – FS at B</b><br>
(iii)	 Add a BS reading taken at B in the second set up of the instrument to<br>
the RL of station B to get the second height of collimation (HC) and<br>
record it in the fifth column as shown in Table 3.5.<br>
<b>HC at second set up = BS at B + RL of station B</b><br>
(iv)	 In the fourth, fifth and sixth rows, subtract staff reading taken at stations<br>
<b>C, D and E from the HC of the second set up of instrument to get their</b><br>
respective RL and record them in the sixth column.<br>
<b>RL at C = HC at second set up – IS at C</b><br>
<b>RL at D = HC at second set up – IS at D</b><br>
<b>RL at E = HC at second set up – IS at E</b><br>
(v)<br>
Repeat the procedures (iii) to (iv) for any instrument set up that follows.<br>
(vi)	 At the end of the table, arithmetic check must be shown. The check for<br>
height of collimation method is given as:<br>
<b>ΣBS – ΣFS = RLLAST – RLFIRST.</b><br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>

a BM and every change point. In Table 3.5 for instance, HC is recorded<br>
at BM change point (CP) B.<br>
(iii)	 In rise and fall method, only magnitude values of rise and fall are<br>
recorded, their associated algebraic signs are not written.<br>

<b>Staff reading along a profile levellin</b><br>
<b>Application of levelling</b><br>
<b>Levelling skills and knowledge are of</b><br>
great demand in our daily life. Apart from<br>
the general problem of determining the<br>
difference in levels between two points,<br>
the main applications of levelling are in<br>
contouring, preparation of longitudinal<br>
and cross-sectional profiles and setting<br>
out of levels in construction works.<br>
<b>Contouring</b><br>
<b>Contouring is the process of creating</b><br>
contours. Contours are imaginary lines<br>
connecting points of equal elevation.<br>
<b>Contouring can be done using direct</b><br>
method or indirect method.<br>
<b>Direct method of contouring</b><br>
<b>In the direct method of contouring, points</b><br>
defining a contour line are pegged out on<br>
the ground and their horizontal positions<br>
determined. The contouring method<br>
involves the following procedure:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>A level is set at a convenient</b><br>
position in the area and a BS<br>
reading is taken at an appropriate<br>
bench mark.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>The height of collimation is found</b><br>
and the required staff reading for<br>
a contour line is calculated. The<br>
required staff reading is determined<br>
by subtracting a contour on the<br>
height of collimation staff reading<br>
(IS or FS) = HC – contour value.<br>
(iii)	 The instrument man asks staff<br>
man to move up and down in the<br>
area till the required staff reading<br>
is found. In Figure 3.81, a staff<br>
reading of 1.3 m is needed to<br>
mark a contour of 101.5 m. The<br>
staff was first held at point 1 and<br>
its reading was higher than 1.3<br>
m, thus it was moved to points<br>
of higher elevations until a staff<br>
reading of 1.3 m was taken.<br>
208<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>BM 100 m</b><br>
1<br>
2<br>
3<br>
1.3<br>
2.8<br>
<b>HC = 102.8</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.81: Direct contouring</b><br>
<b>Then, a surveyor determines the</b><br>
horizontal coordinates of that point using<br>
chain surveying, compass surveying,<br>
plane table surveying or other advanced<br>
surveying. The method is  very accurate<br>
but,  slow and tedious.<br>
<b>Activity 3.10</b><br>
<b>A contour line connecting points of</b><br>
elevation of 105 m is to be established<br>
by direct method from a benchmark of<br>
<b>103.625 m. Determine the staff reading</b><br>
at points where the contour line passes.<br>
<b>Indirect contouring</b><br>
<b>In the indirect contouring method, staff</b><br>
readings are taken at some selected<br>
points and their levels are reduced. That<br>
is the horizontal position is established<br>
first and then the levels of those points<br>
are found.  After locating the points<br>
on the plan, reduced levels are marked<br>
and contour lines are interpolated<br>
between the selected points. During<br>
the interpolation it is assumed that the<br>
ground is uniform between any two spot<br>
heights. The indirect method is further<br>
classified into grid and radiation methods<br>
based on the approach used to select<br>
points for staff reading.<br>
<b>Grid method</b><br>
<b>Grid method is best suited to gently</b><br>
sloping undulating land and fairly<br>
small areas. In this method the area to<br>
be surveyed is divided into grid or series<br>
of squares. The grid corners are marked<br>
on the ground and their spot heights are<br>
determined through levelling. The grid is<br>
plotted to the scale and the spot heights<br>
of grid corners are entered. The contours<br>
of desired values are then located by<br>
interpolation as shown in Figure 3.82.<br>

<b>Radiation method</b><br>
<b>Radiation method is useful for areas</b><br>
located in small hills. The method<br>
establishes a central position on a hill and<br>
set out radiating lines using the prismatic<br>
compass, theodolite or total station.<br>
<b>Points are then marked on ground at a</b><br>
regular interval along the radiating line<br>
and their spot heights are determined<br>
through levelling. The radiating lines and<br>
the marked points are plotted to scale and<br>
the spot heights of the marked stations<br>
are entered. The contours of the desired<br>
values are then located by interpolation<br>
as shown in Figure 3.83.<br>
<b>Figure 3.83: Radiation method of indirect contouring</b><br>

<b>Simple survey and mapping</b><br>
<b>Errors in levelling</b><br>
<b>The fact that surveyors’ personalities</b><br>
and ability to adhere to ethical guides<br>
of survey to be unavoidable. Just as<br>
may be the case with other surveying<br>
methods, errors in levelling exist and<br>
can be grouped into Personal errors<br>
(due to natural causes) and instrumental<br>
errors. Personal errors can include<br>
those resulting from sighting problems,<br>
manipulation errors, error in reading<br>
the staff, those from recording and<br>
computation. Sighting errors can be a<br>
result of visual impairment or failure of<br>
a tool’s cross hair to coincide with the<br>
staff graduations. The later could be a<br>
result of either due to long sights or due<br>
to coarseness of the cross hairs and the<br>
staff. In some cases, natural changes<br>
of atmospheric conditions during the<br>
practice, such as the presence of mist<br>
or fog can impair visibility of the staff<br>
from the instrument man, hence errors.<br>
<b>This category of errors can be accidental</b><br>
and compensative.<br>
<b>Manipulation errors are mistakes or</b><br>
blunders as other surveyor’s call. In<br>
most cases, they originate from failed<br>
adherence to surveying ethics. They<br>
may be caused by careless setting up<br>
of a level; the presence of parallax (the<br>
imperfect focusing on eyepiece and<br>
objective) and  imperfect levelling of<br>
an instrument. Too much trust over<br>
your experiences or assumptions and<br>
instrument’s verticality cause an error of<br>
no-verticality of the staff. The absence<br>
of a staff bubble plate attached to a<br>
staff causes difficulty in holding of the<br>
staff verticality. Other equally defective<br>
causes for errors are incorrect reading,<br>
recording and computation of the staff<br>
reading.<br>
