<b>Maps</b><br>
<b>Maps are scaled representation of all or</b><br>
part of the earth on a flat surface such as<br>
sheet of paper or wood. It is a graphical<br>
representation of places by using points,<br>
lines, symbols and colors to show how<br>
selected cultural and natural features<br>
are located, arranged and related to one<br>
another. Maps can represent distributions<br>
and patterns of settlements, streets,<br>
transport routes, climate and location<br>
of human activities. A person who makes<br>
maps is called a cartographer.<br>
<b>Main characteristics of a map</b><br>
<b>Maps of whatever kinds have more or less</b><br>
common defining identities, acting as the<br>
characterising features which include the<br>
following: A typical map is represented<br>
to scale since the area on which it is<br>
drawn is much smaller compared to<br>
reality on the earth’s surface, which is<br>
much larger with a lot of features. That<br>
is why, every map is drawn to a certain<br>
scale, the size of which depends on the<br>
coverage intended by the cartographer.<br>
<b>Therefore, the amount of reduction by</b><br>
which the ground reality is reduced varies<br>
from one map to another, due to the use<br>
of different types of scales. Usually, the<br>
amount of reduction increases with an<br>
increase in the ground surface coverage<br>
intended to be mapped. Therefore, a map<br>
comprises  scale to show the extent or<br>
degree by which the ground reality has<br>
been reduced to suit the desired area of<br>
the material on which the map has been<br>
drawn.<br>
<b>The map uses signs and symbols. The</b><br>
surface of the earth, that a map represents,<br>
is of  a wide coverage and  consists of<br>
different features such as vegetation,<br>
water bodies, relief, and settlement<br>
patterns among others. Normally,<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Four</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
Map reading and interpretations are important skills in the world today. Geographers<br>
and non-geographers use the skills of reading and interpreting different types of<br>
maps. In this chapter, you will learn about different types of maps, measurements<br>
on topographical maps, presentation of relief on the maps and map interpretation.<br>
<b>The competencies acquired from this chapter will enable you to do some daily</b><br>
activities such as distance measurements, area calculation and decision making<br>
on socio-economic activities of an area with reference to the skills obtained from<br>
map and map interpretation.<br>
216<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
symbolic representation of these features<br>
is conventionally agreed. Different<br>
symbols are used depending on the<br>
nature of the features to be represented.<br>
<b>Common symbols used include point</b><br>
symbols, which stand for features that<br>
appear in point form, such as houses,<br>
line symbols representing features which<br>
appear in the form of lines such as roads<br>
and area symbols that stand for features<br>
of wide coverage such as swamps. All<br>
these symbols are defined in a key which<br>
guides meaningful map interpretation,<br>
since each symbol is purposely selected<br>
to stand for a particular real object or<br>
fact in the real ground surface.<br>
<b>Map is a projection. Maps are drawn from</b><br>
the mathematically transformed curved<br>
earth so as to present them on flat surface.<br>
<b>Therefore, maps are representation of</b><br>
the three-dimensional earth into the<br>
two dimensional flat surface. A map is<br>
a model of reality. It is a representation<br>
of certain geographical features that exist<br>
in real life.<br>
<b>A map is a generalisation of information</b><br>
and is selective. Since it cannot include<br>
everything found in the area,  some<br>
information is generalised.  The extent<br>
of generalisation depends on the<br>
number of factors including the scale,<br>
the purpose of the map and the interest<br>
of the cartographer. Generalisation<br>
normally increases with the coverage<br>
which is determined by the size of the<br>
scale. Symbols which stand for the<br>
real objects on the earth’s surface are<br>
generally selected. For instance, a dot<br>
may be used to represent houses which<br>
are not in the same size and quality.<br>
<b>In addition, a map is communicative</b><br>
as it conveys meaning of different<br>
kinds, depending on the nature of the<br>
area it represents and the purpose of<br>
its production.  With important map<br>
interpretation skills, even a person who<br>
is not familiar with the place on the map<br>
can extract or interpret the map and get<br>
the meaning represented by the map.<br>
A map is a source of geographical data.<br>
<b>It is difficult for human beings to store</b><br>
in mind different trends of events on the<br>
earth’s surface such as land cover and<br>
general spatial distribution of cultural<br>
and natural features. Therefore, the map<br>
is the best medium in which information<br>
is stored. Data from a map can be used<br>
in different fields such as pedology,<br>
engineering, demography, geology,<br>
hydrology and policy formulation for<br>
sustainable development. Map shows<br>
only a static situation. For example, the<br>
map of Babati produced 30 years ago<br>
would not show the current situation<br>
in the area, instead that of 30 years<br>
past.  However, due to changes caused<br>
by nature and the influence of human<br>
activities geographical areas also change.<br>
<b>Due to this it is important for the user</b><br>
of the map to consider the date of its<br>
production to get the most appropriate<br>
information<br>
<b>The value of maps to a geography</b><br>
student<br>
<b>Maps are widely used in human life and</b><br>
particularly in Geography as a discipline.<br>
<b>Maps act as the bases for description of</b><br>
the area depending on the nature of the<br>
fields in which they are applied. Since<br>
maps represent the earth’s surface, they<br>
217<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
portray the existing relationship between<br>
features such as geomorphology and<br>
hydrology.<br>
<b>Maps provide an outstanding base for</b><br>
spatial description of geographical<br>
phenomena. The knowledge and skills<br>
of map reading and interpretation are<br>
keys for understanding the geographical<br>
characteristics of the area in terms of<br>
relief, climate, drainage system, soil type<br>
and their relative significance to human<br>
life. The map orients the geography<br>
student to the knowledge of geographical<br>
skills including cartographical techniques<br>
related to map production and  preparing<br>
them to become the best cartographers<br>
in the future. Maps also enhance critical<br>
thinking of the students as it requires the<br>
integration of knowledge in interpreting<br>
and understanding a certain mapped area.<br>
<b>For instance, to understand clearly nature</b><br>
of drainage, a student has to integrate<br>
basic knowledge about geology,<br>
pedology and geomorphology.<br>
<b>Maps act as the mirror of past geographical</b><br>
events through which a student can see<br>
the mapped area today and detect some<br>
changes that have occurred overtime.<br>
<b>Maps provide much information</b><br>
about the nature and distribution of<br>
geographical phenomena such as<br>
settlement and settlement patterns. The<br>
use of maps enable the student to provide<br>
insight into the  existing relationships<br>
between  igneous rocks and tectonic<br>
activities, relief and drainage, climate<br>
and  vegetation, and the way these<br>
natural relationships influence peoples<br>
mode of life, particularly construction<br>
and distribution of human settlements.<br>
<b>Maps also provide a basis for studying</b><br>
geographical problems such as floods,<br>
storms and  deserts. Maps provide<br>
valuable data for statistical analysis such<br>
as population distribution and rainfall<br>
patterns. Researchers provide valuable<br>
information for research project with the<br>
help of maps.<br>
<b>Types of maps</b><br>
Maps are normally of different types.<br>
Despite the existing varieties of maps,<br>
each of them serves the purpose of<br>
its production in the intended area<br>
of specialisation. The following are<br>
different types of maps and their<br>
respective criteria for classification.<br>
(a) Basing on the function and content;<br>
maps are classified into topographical<br>
and statistical maps:<br>
(i)	 Topographical maps:  are maps<br>
designed to represent both cultural<br>
and natural features. The word<br>
topography  is derived from the Greek<br>
word “topos” which  means “place”.<br>
<b>Topographical maps are  drawings</b><br>
of a part of the earth’s surface.<br>
<b>These maps show location by using</b><br>
compass bearing, grid reference,<br>
latitudes, longitudes and names of<br>
places. They also show  cultural<br>
features  such as roads, railways,<br>
cities, town and dams; landscapes<br>
such as mountains, valley and<br>
plateaus; and drainage  like rivers,<br>
lakes and ocean. Therefore, through<br>
interpretation of  maps, someone may<br>
have a thorough understanding of<br>
an existing area even without their<br>
physical presence in the area.<br>
218<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Topographical maps are useful for</b><br>
describing features of the earth’s<br>
surfaces, planning the best uses of<br>
land and guiding people to reach their<br>
destinations. In general, development<br>
of physical infrastructures such as<br>
roads, agricultural projects and<br>
sustainable human settlement are<br>
all depending on the data from<br>
topographical maps.<br>
(ii)	Statistical (distribution) maps: are<br>
maps which represent  distribution<br>
of geographical phenomenon.<br>
<b>Geographers are also interested</b><br>
in careful investigation and<br>
visualisation of spatial distribution<br>
of different geographical events and<br>
phenomena particularly climatic<br>
elements like rainfall, temperature,<br>
atmospheric pressure, wind speed,<br>
sunshine intensity among others.<br>
Moreover, distribution of animals,<br>
agricultural activities and movement<br>
of goods are among the interests of<br>
geographers. Statistical maps are of<br>
different types such as dot maps,<br>
isoline maps, and Choropleth maps<br>
among others.  Statistical maps are<br>
also called quantitative maps as<br>
they communicate a message of<br>
magnitude. They show variation in<br>
value and quantity over a space.<br>
(b) Basing on degree of accuracy: maps<br>
can be classified as surveyed and sketch<br>
maps.<br>
<b>Surveyed maps: involve mathematical</b><br>
principles and theories in their<br>
production. Since the map represents<br>
the earth’s surface which is spherical in<br>
nature on the flat surface,  mathematical<br>
principles and theories are applied so<br>
as to transform a curved and spherical<br>
shape of the earth to a flat surface. This<br>
application gives us topographical maps.<br>
<b>Sketch maps: are types of maps which</b><br>
are roughly drawn with no mathematical<br>
basis like scale. They can be drawn even<br>
in other subject text books such as history<br>
to illustrate certain concepts or showing<br>
historical sites, in a particular country.<br>
(c) Basing on size of the scale, maps are<br>
classified as small scale, medium and<br>
large scale maps.<br>
<b>Small scale maps: are drawn by using</b><br>
a small scale to give a large coverage<br>
of the earth’s surface even the entire<br>
earth. These maps usually contain large<br>
quantity of features and are less detailed,<br>
depending on the extent of coverage.<br>
<b>Medium scale maps: are drawn by using</b><br>
a medium scale to provide a medium<br>
representation of the earth’s surface.<br>
<b>Large-scale maps: are drawn to a large</b><br>
scale to cover a small area of the earth’s<br>
surface, such as school or part of an urban<br>
area. These maps contain less quantity<br>
of features and are more detailed.<br>
<b>Contents of topographical maps</b><br>
<b>Topographical maps consist of three</b><br>
major kinds of contents which are<br>
natural contents, cultural contents and<br>
supportive contents. Natural contents<br>
are all features that are not man-made,<br>
they include features such as mountains,<br>
valleys, soil types, plateaus, natural<br>
vegetation like natural forest, water<br>
bodies like rivers, lakes, and oceans.<br>
<b>Cultural contents include all man-</b><br>
made features such as roads, railways,<br>
219<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
buildings, dams, artificial or planted<br>
forests and so on. The supportive contents<br>
include information of a map which are<br>
provided with the aim of assisting the<br>
reader of the map.<br>
<b>Supportive information</b><br>
<b>Due to the symbolic nature of maps, its</b><br>
correct and meaningful interpretation<br>
is impossible without supportive<br>
information. These are supportive<br>
contents of a map are written close but<br>
not limited to  the margin of the map.<br>
<b>They include title, boundary, North</b><br>
direction, scale and key or legend.<br>
Others include date of compilation,<br>
sheet number, publisher and copyright,<br>
grid reference, latitudes, longitudes and<br>
projection techniques. They are called<br>
supportive because they help the map<br>
user to interpret the map correctly.<br>
<b>Title</b><br>
<b>The title also known as the heading, is</b><br>
an element in a map that describes the<br>
theme or subject of a map.<br>
<b>The title sometimes appears as the name</b><br>
of the mapped area or as the combination<br>
of the name of the mapped area and the<br>
purpose intended to be shown. When<br>
it appears just as the name, it is called<br>
general title, for instance, “Songwe<br>
region map”. A map with this kind<br>
of a title can show a wide range of<br>
information. However, when it appears<br>
in combination with its purpose it is<br>
called specific title. For instance,<br>
“Songwe region relief features” normally<br>
intending to show specific information<br>
like relief features of Songwe region. A<br>
title is normally communicative to some<br>
extent as it provides some important<br>
clues on the nature of the mapped area<br>
in terms of economic activities, drainage,<br>
soil quality and climate in particular. The<br>
title always should provide an answer to<br>
what, where, and when.<br>
<b>Margin</b><br>
<b>Margin also known as boundary, is the</b><br>
frame that encloses the map to show the<br>
endings and the coverage of the area,<br>
often varying in size from one map<br>
to another. If the map covers a more<br>
extended area, its boundary is likely to<br>
be larger, than when it covers a small<br>
area. Usually, the map boundary has a<br>
uniform space in all sides from the map.<br>
<b>But there are maps which show only part</b><br>
of a place within its boundaries, usually,<br>
maps of this type do not have margin or<br>
boundaries.<br>
<b>Legend</b><br>
<b>Legend also known as a key, is a</b><br>
collection of symbols and signs used in<br>
the map and their respective meaning.<br>
<b>Though some symbols used in the map</b><br>
are self-explanatory, the key is actually<br>
intended to act as the map dictionary,<br>
to facilitate the correct and meaningful<br>
map interpretation through describing<br>
all unknown or unique symbols used<br>
on the map. Since map symbols are<br>
conventionally agreed, the key helps the<br>
map users to translate the map language<br>
into the ordinary language which can<br>
be simply understood. Map language is<br>
the system of conventional symbols and<br>
signs used to represent features of the<br>
mapped area. It also helps the map user to<br>
know the relationship between different<br>
220<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
features on the map. For instance, point<br>
(.) symbols that stand for settlements,<br>
always appear along the line symbol<br>
which stands for roads and rivers.<br>
<b>Map scale</b><br>
<b>Map scale is the ratio between distance</b><br>
on a map and the actual distance<br>
on the ground. Therefore, it shows<br>
representation of distance on the map<br>
by translating the value of distance on the<br>
ground. This simply means that the scale<br>
is the determinant factor of each feature<br>
that is represented in terms of size. Map<br>
scale is important in map making since it<br>
commands the coverage and amount of<br>
information to be included. It also helps<br>
the map users to make the interpretation<br>
of the real features they read on the map.<br>
<b>The scale also acts as the basis of map</b><br>
classification.<br>
<b>Direction indicator</b><br>
<b>A direction indicator is an arrow printed</b><br>
on a map to indicate the orientation of<br>
the map in reference to cardinals N-S-<br>
<b>E-W. The indicator determines the</b><br>
orientation of the map which facilitates<br>
interpretation of the map by locating the<br>
position of each feature. This is a North<br>
arrow (Figure 4.1 (a)-(e)). It shows<br>
the north direction of the map used<br>
to determine other directions, such as<br>
<b>South, West and East. It enables the map</b><br>
user to determine trend and alignment of<br>
features on the map, fix positions of other<br>
features by resection and intersection<br>
method. Other maps indicate direction<br>
by using a compass rose, with arrows<br>
pointing in all four cardinal directions.<br>
(a)<br>
(b)<br>
(c)	        (d)	       (e)<br>
<b>Figure 4.1: Examples of direction indicators</b><br>
commonly used on topographical maps<br>
<b>Date of compilation</b><br>
<b>It indicates the time when the map was</b><br>
published. This guides the readers to<br>
select map versions basing on their<br>
time barred needs. It is very useful in<br>
recognising some changes that might<br>
have occurred in the area within a given<br>
time interval. Normally, land cover such<br>
as vegetation, settlement and water<br>
sources keep on changing with time.<br>
<b>For instance, “Dodoma region map” of</b><br>
twenty years ago will be quite different<br>
from the current map.<br>
<b>Sheet number</b><br>
<b>Maps sharing the same series are</b><br>
distinguished by the sheet number that<br>
shows what geographical area is covered<br>
by the map. It means that maps of the<br>
same series have different sheet numbers<br>
for different areas. Sheet number also<br>
shows how many times a given map has<br>
been up-dated.<br>
<b>Publisher and copyright owner</b><br>
<b>Map indicates the owner of the particular</b><br>
map and the publishing company. The<br>
author or publisher of a map is indicated,<br>
so as to be familiar with the cartographer<br>
to easily get useful clues on information<br>
regarding the extent to which the map<br>
221<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
is biased or reliable. This will be useful<br>
in drawing attention on questions such<br>
as “Does the map maker or organization<br>
have vested interest in how the map is<br>
perceived by the map reader?”<br>
<b>Grid references</b><br>
<b>Grid references refer to the patterns of</b><br>
equally spaced vertical and horizontal<br>
lines that are perpendicularly intersecting<br>
to each other forming squares. It is the<br>
geographical coordinate system by<br>
which different parts and features on<br>
the map are located. The vertical lines are<br>
called eastings as their number increase<br>
eastwards, while the horizontal lines<br>
are called northings  as their numbers<br>
increase northwards. In reading the grid<br>
reference we start with the eastings and<br>
finish with the northings. It is normally<br>
determined by the type of projection<br>
used in the preparation of a particular<br>
map. Apart from locating the position<br>
of different features, the patterns of<br>
squares, are used in determining the<br>
area of features by grid square method.<br>
<b>Latitudes and longitudes</b><br>
<b>These are the geographical coordinate</b><br>
systems by which positions of the<br>
mapped area are determined. Therefore,<br>
by using latitudes and longitudes,<br>
the angular distance of the mapped<br>
area from the equator and the prime<br>
meridian respectively can be identified.<br>
<b>Additionally, latitudes are important</b><br>
elements of climate, drainage and<br>
vegetation interpretation.<br>
<b>Factors that influence the contents of</b><br>
topographical maps<br>
<b>Amount and the nature of the map</b><br>
content are a combined function of a<br>
number of factors. Maps are drawn by<br>
different cartographers, with different<br>
objectives using different scales to<br>
represent the earth’s surface that differ<br>
in terms of nature and amount of the land<br>
cover. The following factors determine<br>
the content of topographical map.<br>
<b>Scale of the map</b><br>
<b>The choice of a scale depends on the</b><br>
size of the area represented, under this,<br>
the emphasis is put in three cartographic<br>
choices of a map scale namely large,<br>
small and medium scale. For example,<br>
small scale maps are less detailed.<br>
<b>Therefore, maps of the same area</b><br>
produced in the same period of time,<br>
by the same cartographer are likely to<br>
differ in their content, if their scales are<br>
not the same.<br>
<b>Purpose of a map</b><br>
<b>Any type of a map is made by a</b><br>
cartographer based on a certain aim.<br>
<b>In this regard, not all information will</b><br>
be depicted on the maps.  The aim<br>
of the cartographer is to construct or<br>
make a map and determine what need<br>
to be shown in a given map. Therefore,<br>
the content that appears on the map<br>
is determined by desired purpose.<br>
<b>If the purpose of the map is to show</b><br>
vegetation distribution, other features<br>
like settlements will automatically be<br>
excluded on the map.<br>
222<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Date of compilation</b><br>
<b>The date shows a period of time at which</b><br>
a map was produced or published.  The<br>
information that exists on earth surface<br>
is not static, it changes over a period<br>
of time. Due to this fact, a map shows<br>
information which was represented by<br>
the time it was produced.  Therefore, the<br>
maps of the same area drawn at different<br>
periods of time possibly differ in terms of<br>
contents.  For example, the map of Dar<br>
es Salaam drawn in 1960’s is definitely<br>
quite different from the map of Dar es<br>
salaam drawn in 2022.<br>
<b>Nature of the land</b><br>
<b>Normally the surface of the earth that a</b><br>
map represents is not uniformly covered<br>
by features. It is covered by features that<br>
differ in terms of amount and nature.<br>
<b>Some parts of the earth’s surface are</b><br>
covered by water bodies like oceans and<br>
lakes while others are covered by forests<br>
and settlements. Therefore, the map<br>
shows what is found in the area including<br>
the differences such as physical features<br>
found in a given geographical area. It<br>
can then be concluded that maps are<br>
mirror images of the real surfaces they<br>
represent.<br>
<b>Level of technology</b><br>
<b>The maps drawn by using modern</b><br>
technology like computer, digital<br>
camera, and satellite image, contain<br>
more features than those produced by<br>
conventional methods such as by using<br>
a hand.<br>
<b>Seasons of the year</b><br>
<b>Map of the same area drawn at different</b><br>
seasons of the year will depict different<br>
information. Almost all places do not<br>
look the same throughout the four<br>
seasons of the year.<br>
<b>Map projection</b><br>
<b>Map projection refers to the mathematical</b><br>
transformation of the three-dimensional<br>
spherical earth into two-dimensional<br>
flat surface. Normally, the process<br>
is associated with some distortion in<br>
area, distance and angles, however the<br>
amount of distortion varies depending<br>
on the type of projection involved. Even<br>
though map projection is associated with<br>
such problems, still it is important in<br>
map production, because it enables the<br>
representation of a three-dimensional<br>
curved earth on a flat surface. Therefore,<br>
a cartographer is supposed to select a<br>
projection technique with minimal and<br>
tolerable distortions. Projection can be<br>
grouped into three geometrical shapes<br>
namely cylindrical projection, conic<br>
projection and azimuthal projection.<br>
<b>Cylindrical projection</b><br>
<b>Cylindrical projection is also known</b><br>
as Mercator projection. In this type of<br>
projection, the surface of the globe is<br>
wrapped on the surface of a cylinder, in<br>
order to transfer the details of the earth<br>
to the surrounding cylindrical surface.<br>
<b>The details are traced on the surface</b><br>
and rolled out to give a flat surface map<br>
(Figure 4.2 (a) and (b)). This type of<br>
projection has maximum distortion in the<br>
polar areas than in other parts because<br>
of the nature of the earth’s shape. Areas<br>
which are more curved are likely to be<br>
highly distorted.<br>
223<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Conic projection</b><br>
<b>This is a method of projecting maps</b><br>
of the earth’s spherical surface on the<br>
surrounding cone which is then flattened<br>
to plane surface having concentric circles<br>
as parallels of latitudes and radiating<br>
lines from the apex as meridians. The<br>
method works best over mid latitudes<br>
(Figure 4.3 (a) &amp; (b)). The cone touches<br>
the globe at all points on a single parallel<br>
of latitude and the resulting map is<br>
extremely accurate for all areas near<br>
the parallel but becomes extremely<br>
distorted with distance from it.  The<br>
surface of the globe is projected onto a<br>
cone, which rests on top of it, resulting<br>
to  a flat map with a  shape of a fannel.<br>
<b>Since the globe only touches the cone at</b><br>
a single line of latitude, this projection is<br>
best used in smaller areas of the world,<br>
such as countries in the temperate<br>
regions including USA and Canada. This<br>
method has less distortion in the middle<br>
latitudes, however, distortion increases<br>
as the distance increases away from the<br>
middle latitude.<br>

<b>Figure 4.3 (a) and (b): Conical projection technique</b><br>
(a)                                                             (b)<br>
<b>Figure 4. 2 (a) and (b): Cylindrical projection technique</b><br>

<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Azimuthal projection</b><br>
<b>Azimuthal projection is also called</b><br>
plane, or Zenithal projection. This is<br>
a map projection in which a globe is<br>
assumed to rest on a flat surface whose<br>
features are projected. In this method,<br>
the global surface is at one point and<br>
the scale is only accurate at this point.<br>
<b>Azimuthal projection is good for map</b><br>
focusing on a hemisphere continent or<br>
the poles.  When used to show a large<br>
area great distortions occur at the edge.<br>
<b>It is common projections when mapping</b><br>
the Arctic and Antarctic regions (Figure<br>
<b>4.4 (a) and (b)).</b><br>

<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Map scale and its importance</b><br>
<b>Map scale</b><br>
<b>Map scale is the relationship or a</b><br>
ratio between the distance measured<br>
on the map and its corresponding<br>
actual distance on the earth’s surface<br>
(ground). The earth’s surface is much<br>
larger than the paper on which a map is<br>
drawn. This necessitates reduction of<br>
such wide earth’s surface, to the extent<br>
which depends on the intended coverage.<br>
<b>Therefore, a scale shows the extent</b><br>
which a given area has been reduced<br>
to fit a particular size of the paper. As<br>
a reflection of the reduced degree, map<br>
scale can be small, large or medium.<br>
<b>Map scale is generally very important</b><br>
to the map maker as it determines the<br>
content and the coverage of the area<br>
being mapped.   On the other hand, map<br>
scale is important to the map user as it<br>
is a tool for interpretation.<br>
<b>In fact, the map scale tells the reader</b><br>
how the map relates to the real world<br>
features it represents. To represent<br>
the earth’s surface on a map requires<br>
sufficient adjustment of the scale to<br>
cover the desired objective. The extent<br>
of reduction is expressed as a ratio<br>
or fraction in which the numerator<br>
represents the distance on the map<br>
while the denominator represents the<br>
corresponding ground distance. The<br>
larger the denominator the smaller the<br>
scale and the smaller the denominator<br>
the larger the scale.<br>
<b>A scale can be expressed as</b><br>
<b>Map scale</b><br>
<b>Mapdistance</b><br>
= Ground distance<br>
<b>Types of map scale</b><br>
<b>The map scale is broadly classified into</b><br>
three types namely; small medium and<br>
large scale (Table 4.1).The selection of<br>
the type of map scale is guided by the<br>
following factors namely; the size of the<br>
area to be represented, the size of space<br>
to represent and amount of details to be<br>
shown on a particular map.  If the area<br>
to be mapped is large, the small scale is<br>
selected but if the area is small the large<br>
scale is chosen (Table 4.2).<br>

<b>Scale units in RF</b><br>

<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Small scale map</b><br>
<b>Small scales are scales ranging from</b><br>
1:250 000 to 1:1 000 000 which are<br>
used when much detail is not required.<br>
<b>The ratio of these scales has the largest</b><br>
denominator indicating the high<br>
reduction of the mapped area which is<br>
reflected on the size of the resultant. A<br>
small scale map contains large quantity<br>
of the content of the covered area. The<br>
map covers a large area such as country<br>
or a continent or the whole world.<br>
<b>Features are greatly reduced and appear</b><br>
very small.  In small scale maps the ratio<br>
has a large number or large denominator.<br>
<b>The bigger the denominator the smaller</b><br>
the scale.<br>
<b>Medium scale map</b><br>
<b>Medium scale ranges from 1: 50 000; to</b><br>
1: 1 240 000 used for maps of medium<br>
sized areas. They are scales that represent<br>
areas which are neither too large nor<br>
too small.  The maps show moderate<br>
contents as the features on the ground<br>
are relatively reduced.  The ratio has a<br>
moderate denominator<br>
<b>Large scale map</b><br>
<b>Large scales have scales ranging from</b><br>
1:5 000 to 1:25 000 and are used when we<br>
want to represent higher levels of detail.<br>
<b>The map using scale of this type shows</b><br>
detailed information as everything can<br>
be seen clearly because of the minimal<br>
reduction. This scale however, contains<br>
little content due to small area covered.<br>
<b>This scale is used to represent areas like</b><br>
schools or hospital compounds and small<br>
scale villages.<br>
Table 4.2: Type of scale surface,<br>
coverage and details<br>
<b>Type of scale</b><br>
<b>Surface</b><br>
area<br>
<b>Detail</b><br>
<b>Small scale map</b><br>
<b>Large</b><br>
<b>Little</b><br>
<b>Medium scale map</b><br>
<b>Medium</b><br>
moderate<br>
<b>Large scale map</b><br>
<b>Small</b><br>
<b>Large</b><br>
<b>Factors determining size of the scale on</b><br>
a map<br>
<b>The content, area represented and the</b><br>
size of the map are the major factors<br>
which determine the size of scale on<br>
a map. The size of scale on the map<br>
depends on the amount of contents to<br>
be shown on a map. For example, large<br>
scale map is more detailed than others.<br>
<b>The scale can also be determined by the</b><br>
area represented. For example, large area<br>
like continent or the whole world can be<br>
shown by using small scale while a map<br>
of a certain school compound uses large<br>
scale. However, the scale may differ in<br>
relation to the size of map, because maps<br>
of different sizes differ in scale though<br>
they can represent the same ground area.<br>
<b>Ways of expressing scale</b><br>
<b>A scale can be expressed in three</b><br>
different ways namely representative<br>
fraction, statement, and linear scale.<br>
<b>Representative Fraction</b><br>
<b>Representative fraction (RF) scale is also</b><br>
known as ratio scale. RF is a form of scale<br>
which is expressed in ratio and fraction<br>
in which the numerator represents the<br>
map distance while the denominator<br>
228<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
represents the ground distance. So, the<br>
<b>RF 1:10 000 means 1 unit on the map</b><br>
represents 10 000 units on the ground.<br>
<b>The size of scale in this form can</b><br>
simply be determined by the size of the<br>
denominator. The scale indicates how<br>
many units on the earth’s surface are<br>
represented by one unit on the map. It<br>
can either be expressed as<br>
1<br>
100 000<br>
or 1:100 000. With regard to the given<br>
example here, it means one centimeter on<br>
the map represents 100 000 centimeters<br>
(1 kilometer) on the ground. Other<br>
common RF scale include 1:63 360<br>
(1 inch to 1 mile) and 1:1 000 000<br>
(1 cm to 10 km). The numerator of a<br>
representative fraction is always 1.<br>
<b>Statement scale</b><br>
<b>It is a written description of a scale, such</b><br>
as ‘One centimetre on the map represents<br>
one kilometre on the ground’ or ‘One<br>
centimetre to ten kilometres.’ Based on<br>
these two statements, the first map would<br>
show much more detail than the second<br>
because one centimetre on the first map<br>
covers a much smaller area than the<br>
second map. It should be noted that both<br>
<b>RF and statement scales are ineffective</b><br>
particularly for a map produced through<br>
photocopying and when the size of the<br>
map is modified. Under this circumstance<br>
there is a possibility of a mismatch of<br>
distances in the produced map and the<br>
original map. This can however, be<br>
addressed using a linear or graphic scale.<br>
<b>Linear scale</b><br>
<b>Linear scale also called line scale, bar</b><br>
scale, plain or graphical scale  is the way<br>
of expressing the scale by the use of line<br>
representing the distance on the ground<br>
(Figure 4.5). It is commonly placed at<br>
the bottom of the map. It consists of two<br>
main parts, the primary section on the<br>
right side with units in kilometres and<br>
the secondary section on the left sidewith<br>
units in metres. It helps the map user to<br>
determine quickly the ground distance<br>
of two points on the map.<br>
<b>However, the scale cannot be directly</b><br>
used to calculate area, slope, and vertical<br>
exaggeration and in redrawing the map<br>
instead demands some skills to prepare<br>
it. One of the major advantages of the<br>
linear scale is that it is not affected by<br>
photocopying.<br>
<b>Figure 4.5: A simple linear scale</b><br>
229<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
Also it is easy to understand. Similarly, it allows direct linear measurement.<br>
However, its construction requires time, special skills and experience. Furthermore,<br>
the scale can be used by those who are familiar with the units of measurement<br>
used in the linear scale.<br>
<b>Scale conversion</b><br>
Dealing with scale is possible to change one form of scale and express it into another<br>
form. For example, from representative fraction scale to statement scale, from<br>
representative fraction scale to linear scale, from statement scale to representative<br>
fraction scale, from statement scale to linear scale, from linear scale to representative<br>
fraction scale, and from linear scale to statement scale.<br>
<b>Example 1:</b><br>
Change the statement form of scale to representative fraction scale given that, one<br>
centimetre on the map represents half a kilometer on the ground.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>

1km = 100 000 cm<br>

1km<br>
= 50 000 cm<br>

<b>How to construct a linear scale</b><br>
<b>The construction of a linear scale can be</b><br>
divided into two ways:<br>
(a) 	Drawing of linear scale<br>
<b>Linear scale can be drawn into forms</b><br>
which are simple or plain or normal scale<br>
and as graphic scale.<br>
<b>Procedures of constructing linear scale</b><br>
are basically the same though graphic<br>
scale will involve some more additional<br>
steps. The following are the procedures<br>
to be considered when constructing a<br>
graphic scale:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Determine the scale given that</b><br>
such as 1: 50 000<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Convert scale into statement</b><br>
scale. For example, 1:50 000<br>
becomes 1cm to 0.5 km<br>
(iii)	 Use statement scale to find length<br>
of scale or length of baseline (If<br>
not guided, choose any reasonable<br>
length of baseline or length of<br>
scale, for instance 12 cm).<br>

(v)<br>
<b>Draw a baseline and divide it</b><br>
into primary and secondary<br>
sections.<br>
(vi)	 Draw perpendicular auxiliary<br>
lines at the starting and the<br>
ending point of the the<br>
secondary section, downwards<br>
and upwards, respectively<br>
from the baseline with ten<br>
division.<br>
(vii)	 Join the points of perpendicular<br>
auxiliary lines to divide<br>
secondary section into ten<br>
equal parts.<br>
(viii)	Draw oblique auxiliary lines<br>
at both ends of baseline. These<br>
auxiliary lines should be<br>
drawn to equal angle between<br>
25° to 30° from the baseline.<br>
<b>Auxiliary lines should have</b><br>
length which can be easily<br>
divided to required sections.<br>
(ix)	 Join the points of auxiliary<br>
lines to divide the baseline in<br>
equal divisions.<br>
(x)<br>
<b>Trace a clear linear scale at</b><br>
the bottom of drawing.<br>

<b>Measurements on topographical</b><br>
maps<br>
<b>With the map scale, the size and length of</b><br>
both natural and man-made features can<br>
accurately be transferred from the map<br>
to the actual ground. It is also possible<br>
to know the extent of steepness on the<br>
real ground surface. The method used<br>
in measurement of the length and size,<br>
depends on the nature of the feature<br>
in terms of shape and straightness.<br>
<b>Among the common measurements in</b><br>
topographical maps include distance<br>
and area.<br>
<b>Distance measurements</b><br>
<b>Measurement of distances on maps</b><br>
involves features that occur in linear<br>
forms such as roads, coastline, rivers<br>
and railway lines. It can also involve<br>
two points that are not connected by<br>
any elongated feature. Basing on such a<br>
concept, distance measured on the map<br>
are of two types namely; straight and<br>
curved or winding distances.<br>
<b>Straight distance</b><br>
Measuring the distance of this kind,<br>
the line is drawn to connect the<br>
identified points, then the distance is<br>
directly measured by the ruler and the<br>
actual distance is obtained with the<br>
aid of the linear scale, or by using the<br>
representative fraction scale. This kind<br>
of distance is much less common on<br>
elongated features.<br>
<b>Winding/curved distances</b><br>
<b>This is very common in the elongated</b><br>
features such as road, railway and<br>
pipeline. The methods for measuring<br>
distance of curved feature includes the<br>
following: a pair of dividers, a string and<br>
a straight edge of the paper.<br>
<b>A pair of dividers</b><br>
<b>It is commonly used to measure short</b><br>
straight distances between two points<br>
on a map.<br>
<b>Procedures:</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the starting and</b><br>
the ending points where<br>
measurements are to be taken.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Divide the feature under</b><br>
measurement in straight units.<br>
237<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
(iii)	 By means of a pair of dividers,<br>
transfer each unit length on<br>
a straight paper to form the<br>
total length.<br>
(iv)	 Change the map distance<br>
into ground distance using<br>
linear scale or representative<br>
fraction scale.<br>
<b>Map scale 1:100 000</b><br>
<b>Figure 4. 9: Marking of distance on linear</b><br>
scale using a pair of dividers.<br>
<b>From Figure 4.9, let the measured</b><br>
distance in the map be 25 cm, which<br>
equals to 25 km by using linear scale.<br>
<b>By Representative Fraction scale, the</b><br>
distance from the map can be converted<br>
into the actual ground distance as<br>
follows:<br>
<b>Data given</b><br>
<b>Map distance  =  25 cm</b><br>
<b>Map scale  =  1:100 000</b><br>
Ground distance   =  ?<br>
<b>Constant 1 km  =  100 000 cm</b><br>
From the data given,<br>
1 km  =  100 000 cm<br>
? = 100 000 cm<br>
= 1km ×100 000 cm<br>
100 000 cm<br>
=  1 km<br>
1 cm→1 km<br>
25 cm→?<br>
= 25cm ×1km<br>
1cm<br>
=  25 km<br>
<b>Therefore, ground distance between two</b><br>
points   =  25 km<br>
<b>String or thread</b><br>
<b>A piece of a thread can be used to</b><br>
measure curved distances on a map using<br>
the following procedures.<br>
<b>Procedures:</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the two end points you</b><br>
want to take measurements.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Lay a piece of a string along</b><br>
the points.<br>
(iii)	 Shift the string onto the linear<br>
scale or ruler to read the actual<br>
distance (using a linear scale)<br>
or converting the map distance<br>
to actual distance using a ruler.<br>
238<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>

<b>Determine the ground distance of the</b><br>
curved line by using thread method<br>

<b>Figure 4.10: Measuring distance by using a</b><br>

<b>Straight edge of a paper</b><br>
<b>A piece of paper with a straight edge</b><br>
can be used to measure curved distances<br>
(Figure 4.11).<br>
<b>Procedures:</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the two end points</b><br>
of the feature you want to<br>
measure.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Divide the distance into small</b><br>
short straight distances.<br>
(iii)	 Take a straight edge of paper<br>
and lay it on the map and<br>
measure all portion of straight<br>
distances along the features<br>
being measured.<br>
(iv)	 Shift the piece of paper onto<br>
the linear scale or ruler to get<br>
the ground distance.<br>

<b>Area measurements</b><br>
<b>In topographical maps, area of natural</b><br>
features such as swamps and cultural<br>
features such as race truck can be actually<br>
determined on the ground.  The method<br>
applied in determining the area depends<br>
on the shape of the particular feature.<br>
<b>Actually, some features are regularly</b><br>
shaped while others are irregularly<br>
shaped. It is the interest of the map<br>
user to determine the area coverage of<br>
features for the social, economic and<br>
cultural purposes.<br>
<b>Regular shaped areas</b><br>
<b>The area of regular shaped features</b><br>
such as triangle, rectangle, trapezium<br>
and circle on the topographical maps<br>
can be simply determined by applying<br>
mathematical formula depending on<br>
the shape of that feature. For instance,<br>
to calculate the area of a rectangle,<br>
multiply its length by width. Remember,<br>
if you want to determine area in ground<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
measurements, use the map scale to convert length and width from map into<br>
ground distances.<br>
Examples of formula for calculating areas of different shapes are shown in Figure<br>
4.12.<br>

<b>Irregular shaped areas</b><br>
<b>Irregular shapes on topographic maps</b><br>
include swamps, lakes, forest and alike.<br>
<b>The area of such feature is obtained by</b><br>
using various methods such as grid<br>
method or square method  or tracing<br>
method, strip method, graph paper<br>
method, and geometrical figures method.<br>
<b>Grid method</b><br>
<b>This is the method which is more accurate</b><br>
for calculating areas of irregular features.<br>
<b>The shape of the land which has to be</b><br>
calculated is covered by complete and<br>
incomplete units (squares).<br>

<b>The strip method</b><br>
<b>The strip method is used to find the</b><br>
area of irregular shaped features which<br>
involves the division of the feature into<br>
strips of equal width. The area of the<br>
feature is the sum of all strips.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>The required area is divided into</b><br>
strips of equal width.<br>
(ii)	 Calculate the area of each strip<br>
(that is, strip 1-5) by the formula<br>
length x width.<br>
(iii)	 The ends of the strips are drawn<br>
across the area boundary to balance<br>
areas left outside the boundary.<br>
(iv)	 Use the map scale to obtain the<br>
actual area.<br>
<b>Example 1</b><br>
<b>Find the area of the feature in Figure</b><br>
<b>4.16 by using the strip method. Use 1:</b><br>
25 000 as your map scale.<br>

by 5cm, strip 4, 1cm by 6 cm and strip<br>
5, 1cm by 5 cm.<br>

<b>The geometrical figures method</b><br>
This is also called division method, it involves the division of the feature into<br>
several geometrical figures such as triangle, rectangle, and square depending<br>
on the shape of the feature itself. The area of the whole feature is the sum of all<br>
individual figures obtained geometrical method. Using the area of the feature can<br>
be calculated as follows;<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Divide the area into convenient figures such as A, B, C, D and E</b><br>
(ii)<br>
<b>By using appropriate methods, calculate area of each figure separately</b><br>
such as, A, B, C, D and E<br>
(iii)	 Determine the area by adding  the area of A, B, C, D and E<br>
(iv)	 Use the map scale to get the actual area on the ground.<br>

<b>Map enlargement and reduction</b><br>
<b>Map enlargement and reduction can be</b><br>
described as a cartographical technique<br>
of increasing or decreasing the size of a<br>
map in a given area according to scale.<br>
<b>Map reduction and enlargement (re-</b><br>
drawing the map) is governed by two<br>
mathematical principles. Firstly, when<br>
the small scale map is redrawn by using<br>
a large scale, its size becomes large,<br>
and the process is referred to as map<br>
enlargement. Secondly, when the large<br>
scale map is redrawn by using a small<br>
scale, its size becomes smaller as well,<br>
and the process is referred to as map<br>
reduction. Both of them involve the same<br>
redrawing procedures as follows;<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)<br>
<b>Study the map or required area</b><br>
of the map to be redrawn.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Identify the map scale and</b><br>
covert it into statement.<br>
(iii)	 Determine changing factor/<br>
scale factor<br>
<b>Scale factor = New scale</b><br>
<b>Old scale</b><br>
(iv)	 Measure the length, width<br>
and side of the grid square.<br>
But if the distances are given,<br>
convert the map distance into<br>
ground distance.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Using the given map scale for</b><br>
re-drawing, convert or change<br>
the old map dimensions into<br>
new map dimensions, by<br>
using the scale factor.<br>
(vi)	 Redraw the map and if<br>
the original map had grid<br>
references then indicate them<br>
in the new map.<br>
(vii)	 Show main features and<br>
remember  to show the title,<br>
key and the scale.<br>
<b>Example 1</b><br>
<b>Re-draw the map provided below by</b><br>
using the scale of 1:100 000<br>

<b>Determining locations and directions</b><br>
of places<br>
<b>Location, direction and distance are</b><br>
everyday ways of assessing the space<br>
around us and identifying our position<br>
in relation to other features and places<br>
of interest.  They are also very basic in<br>
understanding the processes of spatial<br>
interaction that are important in the study<br>
of both physical and human geography.<br>
<b>Location: Location can be classified in</b><br>
two different ways that are absolute and<br>
relative locations.<br>
<b>Absolute location</b><br>
<b>Absolute location also known</b><br>
as mathematical location, is the<br>
identification of place by some precise<br>
and accepted system of coordinates.<br>
<b>There are several such accepted systems</b><br>
of pinpointing positions, for example<br>
251<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
global grid of parallels and meridians<br>
that is latitude and longitude.  Absolute<br>
location is unique to a specific place<br>
and it is independent of any other<br>
characteristics or observation.<br>
<b>Geographers remark location matters a</b><br>
lot although their reference is usually not<br>
absolute but relative. Location that is the<br>
position of a place or things in relation<br>
to that of other place or things expresses<br>
spatial interconnection.<br>
<b>Relative location</b><br>
Relative location tells us that people,<br>
things and places exist in the world of<br>
physical and cultural characteristics<br>
that differ from one place to another.<br>
<b>In reality when geographers talk of</b><br>
location, they refer to the physical and<br>
cultural characteristics like climate, soil,<br>
minerals, and attributes of the place<br>
itself.<br>
<b>Direction</b><br>
<b>This is another important aspect in</b><br>
geography.  It is also expressed in<br>
absolute or relative terms.  Absolute<br>
direction is based on the cardinal points<br>
of North, South, East and West. Relative<br>
direction refers to “far West” or far East.<br>
<b>Distance links location and</b><br>
directions<br>
<b>Distance is also divided into absolute and</b><br>
relative sense.  Absolute distance refers<br>
to the spatial separations between two<br>
points on the earth’s surface, measured<br>
by some accepted standard unit such as<br>
miles or kilometers. Relative distance<br>
transforms those linear measurements<br>
into other units more meaningful for<br>
spatial relationship.<br>
<b>Fixing or locating position on maps</b><br>
<b>Fixing or locating position on maps is</b><br>
an important aspect in map reading. A<br>
person is able to identify a place after<br>
position has been fixed and located. The<br>
position and direction of the place on<br>
the earth’s surface and on maps can be<br>
expressed in various ways such as use<br>
of place names, latitudes and longitudes,<br>
grid reference systems and bearing.<br>
<b>Place names</b><br>
<b>The place in the topographical maps</b><br>
are identified and located by the name<br>
of the particular area. For example, in<br>
the map of Tanzania, different regions<br>
can be identified by their names such<br>
as, Arusha, Dodoma, Pemba and Iringa<br>
(Figure 4.22).<br>
<b>Latitude and longitude</b><br>
<b>These are West-East and North-South</b><br>
angular distances on the earth’s surface.<br>
<b>They are the traditional and mostly used</b><br>
geographical ways  in locating position<br>
of features on the earth’s surface. Most<br>
topographical maps indicate latitude and<br>
longitude along their edges, so as to give<br>
their respective location on the earth’s<br>
surface although not all maps show these<br>
divisions in detail. Therefore, by means<br>
of these lines, one can identify the exact<br>
location of a feature on the mapped area.<br>
In reading the latitude and longitude,<br>
start with latitudes then longitudes.<br>
<b>For example, Dare es Salaam region is</b><br>
located at 6o 48'S and 39o 12'E. Latitude<br>
and longitude in maps are important for<br>
locating places precisely whereby two<br>
reference lines are needed and adopted<br>
on the uniform basis by all countries in<br>
252<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
order to avoid confusion. Using latitude and longitude, we can know how far an<br>
area is from the equator and prime meridian respectively. Also, latitudes are very<br>
important in characterizing the climate of the mapped area.<br>
<b>Figure 4.22: A map of Tanzania locating position by place naming</b><br>
<b>Grid reference system</b><br>
A grid system is a pattern of horizontal and vertical lines of uniform sizes which are<br>
drawn on a map. The vertical lines are called eastings since their values increases<br>
eastward from the grid origin while horizontal lines are called northings since<br>
their values increases northwards. Grid reference system depends on the type of<br>
projection used to prepare the map. For example, in Africa the grid systems are<br>
based on the Universal Transverse Mercator (UTM) projection which divides the<br>
continent into zones. Within each zone, coordinates are measured as northings and<br>
eastings values in meters. These lines are essential for fixing position. The reading<br>
in a grid system is referred as grid references and is provided in a six-digits in<br>
253<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
which the first three digits are eastings and the last three digits are northings. For<br>
example, the position of point A in Figure 4.23 is 280610.<br>
<b>Figure 4.23: Locating position by grid reference</b><br>
<b>Bearing and direction</b><br>
<b>This is another way of fixing position or showing geographical position on the</b><br>
map. The compass bearing can be measured by using a compass. A compass is an<br>
instrument used to find direction and bearing. It consists of free-swinging magnetic<br>
needle which can pivot to align itself with magnetic north. If the local variation<br>
between magnetic north and the true north is known then direction of magnetic<br>
north gives the direction of true north. Compass bearing can be explained along<br>
the points such as compass direction, bearing of compass, direction of a place<br>
(trend and alignment) and North direction.<br>
Compass directions are measured from North 000o to 360o of a circle clockwise.<br>
It is the bearing which determines the direction. For example, when the measured<br>
bearing is 45°, then the direction is North East, when the bearing is 90°, then its<br>
254<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
direction is East. The directions are divided into three categories which are the<br>
four cardinal points, the eight cardinal points and the sixteen cardinal points as<br>
shown in Figure 4.24.<br>

<b>Bearing of compass shows the direction</b><br>
of point with respect to another point<br>
measured clockwise from 000º to 360º.<br>
<b>Bearing is expressed in degrees, which</b><br>
are further subdivided into minutes and<br>
seconds.<br>
<b>Calculation of bearing</b><br>
<b>There are two types of bearings namely:</b><br>
(a)	 Forward bearing (FB).<br>
(b)	 Back bearing (BB).<br>
<b>Forward bearing (FB)</b><br>
<b>This is the degree measured from an</b><br>
observer to the object along the line of<br>
sight or the degree reading to an object<br>
in front of the observer along the line<br>
of sight, with reference to the earth’s<br>
magnetic north pole. An observer can<br>
check FB’s accuracy by taking BB from<br>
the object to his former position.<br>
<b>Procedures of measuring bearing</b><br>
(i)<br>
Identify the two points.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Join the two points with a</b><br>
straight-line. For example<br>
from grid reference A to grid<br>
reference B (Figure 4.25).<br>
(iii)	 At one point either A or B<br>
(depending on the question<br>
asked) draw a line parallel<br>
to the North direction or grid<br>
vertical lines.<br>
(iv)	 Using a protractor, measure the<br>
angle or bearing at point A or<br>
B depending on the question.<br>
255<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
060°<br>
240°<br>

<b>B</b><br>
<b>Bearing of B from A = 060°</b><br>
<b>Bearing of A from B = 240°</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.25: Forward and back bearing</b><br>
<b>Back bearing (BB)</b><br>
<b>This is the degree measured from an</b><br>
object to the observer along the line of<br>
sight. The rule is that, if the FB is greater<br>
than 180º then subtract 180º from the FB<br>
to obtain BB. This implies that BB = FB<br>
-180º. If the FB is less than 180º then<br>
add 180º to the forward bearing to get<br>
BB. This implies that BB = FB + 180º.<br>
<b>The parallel lines and angle transverse</b><br>
<b>Forward and back bearing from one</b><br>
point to the next on the given map can<br>
be revealed by the application of parallel<br>
lines and angles of transverse (Figure<br>
4.26). This rule provides a map-reader<br>
with pairs of corresponding angle and<br>
pairs of vertical opposite.<br>

<b>The significance of backward bearing</b><br>
<b>Backward bearing is useful for checking</b><br>
the accuracy of forward bearing reading<br>
as taken from the observer to the object<br>
along the line of sight. The accuracy<br>
is normally checked by identifying<br>
the difference in degrees between the<br>
forward and backward bearing. The<br>
difference between backward bearing<br>
and forward  bearing is normally 180º.<br>
<b>If the difference appears to be less or</b><br>
greater than 180°, it shows that forward,<br>
backward or both bearings are not correct.<br>
<b>It indicates the  presence of errors which</b><br>
may be caused by different factors during<br>
surveying. The errors are corrected by<br>
mean error method as shown below.<br>
<b>Example 1</b><br>
<b>A prismatic compass surveyor recorded</b><br>
forward bearing as 68o and its back<br>
bearing 250o. Correct the discrepancy<br>
of these readings<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Data given</b><br>
<b>FB0  = 68°</b><br>
<b>BB0 = 250°</b><br>
256<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)  Find the difference between BB<br>
and FB<br>
250°− 68° = 182°<br>
<b>Difference = 182°</b><br>
The difference (D) is above 180°,<br>
implying presence of a positive<br>
error.<br>
(ii) Determine amount of errors<br>
<b>Amount of error = Difference − 180°</b><br>
182° − 180° = 2°<br>
(iii) Find the mean error<br>
<b>Mean error =</b><br>
2<br>
<b>Error = 1°</b><br>
<b>Mean error = 1°</b><br>
<b>Subtract the mean error from a</b><br>
reading with large value.<br>
(iv)	Add the mean error to the readings<br>
with a small value.<br>
<b>BB 1 = BB 0 − 1°</b><br>
250 °−1°<br>
=  249 °<br>
<b>FB 1 = FB 0+1°</b><br>
= 68°+1°<br>
= 69 °<br>
<b>FB 1 = 69 °</b><br>
(v)  Prove the readings,<br>
<b>BB −FB  = 180°</b><br>
249 ° − 69 ° = 180°<br>
Therefore, the correct readings are;<br>
<b>BB 1 = 249°</b><br>
<b>FB 1 = 69 °</b><br>

<b>Amount of error = D − 180°</b><br>
= 179° − 180°<br>
= −1°<br>

<b>Fixing position of an object on the</b><br>
map by intersection and resection<br>
method<br>
<b>Bearing  and direction as the method</b><br>
among others of locating position, is<br>
the basis of intersection and resection<br>
methods.<br>
<b>Intersection method</b><br>
<b>Intersection method is a method of</b><br>
fixing unknown position of object on<br>
the map by taking bearings to it from<br>
two or more fixed points and using the<br>
data to fix the object’s position on a<br>
map. Intersection involves more than<br>
one observer and only the single object,<br>
which is at the unknown position. In<br>
this method, forward bearings are used<br>
but not changed into back bearing. It<br>
is the simplest method of identifying<br>
the position of an object compared to<br>
resection.<br>
<b>When the two forward bearings are</b><br>
provided and their locations are known,<br>
it is possible to locate the position of the<br>
unknown point or the object, by fixing<br>
the positions of the given points and<br>
taking the bearings towards the unknown<br>
point as directed by the bearing method.<br>
<b>Example</b><br>
<b>Suppose the forward bearings of Dar es</b><br>
<b>Salaam and Morogoro from Arusha were</b><br>
353° and 22°30', respectively. Using<br>
<b>Figure 4.26, find the position of Arusha</b><br>
by grid reference, if the grid reference of<br>
<b>Morogoro and Dar es Salaam are 515225</b><br>
and 555223,  respectively.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)	 Identify two points on a map,<br>
for example Dar es salaam and<br>
Morogoro;<br>
(ii)	 Draw the north direction at both,<br>
Dar es Salaam and Morogoro;<br>
(iii)	Measure the FB of the two points<br>
Dar es Salaam and Morogoro;<br>
(iv)	 Draw straight lines from each<br>
point along the angles measured;<br>
and<br>
(v)	 Read the grid reference location<br>
where the two lines intersect. The<br>
point where lines intersect is the<br>
coordinate location of Arusha.<br>
259<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>

<b>Resection method</b><br>
<b>Resection method identifies the location</b><br>
of the unknown object by taking the<br>
bearings from it to two or more known<br>
points located on the map. The readings<br>
are converted to back bearings and angle<br>
lines drawn from the points to meet at<br>
the observer’s position. It involves only<br>
one observer who is at the unknown<br>
position, viewing more than one object<br>
at the same time.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)	 Identify the location of each object<br>
by using grid reference.<br>
(ii)	 Identify the forward bearing of<br>
each object.<br>
(iii)	Change the forward bearings to<br>
back bearings.<br>
(iv)	 Establish the cardinal points at<br>
respective points.<br>
(v)	 Measure the back bearings at their<br>
respective points; and<br>
(vi)	 Draw the straight lines along the<br>
bearings measured. The observer’s<br>
location is the point where two<br>
lines cross each other.<br>
260<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Example 1</b><br>
<b>Ms Audrey observed a feature ‘x’ at</b><br>
grid reference 040160 from a bearing<br>
of 45°and feature ‘y’ at 060115 from a<br>
bearing of 135°.  Locate the position of<br>
<b>Ms Audrey</b><br>
<b>Changing the forward to back bearings</b><br>
<b>BB = FB ± 180°</b><br>
<b>From X</b><br>
45° + 180° = 2250<br>
<b>From Y</b><br>
135° + 180° = 315°<br>

using back bearing of x and y point C is<br>
plotted.<br>
<b>Trend and alignment</b><br>
Trend, also known as direction of  place,<br>
is a general layout of elongated features<br>
such as a river, valley, coastline, railway<br>
or road. Trend is expressed in degrees.<br>
<b>Usually trend is given in compass</b><br>
bearings and the bearings are used to<br>
determine the direction. It helps the<br>
map user to know the general layout<br>
of features and extent of the general<br>
curvature.<br>
<b>Procedures</b><br>
(i)<br>
Identify the two points;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Join the two points with a</b><br>
straight line;<br>
(iii)	 Note the middle point of the<br>
line;<br>
(iv)	 Draw a North direction on the<br>
noted point (middle point); and<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Measure the angles and give</b><br>
their degrees.<br>
<b>Trend involve two angles. The first</b><br>
bearing is measured from the North<br>
direction clockwise until it touches the<br>
drawn line that joins the two points. The<br>
second bearing is drawn in the same<br>
ways but it crosses the drawn line and<br>
touches it in the second part as shown<br>
in Figure 4.28.<br>
<b>If the first angle is well measured, the</b><br>
second angle is given by the use of<br>
mathematical formula.<br>
<b>Using Figure 4.28, let the first bearing</b><br>
be Y° and the second bearing be X°.<br>
<b>X° = Y° + 180°</b><br>
If Y°=55°,<br>
<b>Then, X°=55°+ 180°</b><br>
<b>X°= 235°</b><br>
<b>X°= 235 °</b><br>
<b>Note, the correct second bearing will</b><br>
be obtained only if the first bearing is<br>
properly measured<br>
Trend  is 55° to 235 °.<br>
x°<br>
y°<br>
<b>Figure 4.28:  Trend of a coastline</b><br>
261<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Alignment is a general direction of</b><br>
elongated features such as ridge and<br>
coastline. Usually, alignment is stated<br>
using direction in which the feature lies.<br>
<b>The same procedures of determining</b><br>
trend are used to determine alignment.<br>
<b>But in alignment, the measured angles</b><br>
are used to describe the general direction<br>
of the feature involved. For example, if<br>
the trend of the feature is 55° to 235° as<br>
shown in Figure 4.28, its alignment is NE<br>
to SW. From the illustration above, trend<br>
and alignment can be started in a general<br>
form as 55°   NE to 235°  SW.<br>
<b>NB: The best way of showing trend or</b><br>
alignment is by using both bearing and<br>
direction.<br>
<b>Types of North direction</b><br>
<b>The North direction on a map can be</b><br>
shown by using different types of north.<br>
<b>These are True North (TN), Magnetic</b><br>
<b>North (MN) and Grid North (GN)</b><br>
(Figure 4.29).<br>
<b>TN</b><br>
<b>X</b><br>
<b>GN</b><br>
<b>MN</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.29: The types of North direction</b><br>
<b>True North</b><br>
<b>This is the type of north direction in</b><br>
which all meridians or longitudes<br>
converge in the northern hemisphere.<br>
<b>Its direction is towards the 000° North</b><br>
from any place on the earth’s surface.<br>
<b>It is very near to the point at which the</b><br>
rotation axis of the Earth passes through.<br>
<b>It is shown on the map by the star headed</b><br>
line. It is also called geographical North<br>
<b>Pole and it is always fixed. The angle</b><br>
measured clockwise from geographical<br>
north to the object along the line of sight<br>
is called true bearing.<br>
<b>Magnetic North</b><br>
<b>This is direction indicated by the freely</b><br>
suspended magnetic needle. Magnetic<br>
<b>North is the line representing the</b><br>
direction of the needle of a magnetic<br>
compass pointing when the map was<br>
published. It enables to obtain  the<br>
magnetic bearing. It is the point in<br>
northern Canada where lines of the<br>
earth’s magnetic field intersect. It is<br>
naturally a migratory point caused by<br>
by the shifting behavior of the earth’s<br>
magnetic field which is influenced by a<br>
number of factors including the earth’s<br>
rotation and ionisation of the liquid metal<br>
in the outer core. This is called polar<br>
shift theory, as the world is not static,<br>
but dynamic. The earth is changing<br>
every day, plate tectonic push continents<br>
apart, sea levels fluctuate up and down,<br>
volcanic eruption discharge ash and<br>
smoke on the surface, denudation occurs<br>
and climate changes.<br>
262<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Grid North</b><br>
<b>Grid north is the direction towards the</b><br>
north in maps drawn by grid system.<br>
<b>Grid north is a direction that is parallel to</b><br>
the easting lines found on the Universal<br>
Transverse Mercator (UTM) grid system.<br>
<b>The grid  north aids in obtaining a</b><br>
topographical map since it is used as<br>
a baseline of clockwise direction. It is<br>
shown by bare line.<br>
<b>True bearing</b><br>
<b>A true bearing is an angle in which a</b><br>
line from the observer’s position to the<br>
object meets with a line pointing to True<br>
<b>North. The angle is measured clockwise</b><br>
from true north to the object along the<br>
line of sight.<br>
<b>Magnetic bearing</b><br>
<b>A Magnetic bearing is the angle, which</b><br>
a line from the observer’s position to the<br>
object meets with a line pointing in the<br>
direction of magnetic north. The angle<br>
measured clockwise from the magnetic<br>
north to the object through the line of<br>
sight is called magnetic bearing.<br>
<b>Magnetic variation</b><br>
<b>Magnetic North and the True North</b><br>
never coincide. They normally leave a<br>
gap apart which is always changing with<br>
time due to the shifting tendency of the<br>
magnetic influence. Therefore, magnetic<br>
variation is the angular distance between<br>
the Magnetic North and the True North,<br>
which usually keeps on changing with<br>
time and space. The decrease and the<br>
increase of the angle depends on the<br>
position of  the Magnetic North in<br>
relation to True North. Magnetic North<br>
keeps on changing its position at specific<br>
rate with time in relation to the position<br>
of the True North. It may be located in<br>
the western or eastern part of the True<br>
North which never changes its position.<br>
However, in the African continent,<br>
<b>Magnetic North is located to the west</b><br>
of the True North. Determination of<br>
magnetic variation depends on the<br>
position of MN in relation to the TN.<br>
<b>Principles governing calculations of</b><br>
magnetic variation, magnetic bearing<br>
and true bearing when the magnetic<br>
north is to the west of the True North.<br>
<b>As shown in Figure 4.30, the position</b><br>
of Magnetic North is west of the True<br>
<b>North. The gap between Magnetic North</b><br>
and True North denoted by MV1 is the<br>
initial angle recorded before the change<br>
in position of Magnetic North. As the<br>
Magnetic North started to shift eastward,<br>
slowly the gap started to become smaller<br>
with time, giving the second angle in<br>
between denoted by MV2. The angle<br>
between the new position and old<br>
position of the Magnetic North is called<br>
total change. The observation made from<br>
the Figure 4.30, therefore establishes<br>
that: when the Magnetic North is to the<br>
<b>West of the True North and the rate of</b><br>
annual change is eastward, the final<br>
magnetic variation is the difference<br>
between the initial magnetic variation<br>
and the total change ( TC).<br>
<b>MV2= MV1−TC1</b><br>

<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>

<b>The following are rules governing</b><br>
calculation of true baring, if magnetic<br>
bearing of an object from its observer<br>
is given:<br>
(a) 	 When the magnetic declination<br>
is to the West of the True North<br>
as shown in Figure 4.34, subtract<br>
magnetic declination or magnetic<br>
variation (MV) from the magnetic<br>
bearing (MB) to obtain the True<br>
Bearing (TB).<br>
<b>That is, TB = MB – MV and MB</b><br>
<b>= TB + MV.</b><br>
(b) 	 When the Magnetic North is to the<br>
<b>East of the True North, as shown</b><br>
in Figure 4.34, add Magnetic<br>
deflination or Magnetic variation<br>
(MV) to magnetic bearing (MB)<br>
to obtain two bearings that is, TB<br>
= MB +MV and MB =TB – MV.<br>

<b>Example 3</b><br>
<b>Given that Magnetic Bearing of town Y</b><br>
was 248°05'00" and magnetic variation<br>
was 15°26'00". Find the true bearing of<br>
the town Y.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Data given</b><br>
<b>MB = 248°05'00"</b><br>
<b>MV = 15°26'00"</b><br>
<b>MB = MV + TB</b><br>
<b>MB – MV = TB</b><br>
<b>When the magnetic declination is to the</b><br>
<b>East of the True North, add the magnetic</b><br>
declination to the magnetic bearing to<br>
obtain the True Bearing. It means, if<br>
variation was 15° 26' 00"<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>TB = MV + MB</b><br>
= 15°26' + 232°29'<br>
=  248°05'<br>
<b>Therefore, the True Bearing of the town</b><br>
Y is  248°05'.<br>
