<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Representation of relief on a map</b><br>
<b>The relief of an area is the surface form</b><br>
of the ground which shows size, shape,<br>
slope of the highlands and lowlands. It<br>
can also be defined as the changing nature<br>
of the land or variation in the shape and<br>
form of the earth’s surface as observed<br>
from the sea level. The depiction of relief<br>
and land form on a flat map is an attempt<br>
to show three dimensional forms on a<br>
two-dimensional surface.<br>
<b>Methods of representing relief on a</b><br>
map<br>
There are two methods of showing relief,<br>
which are; quantitative and qualitative<br>
methods. A quantitative method shows<br>
exact number, for example. contour,<br>
spot height, trigonometrical, benchmark<br>
and so on. Qualitative method does not<br>
show numbers but uses hachuring, hill<br>
shading, block diagram (physiographic<br>
diagram), layer coloring, form lines and<br>
naming methods.<br>
<b>Hachuring method</b><br>
<b>Refers to the non-numerical traditional</b><br>
way of showing the nature of the terrain,<br>
by means of their value and their<br>
thickness (Figure 4.35). Their thickness<br>
and values increase with the increase in<br>
the degree of land steepness. Despite<br>
the fact that they are non-numerical,<br>
hachures can successfully communicate<br>
a quite specific shape of the terrain. They<br>
are suitable for mountainous regions for<br>
depicting landforms such as craters. They<br>
also give general ideas on the nature of<br>
the slope. Hachures have the following<br>
limitations; They cannot show the extent<br>
of height and steepness of the landform<br>
they represent. Different relief features<br>
such as mountains, hill, and direction<br>
of the water courses are difficult to be<br>
interpreted with the use of hachures.<br>
<b>They are likely to obscure other features</b><br>
and their use consumes more time.<br>
<b>Figure 4.35: Hachuring method</b><br>
<b>Contour method</b><br>
<b>Contour lines on a map join points</b><br>
representing equal elevation above a<br>
datum or reference line (mean sea level).<br>
<b>For example, the datum line for Nigeria</b><br>
is taken as mean sea level at Lagos. In<br>
<b>East Africa, the dutum line is based on</b><br>
the mean sea level at Mombasa, and<br>
much of the survey work in South and<br>
<b>Central Africa is based on the datum</b><br>
line at Cape Town. Usually, contours<br>
show various features or landforms like<br>
hills, plateaus, mountains and valleys,<br>
basins and plains. Contours, also show<br>
gentle slope when the contour lines are<br>
drawn far apart from one another and<br>
steep-slope when they are drawn closely.<br>
<b>Contour lines are drawn at definite</b><br>
intervals for instance 20 m or 50 feet and<br>
do not cross one another. Contour lines<br>
form a ‘V’ pointing upwards to denote<br>
a valley and ‘V’ pointing downwards to<br>
denote a spur.  The merits of contour is<br>
271<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
to give exact height for a particular area<br>
since the contours are numbered (Figure<br>
4.36). They are also used to give clue<br>
of physical features depending on the<br>
contour layout and it can be integrated<br>
with other methods like spot height and<br>
trigonometrical station.<br>
<b>Example</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.36:  Contour lines</b><br>
<b>Form lines: are usually unnumbered</b><br>
lines drawn on a map joining points of<br>
nearly the same height. They are like<br>
contours but they use  unnumbered lines.<br>
<b>Hypsometric colouring method</b><br>
<b>Hypsometric coloring also known as</b><br>
layer tinting or colouring is a method used<br>
on maps to re-enforce the impression<br>
of relief and to make the land forms<br>
more easily understood by map users.<br>
<b>It is not a complete method in itself</b><br>
because it relies upon the presence of<br>
either contours or form lines to provide<br>
the basis for coloring. By convention, the<br>
lower elevations are colored in various<br>
shades of green, intermediate areas in<br>
yellow, higher altitude zones in brown<br>
or red, then purple and white. The lighter<br>
colour are used  where most details are<br>
in the lowland areas and the brightest<br>
colors where details are sparsely high.<br>
<b>With layer coloring, it is simple to</b><br>
identify the nature of the relief whether<br>
lowland or highlands just by observing<br>
the change in colour rather than striving<br>
to find the values of height as with other<br>
methods like contour lines. It is mostly<br>
used on small scale maps and atlas maps.<br>
<b>Different tones used in indicating the</b><br>
change in elevation make the map more<br>
attractive. For instance, green colour<br>
is used to indicate lowland and white<br>
or red colour is used for highland. A<br>
stereoscopic is produced in which the<br>
warmer colors such as brown and red<br>
appear nearer the map reader while the<br>
cooler colors like green and blue at lower<br>
elevations, appear further away. It may<br>
bring about confusion to some people<br>
since colors have multiple implication<br>
in people’s minds. For example, green<br>
colour may suggest vegetation or fertility.<br>
<b>However, not all lowlands coloured</b><br>
green are fertile. Additionally, layer tint<br>
maps are very expensive to produce.<br>
<b>Lastly the method cannot show a clear</b><br>
boundary between  highland and lowland<br>
areas, though it uses different colors to<br>
indicate them.<br>
<b>Hill Shading</b><br>
<b>Hill shading is a method of showing</b><br>
relief in which parts of the map are<br>
darkened by a tint or stipple of chosen<br>
colour as if they are in shadow cast<br>
by an imaged object. It can give a<br>
fine, modeled impression of relief that<br>
strikes out eyes at once. The slopes can<br>
be shown easily as the shadow tone is<br>
272<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
darker on steep slopes and lighter on<br>
gentle slopes. It tends to make the slopes<br>
in shadow to look steeper  than they<br>
really are. The impression of relief is<br>
sometimes much greater than the sense<br>
of it,  for example, the location of “ups”<br>
and “downs” (Figure 4.37).<br>
<b>Figure 4.37: Hill shading method</b><br>
<b>Trigonometric point method</b><br>
<b>Trigonometric point is the method of</b><br>
representing relief whereby a point is<br>
accurately surveyed and shown on the<br>
map, with their actual height in metres or<br>
feet above mean sea level. The method<br>
is usually represented on the map by a<br>
triangle and a dot with exact height. For<br>
example,<br>
8848 m. After surveying,<br>
the surveyors erect concrete pillars on<br>
the summits of the hills, which act as<br>
corners of the main triangles of the<br>
survey. The method is easy to recognize<br>
the height of a given point on the map.<br>
<b>Sometimes a trigonometric point is</b><br>
called a trigonometric station. Like<br>
trigonometrical stations, the method is<br>
used with contour lines for mathematical<br>
interpretation of the map, such as<br>
determination of the slope and drawing<br>
of relief section.<br>
<b>Spot height method</b><br>
<b>Spot height is the method of representing</b><br>
relief whereby a point is accurately<br>
surveyed and its actual height above<br>
mean sea level is shown in metres or<br>
feet on a map.  The method is normally<br>
represented by dot and exact height such<br>
as ● 5895 m  or represented by small<br>
circle with dot and  height number for<br>
example  5895 m. In this method, there<br>
is no physical evidence of spot heights in<br>
the field, but they appear frequently on<br>
maps, along road and between contours<br>
near sea-level to help in interpretation<br>
of relief.<br>
<b>With trigonometric station and spot</b><br>
height it is easy to recognise the height<br>
of a given point on the map, unlike<br>
with the use of contour lines and<br>
psychometric tinting method. It can be<br>
used with contour lines to prepare the<br>
cross section. It is also very useful in<br>
some mathematical interpretation of the<br>
mapped area such as determination of<br>
slope. It may provide important elements<br>
or clues on the nature of the relief, and<br>
become the basis of the relief description.<br>
<b>The use of trigonometric stations as the</b><br>
method of showing relief features does<br>
not obscure other details. However, with<br>
this method, it is very difficult to clearly<br>
understand the nature of relief features<br>
like mountains, valleys and steep slopes<br>
in a particular mapped area.<br>
<b>Benchmarks methods</b><br>
<b>A benchmark (BM) is a mark on a</b><br>
permanent object, which indicates an<br>
elevation of a given area. Benchmark<br>
serves as a reference point from which<br>
measurement of topographical surveys<br>
273<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
may be made. They can be found in the<br>
brick or stone of a building or a wall.<br>
<b>It is shown by a symbol followed by a</b><br>
numerical height, for example BM 1554.<br>
<b>Naming method</b><br>
<b>Naming method is the method of showing</b><br>
relief whereby specific landforms (relief<br>
features) are identified on topographical<br>
map by using their names.<br>
<b>Map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Once the conventions and alphabet of</b><br>
maps are mastered, it becomes possible<br>
to spell out situations in the landscape<br>
from the association and relationships of<br>
symbols. In topographical maps all the<br>
information about location, landscape<br>
and cultural features are depicted with<br>
the assistance of conventional signs<br>
and symbols. It should be noted that<br>
convection signs and symbols are used<br>
to define the features represented on the<br>
map. Thus, they should be relevant to the<br>
actual features represented. They should<br>
also be common, familiar and widely<br>
accepted by map readers;  otherwise<br>
there will be misinterpretation of the<br>
information. From such information,<br>
one can read and interpret the position,<br>
climate, relief, vegetation, drainage,<br>
settlement, rocks and human activities of<br>
a particular location shown on the map.<br>
<b>Position</b><br>
<b>The use of latitude and longitude of an</b><br>
area studied will tell where the piece of<br>
land lies in the world. When one knows<br>
this, it becomes easy to picture out the<br>
chief geographical characteristics of the<br>
area by applying general knowledge of<br>
geography. In addition to that, latitude<br>
degree helps to determine hemispherical<br>
position (location) of the mapped area. If<br>
a latitude degree is  followed by the letter<br>
‘S’ means it is taken from the Southern<br>
hemisphere and letter ‘N’ indicates the<br>
<b>Northern Hemisphere. For example, a</b><br>
map of Vanga, sheet No. 111/1 lies from<br>
4°40'00" S to 4°45'00" S indicates that it<br>
is taken from  the southern hemisphere.<br>
<b>Therefore, it is possible to find a position</b><br>
of a piece of land or object on the earth<br>
surface by using latitudes and longtudes<br>
(graticule system).<br>
<b>Example 1. Locate Dodoma town which</b><br>
is found at grid reference 328590 by<br>
using longitude and latitude, starting<br>
with 30° E and 05°, S respectively (refer<br>
Figure 4.23).<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Longitude</b><br>
(i) Find difference in degrees<br>
<b>40° E  ̶  30° E = 10° E</b><br>
<b>If 14.8 cm = 10° E</b><br>
<b>8.2 cm = x</b><br>
x × 14.8 cm<br>
<b>14.8 cm</b><br>
= 8.2 cm × 10°<br>
<b>14.8 cm</b><br>
x = 5.54°<br>
(ii) To change degree to minutes<br>
<b>If  1° = 60'</b><br>
0.54° = x<br>
x × 1°<br>
1°<br>
= 0.54° × 60'<br>
1°<br>
x = 32.4'<br>
(iii) To change minutes into seconds<br>
<b>If  1' = 60"</b><br>
0.4' = x<br>

1'<br>
x = 24"<br>

(ii), and seconds in (iii) gives 5°32'24"<br>
<b>Thus,	 30°00'00" E</b><br>
+	   5°32'24"<br>

grid reference 328590 lies at longitude<br>
35° 32' 24" E and latitude 6° 10' 48" S.<br>
<b>Climate</b><br>
<b>A great deal of climatic information can</b><br>
be deduced from the topographical map<br>
using latitude, drainage (water bodies),<br>
relief and vegetation as follows;<br>
<b>Latitude</b><br>
<b>This is the most common hint used in the</b><br>
mapped area. It gives general indications<br>
concerning rainfall and mean annual<br>
temperature. For instance, areas between<br>
0º to 5º north or south of the equator fall<br>
under equatorial climate. These areas<br>
are characterised by high temperature<br>
and high rainfall. Area between 5º and<br>
15º North or South of the Equator are<br>
in category of tropical climate with<br>
moderate rainfall marked by wet and dry<br>
seasons and  the temperature is also high.<br>
<b>Areas between  15º and 35º North or</b><br>
<b>South of the Equator are categorised  as</b><br>
desert climate with very high temperature<br>
and little or non rainfall.<br>
<b>Altitude: the mean temperature decreases</b><br>
with altitude at an average rate of 6.5º<br>
<b>C for every 1 000 m. Thus if one notes</b><br>
the height of a place can make an easy<br>
arithmetic guess whether the area<br>
experiences high or low temperature.<br>
<b>Rainfall  type can also be determined by</b><br>
the presence of mountains which induce<br>
orographic rainfall. So, the latitude<br>
sometimes does not give  exact type of<br>
climate. When relief interrupts the  area,<br>
it results to  modified climate such as<br>
modified equatorial climate, modified<br>
tropical climate and mountainous<br>
climate.<br>
<b>Water bodies</b><br>
<b>These are very good guide to climate</b><br>
types. The presence of many rivers<br>
275<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
indicates high rainfall; hence,  suggests<br>
equatorial climate, while the presence<br>
of salty lakes, bore holes, water holes,<br>
springs and wells imply dry conditions<br>
or semi-arid climate. Seasonal lakes and<br>
swamps suggest low rainfall and the area<br>
is marked by wet and dry seasons which<br>
denote a tropical climate.<br>
<b>Vegetation</b><br>
<b>This is  a good guide for map</b><br>
interpretation. The presences of forests<br>
and bamboo trees indicate high rainfall,<br>
which denotes equatorial climate in the<br>
area. Moreover, woodlands or grasslands<br>
reflect medium rainfall while scrubs,<br>
thickets, thorny bushes and scattered<br>
bushes imply low rainfall which<br>
represent a semi-arid climate.<br>
<b>Crops</b><br>
<b>Crops can indicate the type of climate of</b><br>
an area. Some of the crops such as coffee,<br>
rubber and tea are grown in areas which<br>
receive high rainfall, hence indicate<br>
presence of equatorial or mountainous<br>
climate. Other crops such as sisal, millet<br>
and cassava tolerate dry conditions and<br>
survive under moderate rainfall, hence<br>
indicate presence of tropical climate.<br>
<b>Relief</b><br>
<b>This can be defined as the changing</b><br>
nature of the land as observed from<br>
the sea level with its characterising<br>
landforms. An area can be described as<br>
highland relief if its average elevation<br>
is  higher than one thousand metres<br>
above mean sea level. Highland relief<br>
is normally characterised notably by<br>
highland landforms such as mountains,<br>
escarpments, ridges, plateaus, valleys<br>
and spurs as well as, hills and ridges.<br>
<b>Lowland relief is normally with the</b><br>
average elevation below one thousand<br>
metres above the mean sea level. It is<br>
characterised by lowland features such<br>
as deltas, plains, estuaries and cliffs.<br>
<b>Relief on topographical map can be</b><br>
shown by using various methods of<br>
representing relief such as contour lines,<br>
spot height, trigonometric stations or<br>
hill shading.<br>
<b>Contour lines form a ‘V’ pointing</b><br>
upstream to denote a valley and pointing<br>
down to denote a spur. Where contour<br>
opens is where the river and water<br>
streams go and  when several contour<br>
lines are merging it indicates a cliff.<br>
<b>Transport and communication</b><br>
<b>Though all types of transport are shown</b><br>
in topographical maps, the most common<br>
is land transport which includes railway<br>
lines, pipelines, and roads. Roads are<br>
shown in different forms like tarmac<br>
roads also called all weather roads<br>
which are represented by thick red line.<br>
<b>Loose surface roads are indicated by</b><br>
thick broken line, dry weather roads<br>
are indicated by continuous white line<br>
and footpath. Some topographical maps<br>
show well developed and distributed<br>
transport and communication routes<br>
than others. This shows that there is<br>
unequal development of transport<br>
and communication systems. Some<br>
of the factors that determines unequal<br>
development and distribution of transport<br>
and communication includes drainage,<br>
relief, human activities and climate.<br>
276<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Apart from land transport, topographical</b><br>
maps also show air transport which is<br>
indicated by airfield runway and water<br>
transport indicated by large water bodies<br>
like lakes, sea and oceans.<br>
<b>Land use and functions</b><br>
<b>Different land uses both in urban</b><br>
and rural areas are well shown in the<br>
topographical maps. Urban land use<br>
is indicated by commercial land use,<br>
industrial land use, residential land use<br>
and open space land use. Rural land is<br>
used for different economic, political and<br>
social activities including commercial<br>
cultivation. The common land uses in<br>
rural areas are intensive cultivation land<br>
use, forestry land use, grazing land use<br>
and village settlement land use.<br>
<b>Drainage system</b><br>
<b>Drainage can be defined as the runing</b><br>
water in an area by natural or artificial<br>
streams, rivers, and infiltration as it is<br>
commanded by the nature of geology and<br>
relief of the particular area. Variation in<br>
the nature of relief and rocks determine<br>
the quality, and types of drainage<br>
patterns. Drainage pattern is the layout or<br>
plan made by rivers and their tributaries<br>
on the landscape.  Streams and rivers are<br>
by far the most important landscapes<br>
forming elements.  In map reading and<br>
interpretation, the drainage basin forms<br>
a convenient unit of study which can be<br>
outlined and readily defined on a map.<br>
<b>Drainage can be classified according to</b><br>
the pattern formed in relation to surface<br>
relief or the slope of the land, differences<br>
in rock hardness and rock structure.<br>
<b>Various drainage patterns are related to</b><br>
the surface rock structure.<br>
<b>Dendritic pattern</b><br>
<b>It is a shaped like trunk and branches of</b><br>
a tree usually with tributaries converging<br>
on the main stream from many directions<br>
and merge at acute angles (Figure<br>
4.38).  They occur in rocks which<br>
are homogeneous (similar). They are<br>
commonly formed on massive crystalline<br>
rocks like granite or horizontal to gently<br>
dipping sedimentary strata.  They are not<br>
related to rock structure or differences<br>
in rock hardness.<br>
<b>Tributaries</b><br>
<b>Main river</b><br>
<b>River mouth</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.38: Dendritic drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Trellis drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>It is a linear pattern in the shape of a lattice</b><br>
with the chief tributaries joining the main<br>
stream approximately at right angles<br>
(Figure 4.39). Usually, minor tributaries<br>
join the chief tributaries at right angles<br>
and flow more or less parallel to the<br>
main stream. The pattern is strongly<br>
related to structure or differences in rock<br>
hardness (heterogeneous rocks) and is<br>
commonly found in scarp land areas and<br>
regions of folded rocks. Normally, it is<br>
found in folded sedimentary rocks and<br>
metamorphic rocks. The chief tributaries<br>
are usually aligned along down fold or<br>
parallel zones of weak rock separated<br>
by resistant uplands.<br>

joining the<br>
main river<br>
<b>Main river flow</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.39: Trellis drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Radial drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>This is an arrangement of streams flowing</b><br>
outwards down the flanks of a dome<br>
or cone-shaped upland such as a large<br>
volcano (Figure 4.40). It is common in<br>
volcanic region and  controlled by the<br>
gradient of the land.<br>
<b>Figure 4.40: Radial drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Annular drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Is the pattern with streams often joining</b><br>
at sharp angles but arranged in a series<br>
of curves about a dissected dome, basin<br>
or crater area. On a dissected dome<br>
with alternating bands of hard and soft<br>
rock the pattern may appear as several<br>
concentric curves (Figure 4.41). It is<br>
common in volcanic region.<br>
<b>Figure 4.41: Annular drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Rectangular drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>This pattern is similar in plan to the</b><br>
trellis, with tributaries joining each<br>
other at approximately right angles.<br>
<b>Rectangular pattern also tends to have</b><br>
individual streams taking sharp angular<br>
bends along their course (Figure 4.42).<br>
<b>It is the result of structural control, with</b><br>
streams following joints or fault lines<br>
in the rock. It is common in volcanic<br>
region, and granitic rocks.<br>
<b>Tributaries</b><br>
joining the main<br>
river<br>
<b>Main river flow</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.42: Rectangular drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Braided drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>This is common on broad flood plains</b><br>
with low gradients, often due to back<br>
tilting (Figure 4.43). A good example<br>
is in Mazinde area at Lushoto in Tanga<br>
region.<br>

<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.43: Braided pattern</b><br>
<b>Regulated drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Regulated pattern is common in flat</b><br>
sediments of recent age in coastal zones,<br>
associated with tidal rivers, numerous<br>
cracks and coastal swamps (Figure 4.44).<br>
<b>It is very common along the coasts of</b><br>
West and East Africa.<br>
<b>Figure 4.44: Regulated drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Anastomotic drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>It is very common in area on flood plains</b><br>
in coastal zones or reduced gradients<br>
inland due to back tilting (Figure 4.45).<br>
<b>It is very common for numerous double</b><br>
channels, ox-bow lakes, cut-offs, and<br>
abandoned meanders.<br>
<b>Rivers or streams will generally follow</b><br>
the line of least resistance such as along<br>
a band of softer rock, a fault line or the<br>
crushed fault breccia between two faults.<br>
<b>Figure 4.45:  Anastomotic pattern</b><br>
<b>Centripetal drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>This drainage is opposite to the</b><br>
radial drainage pattern because it is<br>
characterised by the streams which<br>
converge at a point which is generally<br>
a depression or basin. This  drainage<br>
is formed when a series of streams<br>
converge in a central low  basin or crater<br>
lake (Figure 4.46). A good example of<br>
this drainage pattern can be found in<br>
Kondoa District.<br>
<b>Stream flow</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.46: Centripetal drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Parallel drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Parallel drainage pattern comprises</b><br>
number of rivers which are parallel<br>
to each other  and follow the original<br>
slope (Figure 4.47). This  pattern is<br>
more frequently developed on uniformly<br>
sloping and dipping rock beds such as<br>
Cuesta or newly emerged coastal plains.<br>
279<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Tributaries</b><br>
<b>Stream flow</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.47:  Parallel drainage pattern</b><br>
<b>Vegetation</b><br>
<b>A topographical map shows both natural</b><br>
vegetations such as forest, Savanna,<br>
thicket, shrubs, thorny, swamp vegetation,<br>
bamboo, mangrove swampy, scattered<br>
bushes and others as well as artificial<br>
vegetation which may be indicated by<br>
plantations. Sometimes vegetation is also<br>
indicated by pictorial symbols which<br>
shows increasing density by placing the<br>
symbols closer together or giving the<br>
area a green tint.  Dark green color may<br>
be used. In interpretation of vegetation,<br>
a geographer should consider the nature<br>
or type and the way it is distributed. To<br>
determine what vegetation appear in<br>
which part of the mapped area, consider<br>
the cardinal points or names to indicate<br>
the nature of distribution. For example,<br>
the Eastern side of the mapped area is<br>
covered by scattered trees and scrubs<br>
while in the North West part is dominated<br>
by thick forest. In describing vegetation<br>
distribution, a geographer should also<br>
consider factors which account for<br>
the presence or absence of vegetation<br>
in the particular area. Normally the<br>
number of factors including soil quality,<br>
relief, drainage, human activities and<br>
climatic condition influences vegetation<br>
distribution. A detailed description of<br>
vegetation, soil and climatic relationships<br>
is presented in Table 4.3.<br>

<b>Settlement</b><br>
<b>Settlement refer to an organised</b><br>
permanent or temporary inhabitation<br>
of humans on a small or large area of<br>
land including slum or the requisite<br>
infrastructural facilities. Settlement<br>
range in size from small to largest.<br>
<b>Settlement is another basic aspect on</b><br>
topographical map interpretation.<br>
<b>Types of settlement</b><br>
There are two types of settlement,<br>
namely urban and rural settlement.<br>
<b>Urban settlement is an area where its</b><br>
population approximately 80% are<br>
engaged in non-agricultural activities.<br>
<b>Its is commonly found in district head</b><br>
quarters, regional administrative centres<br>
and along transportation routes. Rural<br>
281<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
settlement is an area where its population<br>
approximately 80% are engaged in<br>
agricultural activities. Commonly<br>
found in a countryside and villages. In<br>
interpretation of settlements geographers<br>
have to consider how and where the dots<br>
representing people, house or buildings<br>
and the town are located. Urban<br>
settlement is simply identified through<br>
highly built areas, as well as densely<br>
linear and nucleated settlement patterns;<br>
while rural settlements are revealed in<br>
the map by sparsely hurts, light linear<br>
and nucleated settlement patterns.<br>
<b>In actual sense, settlement should be</b><br>
studied systematically looking on aspects<br>
such as site, situation, form, pattern and<br>
function.<br>
<b>Site: is the nature of land on which</b><br>
the settlement is built or where town<br>
or village is built.  The site may be<br>
chosen for different reasons such as<br>
relief and gradient. For example, flat<br>
areas may be preferred for easy building.<br>
<b>Areas with availability of water bodies</b><br>
such as  of rivers, streams, and lake;<br>
fertile soil; presence of transport and<br>
communication network, defensive<br>
site such as mountainous area can<br>
also be chosen for the purposes they<br>
serves. Sometime, site choice can be<br>
influenced by government policies.  The<br>
government can allocate the use of land<br>
depending on its potentiality.<br>
<b>Situation: is the settlement’s position</b><br>
in relation to other conditions such as<br>
physical features, important economic<br>
zones and  communication. For example,<br>
the village may be situated on a river<br>
bank above flood level. The site will be<br>
convenient because of availability of<br>
water nearby. This means that, settlement<br>
is well established in an area when<br>
site and situation conditions are not<br>
conflicting.<br>
<b>Form: this refers to the general shape of</b><br>
the settlement. Usually, a topographical<br>
map may indicate the features which<br>
have influenced the form of the<br>
settlement.  For example, the basic<br>
urban forms are linear which may result<br>
from a constructed site such as valley,<br>
the alignment of a route way or river<br>
or an elongated relief feature such as a<br>
ridge or escarpment base. Other forms<br>
of settlement are as follows: concentric<br>
circular form which is the form where<br>
growth has been generally outward<br>
from a central core or nucleus. With<br>
rectangular form, the town develops in<br>
blocks. A good example is Hastings town<br>
in Sierra Leone.<br>
<b>Settlement pattern: this is how the</b><br>
settlement is distributed.  In this aspect,<br>
we can examine the size of settlements in<br>
relation to the environment and we can<br>
study the pattern and shape of settlements.<br>
<b>Normally settlement morphology</b><br>
(pattern) is determined or controlled<br>
by its main function. In other words, it<br>
depends on the human activities. There<br>
are three types of settlement pattens,<br>
namely linear, nucleated and dispersed<br>
settlement patterns (Figure 4.48).<br>
<b>Linear settlement pattern: occurs in the</b><br>
area where the marketing of cash crops<br>
depends upon ease of transport, or along<br>
communication and transport routes such<br>
282<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
as roads and railways.  Some areas follow the pattern of river valleys.  This<br>
settlement is linear in shape where business and collecting are the major activities.<br>
Nucleated settlement patterns: represents settlement areas characterised by clustered<br>
or closed buildings normally for economic and social purpose. It is common in<br>
town centers, village centers, borders of two countries and around the coastal<br>
areas. It is common in places where  there is settlement around nuclei areas such<br>
as market centre or mining centers.<br>
Dispersed settlement pattern: occurs in the area with sparsely or scattered people,<br>
with few houses  far apart. Such pattern can be found in pastoral societies.<br>
<b>Dispersed</b><br>
<b>Linear</b><br>
<b>Nucleated</b><br>
<b>Figure 4.48: Three types of settlement patterns</b><br>
<b>NOTE: Settlement morphology or</b><br>
pattern changes with time due to social,<br>
political and economic development.<br>
<b>An area with scattered settlement may</b><br>
over time be dominated by nucleated<br>
settlement.<br>
<b>Function: is another technique of</b><br>
interpreting settlement.  Land use pattern<br>
within a form of larger settlements<br>
may reveal well-defined functional<br>
zones, in which there are varieties of<br>
specified activities. Such zones include<br>
the Central Business District (CBD),<br>
which is a rectangular grid pattern of<br>
high class and specified shops, offices<br>
and banks located near the centre of the<br>
settlement. The zone is neither residential<br>
nor industrial. Generally function of a<br>
settlement reflects its economic and<br>
social development activities. More<br>
examples of functional zones which can<br>
be indicated on a map are as follows:<br>
<b>The administrative function is</b><br>
indicated by government offices such<br>
as Headquarters (HQ), District Council<br>
(DC) and Regional Council (RC).<br>
<b>Recreational function is usually indicated</b><br>
by different man-made features such as<br>
car parks, gardens, football grounds,<br>
boating lakes, race courses, golf courses<br>
and zoos.<br>
<b>Cultural function is normally is indicated</b><br>
by cinemas, museums and libraries.<br>
<b>Educational function is depicted by</b><br>
schools, colleges, universities, research<br>
centers library building and education<br>
offices.<br>
283<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Industrial function is usually spacious</b><br>
in layout and well away from the main<br>
centre and residential areas and very<br>
often downwind to avoid pollution.<br>
<b>Cultivation function: is normally located</b><br>
in margins of most large towns and cities.<br>
<b>Horticulture is practiced to serve the</b><br>
industrial and business purposes.<br>
<b>Marketing/Trade function is indicated by</b><br>
buying posts town, market, and nuclear<br>
settlement pattern.<br>
<b>Residential function is classified on</b><br>
the basis of low, medium and high<br>
density. The infrastructure serving the<br>
settlement like electricity, water pipes,<br>
dams, railways lines  and roads are good<br>
indicators of residential zone.<br>
<b>The number of institutions such as</b><br>
government offices, police stations,<br>
army barracks, churches, colleges and<br>
alike; are significant to support living,<br>
hence considered important indicators<br>
for this zone.<br>
<b>Functional zones may be more easily</b><br>
defined in large urban centres than<br>
in smaller settlements. In smaller<br>
settlements one looks for emerging or<br>
embryo functional zones and tries to<br>
depict what future development they are<br>
likely to produce.<br>
<b>Types of rocks</b><br>
<b>Rocks</b><br>

in<br>
hardness<br>
with<br>
metamorphic being the hardest while<br>
the sedimentary being the softest.<br>

<b>Describing forces affecting the landscape</b><br>
<b>In topographical maps it is possible</b><br>
to spell out exactly about geomorphic<br>
activities that reshaping the mapped area.<br>
<b>A geographer can identify both endogenic</b><br>
(folding ,faulting and vulcanism) and<br>
exogenic (denudation and deposition)<br>
activities. This can be done with the help<br>
of the nature of relief depicted and its<br>
associated landforms. Table 4.5 shows<br>
some  hints for description of forces<br>
affecting the landscape.<br>

<b>Human Activities</b><br>
<b>Maps describe the way  man feeds</b><br>
himself and earns his living.  The<br>
following are some simple hints being<br>
used to describe economic activities.<br>
<b>Cultivation</b><br>
<b>Farming is among of the basic and the</b><br>
widespread  economic activities of man.<br>
<b>The topographical maps show both</b><br>
small and large-scale farming. Small<br>
scale farming is represented by scattered<br>
settlements, scattered cultivation and<br>
inland water bodies while large scale<br>
farming is portrayed by estates or<br>
plantation.<br>
The presence of ginneries for cotton;<br>
hullers for coffee; jiggery works for<br>
sugar; tea factories Cotton Buying Post<br>
(CBP) and maize mills indicates farming.<br>
<b>In addition, cultivation can be presented</b><br>
by the word scattered cultivation.<br>
<b>Pastoralism</b><br>
<b>Livestock keeping is not easily seen on</b><br>
maps. Grassland areas with scattered<br>
clusters of dwellings and perhaps water<br>
pans, water holes, dams and boreholes<br>
are the likely settings to represent<br>
pastoralism. In addition, look out for<br>
named veterinary installations, water-<br>
pump tanks, cattle and quarantine camps,<br>
trough races, cattle and creameries.<br>
<b>Manufacturing Industries</b><br>
<b>Ginning, hulling, tea and sisal processing</b><br>
are often done in the countryside, but<br>
<b>East African industries are in or near the</b><br>
larger towns. Normally urban settlement<br>
indicates industrial activities. Special<br>
symbols and signs also can be used to<br>
identify  industries. Some words such<br>
as ginnery, factory or industry can be<br>
applied. Similarly, large scale plantation<br>
indicates  the presence of processing<br>
industry.<br>
<b>Trade</b><br>
<b>It focuses on buying and selling goods</b><br>
and services. It is depicted by the<br>
presence of communication network<br>
285<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
(roads, airways and railways), markets,<br>
shops, trading centres and settlement.<br>
<b>Mining and quarrying</b><br>
<b>Some places in the drier rift valley</b><br>
floor are exploited for salt or soda.<br>
<b>Nevertheless, mineral symbols, salt</b><br>
works, mineral works and quarries are<br>
mostly preferable. They are also indicated<br>
by presence of brick or cement industries<br>
since they depend on limestone or sand<br>
as their raw materials.<br>
<b>Logging</b><br>
<b>Logging also known as lumbering refers</b><br>
to the activity of cutting down trees in<br>
order to use their related products like<br>
wood. Woods are used to produce timber<br>
(lumbering), charcoal and poles for<br>
electricity, furniture as well as different<br>
industrial materials. On a map, evidence<br>
such as saw mills and minor roads ending<br>
in forested area depict logging. Some<br>
forest reserves are exploited for timber,<br>
but many are not meant to be exploited.<br>
<b>Fishing</b><br>
<b>Settlement along water bodies such as</b><br>
rivers, sea, ocean and dams indicate<br>
fishing activities. Similarly, this is<br>
indicated by the presence of fish trap,<br>
fishing market centers and net drying<br>
yard.<br>
<b>Tourism</b><br>
<b>The presence of National Parks or</b><br>
<b>National Reserves antiquities, volcanic</b><br>
features and coastal features, hotels,<br>
gymnasium, cultural features, physical<br>
or scenery can be preferred to indicate<br>
tourism in the mapped area.<br>
<b>Social Activities</b><br>
<b>Social activities are services provided</b><br>
for the benefit of the community. They<br>
are activities which aim at solving social<br>
problems or need and therefore generate<br>
social impact. Some social activities<br>
are like; religious activities, health<br>
services, educational activities, transport<br>
and communication, administrative<br>
activities, Security services, power<br>
supply services, banking services and<br>
recreational activities. These social<br>
activities can be presented on a map in<br>
different ways.<br>
<b>Religious activities can be indicated by</b><br>
worshiping centres such as churches,<br>
mosques, and temples. Health services<br>
are indicated by health facilities such as<br>
health centres, hospitals, dispensaries<br>
and clinics. Educational activities are<br>
depicted through schools, colleges<br>
and training centres. Administrative<br>
activities, are indicated by Headquarters<br>
(HQ) offices and local government<br>
<b>Headquarter (LGHQ). Security services</b><br>
are shown through Police post and<br>
military camps. Recreational activities<br>
are denoted by play grounds, such as<br>
tennis grounds stadium, cinema halls,<br>
race tracks, camp sites, and resorts.<br>
<b>Water supply services are denoted by</b><br>
water tank, tap, large water body, or<br>
underground pipe line. Power supply<br>
services are shown through power<br>
station, power house and electricity<br>
poles. Transport and communication are<br>
indicated by road, railway line, airport,<br>
aerodrome, port, large water body, and<br>
telephone line.<br>
286<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
<b>Relief section</b><br>
<b>A relief section also known as cross</b><br>
section shows the shape of a feature<br>
(such as a mountain) viewed from the<br>
side, as if cut through with a knife. It is<br>
lateral view of the relief. It is a graphical<br>
representation of the two dimensions<br>
of the part of the mapped area in three<br>
dimensions.There are three (3) forms of<br>
cross section;<br>
<b>The fisrt is simple relief section shows</b><br>
only relief between two points. The<br>
second is annotated section that shows<br>
relief with other information that crosses<br>
between two points including vegetation,<br>
road and river. Lastly, sketch transcend<br>
section that shows only the highest and<br>
the lowest points. It portrays a rough<br>
picture of general appearance of the<br>
landscapes.<br>
<b>Procedures of constructing a relief</b><br>
section<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify the two given end points</b><br>
and mark their positions, features<br>
and heights.<br>
(ii)	 Draw a light-pencil line joining the<br>
two points.<br>
(iii)	 Transfer the line drawn on the map<br>
between two points to the graph<br>
paper<br>
(iv)	 Place or lay the straight edge of<br>
the paper along the drawn line,<br>
and mark the point of elevations<br>
on the paper. If you cross the same<br>
contour twice or more, mark it<br>
with the same number.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Choose a vertical scale which will</b><br>
show the variations in relief but<br>
without exaggerating them too<br>
much. For suitable vertical scale,<br>
use guidelines by observing the<br>
type of scale given<br>
(vi)	 Draw the vertical lines and plot<br>
the points.<br>
(vii)	 Link the dots, produced with a<br>
continuous smooth line to indicate<br>
relief.<br>
<b>Example 1</b><br>
<b>Study the contour map and answer the</b><br>
following questions.<br>
(a)	 Prepare simple cross profile<br>
between point A and B.<br>

<b>Vertical exaggeration</b><br>
<b>Vertical exaggeration is a scale that is</b><br>
used in raised- relief maps, plans and<br>
technical drawing in order to emphasize<br>
vertical features, which might be too<br>
small to identify relative horizontal<br>
scale. Alternatively, it is defined as the<br>
number of times the horizontal scale<br>
is greater than the vertical scale. For<br>
example, from the above relief section<br>
vertical exaggeration can be calculated<br>
as follows:<br>
<b>Vertical Exaggeration= Vertical scale</b><br>
<b>Horizontal scale</b><br>
<b>Or = Dominator of horizontal scale</b><br>
<b>Dominator of vertical scale</b><br>
<b>For example, if</b><br>
<b>VS = 1:10 000</b><br>
<b>HS = 1:50 000</b><br>
<b>VE = 1:10 000</b><br>
1:50 000<br>
<b>OR = 50 000</b><br>
5<br>
10 000 =<br>
<b>VE = 5 times horizontal scale</b><br>
Note: V.E Has no unit.<br>
<b>Gradient</b><br>
<b>Gradient refers to the steepness or</b><br>
gentleness of the ground in relation to the<br>
horizontal plane. Gradient is expressed<br>
as the ratio or fraction of vertical rise to<br>
the horizontal equivalent, in which the<br>
numerator represents vertical rise and the<br>
290<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Maps and map interpretation</b><br>
denominator represents the horizontal<br>
equivalent. The extent of steepness<br>
or gentleness depends on the size of<br>
denominator, in the sense that, when<br>
the denominator is too small, gradient<br>
is steeper and when the denominator is<br>
too large,  the gradient is gentle.<br>
Procedure for calculating gradient.<br>
(i)	 Identify the two points by reading<br>
grid reference or any other means<br>
of locating position as instructed in<br>
the question<br>
(ii)	 Join the two points by a straight line<br>
and measure the map distance apart<br>
(iii)	Identify the highest and the lowest<br>
point of elevation, if they are in feet<br>
change them into meters.<br>
(iv)	 Convert the map distance measured<br>
into ground distance in meters to<br>
get horizontal distance.<br>
(v)	 Divide the difference between the<br>
highest and the lowest elevation<br>
or V.I by horizontal equivalent.<br>
<b>Leave your answer in the simplest</b><br>
form of fraction such that gradient<br>
= a<br>
b  where  “a” represents vertical<br>
increase and “b” represents<br>
horizontal equivalent.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Five</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>Photography interpretation is a skill every person practices every time and</b><br>
everywhere. It is a human instinct to interpret, identify and grasp information<br>
portrayed by photographs. For instance, you may have seen friends laughing<br>
alone before a photo, picture or television advertisements. Laughs are effects of<br>
interpretations given to different phenomena. In this chapter, you will learn about<br>
types of photographs, how to make photograph and image interpretations; their<br>
characteristics, methods of object identification and how to extract information<br>
from photographs. You will also learn the differences between satellite images<br>
and normal photograph and their measurements. The competencies acquired<br>
from this chapter will enable you to interpret images and photographs or deduce<br>
information from them.<br>
Basic concept of photograph,<br>
image and sensors for imaging<br>
and photography<br>
<b>Geography students, like any other</b><br>
persons, may have faced the contradiction<br>
in distinguishing between photographs<br>
and images in their common uses. They<br>
may have been treated the two  equally<br>
in some cases. However,  photographs<br>
and images are different.<br>
<b>The fact that both photograph and image</b><br>
presents geographic information, does<br>
not make them identical.The word<br>
photography originates from two<br>
<b>Greek words, photos which means</b><br>
“Light” and graphein which means “to<br>
draw”. Specifically, photographs are<br>
images  recorded on a photographic<br>
film or storage chips of cameras. Many<br>
of the photos are recorded within 0.3<br>
micrometer (µm) to 0.9 micrometer (µm)<br>
range of visible and reflected infrared<br>
wavelengths. Therefore, photographs<br>
are images which represent an object.<br>
<b>Although the term “image” refers to any</b><br>
pictorial representation of a feature or<br>
object, a photograph must be an image.<br>
<b>All images represent some aspects of</b><br>
objects. Images are categorised based<br>
on wavelength of electromagnetic<br>
radiation. Names like X-rays pictures,<br>
infrared images, radar pictures and<br>
photographs (Figure 5.1) are commonly<br>
used for differentiation of the images<br>
in the environment. They are recorded<br>
by cameras or sensors within any<br>
wavelength. Therefore,  photograph is<br>
both a picture and an image, but not all<br>
images are photographs. Photographs<br>
297<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
are employed in many fields of science,<br>
manufacturing and business,  film and<br>
video production, recreational and mass<br>
communication.<br>
<b>Another distinction between photographs</b><br>
and images is based on sensors.  All<br>
cameras detect a particular wavelength<br>
and represent it in their own way. A<br>
camera measures the visible part of<br>
electromagnetic spectrum and represents<br>
the wavelength as a photograph. Yet there<br>
are x-ray sensors which detect that part<br>
of spectrum and produces x-ray images.<br>
<b>Therefore, all cameras are sensors but</b><br>
not all sensors are cameras.<br>
(a) Photograph<br>
(b) Infrared image<br>
(c) X-Ray<br>
<b>Figure 5. 1 Categories of images under</b><br>
different wave length<br>
<b>Historical background of photographs</b><br>
<b>The invention and development of</b><br>
photographs is not only associated<br>
with the human struggle to master their<br>
surroundings, but also society problems<br>
that  demanded photogrammetric skills<br>
for reference. Photographs were formally<br>
invented by Daguerre in 1839 and Niepce<br>
in 1927. Since then, photography and<br>
photographic fields have been growing<br>
with technological development. In the<br>
rudimentary stages of photographic<br>
technology camera were  mounted on<br>
birds and wild animals to capture static<br>
and moving objects cameras could take<br>
a minute or even more to capture image<br>
of an object (Figure 5.2).<br>
298<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Figure 5.2: A camera mounted on a pigeon</b><br>
<b>Also, the achievement of pioneering</b><br>
generation made invention of terrestrial<br>
and balloon photographing which is now<br>
called aerial photograph. The analogue<br>
<b>Photogrammetric period marked by</b><br>
introduction of operational airplanes and<br>
cameras formed a stand of the aerial<br>
survey techniques done today and the<br>
surge of stereo plotter to support reading<br>
and interpretation of photographs.<br>
<b>The invention of computers and its</b><br>
programme and the increase of the<br>
photographic adjustment theories marked<br>
the  analytical phase of history.  Recently,<br>
with the improvement of storage devices<br>
and special microprocessors or chips<br>
make easy access and use of  digital<br>
imagery, instead of photographs.<br>
<b>With the development of technology</b><br>
and increased need for environmental<br>
management, photographs are<br>
importantly integrated into spatial<br>
thinking and Geo-technologies related<br>
to global positioning systems (GPS),<br>
remote sensing, and geographic<br>
information system (GIS). Photographs<br>
as source of information in geography<br>
are now cheap and freely available from<br>
a several sources, including google earth<br>
and earth explorer.<br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Interpretation entails giving analysis to</b><br>
an image. Everyone to a certain extent,<br>
is a photo-interpreter. Interpretation,<br>
therefore, involves observing objects<br>
to recognise and analyse their shapes,<br>
alignments and relationship to the<br>
environment. Photograph interpretation<br>
is a critical examination of the presented<br>
photographic object to identify and then<br>
judge their significance and meaning.<br>
<b>Photograph interpretation is an interplay</b><br>
of several objects and interpreter’s<br>
variables. Variables which can also be<br>
considered as the principles to objective<br>
interpretation, include prior knowledge<br>
of the captured area and understanding<br>
of the subject matter. Experiences<br>
on shapes, sizes, tone, organisation,<br>
shadow, texture among others are the<br>
key variables to photo interpretation.<br>
<b>During photo interpretation, all variables</b><br>
are applied simultaneously to deduce<br>
or induce information. For example,<br>
looking at  Figure 5.3, one  recognises<br>
that the objects in the photo are lions and<br>
not elephants or hyena.This recognition<br>
is made by looking at the shape, size, own<br>
experience, tone and texture variables<br>
such as shadows, shadow point, area<br>
of maximum shadow, effects of sun’s<br>
altitude effects of sunlight.<br>
299<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Figure 5.3: Lions</b><br>
<b>Although shadow is a pertinent element</b><br>
to help photograph interpretation, it can<br>
obstruct a feature from identification.<br>
<b>Shadow can cover objects and cause</b><br>
difficulty in recognition. There are times<br>
when the relief displacement of an object<br>
matches exactly with the shadow, thus<br>
it cannot be seen. The point where the<br>
relief displacement of objects exactly<br>
matches with the shadows such that no<br>
shadow is visible is termed as no-shadow<br>
point or Hot spot (Figure 5.4).<br>
<b>Figure 5.4: Shadow point</b><br>
<b>Also, there are cases where too much</b><br>
brightness hinder identification of<br>
features in a  photograph. Depending<br>
on the time when the photography was<br>
taken, a camera records the greatest<br>
proportion of shadow accentuated by<br>
glare in areas which are away from the<br>
sun. The quality of one’s interpretation<br>
can be affected. On recording or taking<br>
picture, the place where the photographer<br>
is located determines the shape, size,<br>
tone, organization and type of the<br>
photograph.<br>
<b>Photographs can be grouped into ground</b><br>
photographs, aerial photographs and<br>
satellite images photographs.<br>
<b>Ground photographs as the name</b><br>
suggests, are camera pictures taken<br>
horizontally or obliquely from the<br>
ground level. They record objects with<br>
a camera carried by a person or vehicle<br>
which is on the ground. Photographs<br>
under this category give a horizontal<br>
view of the object recorded. Ground<br>
300<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
photographs are further categorise into ground horizontal photographs ground<br>
close-up and ground oblique photographs.<br>
<b>Ground horizontal photograph</b><br>
<b>Ground horizontal photographs are taken from the ground perspectives when  the</b><br>
axis of the camera is placed horizontally towards the object. Ground horizontal<br>
photograph is the most common type of photograph. The main characteristics<br>
of all ground horizontal photographs is that, they show side or front view of the<br>
objects clearly (Figure 5.5).<br>
<b>Figure 5.5: Ground horizontal photograph</b><br>
<b>Ground close up photograph</b><br>
<b>Ground close-up photographs also</b><br>
known as ground view photographs<br>
are camera pictures taken closer to the<br>
object. They are characterized by large<br>
objects at fore ground (Figure 5.6).<br>
<b>Ground general view photograph shows</b><br>
objects in a progressively decreasing in<br>
scale size from the camera tilt angle. The<br>
photograph shows objects whose  sizes<br>
decrease from the foreground to the back<br>
ground. The main object to be shown are<br>
somehow apart from a photographer.<br>
<b>Some of the photographs part include</b><br>
small horizon in their background. The<br>
photographs are easily divided into three<br>
main parts, namely the fore ground<br>
(front view), middle ground (middle<br>
view) and back ground (back view). A<br>
picture is always clear and does not show<br>
the top view .<br>
301<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Figure 5.6:  Ground close up photograph</b><br>
<b>Ground oblique photograph</b><br>
<b>Ground oblique photographs are taken</b><br>
from up heaved land. They can be taken<br>
from house roofs, hills or mountain at an<br>
angle between 30˚ and 60˚. The pictures<br>
can easily be divided into foreground,<br>
middle ground and background. The<br>
amount of horizon at the background<br>
depends on the camera’s tilt angle.<br>
<b>Objects from the foreground decreased</b><br>
their sizes towards the background. This<br>
top part of the object partly seen relative<br>
to the camera’s angle. This means that,<br>
photographs taken at an acute angle<br>
greater than 50˚ have their top seen than<br>
those taken at 50˚ (Figure 5.7).<br>
<b>Figure 5.7:  Ground oblique photograph</b><br>
302<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Advantages of ground oblique</b><br>
photographs<br>
<b>Ground oblique photographs have served</b><br>
a very important role in human social<br>
and economic sustainable development.<br>
<b>It provide relevant landscape data</b><br>
timely and  at  the  required  place.<br>
<b>Also, supplements data which cannot be</b><br>
collected by high geotechnologies such<br>
as the intensity and extent of flooding or<br>
the effect of locusts in a recognisable<br>
area on global geotechnologies.<br>
<b>Furthermore, oblique photographs can</b><br>
aid field sketching as features are large<br>
and can be clearly seen. However, its<br>
inability to cover a large area makes it<br>
unsuitable for interpreting features on a<br>
large area. It can easily be interpreted<br>
to provide a conclusion which cannot<br>
be generalized as the area covered may<br>
not be representative of the whole area<br>
under study. However, compared to<br>
the ground close-up, ground oblique<br>
photograph represents more features. It<br>
provides more information that could<br>
support land use planning. The relief<br>
of a given landscape is much clearer<br>
than ground horizontal photographs. It<br>
shows  top view and side of an object. It<br>
is important in producing small sketch<br>
maps in the field.<br>
<b>Disadvantages of ground oblique</b><br>
photographs<br>
<b>Ground oblique photographs tend to</b><br>
distort uniformity of the photo since<br>
the scale decreases from the foreground<br>
to the background. It is difficult to<br>
determine the scale of the photograph<br>
unless the photographer knows the size<br>
of an object. The photograph cannot<br>
be used for large area map production.<br>
<b>Horizon cannot be seen properly. It is</b><br>
important to note that the  main feature<br>
of  oblique photographs is that they show<br>
top and side or front view of the objects.<br>
<b>Ground photography analysis</b><br>
<b>The basic hints  or techniques for ground</b><br>
photograph analysis are as follows:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Identify parts, section or position</b><br>
of photograph that means fore,<br>
middle and back ground of<br>
photograph and make a note of<br>
important features shown;<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Read the caption and look at the</b><br>
position of the feature and draw<br>
a simple sketch map of the area.<br>
<b>If the objects decrease from left</b><br>
to right hand side, they indicate<br>
that the photographer was on<br>
the left side. If the objects<br>
decrease from right hand side<br>
it indicate that the photographer<br>
was  on right hand side. If the<br>
objects decrease from the fore<br>
to back ground of the photo they<br>
indicate that the photographer<br>
was in front of the objects;<br>
(iii)	 Lay a piece of tracing paper<br>
over the photograph and draw<br>
a trace diagram showing more<br>
important features;<br>
(iv)	 Write short descriptions of the<br>
features shown; and<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Describe  the process which</b><br>
have led to the formations of<br>
any landforms and land scapes.<br>

in analysing photographs. The effects of<br>
perspective objects where  objects appear<br>
much larger than those further away.<br>
<b>Each picture has a fore ground, middle</b><br>
and background section. The clarity of<br>
the photograph must also be  considered.<br>
<b>This varies due to atmospheric conditions</b><br>
and quality of the film. The caption if<br>
provided should be considered carefully.<br>
<b>The time of day which a photograph was</b><br>
taken is important as long evening may<br>
obscure important details.<br>

<b>Aerial photographs</b><br>
<b>Aerial photograph is another type of</b><br>
photographs taken from above. You<br>
should not be confused with the phrases<br>
‘Aerial photography’ and ‘Aerial<br>
photograph’.  The latter is a product<br>
and end result of the aerial photography.<br>
<b>Since its invention in 1830s, aerial</b><br>
photography has been the most effective<br>
photographs used for a map making,<br>
showing boundary location, determining<br>
road alignment and vegetation delineating<br>
among many other activities. This is the<br>
essence you should learn about aerial<br>
photographs. Aerial photographs are<br>
pictorial representations captured by<br>
sensors mounted on flying plane such<br>
as kites, helicopters and drones (Figure<br>
5.8). Formally, helicopters and airplanes<br>
were highly valuable, though recently,<br>
drones are out pacing.<br>
<b>Figure 5.8: Drone</b><br>
<b>Classification of aerial photography</b><br>
<b>On the basis of camera orientation, aerial</b><br>
photographs can be categorised on the<br>
basis of several  criteria as orientation<br>
of camera axis (such as, vertical<br>
photograph and oblique photograph);<br>
angular coverage and  emulsion type<br>
such as, panchromatic black and<br>
white photographs, color photographs,<br>
infrared,  black and white photographs<br>
and false colour composite photographs.<br>
<b>In this section, definition of types are</b><br>
based on orientation of camera axis.<br>

<b>Types of aerial photographs</b><br>
Aerial photographs can be categorised into vertical aerial photographs and oblique aerial<br>
photographs (Figure 5.9)<br>
<b>Figure 5.9: How different types of aerial photographs are taken</b><br>
<b>Vertical aerial photograph</b><br>
<b>Vertical aerial photographs also known</b><br>
as terrestrial photographs are those taken<br>
by a camera with its axis perfectly or<br>
nearly vertical. Turbulence that shake<br>
airplanes and drones can distort the<br>
verticality. They are characterised by<br>
showing only the top view of objects<br>
than side views (Figure 5.10). Extra<br>
terrestrial photograph are the picture<br>
taken by Satellite at very high altitude.<br>
<b>Figure 5.10: (a) Flying aircraft taking a</b><br>
vertical aerial photograph<br>
<b>Figure 5.10: (b) Vertical aerial photograph</b><br>
<b>Vertical aerial photograph cover</b><br>
relatively large area compared to any<br>
other type of ground photographs.<br>
<b>Though its scale is almost the same</b><br>
especially at the centre, however it<br>
decreases sideways the captured area and<br>
it is mostly small. The sizes of images<br>
are small. The centre of the photograph<br>
is known as the principal point.<br>

<b>Oblique aerial photograph</b><br>
<b>On the other hand, oblique aerial</b><br>
photographs are camera pictures taken<br>
at an angle less than 90°. They are<br>
taken by a flying object which has its<br>
axis directed between the vertical axis<br>
and horizontal plane. Oblique aerial<br>
photographs can also be divided into low<br>
oblique aerial  photographs which do not<br>
show the horizon and high oblique aerial<br>
photograph which show the horizon<br>
(Figure 5.11).<br>

<b>Figure 5.11 (b) High oblique photograph</b><br>

<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Oblique aerial photographs give a wide</b><br>
panoramic view of a large area. They<br>
provide supplementary information<br>
to ground low oblique and ground<br>
horizontal photographs. They also cover<br>
large area than ground photographs.<br>
<b>Moreover, it gives a clear picture of the</b><br>
relief of a given landscape. In addition,<br>
the scale of the photograph is medium<br>
and less uniform compared to vertical<br>
scale.<br>
<b>Based on extent of area, high oblique</b><br>
aerial photographs extract more<br>
information than ground photographs.<br>
<b>High oblique aerial photographs are</b><br>
used to supply instant data of such<br>
moving objects as vehicles, ship, train<br>
and landslide. It can clearly show<br>
layout of the different infrastructure<br>
like railway, road and houses render<br>
suitable for  land use planning.  Oblique<br>
aerial photography have been very<br>
useful in wild animal census, locating<br>
archaeological sites, strategic military<br>
and civilian surveillance and map<br>
making.<br>
<b>Advantages of vertical aerial</b><br>
photographs over maps<br>
<b>Aerial photographs  facilitate studies</b><br>
on the previously inaccessible areas<br>
and landscapes such as dense tropical<br>
forests, desert interiors and swampland.<br>
<b>Aerial photographs are significant</b><br>
decision support systems to the<br>
world’s environment management.<br>
<b>They have provided the world with</b><br>
tools to predict the future conditions<br>
of our surroundings and set policies<br>
and strategies to countermeasure them.<br>
<b>By their nature, aerial photographs are</b><br>
raw materials for land use planning,<br>
they have also supplied images to be<br>
used for planning and mapping. Plans<br>
for settlement, infrastructure layout,<br>
disaster management and evacuations,<br>
and  establishment of developmental<br>
programs and projects have made an<br>
intensive use of aerial photographs.<br>
<b>Aerial photographs also serve as raw</b><br>
materials for other land and hydrographic<br>
surveys. They are important sources of<br>
information for land surveys carried for<br>
multiple purposes. They are baseline<br>
data for other activities, projects and<br>
programme. Moreover, based on their<br>
ability to capture and record terrestrial<br>
and aerial moving objects, aerial<br>
photographs have become the tracking<br>
systems of traffic and theft management<br>
in a larger part of the world.<br>
<b>Disadvantages of vertical aerial</b><br>
photographs<br>
<b>However, set-backs associated with</b><br>
their production could be a barrier to<br>
the utilization of aerial photographs.<br>
<b>Aerial photographs, other than those</b><br>
taken by non-commercial firms, have<br>
been costly. Their availability for<br>
local land use planning for example,<br>
is impaired by their cost of availability<br>
or production. Again, hindrances<br>
from natural weather phenomenon<br>
like heavy rain, clouds, fog and mist<br>
during acquisition have decelerated the<br>
speed at which supply could match the<br>
demand.  The technological development<br>
in geographical information system<br>
(GIS) and the emergence of free<br>
online geographic data sources such as<br>

google earth, earth explorer and many<br>
others provide the justification for<br>
the deteriorating production of aerial<br>
photographs.<br>
<b>The techniques for interpreting</b><br>
vertical aerial photographs<br>
(a)	 Tone or colour or shade: This<br>
element considers the darkness<br>
and brightness of the objects.<br>
<b>Some areas like forest area, large</b><br>
water bodies, including ocean, lake,<br>
rivers appear dark in colour because<br>
of less reflective capacity. Some<br>
features like beach, sandy desert,<br>
glaciated area and all weather roads<br>
appear bright in color because of<br>
high reflective capacity.<br>
(b)	 Shape: Some objects such as  rivers<br>
and roads can be easily identified in<br>
a curved elongated shape. Pitch or<br>
stadiums are in oval rounded shape;<br>
round about and water tank can be<br>
identified with circular shape.<br>
(c)	 Texture: This refers to the roughness<br>
and smoothness of object. Some<br>
features like forest, residential area<br>
and mountainous area are shown<br>
with rough texture while features<br>
like water bodies, grassland and<br>
snow appear smooth in texture.<br>
(d)	 Pattern: This refers to arrangement<br>
of the objects, where objects can<br>
be in regular or irregular pattern.<br>
Normally, urban settlements,<br>
planted vegetation appear in regular<br>
pattern while natural forest, shanty<br>
town, mountainous regions appear<br>
in irregular pattern.<br>
(e)	 Size: If the scale of the photograph<br>
is known then objects of known size<br>
such as football pitches and tennis<br>
courts can be identified  based on<br>
their sizes.<br>
(f)	 Site: This technique enables a<br>
photo interpreter to identify a<br>
feature according to environment<br>
where such  features are found.<br>
<b>For example, feature cutting a river</b><br>
channel can be a bridge, vegetation<br>
along side of the river such as<br>
bamboo and in coastal areas can<br>
be mangroves.<br>
(g)	 Association: Is the technique of<br>
interpreting features from the<br>
existing features. For example,<br>
building with chimney indicates<br>
commercial or industrial  area.<br>
<b>Forest with wild animals such as</b><br>
<b>Giraffe and Elephant indicates</b><br>
national parks and existence  of<br>
tourism industry.<br>
(h)	 Shadow: It helps to give an<br>
impression of the depth to a<br>
vertical air photography. It  helps<br>
to distinguish objects such as raw<br>
of trees or   height of building by<br>
looking direction of  shadow, to the<br>
position of the sun and shape of<br>
the shadow. It can also be used to<br>
estimate the time when the object<br>
was photographed.<br>
(i)	 Background  information: If all<br>
attempt to identify objects have<br>
failed the interpreter must then refer<br>
to maps and written descriptions of<br>
the area.<br>

<b>The concept of satellite and image</b><br>
<b>Satellites are just man-made car-like</b><br>
carriers of sensors. They are platforms on<br>
which photographic and imaging sensors<br>
are mounted. They function like human<br>
hands or camera stands we are used to.<br>
<b>They are not sensors but they just hold</b><br>
sensors intact and provide support to<br>
sensors. There are various satellites in<br>
the space,  developed and launched by<br>
different nations and companies for<br>
different purpose (Figure  5.12).<br>
309<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Figure 5.12: Different satellites and their orbits</b><br>
<b>These platforms carry sensors (including</b><br>
camera) which collect images for<br>
different purposes. Satellite sensors have<br>
supplied billions of free and commercial<br>
images which can easily be downloaded<br>
and used for all other photographic<br>
applications. Some sensors do not<br>
produce photograph-like-images rather<br>
they provide reflective and emission<br>
signatures of the world’s resources and<br>
conditions. Every earth’s object has<br>
its unique reflection and or emission<br>
signature. This means that the reflective<br>
properties of vegetation differ from that<br>
of water while the reflective properties of<br>
human bodies are different from that of<br>
soil. Therefore, satellites provide images<br>
which can require different techniques<br>
and skills of reading and interpretation.<br>
<b>Satellite images or imageries</b><br>
<b>Satellites are placed on particular path</b><br>
called an orbit. There are three types<br>
of satellite orbits: sun-synchronous<br>
satellite (placed at about 500 km -<br>
900 km); geostationary satellites or<br>
also called geosynchronous (placed at<br>
about 36 000 km) and low Earth orbits.<br>
<b>Geostationary satellites carry sensors for</b><br>
meteorological and telecommunication<br>
monitoring. Sensors at sun synchronous<br>
satellite are generally used for remote<br>
sensing studies. Low Earth orbiting<br>
(LEO) satellites can be used for spying.<br>

<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>

including, but not limited to, visible (Figure 5.13), near infrared and mid infrared.<br>
Generally, the process of acquisition of information by a recording device that is<br>
not in physical contact with the object under study is called remote sensing.<br>
<b>Figure 5.13: Satelite image</b><br>
<b>Satellite sensors produce a multitude of image in these wavelengths which can</b><br>
be called photographs to be interpreted and used for our daily lives. They provide<br>

regional planning. They provide images on which much of decision regarding<br>
land use planning,  management and mapping can be categorised on the bases of<br>
their imaging systems. Table 5.1 summarises imaging systems.<br>

<b>Photograph and image interpretation</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation is the process</b><br>
of reading, examining and interpreting<br>
photographs for obtaining reliable<br>
information about natural or cultural<br>
features presented. It requires skills<br>
and knowledge of many professions.<br>
<b>For example, it requires knowledge on</b><br>
types of vegetation to help in analysing<br>
climate, types of soil and can be used to<br>
identify the possible crops that can grow<br>
in the area. Yet, knowledge regarding<br>
drainage can help to analyse types of<br>
rocks while knowledge on crops and their<br>
properties is helpful in analysing the type<br>
and nature of soils in the photograph.<br>

<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
Therefore, photograph or image interpretation, as used in this section, is an interplay<br>
of interpersonal qualities, environmental knowledge and many others variables. On<br>
the other hand, characteristics associated with objects on photographs are equally<br>
important in photograph   interpretation. Elements such as a shape of an object,<br>
size, tone, pattern, shadow, texture, location, height, depth, and site or situation<br>
or association have a significant role in analysing  relationships between features.<br>
<b>All elements are within the object. However, interpreter’s elements which help</b><br>
in photograph interpretation include interpreter’s knowledge of the area and of<br>
the subject.<br>
<b>For example, using the visible photograph (Figure 5.15), you can identify the</b><br>
type of image and the angle of the sensor’s axis, identify activities, soil type and<br>
many other variables.<br>
<b>Figure 5.15: Tea production farm</b><br>
<b>The photograph in Figure 5.15 shows one part of the area in Tanzania where</b><br>
tea is produced. Therefore, analysis of the photograph will be assisted by prior-<br>
knowledge of the areas associated with the cultivation of such crop. Type of crop<br>
can support to understand type of soil, climatic conditions provided you already<br>
know the characteristics of the crop seen in the photograph. The crop can also help<br>
to describe the nature of drainage of the area and the general view of the photo<br>
can help to tell the type of photograph.<br>

<b>Parts of a photograph</b><br>
In the process of interpreting images,<br>
a number of skills are applied. For<br>
example, you may see a hygrophyte<br>
or xerophyte plant on a photo and you<br>
want to describe it. In that case, you<br>
are required to section a photograph. A<br>
photograph can be sectioned horizontally<br>
into the; foreground; middle ground and<br>
the background (Figure 5.16). At the<br>
fore ground features appear to be big<br>
and clear. The middle ground is the area<br>
in the middle distance away from the<br>
camera. At this section the size of the<br>
objects appears to be relatively small but<br>
moderately clear. The area farther away<br>
from the camera with tall trees including<br>
the horizons is called the background.<br>
<b>At this part features appear to be small</b><br>
and not clear.<br>

<b>Back ground</b><br>

<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
As the need may arise, you can divide photograph further into three equal vertical<br>
parts:  left, center and right. When describing a side of a particular object,  you can<br>
then refer them as  located to the left at the  foreground centre or background  and<br>
to the  right at foreground (Table 5.4 and Figure 5.17). The centre or back ground<br>
term should be used to address the object’s location relative to other objects in<br>
the photograph. Words like top or bottom, North or East are not recommended  in<br>
photograph interpretation.<br>
