<b>Factors which may affect the quality</b><br>
photograph<br>
<b>Nature of camera to be used: Camera</b><br>
focal-lens, film used and filtration<br>
capacity are the most important factors.<br>
<b>Where, a camera with large focal-</b><br>
lens, high filtration capacity towards<br>
different aspects such as cloudcover,<br>
dust and fog may produce sharp and<br>
good photographs contrary to the<br>
camera with small focal-lens.<br>
<b>Knowledge of photographer:</b><br>
<b>Competence</b><br>
of<br>
the<br>
photographer<br>
on using camera and selecting an<br>
appropriate location for the caption also<br>
determine the quality of photograph.<br>
<b>Whereby, well trained photographer is</b><br>
capable to produce best photograph.<br>
<b>Position of photographer: When a</b><br>
photographer will be near the objects<br>
in term of distance and height, the<br>
caption may be good as well as angle<br>
at which is taken determine the quality<br>
of photograph. That is why the ground-<br>
level photograph is regarded as the best<br>
qualitative photograph.<br>
<b>Nature of the targeted area: Levelled</b><br>
area<br>
influences<br>
good<br>
photograph<br>
especially for ground photograph<br>
318<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
unlike the slope and mountainous area<br>
which may influence image distortion.<br>
<b>Weather condition: A good photograph</b><br>
should be taken at the clear day with<br>
free from clouds, fog and extream<br>
sunlight.<br>
<b>Time at which photograph taken: The</b><br>
best photograph should be neither<br>
taken during the night nor sunny noon<br>
time.<br>
<b>Limitations to quality photograph</b><br>
interpretation<br>
<b>Efficiency and the accuracy of photo</b><br>
interpretation is subject to some barriers<br>
developed from the photographing and<br>
imaging sensors. The resolution level of<br>
the image,  the quality and the clarity of<br>
the photograph may affect interpretation.<br>
<b>Photograph interpretation is affected by:</b><br>
weather condition at the time when the<br>
photograph was taken; for example cloud<br>
cover and fog may affect the quality of<br>
the photograph. Also, the angle of the<br>
sun when the photograph was taken may<br>
determine the darkness or brightness of<br>
the photographs. Moreover, quality of<br>
the camera lens (size) may determine<br>
the quality of the photograph and the<br>
type of the paper as well as the method<br>
used to print the image.<br>
<b>Other additional factors include season</b><br>
of the year,  during rainy season when<br>
vegetation is green the photograph will<br>
appear darker, but during dry season when<br>
vegetation is less green the photograph<br>
will appear brighter; the position of the<br>
camera towards the object, when the<br>
photo is taken with a camera which is<br>
near the objects, the photograph will<br>
appear clearer than when the camera, is a<br>
bit far. Colour of the object for example,<br>
objects with black colour may not be<br>
clear due to their dark tones while bright<br>
coloured objects will be clear.<br>
<b>Other general skills for the</b><br>
interpretation of photographs<br>
<b>Apart from the qualities and elements that</b><br>
can help you make a better interpretation<br>
of photographs, there are several skills<br>
you need to develop for photograph<br>
interpretation.  The understanding of<br>
how you can  determine title, estimating<br>
time and the season, identify and explain<br>
human activities, estimate the size of<br>
features, suggest location of the scenery<br>
in the place, and estimate direction<br>
are pertinently necessary in photo<br>
interpretation. These  interpretation skills<br>
could be achieved in different ways:<br>
<b>Determination of the title can be</b><br>
extracted from photograph information<br>
itself. Geographer must be careful in<br>
studying the fore ground, middle ground<br>
and the background. However, the<br>
familiarity of a photographed area can<br>
give a clue for suggestion of the title of<br>
the photograph. Similarly, time of a day<br>
when the photograph was taken can be<br>
estimated.  This can easily be detected<br>
by using direction of shadow of objects.<br>
<b>For example, if a photograph was taken</b><br>
during or around noon  shadows casted by<br>
objects will be short and centred around<br>
the object. Shadows are shortest around<br>
midday and they are longest early in the<br>
morning or late in the afternoon. The<br>
time would be morning if the shadows<br>
319<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
are in the left hand side of the object, if<br>
the shadow is on the right hand side of<br>
the object the photograph was taken in<br>
the evening.<br>
<b>Consistently, it is also possible to</b><br>
determine the hemisphere in which the<br>
photograph was taken or the direction<br>
in which the camera faced.  Beyond the<br>
tropics, the sun never gets overhead but<br>
the shadow behave similarly to those<br>
in the tropics; that is, they are shortest<br>
at midday and point pole wards. Bright<br>
clear skies with dry vegetation could<br>
indicate a dry period or season.  On the<br>
other hand, luxuriant vegetation, young<br>
crops in the field, flowering plants,<br>
and clouds in the sky could indicate a<br>
rainy period or season. Winter could be<br>
indicated by the presence of snow on<br>
the ground.<br>
<b>The type of clothing worn by the people</b><br>
can also indicate the  temperature at<br>
the time when the photograph was<br>
taken. Also, the activities shown on the<br>
photograph can help to determine the<br>
time of the year when the photograph<br>
was taken. Activities taking place in the<br>
photograph can also enable us judge the<br>
time when the photograph was taken.<br>
<b>For example, cotton is always harvested</b><br>
during noon hours, tea harvest suggest<br>
morning time since the leaves have to<br>
be taken to the factory in the evening.<br>
<b>Estimating sizes of features on a</b><br>
photograph may be difficult because<br>
unlike in maps where the scale is constant<br>
throughout the map, in photograph<br>
there is scale distortion; such as images<br>
become progressively smaller from the<br>
camera towards the middle ground and<br>
background. The   sizes of objects can<br>
also be approximated. First, the size of<br>
the familiar object is estimated.  For<br>
example, a coin or a pen may be placed<br>
against the rock or any other feature.<br>
<b>The size of the coin or pen can then be</b><br>
used to determine the size of the rock<br>
or any other object.  Heights of trees<br>
and houses, could be estimated using<br>
familiar objects in the photograph such<br>
as cars or persons.  However, comparison<br>
should only be done for features or<br>
objects which are on the same level and<br>
at the same distance from the camera.<br>
<b>For example, a person in the foreground</b><br>
will appear bigger than an elephant in<br>
the background.<br>
<b>Weather and climate do not appear</b><br>
directly on photographs.  Information<br>
has to be gathered from the photograph,<br>
both on physical features and human<br>
activities.  Conclusions can be made<br>
by judging from this information.<br>
<b>The nature of the sky by the time the</b><br>
photograph was taken can tell  the<br>
weather conditions of that time; presence<br>
or absence of clouds.  The type of clothes<br>
worn by the people in the photograph<br>
can also indicate the weather condition<br>
experienced at the time.<br>
<b>The types of houses and style of buildings</b><br>
are also indicators of the climatic<br>
condition of an area. For example short<br>
house with mud roofs in rural areas in<br>
Tanzania indicate semi-arid conditions.<br>
<b>The type of crops grown in the field and</b><br>
the type of animals kept can also help to<br>
determine the climatic condition of the<br>
area. For example, presence of sugarcane<br>
320<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
plantations would mean the area is<br>
generally warm, while the presence of<br>
tea and coffee plantation indicate cool<br>
climate. Tea and coffee also indicate<br>
presence of high rainfall (reliable and<br>
abundant) which is evenly distributed<br>
throughout the year. Sisal is a drought<br>
resistant crop; thus it indicates low<br>
rainfall in the area. Natural vegetation<br>
present on the photograph also can help to<br>
determine the climatic condition within<br>
the area. For example, dense forests with<br>
tall trees suggest that the area receives<br>
heavy and reliable rainfall. Bushes and<br>
grass land indicate dominance of low and<br>
unreliable rainfall. Table 5.5 summarises<br>

<b>Human activities</b><br>
<b>There are several activities that can</b><br>
be identified or need to be identified<br>
on a photograph. These include<br>
farming, transport, fishing, forestry and<br>
communication .<br>
1.	 Agriculture or farming: Agriculture<br>
includes crop cultivation and<br>
livestock keeping.<br>
<b>Subsistence crop farming: is</b><br>
characterised by several features<br>
such as permanent and temporary<br>
houses, the land is segmented into<br>
small portions and fields separated<br>
by hedges sisal or planted trees.<br>
<b>Subsistence farming also use</b><br>
rudimentary tools such as simple<br>
hand hoe and machetes.<br>
<b>Commercial crop farming: is</b><br>
characterised by presence of cash<br>
crops such as tea, coffee, sisal.<br>
<b>Presence of modern machineries</b><br>
and processing factories also are<br>
good evidence. Presence of feeder<br>
routes within the farm and facilities<br>
of collecting produced goods also can<br>
help to determine the type of farming.<br>
Size of the farm is large.<br>
<b>Livestock farming: may be grouped</b><br>
into traditional, ranching and<br>
dairying. Traditional livestock<br>
keeping is characterized by cattle<br>
grazing in natural grassland<br>
especially in semi-arid areas.  Also,<br>
traditional livestock keepers keep<br>
large herds of traditional breeds of<br>
cattle, sheep and goats for example<br>
Zebu cattle.<br>
<b>Ranching can be evidenced by presence</b><br>
of large field divided into paddocks,<br>
presence of cow sheds near the farm<br>
houses. Presence of windmills and<br>
water supplies, for example, water<br>
tanks, ponds, water holes, or reservoirs<br>
accompanied with cattle dips on the farm<br>
also may indicate ranching. Dairying is<br>
evidenced by high-grade cows with big<br>
udders, milk processing plants and zero<br>
grazing units.<br>
322<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Plantation farming is evidenced by a</b><br>
single crop covering extensive stretches<br>
of land, for example, sugarcane, tea,<br>
sisal and  wheat. Storage facilities near<br>
the farm also are good evidence. Many<br>
laborers in the fields for example, picking<br>
tea or coffee and nucleated settlement<br>
within the farm meant for workers can<br>
tell a lot about plantation farming.<br>
<b>There are two aspects to consider on</b><br>
describing the scale of production, these<br>
are:<br>
<b>Small scale production: it is suggested</b><br>
by presence of food crops cultivation,<br>
use of local tools such as hand hoe, axes<br>
and machete (panga), uses of family<br>
labour force and size of the farm should<br>
be small in size.<br>
<b>Large scale production: it is suggested</b><br>
by cash crop production, use of modern<br>
tools such as harvesters and area of<br>
the  farm should be large and well<br>
mechanised.<br>
2.	 Tourism: it is indicated when<br>
photograph show large water body,<br>
forest, wild animals, hills, coast,<br>
caves, historical sites or mountains.<br>
3.	 Trade: this may be evidenced by the<br>
presence of town and shop malls.<br>
4.	 Fishing:  is indicated by the presence<br>
of boat, large water body such as<br>
ocean, rivers, lake, dams and  net<br>
drying yard.<br>
5.	 Lumbering: is evidenced by the<br>
presence of forest, saw mills, chain<br>
saw and  timber or logs.<br>
6.	 Industrial manufacturing: is indicated<br>
when the photograph shows industry,<br>
ginnery or large plantation of sisal,<br>
tea or coffee. These crops require<br>
processing industry within the farm.<br>
7.	 Mining: in a photograph is  indicated<br>
by the presence of quarry or quarries.<br>
<b>Studying physical features on</b><br>
photographs<br>
<b>The landscape is formed by a several</b><br>
features.The common features we expect<br>
to find on photographs include relief,<br>
settlements and drainage.<br>
<b>Relief</b><br>
<b>Relief features are many and varied in</b><br>
nature:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Flat landscape</b><br>
<b>These can be found in both lowlands and</b><br>
uplands. In the lowlands, flat landscape<br>
is called a plain. They are normally less<br>
than 500 m above mean sea level and are<br>
associated with meandering rivers.  Flat<br>
areas in uplands (above 500 m above<br>
mean sea level) are called plateaus.<br>
<b>Plateaus always have steep sloping</b><br>
edges.<br>
<b>Sometimes flat lands can be identified by</b><br>
the type of crops grown in the area. For<br>
example, sugarcane and rice growing are<br>
practised on flat lands. Activities such<br>
as irrigation also indicate that the area<br>
is relatively flat.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Mountainous landscape</b><br>
<b>Is an elevated landform of more than</b><br>
2000 m above mean sea level is referred<br>
to as mountainous. It is characterised<br>
by steep slopes on an individual block<br>
of land or an extended mountainous<br>
range. Crops grown in an area can also<br>
be used to deduce the altitude of an area.<br>
<b>Crops such as pyrethrum, tea, wheat and</b><br>
coffee are highland crops. For example,<br>
323<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
tea is grown in the Southern highlands<br>
in places such as Mufindi (Iringa) and<br>
Rungwe (Mbeya) in Tanzania.<br>
<b>Settlements</b><br>
<b>Settlement can be divided into two major</b><br>
types: rural and urban. Rural settlement<br>
can be evidenced by simple architectural<br>
designs of semi-permanent houses.<br>
<b>The dwellings are also not planned and</b><br>
unevenly distributed.  There can also be<br>
evidence of farming or fishing activities.<br>
<b>Urban settlements can be indicated by</b><br>
permanent buildings, regular street<br>
patterns, buildings with several stores,<br>
industrial areas and warehouses, high<br>
population density, port facilities such<br>
as docks, cranes, containers, and a well-<br>
developed communication network<br>
(Figures 5.18).<br>
<b>Figure 5. 18: (a) Urban  settlements</b><br>
<b>Figure 5. 18: (b) Rural settlements</b><br>
324<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Drainage</b><br>
<b>Drainage is the natural  or artificial flow</b><br>
of water  from an area by streams  to the<br>
rivers and draining water from the land to<br>
the lakes or oceans. However, drainage<br>
may include features such as swamps,<br>
water holes, ponds and reservoirs. Water<br>
features are easily recognisable in all<br>
types of photographs. Deep waters in<br>
lakes and ocean appear darker while<br>
shallow water in rivers and continental<br>
shelves appear brighter. Presence of<br>
waterfalls and rapids indicates that the<br>
river is flowing along a steep region or<br>
landscape. River meanders show that<br>
the river is in the middle or old stages. A<br>
river delta can be identified by presence<br>
of many channels or dis-tributaries<br>
before the river enters the lake or ocean.<br>
<b>In aerial photographs river patterns, for</b><br>
example dendritic, trellis and  radial can<br>
easily be recognised.<br>

<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>Exercise 5.4</b><br>
1.	 Describe types of photographs.<br>
2.	 Account for the uses of photographs<br>
in social and economic activities.<br>
3.	 Explain factors which affect the<br>
quality of a photograph.<br>
4.	 Briefly describe the criteria for<br>
categorising the settlement patterns<br>

7.	 Ajmal is a new Geography student<br>
at Chimbe chimbe Secondary<br>
school. He wanted to interpret a<br>
ground photograph but he did not<br>
know how to begin. Give him hints<br>
on how to interpret his photograph.<br>
<b>Photographic scale</b><br>
<b>The scale of a vertical aerial photograph</b><br>
is a function of the camera’s focal length<br>
(f) and the altitude or height from which<br>
the exposure is made or of air craft.<br>
f<br>
<b>S = H</b><br>
<b>Where:</b><br>
f  = Camera’ focal length<br>
<b>H = Flying height of the aircraft</b><br>
<b>S = Scale</b><br>
<b>Note: This formula is used when  the</b><br>
photograph is taken on a flat surface.<br>
<b>Vertical aerial photographs present a</b><br>
true record of angles. However, the<br>
horizontal distances are subjected to<br>
wide variations due to the flying height<br>
of the aircraft and the focal length.<br>
<b>Flying height of the aircraft usually is</b><br>
taken from a specified elevation above<br>
mean sea level. Focal length of aerial<br>
camera varies according to specified<br>
need and purpose. In Tanzania the most<br>
common used focal lengths are the f =<br>
152 mm and 132 mm.<br>
<b>Note:  If one knows the focal length</b><br>
used and the altitude of the aircraft then<br>
it is possible to calculate an approximate<br>
scale.<br>
326<br>
<b>Practical Geography For Advanced Secondary Schools</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book  Form 5 & 6</b><br>
<b>Photograph interpretation</b><br>
<b>However, when the photograph is</b><br>
taken on a rough surface, the following<br>
formula is used compute the scale.<br>
<b>S =</b><br>
f<br>
<b>H −h</b><br>
Where;<br>
h = Average elevation of the photographed<br>
area.<br>
<b>New line scale average is applicable when</b><br>
the altitude of the aerial photographs is<br>
unknown or not given. In such cases<br>
scale may be determined as shown in<br>
the following example.<br>

<b>Glossary</b><br>
<b>Bench mark</b><br>
a reference mark of known elevation cut or set in stone, concrete<br>
or other durable  and used in the determination of altitudes.<br>
<b>Data</b><br>
information or facts about a particular phenomenon.<br>
<b>Database</b><br>
an electronic memory.<br>
<b>Electromagnetic</b><br>
consisting of electromagnetism.<br>
<b>Multi spectral</b><br>
operating in or involving several parts of the electromagnetic<br>
spectrum occurring or recurring simultaneously.<br>
<b>Parameter</b><br>
a descriptive property of the population while statistic is a<br>
descriptive property of a  sample.<br>
<b>Population</b><br>
a group of people, events, things, or other phenomena that you are<br>
most interested in; it is often the “who” or “what” that you want<br>
to be able to say something about at the end of your study. The<br>
set of all elements that share one or more characteristics which<br>
we wish to make an inference.<br>
<b>Radar</b><br>
an instrument which discovers the position or speed of objects<br>
such as aircraft or ships when they cannot be detected, by using<br>
radio signals.<br>
<b>Radiometer</b><br>
instrument for the detection or measurement of radiant energy<br>
<b>Sample</b><br>
a smaller (but hopefully representative) collection of units  from<br>
a population used to determine truths about that population in a<br>
research process.<br>
<b>Satellite</b><br>
object which has been sent into space in order to collect information<br>
or to  be part of a communication system.<br>
<b>Scan</b><br>
examine it using a machine that can show or find things inside it<br>
that cannot be seen from the outside.<br>
<b>Sensor</b><br>
instrument which reacts to certain physical conditions or impressions<br>
such as heat or light, and which is used to provide information.<br>
<b>Spectrum</b><br>
a range of different colours which is produced when light passes<br>
through a glass prism or through a drop of water.<br>
<b>Trigonometric station</b><br>
a fixed surveying station, used in geodetic surveying and<br>
other surveying projects in its vicinity<br>
<b>Variable</b><br>
is any characteristic, number or quantity of a person, object or<br>
phenomenon that can be measured or counted.<br>
<b>Wavelength</b><br>
distance between a part of a wave of energy such as light or sound.<br>
<b>X-rays</b><br>
a type of radiation that can pass through most solid materials.<br>
